HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:
HARTFORD: A LAB FOR COMBATING GANGS
Like any city where gangs have run rampant, Hartford has taken a beating in the last few years. In response, Connecticut's capital has become a virtual laboratory for methods of quelling gang violence. Its efforts include a joint Federal, state and local task force, a beefed-up gang prosecution unit and many police officers working lots of overtime. It is emphasizing after-school and recreational programs and has an institute to mediate gang disputes. In one experiment, a member of the city's largest gang, Los Solidos, makes $25,000 a year as youth coordinator for the Hartford Housing Authority. His job is to steer street toughs to after-school programs and away from drugs and the gang life. Page 1.
 

DRAFT PROPOSAL TO CUT MEDICAID COSTS
A month before New York's new Republican administration must propose its first budget, Governor-elect George E. Pataki's chief adviser on health care has drafted a proposal to cut more than $1 billion from Medicaid, the costly and politically sensitive health program for the poor, disabled and elderly. The proposed cuts, sought by Lieut. Governor-elect Elizabeth P. McCaughey, are large but not so different from those explored by Governor Cuomo. Page 1.
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
BURN VICTIMS FACE A STRUGGLE
 Twelve people who were riders on a subway train in lower Manhattan last Wednesday when a firebomb turned their car into a furnace are patients in the burn center at New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center. Each faces a long, painful struggle, and doctors caution that a few may not survive the fight. One of those most seriously injured is Edward J. Leary, 49, the man who is accused of carrying the bomb. He drifts in and out of consciousness, his senses dulled by pain and morphine. Mr. Leary and two others, Renfield Edey, 60, and Brenda Dowdell, 40, remained in critical condition yesterday. Page 43.
 
ANOTHER POLICE OFFICER COMMITS SUICIDE
 A police officer on a meal break in a Times Square restaurant became suddenly distraught early Christmas morning, drew his .38-caliber service revolver and killed himself with a single shot to the head, the police said. The suicide was the 12th this year by a city police officer, the highest number in recent history. A police spokesman said the officer had recently been through a divorce and was having emotional problems. Page 43.
 
WHAT JUST WENT UP MAY SOON COME DOWN
 While New Yorkers weren't looking last week, workers completed the four-year, $70 million renovation of part of the West Side Highway and gave motorists a newly smooth ride between 57th and 72d Streets. Now the city can get ready for the next phase of renovation: tearing down the renovated road and building a new one. The refurbished road will indeed come down if Donald Trump and partners go ahead with their planned housing development on the Hudson River. Metro Matters, Page 43.
 
FOR SOME, A SEASON FOR SHOPLIFTING
 Christmas was much like any other day in the arraignment section of Criminal Court in Manhattan, with a few exceptions. There were, however, more cases than usual for a holiday, and many more people were charged with shoplifting. There were about 20 arraignments that dealt with someone's stealing something from a store, from pants to a videotape. "I don't know whether this is a result of the economy or the season," Acting Supreme Court Justice Paul P. E. Bookson said, "but there was a high preponderance of shoplifting." Page 42.
 
REGION
 
A CITY COUNCIL UNDER SCRUTINY
 Recent crackdowns on building code violations, property tax delinquents and drug-infested taverns in Asbury Park, N.J., have offered glimmerings of hope that the gloom hanging over the scruffy city of 16,273 will eventually lift. But the City Council that presided over those changes is up for review, accused of being insensitive to minorities and unrepresentative of them. Voters will decide at a special referendum on Jan. 10 whether to change the present form of government. If the change is approved, a special election will be held in the spring for the entire government. Page 44.

HEADLINE: Gov. Weicker Packs His Bags, With No Room for Nostalgia

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HARTFORD, Dec. 23

 BODY:
Winter sun streams through the windows onto the half-empty boxes of awards and mementos that Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. has begun packing. His heavy wooden desk sits ready to be cleared and carted off, along with his bound Watergate Committee volumes and lamps made from 105-millimeter shell casings -- all possessions with which he surrounded himself in office.
Most people would be forgiven a little sticky sentimentality just now. But Mr. Weicker has never been most people, and in these closing days of his administration, he shows no signs of looking back with even the remotest trace of gauzy haze. Even posing the question of whether, at 63, he feels nostalgic about his three decades in public life is to invite a typical Weicker jeremiad. Not only does he feel no nostalgia himself, he thinks no one else should, either. It's a societal flaw. 
 "I hate nostalgia with a passion," he said. "This country is surfeited with it right now. That's one of our problems."
"We just sit there and wallow in the good old days, which, quite frankly, weren't necessarily so good for too many people," he added.
Mr. Weicker has made a career of saying just what is on his mind and disregarding convention as a sort of counterpoint soloist in a world of ensemble players. Now at what may be his exit from the public stage, he seems utterly unbowed in that role, still blowing his own horn in a way that has infuriated and captivated a generation of Connecticut voters.
For example, here is Mr. Weicker on his accomplishments as a Senator and as a Governor: "Most of the disabilities laws that are on the books of the United States today were written by Lowell Weicker as the lead horse and certainly with the help of many others. And the recovery of Connecticut, that was Lowell Weicker -- so you can achieve in both areas."
Speaking of the future, Mr. Weicker said in an interview: "I intend to go ahead and make life as tough as I can for the ideas I know are fraudulent. I don't intend to in any way remain silent -- no way."
Mr. Weicker, who chose not to run for a second term, spent 18 years in the United States Senate as a Republican who liked to fight with Republicans (especially those in the Nixon Administration during the Watergate era) and four tumultuous years as Connecticut's first independent third-party Governor in a century, battling both parties for his own agenda.
That agenda included a drastic overhaul of the state revenue system, the imposition of an income tax in 1991 and the introduction last year of a plan to encourage racial and economic integration of public schools. He is also credited with having more or less single-handedly foiled a legislative plan to legalize casino gambling in Bridgeport.
And although the independent A Connecticut Party that he founded in 1990 has not taken off, Democrats and Republicans say that by hastening the erosion of the two-party system and proving that an independent administration can work, Mr. Weicker has also left a permanent mark.
And he apparently has enough bile left for several careers to come. Without actually saying that he will run for President as a third-party independent in 1996, or that he even wants to, he has kept the door wide open for a draft.
Mr. Weicker has been equally vague about which political allies or organizations might be involved in such a bid, but he misses no opportunity to identify its niche -- centrist, filling the void he believes has been left in politics by the Republican Party's surge to the right.
Meanwhile, his popularity in Connecticut is near its four-year high and his reputation for having advanced his agenda in the legislature without the benefit of a single member of his own party has already taken on a patina of legend, even among his enemies.
"You've got to grant the Governor this -- he is probably the most able politician seen in Hartford in decades," said State Representative Carl J. Schiessl, a Democrat who opposed Mr. Weicker on most major issues. "I haven't been able to pin a label on it besides calling it Weicker's Way."
But now Mr. Weicker's private life will get first claim, and there is a note of ambivalence as he speaks about what he really wants from the future. He takes over as chairman of a Maryland-based health care company on Jan. 5, the day after the inauguration of his successor, John G. Rowland. He plans to teach, and to promote his autobiography, which is to be published next year.
He also wants to spend more time with his six grandchildren and in his beloved St. Croix, V.I., indulging his passion for scuba diving.
He went through two marriages in his younger days, and by his own admission was more or less an absentee father for the first three of his seven sons while he pursued his career. He intends to make up for some of that now, he says.
"I'm 63 years old, which is an honorable age and a grandparently age," Mr. Weicker said. "And I don't see myself spending as much time as grandparents do with their grandkids. This is a very real thing for me because I love them, I like being with young people. I've given up on my own damn generation. As far as I'm concerned, we're all too grumpy -- we're all hanging on to things long past where we ought to be hanging on to them."
This is how Mr. Weicker expresses himself: saying what he cares about is almost invariably followed by the quick knife thrust at what he condemns. Even now, in his last days in office, the world is a place of dark and light where everything that deserves praise is linked with something that merits a withering attack.
Leaning back in his chair, Mr. Weicker reminisced, for example, about diving off the coast of Cuba with Fidel Castro in the early 1980's. (Mr. Castro is a hold-your-breath diver, Mr. Weicker said, with huge lung capacity despite the cigars.) But the vivid picture was quickly followed by sharp jab at United States policy toward Cuba.
In talking about his faith in the future of democracy and the younger generation, he expressed a belief in the American spirit of renewal and common sense that became, as he continued, an attack on the Republican Party, which Mr. Weicker believes has moved so far to the right it has insured its own self-destruction. To fulfill their promise to cut taxes, he said, the Republicans will have to cut spending on middle-class programs like Social Security or see the Federal deficit grow sharply. And each course will rebound against them.
"I compliment my friends in the Republican Party, the far right, for what they achieved," he said, referring to the G.O.P. majorities in Congress. But speaking of the Republicans' agenda, he added, "I don't think it makes any sense, and because it doesn't make any sense it's only a matter of time before it's bounced out."
As for his own Washington plans, Mr. Weicker sidesteps every question about whether he will actively initiate a try for the Presidency.
"I don't want to foreclose anything when it comes down to that," he said. "But have I all of sudden had pangs of regret and want to get back in? No. I'm racing toward my new life."

GRAPHIC: Photo: "I hate nostalgia with a passion," said Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. of Connecticut. "This country is surfeited with it right now. That's one of our problems." (Steve Miller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: China-GATT Trade Rift Spurs Debate

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: BEIJING, Dec. 23

 BODY:
In the wake of the collapse of China's bid to join the World Trade Organization, some economists are wondering whether China's top Communist Party leaders gave their trade negotiators the kind of authority and latitude necessary to make tough compromises.
Those compromises might have had an important effect on some of China's most powerful ministries, state-owned enterprises and military industries. 
 "The issue is whether President Jiang Zemin was involved," said a Western economist, who spoke on condition of anonymity. "Basically, a negotiation like that requires that kind of political clout, someone who will say this is in the best interest of the country in the long run even though there are going to be winners and losers."
After eight months of negotiations with China, the governing body of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, which will soon be reborn as the World Trade Organization, decided early last week that negotiations for China's acceptance as a member nation could not be concluded this year.
The governing body made an offer to resume the talks in February, but China has not yet responded. GATT delegates estimated it could take another six months to negotiate China's application.
Some Western diplomats here said they saw a strong resemblance between China's approach to GATT and the country's successful strategy last spring to persuade President Clinton to put trade relations with China ahead of American concerns about human rights.
In both cases, China put minimal concessions on the table, then dug in its heels, demanding that the international community recognize its strategic and economic importance in the world as the most important criterion for acceding to its demands.
In the case of GATT, Chinese officials may have believed incorrectly that European Union leaders, and perhaps even American officials, would make a purely political decision to let China enter the new trade organization despite a highly discriminatory tariff and trade structure.
A World Bank study on trade reform earlier this year said that "China's tariffs remain higher, more numerous and more dispersed than those of most other large developing countries."
One international banker here noted that the Communist Party laid out a strong agenda for economic reforms in November 1993 -- including trade reform and the convertibility of China's currency. But Chinese negotiators might have been instructed to first measure the resolve of their negotiating partners in GATT before top Chinese leaders gave approval for major new concessions.
Western experts say a substantial reassessment of China's negotiating position by its top leaders will be needed before Beijing's negotiating team can return to Geneva with concessions.
And such a reassessment is likely to take months because of the approaching Chinese New Year and, more importantly, because of the crisis with the health of Deng Xiaoping, China's senior leader, whose condition has deteriorated through the fall.
With an undercurrent of official rancor, Beijing reiterated this week that China remained committed to economic reform and trade liberalization, its inability to join the trade group notwithstanding.
"China has already made the maximum concessions that a developing country can bear at its current state of development," a Foreign Ministry spokesman, Chen Jian, said on Thursday. China's failure to enter the trading organization, he said, "was due to the fact that certain signatory parties insisted on the excessive demands."
He added that it was "most unwise to exclude" China, which has a population of 1.2 billion and the world's 11th-largest trade volume. "The global trade system cannot be complete and its smooth operation is bound to be affected, which will do no good to all," he said.
But Mr. Chen's warning may conceal the fact that China was only prepared to make a minimal set of concessions during this round of negotiations, Western economists said.
"It is very difficult to accede to international trading rules when your existing policies are in conflict with those rules," said a Western economist who served as a negotiator during the GATT talks. "It means you have to change your policies," he added.
Mr. Chen said: "China will continue to adhere to its principle of reform and opening to the outside world. However, China will proceed with its reform and opening up according to its own schedule."
Indeed, in the last year, China's powerful State Planning Commission has appeared to be adding -- not subtracting -- protectionist measures with its "pillar industries" plan to develop state-owned conglomerates under preferential policies that would protect them from both foreign ownership and foreign competition.
Pillar industries will include the automobile industry, petrochemicals, telecommunications and transportation.
Asked whether China would resume negotiations in February, which is in the midst of China's month-long spring festival holiday, Mr. Chen said, "That's quite a specific matter which needs to be decided upon."

HEADLINE: U.S. Losses In Mexico Assessed

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
The steep decline in the value of the Mexican peso has resulted in a potential loss of roughly $8 to $10 billion for American investors in Mexican stocks and bonds, according to estimates by Wall Street investment experts.
American owners of Mexican stocks took the biggest hit, suffering not only from the peso's devaluation but also from falling stock prices. Their holdings were worth roughly $20 billion on Dec. 1 and $13.5 billion on Friday, the experts estimated. In addition, the $12 billion to $15 billion in peso notes and bonds held by Americans shed $3 billion or so as a result of last week's devaluation. 
 "These losses are spread out over a lot of people and institutions in the United States," said Lawrence Brainard, head of research for Chase Securities Emerging Markets Group, a subsidiary of Chase Manhattan Bank. "And now these investors are trying to figure out whether to stay in Mexico or sell their securities, take their losses and leave."
Mr. Brainard, Nariman Behravesh, international economist at DRI/McGraw-Hill, and Brian Barish, head of emerging market research at Lazard Freres, said Americans represent $45 billion to $50 billion of the $73 billion in foreign investment in Mexico. Roughly $15 billion is in direct investment in factories, machinery and buildings -- investments that are largely immune to devaluation, particularly if their goal is to make goods for export from Mexico. The remaining $30 billion to $35 billion is in stocks, Government bonds and other forms of interest-bearing peso securities.
The $8 billion to $10 billion in losses, if they are realized, are a significant sum, and Mexico's economic future might hinge on whether American investors will decamp, at least for a while, as they did after financial crises, in the 1970's and 1980's. Already, Mexican Government officials and some American bankers and financiers are arguing that nothing has really changed; that the Mexican peso, having lost nearly 27 percent of its worth in American dollars in a single week, is now at its proper level, and Americans can once again invest safely and profitably in Mexico.
That was the message from Mexico's Finance Minister, Jaime Serra Puche, when he met with American executives at the Federal Reserve Bank in New York on Friday. He said that Mexico would hold to policies that restrict domestic credit, balance the budget and maintain the peso's value, now that the pressure on the peso had been relieved. While some Americans at the meeting raised doubts about the future, others backed Mr. Serra's effort to get investment back on track.
"If the Mexican Government implements the program as outlined by Minister Serra, this should give additional opportunities for accelerated Mexican export growth and therefore make Mexico attractive for investment," said William R. Rhodes, vice chairman of Citibank, a major holder of Mexican debt.
Mutual funds and pension funds, the biggest holders of Mexican debt, apparently suffered the biggest losses from last week's fall in the peso, and some brokerage houses that trade in Mexican securities also may have lost money. No one has announced actual losses from the sale of securities as prices dropped last week, but there were broad indications of paper losses.
Fidelity Investments, the big Boston-based mutual fund company and one of the biggest investors in Mexican stocks and bonds, said that eight of its funds had held about $800 million in Mexican securities on Dec. 19, the day before the Mexican Government announced a 14 percent devaluation in the peso, beginning a process that accelerated when the Government decided to float the peso, which finished the week down nearly 27 percent.
For Fidelity, that would be roughly a $215 million loss just from devaluation if all of the securities were held. A Fidelity spokeswoman did not say what might have been sold in the heavy trading last week.
Clients of Bear, Stearns & Company held more than $1 billion in Mexican securities, according to Alan Greenberg, chairman of the Wall Street house. That would mean a paper loss of about $265 million or more as a result of the fall in the peso, if the securities were held all last week.
Bear, Stearns is one of the most active American investment houses in the Mexican market, trading not only for clients but investing tens of millions of dollars of its own money. Mr. Greenberg and Warren Spector, the firm's executive vice president, declined to say if Bear, Stearns itself had lost money as a result of the falling peso, but in a telephone interview on Friday they suggested that the firm had not.
"We are very pleased with our performance," Mr. Spector said, "and we are not reducing our commitment to Latin America."
Mr. Spector and Mr. Greenberg in effect lined up with Mr. Rhodes in arguing that Mexico remains a good investment, because its economy is slowly gaining in efficiency and industrial production. "People will get their confidence back and once again they'll be buying Mexican securities," Mr. Greenberg said.
The Mexicans certainly hope so. American investors have provided the dollars to pay for much of their estimated $29 billion deficit in trade and other transactions this year. American investors are also big purchasers of the bonds floated for spending on roads, harbors, airports, power stations and the like.
But others on Wall Street are less sanguine than Mr. Greenberg and Mr. Rhodes. "The Mexican Government still needs to spell out a coherent set of economic policies given that the peso has been devalued," Mr. Brainard said.
Taking a similar view, Moody's Investors Service reaffirmed on Friday its ratings of Mexican securities as less than investment grade.
"Our ratings reflected and still reflect a structural dilemma in Mexico," said David Levey, managing director for sovereign risk at Moody's. "The dilemma was the overvalued peso which drew money and imports into Mexico and brought about the huge deficit in international transactions. Correcting this current account deficit is likely to require a recession or a number of years of slower growth than many people would like."

HEADLINE: Some Roses Brighten UpCourt Date For a Wife

BYLINE: By GEORGE JAMES 

 BODY:
Christmas was much like any other day in the arraignment section of Criminal Court in Manhattan. Except for the man with the roses and the large number of shoplifting cases.
Usually, there is little variety in the transgressions that come before the bench -- from disorderly conduct to murder -- or in the proceedings. But this year appeared to be a little different. First, there was the husband who brought two dozen roses for his wife, who was being arraigned on a charge of misdemeanor drug possession. 
 "I was told she would definitely be here today," said Joe Rabbitt, a forklift operator who had been waiting for his 23-year-old wife, Jennifer, to appear for arraignment since she was arrested on Friday evening on her way to his parents' house. "I wanted to cheer her up and show that I love her," he said.
Mrs. Rabbitt, who said she had not known there were two grams of cocaine in the glove compartment of her car, was released on her own recognizance for a hearing in January. In the hallway afterward, she practically leaped into her husband's arms.
"I must say," said Acting Justice Paul E. Bookson of State Supreme Court, "this is the first time I ever saw someone come into court with flowers."
The day at the Criminal Courts Building at 100 Centre Street was also different because there were more cases than is usual on a holiday and many more people were charged with shoplifting offenses.
Last Christmas, during the day court session, there were 56 arraignments, said Steven Kufs, deputy chief clerk of arraignments. There were more than 70 yesterday between 10 A.M. and a little after 5 P.M. Of those, about 20 dealt with someone stealing something, from pants to a videotape.
"I don't know whether this is a result of the economy or the season," Justice Bookson said, "but there was a high preponderance of shoplifting."
Justice Bookson, a jurist for 18 years, said people tend to think of life in court on a holiday as one in which the unusual occurs. But, he said, "It's rare that you get a story from a defendant who says, 'I jumped the turnstile, your honor, because I was late and so I could get to midnight Mass.' "
Alan Flexer, one of three Legal Aid lawyers who represented defendants during the day session, said of Christmas, "It's just another day in the system, because there's nothing that happened today that didn't happen yesterday or the day before."
Mr. Flexer, a spirited man with red hair and a red mustache who has been with Legal Aid since 1987, said that occasionally a case is directly related to the holiday. He recalled a fight between parents some years ago over a place in line to see Santa Claus.
Like Justice Bookson and other officers of the court, Mr. Flexer thought yesterday was "unusually busy" for Christmas. The eight rows for the public were packed at times with people waiting to see relatives or friends who had been in detention for as long as two days while the cases worked their way from a precinct through central booking to the complaint room and into the clerk's office and the court.
A Russian man and woman waited for their son, who had been arrested for allegedly shoplifting a videotape. Sometimes, women with infants left the room when the children o cried.
A defendant who had a record for shoplifting was charged with petty larceny for allegedly taking six pairs of pants from Macy's.
"Six pairs of pants, six months," said the judge. But the defense decided not to plead, and the man was remanded with a high bail.
The Russian man and woman who had been sitting in the arraignment part for two days finally caught sight of their son, a teen-ager with a long pony tail. The father, who wore a yarmulke, stood so that his son could see him.
The youth had taken the videotape because, according to his statement read by Assistant District Attorney Aytan Bellin, "I thought it was for free."
The defense said the boy had no arrest record and the parents were in court.
"Do you speak English?" Justice Bookson asked.
"Yes," the boy replied.
He adjourned the case in contemplation of dismissal, meaning that if the defendant stayed clear of the law for the next six months, charges would be dropped.
"America is a wonderful country," the judge told him, "but you can't take things for nothing. Remember that from now on."
Justice Bookson said that people expect judges to take a holiday into consideration and to be more compassionate, but, he said, "I don't make a deliberation based on the day."

HEADLINE: Japan Posts 1994 Gains For Electronics Output

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Dec. 25

 BODY:
Japan's electronics industry, creeping back from a long slump, has registered an increase in production for 1994 for the first time in three years, according to the Electronic Industries Association of Japan.
The gain resulted from a slight improvement in demand for electronics products in Japan, but came mainly from strong shipments to the United States and Asia of microprocessors and flat-panel displays to be installed in personal computers and other products. 
 Production of consumer electronics equipment in Japan, though, fell nearly 9.7 percent in 1994 as Japanese companies shifted their manufacturing of television sets, videocassette recorders and stereo systems to countries with lower wages, in part to escape the effects of the strong Japanese yen.
In the fiscal year that ended in March, 48 percent of consumer VCR's and 72 percent of color televisions made by Japanese companies were made outside Japan, the trade group said.
The figures, compiled in the association's annual production summary and forecast for the new year and released last week, highlight changes that have been occurring in Japan's electronics industry.
While "made in Japan" was once almost synonymous with audio and video products, such products now account for only 13 percent of Japan's total electronics production, down from nearly 19 percent in 1988. Instead, Japan is becoming a maker of industrial equipment and of sophisticated components that are assembled into final products elsewhere.
Japan's overall electronics industry, which once seemed unstoppable, has been hurt by the three-year slump in Japan's economy, by the rising yen, which makes Japanese exports less competitive, and by a lack of big new hit products to follow the VCR and compact disk. In areas like computers and telecommunications, Japan's international competitiveness is impeded by a weakness in software development.
Total production of electronics products in Japan dropped about 17 percent from its peak in 1991 to its 1993 level of 20.83 trillion yen, or about $208 billion at today's exchange rates.
For 1994, production is estimated to have climbed 2.0 percent, to 21.25 trillion yen, or about $212 billion, the association said. It forecast that an improved economy in Japan will lead to a further growth of 2.2 percent in 1995.
Price cuts and deregulation helped spur demand for some products. Production of computers and related equipment grew for the first time in three years, rising 5.5 percent, to 5.05 trillion yen, or about $50 billion. Demand for personal computers spurted this year because formerly sky-high prices were lowered in a price war started by the entry into the market here of American manufacturers like Compaq and Dell.
Production of communications equipment is also estimated to have grown slightly in 1994, largely because of a strong gain in production of cellular telephones after mobile phone service was partly deregulated in Japan and prices dropped.
Production of components and devices is estimated to have risen 6.4 percent in 1994, to 8.46 trillion yen, or $84 billion, and is forecast to grow 4.5 percent in 1995. Spurred by strong demand for PC's in the United States, Japan's production of liquid crystal displays shot up 38.5 percent while output of integrated circuits climbed 13.5 percent. But production of less sophisticated components shrank as manufacturing shifted offshore.
In its predictions for next year the industry association sees a continuation of this year's trends. Consumer electronics production is forecast to shrink another 5.3 percent. Production of industrial equipment is forecast to increase 2.4 percent because of growth in computers and mobile communications. And component and device production is expected to grow 4.5 percent.

HEADLINE: METRO MATTERS;
New Political Landscape May Alter Highway Plan

BYLINE: By Joyce Purnick 

 BODY:
WHILE New Yorkers weren't looking last week, workers completed the four-year, $70 million renovation of part of the West Side Highway and gave motorists a newly smooth ride between 57th and 72d Streets. Now the city can get ready for the next phase of renovation: tearing down the renovated road and building a new one.
Talk about planned obsolescence! But the refurbished road will indeed come down if Donald Trump and partners go ahead as planned with the Riverside South housing development on the Hudson River. 
 Right now, the site between 59th and 72d Streets is 74.6 acres of weeds, rubbish, a temporary garden and plenty of promise. Between the property and the river stands an elevated piece of the West Side Highway -- the piece just renovated.
In the late 1980's, some civic groups came up with the ambitious idea of dropping that highway segment down to ground level, curving it toward the east and building a waterfront park above so that people could stroll to the river without crossing a highway. Highways separate New Yorkers from their waterfront throughout the city; here was a chance to break that pattern.
At the time, nobody in the city and state governments paid much attention, and soon the highway fell into such dangerous disrepair that it had to be strengthened before it collapsed. There was no time to redesign it. If there had been a realistic opportunity to move the highway down and away from the river, it was lost.
MEANWHILE, a coalition of the civic groups resisting Mr. Trump's plans to build what he originally called Television City in 1985 negotiated a peace treaty appealing to both sides. Mr. Trump reduced the size of his project significantly, and included a generous waterfront park.
But there remained the problem of the elevated highway. The park could easily go under the present highway, but that is not what anyone involved wants. Mr. Trump and associates seek unobstructed views from their apartment towers. And civic groups resent obstruction of the waterfront.
"I think we would get a terrible park and a terrible bunch of apartments if the highway isn't moved," said Richard A. Kahan, board chairman of the Riverside South Planning Corporation. "The project would be in serious jeopardy. You're talking about marketing apartments overlooking a park and a river, versus marketing apartments overlooking an elevated highway."
What to do? The eclectic supporters of Riverside South came up with a novel approach: in effect, they turned back the clock and proceeded as if the renovation hadn't happened.
At their urging, the State Urban Development Corporation has hired consultants to work on an environmental impact statement on the new development -- a prerequisite to building the project and a new road. A few years ago, Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan had money designated in the Federal highway bill to pay for that study and design -- about $22 million for a demonstration project, of which approximately $7 million has been allocated for the environmental study.
The idea is to persuade Federal, state and city officials to replace the elevated road with a new road costing at least $100 million, 80 percent in Federal funds, 20 percent from the state.
BUT it isn't quite that simple. Officials in Washington -- a new, Republican Washington -- will have to be convinced that the project rates Federal dollars.
Will Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, supportive so far, want to send highway money -- often considered political pork -- to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's New York City? After all, it was Mr. D'Amato who regaled the Senate with his anti-pork rendition of "Old MacDonald." And other parts of New York will be competing for any Federal highway money allocated to the state. Will Buffalo or Peekskill support giving West Siders a nice park and apartment dwellers a great view? Will George E. Pataki?
The just-concluded renovation should keep the highway strong and safe for at least 25 years. It is hard to imagine elected officials agreeing to tear it down so soon after its completion. Then again, nobody can say when construction of Riverside South, on the drawing boards in one or another configuration since the 70's, will be built.
"The real question is how many years it is when the project really gets going and whether the highway has really amortized its cost by then," said the Manhattan Borough President, Ruth W. Messinger, a supporter of the Riverside South compact. "Whether it will make sense, given the funds available and the priorities, to build a park that is out from under the highway is something another generation of people will decide."
Another generation? Ms. Messinger, who recognizes looming political trouble when she sees it, is either yielding to realism or hedging. Or maybe she is hoping.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Complaint, Injunction And Then Play Ball?

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
The quickest way to get the real major league baseball players back on the field may be through a double play -- complaint and injunction.
If the National Labor Relations Board issues a complaint against the club owners, then seeks and is granted an injunction in Federal court, players most likely would end their strike and return to work. 
 Donald Fehr, the players' labor leader, declined to comment yesterday on the question of whether he would recommend that the players return under those circumstances. "I won't comment on what I would do until we get there," he said.
But if he is consistent with his testimony last September before a subcommittee of the House of Representatives, Fehr would recommend an end to the strike if a judge granted an injunction. Based on their comments in the past, other union officials would apparently favor that recommendation as well.
There also is the possibility that given the sudden interest in the lingering dispute among its members, Congress conceivably could move faster than anyone would have anticipated before the owners implemented their salary cap.
If both the Senate and the House put legislation on a fast track and its supporters managed to reduce what always has been substantial opposition to such action, passage of a bill limiting baseball's exemption from antitrust laws could send the players back to work.
Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, Democrat of New York, stunned baseball people last Friday with his announcement that he would use his seniority to introduce legislation on Jan. 4, the first day of the new term of Congress, to repeal the exemption.
In the last term of Congress, bills were introduced in both the Senate and the House, but one bill didn't get out of committee and another ran out of time.
Fehr and Orel Hershiser, the Los Angeles Dodgers' pitcher, testified before the House subcommittee on economic and commercial law on Sept. 21 on the merits of the latter bill, sponsored by Mike Synar, an Oklahoma Democrat, and Jim Bunning, the former pitcher and a Kentucky Republican.
The bill proposed to subject baseball to the antitrust laws in labor matters if the owners unilaterally imposed new terms and conditions. As originally written, it also would have granted the players an automatic stay of the new economic system if they challenged it in court.
Both Fehr and Hershiser, a member of the union's negotiating subcommittee, told the Congressmen they would support an end to the strike if the bill were passed. But before the subcommittee sent it to the House Judiciary Committee, the bill was amended to remove the automatic-stay provision. That tempered Fehr's view. He said the bill "could still be a positive step," but that he didn't know if he could make the same recommendation.
Nevertheless, an injunction granted by a Federal judge would serve the same purpose as the original automatic-stay provision. It would block the salary cap and restore the old system while the merits of the N.L.R.B. complaint were argued before an administrative law judge.
The players then could end their strike and return to work while awaiting the outcome of the hearing and subsequent appeals.
The first step would be for Daniel Silverman, regional director of the New York office, to issue a complaint. If an investigation of the unfair labor-practice charge the union plans to file tomorrow or Wednesday results in a complaint, Silverman could issue it by the middle of February.
If he also recommended that the board seek an injunction and the general counsel and the board agreed, a judge could hear the case early in March. The owners, of course, would throw everything they have into convincing the judge that the players would not suffer irreparable harm if an injunction were not granted.
"It would be very hard to get an injunction," a union lawyer acknowledged, preferring not to underestimate the task.
But if the judge granted an injunction, the players could end the strike and be back on the field before the end of spring training and be ready to begin next season on time, April 3.
They also could do that if Congress worked quickly and enacted the Moynihan legislation. Based on the current climate, either one of those avenues would figure to result in baseball as it has been played before negotiation would.
Bud Selig, the acting commissioner, and members of the owners' negotiating committee, have stressed that no matter what the N.L.R.B., the court or Congress does, the two sides must forge a new collective bargaining agreement through negotiation.

HEADLINE: New Parks Mix Public and Private, Uneasily

SERIES: Who Owns the West? -- Fourth article of a periodic series.

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY EGAN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DEATH VALLEY NATIONAL PARK, Calif.

 BODY:
In November, soon after Congress created the largest single expanse of parkland and protected wilderness in the 48 states, a curious Canadian couple pulled into the Mojave Desert outpost of Cima, population 15.
What the visitors saw, about 120 miles south of here in one of the driest places on earth, was a cluster of sun-baked cows, a handwritten sign criticizing the Federal Government and another one advertising beer and soda. Behind a fence, a few abandoned cars were perched on blocks. 
 "They wanted to know where was the land that had just been added to the national park system," recalled Irene Ausmus, the Cima postmistress. "I told them they were in it. Boy, were they surprised."
As one of its last acts, on Oct. 8, the 103d Congress ended a decade of wrangling over nine million acres of Southern California desert and created two new national parks, a large preserve and a huge patchwork of wilderness.
More than 90 mountain ranges, some reaching 11,000 feet above the desert floor; 2,000 kinds of plants and animals unique to a world where rain is a phantom; sand dunes as high as 700 feet, and a host of archeological treasures were given protected status under the new law.
Death Valley, upgraded from a monument, is now the largest national park in the contiguous United States -- nearly a million acres bigger than Yellowstone National Park.
But these parks and wilderness areas will not be strictly nature sanctuaries. In a flurry of amendments intended to accommodate proponents of property rights, Congress essentially wrote a dozen cattle ranchers, several miners, a large corporate property owner and assorted dry-country loners into the new park system -- freezing a snapshot of desert commerce into the ageless tableau of dunes, cinder cones and Joshua tree forests.
But never, perhaps, has such an eclectic mix of people, business and landowners been written into a park as was done in the California desert.
Hunting, prohibited in virtually every other national park except under special circumstances, will be allowed in the Mojave National Preserve. Grazing of domestic cattle, which now goes on in a limited fashion in only one small park in Nevada, will be permitted throughout much of the protected desert land. A couple of working mines will continue operations.
As a result, the newest additions to the national park system contain not only some of the oldest trees in the world, the hottest place on the planet and California's only known dinosaur tracks, but also feature a desert cowboy named Clay Overson and a Death Valley prospector who goes by the name of Sparrow.
The question, as the Mojave Desert prepares for a surge of visitors, is whether all these users can get along, or whether they will undermine the special features of land that is supposed to be set aside for the ages.
When the Desert Protection Act was signed by President Clinton on Oct. 31, it enshrined two sometimes conflicting tenets of American thought: the national park idea, created with Yellowstone in 1872 and since copied around the world, and a reverence for private property.
National parks try to preserve something unique and wondrous. America has no shortage of cows, so, park supporters argue, why let them wander through some of the most fragile land in the country? Ranchers and other desert property users argued that much of the desert parkland is no different than any other shank of the Mojave. But having lost that argument, they now fear that the National Park Service will be a heavy-handed regulator.
In the past, national parks have been drawn around everything from army bases to gold mines, usually with the provision that "inappropriate" activities will be phased out. An Indian war once passed through Yellowstone, astonishing tourists.
Senator Dianne Feinstein, the California Democrat who is the chief architect of the new law, said the ranchers were part of "our Western heritage," and she agreed to a provision that allowed grazing inside the new parks for perpetuity.
Under the new law, almost 90 percent of Death Valley is classified as wilderness, which prohibits development and road-building. At the same time, several hundred people -- including about 30 members of the Timbisha Shoshone tribe, whose ancestors have been here for hundreds of years -- will live year round in an oasis surrounding a golf course inside the new park. It is an island of private land once advertised as an area said to have "all the advantages of hell without any of the inconveniences."
Entering the park from the east, a visitor may be overwhelmed by the vistas, the clarity of the air, the sense of solitude and all the sunset-colored rings of exposed geology. But only a few miles from that entrance is a working borax mine and a nearly abandoned workers' village protected by four vocal, large-toothed dogs.
Originally, the Billie Mine and the near-ghost-town of Ryan were supposed to be part of the park, but they were later drawn out of it, a distinction that will be difficult to discern for anyone setting out to explore the park.
Most people who study the desert are happy simply to have the broad new Federal protection for the area.
"The Mojave is not quite ready for the critical list," said Dr. Charles Douglas, a professor of biology at the University of Nevada at Las Vegas, "but it's been approaching it."
Dr. Douglas said new communities sprouting in the desert had drained water from springs that a variety of animals, including bighorn sheep, depended on. And off-road vehicles have ripped up the habitat of desert tortoises, a threatened species.
With passage of the desert act, the Government has a chance to avoid some mistakes of the past that have led to traffic jams and excessive commercial development at other national parks, some park advocates say.
"This may well be the last major park area created in the nation," said Norbert Riedy, an official with the Wilderness Society who has been working on the California desert issue since 1986. "I look at this as an opportunity to build a park for the 21st century. But we also should look down the road, 200 to 500 years from now, when these public lands will be the backyard of millions of people."
By this view, the desert wilderness will be best appreciated as a refuge for urban-harried humanity. As Edward Abbey wrote in "Desert Solitaire," his tribute to arid America, "Out there is a different world, older and greater and deeper by far than ours, a world which surrounds and sustains the little world of man."
Mr. Abbey wrote his book in 1968, when the predominant view of the desert was still that of a wasteland, a place to dump nuclear garbage and use as a target range for new weapons. Now, more than 22 million people, mostly in the Los Angeles metropolitan area, live within a half-day's drive of one of the new desert parks. And the fastest-growing city in the United States is a Mojave Desert mecca, Las Vegas, just two hours from most of the new parkland.
Joshua Tree, which has been upgraded to a national park from a national monument, with its exotic yuccas and slabs of perfect climbing rocks, has seen attendance double over the last decade, to more than 1.4 million people a year. More than one million people visited Death Valley last year, a record. But for all the Mojave's newfound popularity, for its cattle and mines, it remains a place where people find solitude.
"You will be able to go there and experience what it was like in the Garden of Eden," said Peter Burk, a Barstow, Calif., librarian who first came up with the idea of a park in the eastern Mojave 15 years ago.
At the same time, some of the desert dwellers encircled by the new parks are restless. They want to continue doing what they have always done, riding off-road, shooting guns and exploring new mining claims, unimpeded by new regulations that come with national park status. Some of these activities will be allowed, but under significant restrictions.
"If you live around here and use the desert, you don't want to have a park ranger holding your hand, or be unable to camp anywhere but an asphalt parking lot," said Mike Smith, who lives in Twentynine Palms, just outside Joshua Tree.
Earlier this month, a leader of a group opposed to Federal protection of more land told about 100 people who live near the Mojave preserve to fight the National Park Service every step of the way as the land was put under new guidelines.
The cattlemen, miners and hunters who operate within the new additions to the park system "are the last vestiges of our pioneering spirit in this country," said Chuck Cushman, director of the American Land Rights Association, which represents property owners surrounded by Federal land.
Connie Connely is one of those people whose new landlord will be the park service. She lives next to the railroad track with her mother and eight dogs. Her father was a prospector who built his house on land leased from the Government, inside what is now the Mojave preserve.
The land is 1.4 million acres of rust-colored mountain ranges, odd-shaped lava formations and wind-sculptured dunes wedged between the two new national parks. It also contains high-voltage transmission lines, dune-buggy roads and natural-gas lines.
"I guess I'm just a hermit type," Ms. Connely said, adding that she lives in the desert because of the peace.
But since the area became part of the park, she sees a lot more people, particularly on weekends.
"Ran into a bunch of people here the other day looking for turtles," Ms. Connely said, holding a conversation while trying to quiet her dogs. "They said they came out here to see their new park. Their new park! I said it's my park, too. I'm part of it."
Actually, the east Mojave was supposed to be a national park, like Joshua Tree and Death Valley, under the terms of earlier legislation passed by Congress. It was changed to a preserve to allow hunting, after heavy lobbying by the National Rifle Association. In an average year, hunters there kill about 25 deer and 5 sheep.
But in trying to accommodate a handful of hunters, Congress may have hurt the potential for big tourist dollars.
"There are more deer killed on the George Washington Parkway every year than will be shot in the Mojave," said Martin Hayden, a lobbyist for the Sierra Club, referring to the roadway in the Virginia suburbs of Washington. "People don't know what a preserve is, and by downgrading it, you cost the economy around there a lot of money."
Still, Mr. Hayden and other environmentalists are ecstatic about the desert act. "This is a gift to our grandchildren," he said.

HEADLINE: Pataki's Health Adviser Pushing $1 Billion Reduction in Medicaid

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Dec. 24

 BODY:
A month before New York State's new Republican administration must propose its first budget, Governor-elect George E. Pataki's chief adviser on health care is proposing to cut more than $1 billion in state spending from Medicaid, the costly and politically sensitive health program for the poor, disabled and elderly.
While Mr. Pataki complained often during the campaign that the program was too generous, the cuts sought by the aide, Lieut. Governor-elect Elizabeth P. McCaughey, are large but not radically different in scope from those explored by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo. 
 They include a spending reduction of up to 30 percent in nonmedical services for the homebound, like cooking, cleaning and shopping. Ms. McCaughey would also rely for major savings on an approach Mr. Cuomo and the Legislature adopted in 1991: assigning poor people to some form of managed care, which has cut costs by about 9 percent among those already enrolled.
The proposal would preserve most so-called optional services, like eyeglasses and podiatry, and would keep those who receive home relief in the program.
Mr. Pataki has not acted on any of the proposals, which are being evaluated by the transition advisers who are drafting his budget, and he is reportedly still considering more drastic cuts. Ms. McCaughey concedes that the plan does not represent a new philosophy on Medicaid spending, but rather a systematic search for cuts large and small. She said many of the suggestions came from officials appointed by Mr. Cuomo in the State Department of Social Services and many were proposed but not put in place in the past.
Medicaid has been the most pressing cost on local governments, rising well above the rate of inflation and forcing local taxes to rise. In New York City, which is facing a $2 billion budget deficit next year, aides to Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani have circulated a proposal to cut welfare and Medicaid by $1.2 billion, or about 25 percent. In recent years, Mr. Cuomo has achieved Medicaid cuts nearly as large as he, too, struggled to close budget gaps.
Ms. McCaughey said her plan would save between $1.1 billion and $1.3 billion in state funds from projected Medicaid spending of $22 billion next year, translating into reduction for local governments of between 10 and 25 percent. This does not amount to an actual cut in the program, which costs over $19 billion this year, but a reduction in its expected growth.
Ms. McCaughey discussed her proposal late Friday afternoon, and many officials could not be reached for comment over the Christmas weekend. James R. Tallon, president of the United Hospital Fund of New York and a former Democratic Assemblyman who specialized in Medicaid, said that on the whole, Ms. McCaughey's proposals were in the "mainstream" and that many were "probably ultimately achievable," though not without what may be a major fight in the Legislature.
"In some respects, people I've heard are giving her credit for doing this with a scalpel and not an ax," he said "but it's a big scalpel."
There will be plenty for the Legislature, interest groups and advocates on all sides to haggle over in her proposals, as Mr. Pataki pursues his pledge to cut taxes as well as his obligation to fill a projected budget gap as high as $5 billion next year. Like many Republicans, Ms. McCaughey did not propose a reduction in state payments to hospitals and nursing homes -- an initiative supported by many Democrats in the Assembly. A similar plan was announced last month in New Jersey, where officials said they planned to cut such payments by 20 percent starting in March.
The proposed cuts in nonmedical services, also known as personal care, have been opposed by unions representing home attendants as well as by advocates for the elderly, who receive most of these services.
Moreover, many advocates for the poor have argued that managed care -- which often means enrolling Medicaid patients in health maintenance organizations -- translates into less-attentive care for poor people.
For her part, Ms. McCaughey, who has been working on the proposals since the election, said that while she expects negotiation, her proposals are sound. "I'd like to stress this is a work in progress," she said in a telephone interview.
"We've examined the system with a microscope, looking for ways we can reduce spending without actually reducing the health care. Very few of the proposed changes would involve reducing care," she said.
The proposals were the product of a task force Ms. McCaughey headed, which was composed largely of government officials and hospital executives. Advocates for Medicaid patients complained that their interests were not fully represented.
Richard M. Cook, Mr. Cuomo's deputy director for health, cautioned that his administration's efforts at broad cuts had met with yearly frustration, and he questioned whether Ms. McCaughey's proposals would save as much as she estimates, especially in managed care. "In the past, it has been exceedingly difficult to build a political and programmatic consensus for a billion dollars in savings in one year," he said.
Local governments complain that Medicaid spending, which has risen in double digits in recent years, has forced local taxes to rise; and while local officials, including many Republican county executives and the Republican Mayor of New York City, Mr. Giuliani, have pressed for a state takeover of local costs, Mr. Pataki has argued that the state's finances are too dire for such a transfer. The program is paid for by Federal, state and local money.
One of the biggest proposed cuts is $345 million in the state's share of the $2.25 billion budget for personal care. Ms. McCaughey said that the cuts could be made by dropping from the program people whose need for such care is minimal, like those who only need help shopping. Those who need help with an array of services would not have their care reduced, she said.
She added that an acceleration of the state's managed care program -- which would mandate poor people to enroll, unlike the current voluntary program -- would save $200 million next year and would require Federal permission.
The only optional benefit that Ms. McCaughey has proposed eliminating -- after a campaign in which Mr. Pataki mistakenly said that Medicaid was so extravagant that it paid for hair transplants -- is non-emergency dental care for adults. That would save $29.1 million in state funds, she said.
Other proposals include: cutting down on transportation costs; allowing doctors to write prescriptions for 90 days rather than 30; reducing the amount of family assets an individual can keep when a spouse enters a nursing home, and fingerprint imaging to cut down on fraud.

HEADLINE: Political Memo;
Some Republicans See Prospect of Sluggish Economy by 1996 as a Threat

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 25

 BODY:
For years the conventional wisdom has held that a sluggish economy hurts the party of whoever resides in the White House. But with Republicans planning to use their new control of Congress to dominate economic policy-making over the next two years, the conventional wisdom may well prove wrong.
Or at least that's what more and more Republicans are beginning to worry about. 
 Officials of the Federal Reserve Board have been unusually frank in saying that the six increases in the interest rate this year will probably succeed in slowing the economy over the next two years. While the timing and extent of any slowdown cannot be pinpointed, most economists think its full brunt will be felt by early 1996, just as the Presidential primary season gets under way.
The extent of any slowdown in 1996 is crucial, Democrats and Republicans say. If economic growth drops from the 4 percent pace this year to 2.6 percent in 1996 -- the current White House forecast -- then the creation of new jobs may slow somewhat.
Even a small slowdown could turn the economy into a much greater political issue than it was in this year's Congressional elections, said Fred P. Steeper, a prominent Republican pollster, adding, "When I was polling for President Bush, it seemed like he had trouble if growth was under 3 percent."
The White House prediction of 2.6 percent growth in 1996 is only slightly higher than the current consensus of blue-chip private economists. But more and more economists have begun adding a caveat to their forecasts, saying the economy may fare a lot worse than their forecasts. Medium-term interest rates in financial markets have risen to a level suggesting that loans will become so expensive that the economy could stall within a year.
It is this possibility that has Republicans worried. Some are beginning to plan responses. Representative James H. Saxton, a New Jersey Republican who will become vice chairman of the Joint Economic Committee of Congress next year, said that if a recession did come, "our message will be that high interest rates and high taxes that were caused by the Federal Reserve and the Federal Government brought on this recession."
Some Republicans are even calling for the party to sound the alarm now.
"The thing most likely to avoid misunderstanding with the American public is for the Republicans to begin talking early and often about the 1996 recession, to make clear it's the result of policy decisions made before the 1994 election," said John D. Mueller, who was the economic counsel to the House Republican Conference from 1981 to 1988 and who remains active in Republican circles as an economic forecaster.
A popular political rule of thumb these days is that an ailing economy hurts incumbents much more than a healthy economy helps them. This year's strong economy did not seem to help Democrats much, but President George Bush suffered in 1992 from the economy's lingering weakness after the 1990 recession.
So far, detailed discussion of the possible threat to Republicans has been largely limited to strategists like William Kristol, who was former Vice President Dan Quayle's chief of staff. But Mr. Kristol said that at a recent closed-door seminar in Baltimore for new members of Congress, "there was a lot of interest from the freshmen Republican Congressmen about this issue."
Republican leaders have mostly kept quiet about the economy, but a few hints of nervousness are apparent. For example, a Federal Reserve official who insisted on anonymity said that Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the incoming majority leader, and Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, the next Speaker of the House, have asked for a meeting with Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve.
The two Congressional leaders did not indicate why they wanted a meeting, and the meeting has not yet been held, the board official said.
When asked at a news conference on Dec. 9 about rising interest rates, Mr. Gingrich replied, "I am concerned, and I think that there's a real problem." But he backpedaled moments later, saying he respected the Federal Reserve's independence and would not discuss its policy decisions.
The worst-case scenario for Republicans is that voters give President Clinton the credit for the last two years of economic growth and then blame Republicans and their legislation for any economic slowdown in the next two years.
"There's no doubt that incumbents have a problem," said Patrick J. Buchanan, the columnist who challenged Mr. Bush for the Presidential nomination in 1992. "That the Republicans now have power -- real power -- they are in danger of being indicted as participants in bringing down the recovery."
On Dec. 16 Haley Barbour, the Republican national chairman, began laying the political groundwork for an effort to blame the Clinton Administration.
"The fact of the matter is, Bill Clinton inherited a very strong economy, much stronger than the public knew in 1992," Mr. Barbour told reporters, "and the economic growth that we've had in 1993 and 1994 has been in spite of his economic policies, not because of them.
"If in fact the negative potential of those economic policies bites, because the growth that was taking place in '92 runs out of steam, the economy could turn down. But the policies that we advocate will be policies that are pro-growth, pro-jobs and beneficial to our competitiveness."
Yet most economists, including Republicans, believe that neither the Clinton Administration nor the Bush Administration has changed the course of the economy dramatically, nor will a Republican-controlled Congress do so. The real power in economic policy-making lies at the Federal Reserve and its control over interest rates.
Mr. Mueller said that if the economy was now heading down, Republican tax cuts and deregulatory initiatives in Congress could not stop it. Even if the most optimistic predictions come true for the economic effects of lower taxes on investment profits and other Republican initiatives, he said, they would not be enough to stop the economy's boom-bust cycle.
Some Democrats in Congress think that the White House will be blamed for any harm to the economy as a result of higher interest rates almost regardless of what happens in Congress, and despite the fact that the Federal Reserve is responsible for raising the rates.
Rising interest rates "are going to be disastrous to the economy, and that's going to be disastrous to the Democrats," said Representative Maurice D. Hinchey, an upstate New York Democrat on the House Banking Committee who follows monetary policy.
And some Republicans are already planning how to make sure this happens, so as to avoid blame themselves.
"We want to have fights with Clinton," Mr. Kristol said, "so that we can remind voters in '96 that there were certain things we wanted to do and Clinton prevented them."

HEADLINE: Concerned About Pollution From Proposed Mine, Wisconsin Tribe Takes On a Giant

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CRANDON, Wis.

 BODY:
At its most basic level, a fight over a proposal by the Exxon Corporation to build an immense copper and zinc mine near the Sokaogon Chippewa reservation here seems like a mismatch between the world's largest energy corporation and Wisconsin's smallest Indian tribe.
But Exxon's plan to put a mine on 865 acres of Wisconsin's North Woods, a region of dense forests and clear rivers, has turned into one of the country's fiercest grass-roots environmental face-offs. Not only does the tribe view it as a life-and-death struggle but it also has enough money and outside support to make a fight of it. 
 On Exxon's side are Gov. Tommy G. Thompson, other top state politicians and the state's business leaders. They contend that the mine would help the area's economy but not pollute its streams and lakes.
On the other side are the Chippewas, who say that the mine would ruin a way of life dependent on clean water to provide fish and wild rice. Joining them are some small-business owners who argue that a project of the magnitude planned by Exxon -- with giant pulverizing mills and shafts as deep as 2,800 feet -- could wreck a healthy rural economy based on forestry, recreation and light manufacturing. The unemployment rate in the area is about 5 percent, a quarter of what it was six years ago. Several business owners said they had worked hard to establish an economy based on steady use of natural resources and were fearful of the boom-and-bust pattern of the mining industry.
"Right now, a lot of us are looking at what it means to be a small town," said Dorothy Kegley, the former Mayor of Crandon, population 2,000. "You can sell it out pretty cheap." She is running for her old seat in the April election on a platform of opposing the mine.
The fight here is one of several that Indian tribes are waging against huge natural-resource projects. Though some tribal leaders have sought projects that many Indians as well as environmentalists oppose, other reservations have become active centers of environmentalism in the 1990's.
The Cherokees helped force the closing of a nuclear processing plant in Oklahoma, for example. The Navajos have fought timber cutting in Arizona, and the Sioux kept new garbage dumps from their reservation in South Dakota.
"These fights have developed outside the framework of the mainstream environmental movement," said Al Gedicks, a sociologist at the University of Wisconsin at La Crosse.
It will be several years before a winner and loser can be declared in the Wisconsin battle. But the Sokaogon (pronounced so-COG-in) tribe, using Federal and private grants and revenue from its two casinos, has filed lawsuits and hired experts to evaluate the proposed mine, which would be in what is now a dense forest just east of and upstream from the tribe's Mole Lake Reservation. The Sokaogon Chippewas have also built alliances with other tribes, sought help from environmentalists and joined forces with many white residents here who are concerned about pollution.
Among the most important allies the Chippewas have recruited are anglers' groups concerned that mining wastes would ruin fishing in the nearby Wolf River. Only a few years ago, fishermen and Indians were at war in northern Wisconsin over the tribe's practice of spearfishing walleyed pike during spawning season.
Exxon's opponents point to other regions of the country, from the West to Appalachia, where mining wastes have heavily damaged thousands of miles of streams and rivers and required million-dollar cleanups financed by taxpayers.
Exxon discovered the zinc and copper deposit here in 1975. In 1986, it temporarily abandoned its plan to open the mine, citing low mineral prices, but neither the company nor the tribe expected the withdrawal to be final. In September 1993 Exxon announced that it had joined with Rio Algom Ltd., a Canadian mining company, to form the Crandon Mining Company to pursue the project. In February, the two companies notified the state Department of Natural Resources that they intended to acquire the more than 40 permits needed to begin construction.
"We expect to continue to try to do everything we can to address the concerns of everybody," said Jerome D. Goodrich Jr., one of Exxon's top mine supervisors and the president of Crandon Mining. "We expect to be here a long time. You don't win in the long term by bulldozing people."
The company has spent millions of dollars on studies to prove to the state and the tribe that the mine would not leak.
The principal product of the mine would be zinc, a metal used to prevent corrosion in cars, bridges and buildings. Copper, lead and small amounts of gold and silver would also be mined. Mr. Goodrich said about 800 people would be employed building the mine, with 400 to 500 people then needed to operate it. Wages would average $30,000 to $40,000 a year.
Mayor Vern Kincaid of Crandon said: "If the mine is done in a way that makes it environmentally safe, then I'd say the majority of people here want it. It will be a good thing for our young people, who can stay around here to get a job instead of having to leave."
Chippewa leaders said they did not believe that Exxon or its partner could operate a mine that would not pollute, and they have rejected appeals to meet with Exxon. The tribe contends that Exxon and other mining companies are considering at least 10 other mineral deposits for development in northern Wisconsin, a number the industry does not dispute. Halting the Crandon project, they argue, could prevent the development of other big mines.
"Talking with them is participating in our own destruction," said Fred Ackley, a Sokaogon tribal judge. "Our goal is to stop this project."
George E. Meyer, the Secretary of the Wisconsin Department of Natural Resources, said in an interview that his agency would set strict environmental standards. "If they can't meet those standards, then they won't be issued the permits," Mr. Meyer said.
Mr. Goodrich acknowledged that keeping wastes out of the water would not be easy. The mine would produce ores and wastes laced with sulfides. Such wastes, when mixed with air and water, produce low concentrations of sulfuric acid, known as acid mine drainage.
Mr. Goodrich said that his company had designed a 365-acre storage complex to prevent acidic substances from forming and that water draining from the waste piles would be treated to remove pollutants.
But Frances Van Zile, the tribe's chief records clerk, said the tribe had found no evidence that a metallic sulfide mine like the one proposed here had ever prevented acid mine drainage. Ms. Van Zile said she feared that wastes would seep into a creek that feeds Rice Lake on the reservation, harming the wild rice that is crucial to the tribe's culture and economy.
"If it wasn't for pure water," she said, "we wouldn't be here.
"When you were born, you were born in pure water in your mother's womb. What's the first thing you did today? You turned on the water. And that's what they are going to pollute. They are going to destroy our way of life."

In the background is Rice Lake, which the tribe fears would be polluted by mining wastes. (Chris Corsmeier for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Hundreds of Jobs Likely to Go In Cuts by Bankrupt County

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Dec. 25

 BODY:
Hundreds of people are likely to lose their jobs in coming months as health clinics, programs for children and other services are cut to help offset the losses that led Orange County to declare bankruptcy, officials said on Saturday.
The county, in suburban Los Angeles, hopes to save $120 million over the next 18 months, including $40 million in the next six months and $80 million in the fiscal year that begins on July 1, said Tom Uram, a member of the team that is reshaping the county's budget. 
 To realize the savings, health clinics and some health-care programs for children will have to be eliminated, said Mr. Uram, who is also director of the Health Care Agency, which runs the county's clinics and health programs.
"We'll have to close clinics and reduce overall services, reduce or eliminate child physicals," Mr. Uram said in an interview. "We just don't have the resources to do all this anymore. These are mammoth-type cuts."
The members of the council charged with making budget recommendations -- Mr. Uram, Sheriff Brad Gates and District Attorney Michael Capizzi -- have refused to speculate on the number of jobs that will be cut. But the Orange County Employees Association estimates that spending cuts will result in 700 to 1,000 layoffs, said John Sawyer, general manager of the association.
Supervisor Roger Stanton and an incoming county Supervisor, Marian Bergeson, have already told some employees that their jobs will be eliminated.
News of the impending layoffs has enraged residents, and two county supervisors received telephoned bomb threats on Friday.
"They're shocked and angry," Mr. Sawyer said.
The cost-cutting measures are just the first small steps on the path to bail out the county, which declared bankruptcy on Dec. 6 after disclosing large losses from high-risk investments.
The scandal forced the resignation of the county's Treasurer, Robert L. Citron, provoked calls for the recall of county supervisors and led to investigations by the Securities and Exchange Commission and other agencies.
"What will happen in the future we're not even sure," Mr. Uram said. Referring to the county's 18-month cost-cutting plan, he added: "It's Draconian. But I suspect this is just the start."
Orange County had borrowed heavily to invest in complex interest-sensitive securities known as derivatives. The investment pool was meant to capitalize on falling interest rates. When rates rose, losses mounted.
Officials said the county's share of the investment pool's $2 billion loss might reach $700 million, far more than the $120 million in savings outlined by Mr. Uram.

Dispute With Ex-Treasurer
 SANTA ANA, Calif., Dec. 25 (AP) -- Orange County officials are threatening not to pay the legal costs for Mr. Citron, the former Treasurer.
Mr. Citron's lawyer, David Wiechert, said today that the county's top lawyer, Terry Andrus, had called him on Friday to report that a letter had been drafted informing Mr. Citron of the decision not to pay legal expenses. Mr. Wiechert said Mr. Andrus had described the decision as "political." He added that it seemed to be an effort to shield other officials from sharing blame for the county's bankruptcy.
Mr. Wiechert contended that the only basis for refusing to pay costs in civil suits and investigations like those Mr. Citron faces would be fraudulent, malicious or corrupt conduct and that he was not guilty of any of those.
Mr. Wiechert said he had persuaded Mr. Andrus not to send the letter, agreeing that a legal deadline for such a notice could be extended until Jan. 5.
Mr. Andrus was not available for comment because of the holiday.
