HEADLINE: What Is a Nation?

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, Dec. 23

 BODY:
The war in the breakaway Russian republic of Chechnya is crystalizing two issues that have been debated only sporadically here despite the rise of ethnic separatism around the world: How inviolate is national sovereignty? And what should the limits of self-determination be?
Those questions have been raised at the United Nations since mid-1992, when Secretary General Boutros Boutros-Ghali published "Agenda for Peace," a report written for the Security Council at a time when hopes were still high and horizons still seemed broadened because of the cold war's end. 
 In those days, Mr. Boutros-Ghali wrote "that an opportunity has been regained to achieve the great objectives of the Charter." He included not only peacekeeping but peacemaking, or interposing force between warring parties whether or not they agreed. He also emphasized the promotion of human rights and higher living standards.
A few years later, nearly 150 wars or skirmishes are in progress in which thousands of people are dying -- more civilians than soldiers by most reckonings -- and hundreds of thousands are becoming refugees.
With more than 100 of those battles taking place within countries rather than between them, the United Nations must find a way to intervene to save both lives and states, diplomats say. But to do so, the organization will have to use new tools and concepts. There have been some significant moves in that direction.
The General Assembly approved a sweeping resolution today calling on the Secretary General to take steps to promote democratization and strengthen the election process worldwide. It also asked the newly appointed High Commissioner for Human Rights, Jose Ayala Lasso of Ecuador, to support education on human rights.
Madeleine K. Albright, the chief American delegate, welcomed the Assembly vote as a means to expand the scope of the United Nations in promoting political rights, one way to dampen the discontent that often leads to rebellion. But these are long-term goals.
For the moment, the major nations represented here, and the United Nations as an organization, remain committed to guarding national integrity above the rights of peoples. The case of the former Yugoslavia -- in particular Bosnia and Herzegovina -- is both an exception to that concept and, to many, an instance of the terrible price paid when recognition is quickly extended to entities that might not be viable as countries.
"If you look at the Security Council resolutions in the last two or three years, you will find that in almost every crisis we insist -- sometimes apparently out of the blue -- on preserving the sovereignty and territorial integrity of the state," said Emilio J. Cardenas, the chief delegate from Argentina, which holds one of the 10 rotating seats on the 15-member Council.
"We went as far recently in making that point about Iraq, where we believe that you should protect the Shias and Kurds without breaking the national identity -- providing that the country respects diversity," he said.
Mr. Cardenas and others say there is a perceptible shift away from almost automatically embracing self-determination for minorities. Instead the Council is looking toward minority problems and secessionist movements as political, economic and human rights issues in which the United Nations may have some standing. That trend was reflected in the General Assembly vote on democratization and human-rights education.
"We talk less and less about self-determination, because of the fear of secession," Mr. Cardenas said in an interview. "We talk more and more about the need to protect minorities inside the state."
Secretary General Boutros-Ghali made that point in his "Agenda for Peace." Commenting on the role of nations in taking "an integrated approach to human security," he reiterated the support of the United Nations for national sovereignty. But he added:
"The time of absolute and exclusive sovereignty, however, has passed; its theory was never matched by reality. It is the task of leaders of states today to understand this and to find a balance between the need of good internal governance and the requirements of an ever more interdependent world."
The debate over national sovereignty and self-determination after the cold war is rich in paradox. In the case of Chechnya, for instance, the Moscow that has been so outspoken in defending the rights of Russians in newly independent states has not shown much sympathy for the Muslim Chechens within its own borders. Nor has it accepted the right of Muslim nations to offer assistance to them.
Russia has also been firm in fending off calls for international mediation in Chechnya.
"Lately the Russians have been very active in saying that political and diplomatic means are better than military means for solving conflict," said Danilo Turk, Slovenia's delegate. "I am surprised that they are not repeating that wisdom now. It is difficult to see how a problem like this can be solved militarily."
But as Russian bombs and artillery barrages landed this week on Chechnya, the United Nations was on the sidelines, grappling with what may be its central problem: the larger or more influential the country directly involved in a conflict, the less likely that there will be a role for this or any other international organization.
The feeling of irrelevance here was underscored on Wednesday, when reporters meeting with the Russian delegate, Sergei V. Lavrov, wanted to know if Moscow might seek the intervention of the United Nations.
"Why?" he replied.
Around the world, some of the very nations that gained independence in the flush of self-determination that followed World War II and reached its height in the 1960's are now on the other side of the argument.
India, which set a standard for nonviolent opposition to colonial rule, now administers Kashmir with martial law and three quarters of a million troops. Indonesia, with its history of struggle against the Dutch and, briefly, the Japanese, now denies the East Timorese the right to talk about independence. China, fearful of losing Tibet, joins those two countries in international forums in condemning human rights campaigns, with support from Iran and other nations with authoritarian governments.
If the United Nations General Assembly or Security Council, both subject to paralysis when powerful individual governments resist action, cannot do much unless it is invited into a country in turmoil, other parts of the United Nations system can play a role.
In recent years, the office of the High Commissioner for Refugees, under Sadako Ogata, has been more aggressive about moving into areas when people are displaced and about publicizing issues of human rights. Strong international human rights organizations try to keep abuses in public view, and they are gaining more standing in the United Nations system.
At the same time, experts are reviewing a passage in the United Nations Charter that nations have routinely cited to avoid interference. The relevant language, in Article 2 (7), says, "Nothing contained in the present Charter shall authorize the United Nations to intervene in matters which are essentially within the domestic jurisdiction of any state."
Jose E. Alvarez, an associate professor of law at the University of Michigan, said at a seminar convened two years ago by the Academic Council of the United Nations System, based at the Watson Institute at Brown University, that interpretation of this 1945 language has in fact been considerably muddied over the years.
Professor Alvarez argues that the General Assembly has passed so many covenants and authorized so many new procedures -- election-monitoring, for instance -- that it has reduced areas of national life that can be considered to be completely outside international law.
But Mr. Turk of Slovenia, the chairman of a working committee on rights in the General Assembly, said the words "essentially within domestic jurisdiction" were chosen carefully and are elastic.
"The framework of domestic jurisdiction has narrowed," he said. "Things that were considered domestic 30 to 40 years ago are now international jurisdiction, and we could act on them." He said that if the United Nations were not "unduly legalistic," it would recognize that it already had the power to play a role in protecting human rights within the broadened definition of international jurisdiction.
He said the Organization for Security and Cooperation in Europe had already gone very far, at least on paper, in rejecting the argument of domestic jurisdiction.
"If people are being killed, one should have the right to be asking human rights questions," he said, adding that this included the case of Chechnya.

HEADLINE: Retailers' Christmas Wishes Didn't Come True This Year

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

 BODY:
The predictions of a strong holiday shopping season were apparently wishful thinking for many retailers, particularly in apparel.
While the numbers will not begin to come out until tomorrow, the early indications are that the season's sales, while above last year's, have not been what retailers hoped for or stocked for. "It's not been a happy season," said Edward Johnson, the retailing analyst who compiles the Johnson Redbook Service, which tracks sales. 
 One sign of the problems was that many people held off on their shopping until just before Christmas, forcing retailers to slash prices in an effort to lure customers. Shoppers can expect an even greater number of markdowns in the coming weeks as stores try to clear their shelves for spring merchandise.
The extent of the pre-Christmas markdowns shocked many people who follow the retailing industry, and implied a level of desperation last seen in the depths of the 1990-91 recession.
Two days before Christmas, for example, Brooks Brothers was running a storewide sale, with prices cut 25 percent to 40 percent on a wide range of merchandise. At Lerners New York, crimson signs promised 50 percent off everything, even merchandise previously marked down. Eddie Bauer sent out a sale catalogue two weeks before Christmas.
On Saturday in Manhattan, the streets were fairly empty. For the first time since Thanksgiving, there were no lines outside F.A.O. Schwarz and the Warner Brothers Store. And Saks Fifth Avenue, Bergdorf Goodman and Bloomingdale's were hardly packed with shoppers.
There were some exceptions: sales were apparently good at certain retailers of merchandise for the home, as well as for computers, software, consumer electronics and jewelry.
Entering the season, retailers had been more optimistic than last year heading into the fourth quarter, with roughly 10 percent more inventory than they had on hand a year earlier, said Dr. William Ford, senior economic adviser to Telecheck Services Inc., a check-clearing service.
But he added: "My sense of it is that when the smoke clears early next week and we start getting some hard data, we're going to see that while sales are up, they're not nearly as good as what retailers hoped for and what they stocked up for."
Telecheck said the dollar amount of purchases made using checks was up a narrow 2.2 percent for the 27 days from the day after Thanksgiving through last Wednesday. While heavy use of credit cards probably accounted for some of the slower pace reflected in Telecheck's numbers, sales this year were fundamentally less robust than anticipated, Dr. Ford said.
To be sure, Mastercard and Visa have both been reporting extraordinary increases in purchases made with their credit cards, and many have used those numbers to proclaim the 1994 holiday sales season a barn-burner.
But many analysts say the sharp increases reported by Visa and Mastercard do not provide an accurate snapshot of sales. For one thing, many consumers are shifting their spending from cash and checks to credit cards that offer them mileage on airline flight plans or discounts on cars and gasoline. And the number of stores accepting Visa and Mastercard has risen sharply.
In addition, some credit-card transactions included in the figures are made in grocery stores, which are not traditionally a source of Christmas presents.
Analysts said many retail companies might meet their sales goals but would have sacrificed profits to do so. "They're buying sales with promotions at this point," said Walter F. Loeb, president of Loeb Associates Inc., a retail consulting firm based in New York. "Business is being generated not because customers are coming in voluntarily, but because the retailers have their hooks out."
He said that a few companies had declined to hold sales in an effort to preserve their profit margins, but that for the most part, big markdowns were likely to eat into fourth-quarter profits. Anticipating those results, the stocks of many retailing companies are trading close to their 52-week lows.
Mr. Johnson of Johnson Redbook estimated that sales last week would increase from the corresponding week a year earlier by a percentage in the mid-teens, largely because retailers had one more selling day before Christmas than they had last year. "That's what's going to save sales for most of them," he said. "But so far, it's been a little below expectations for most stores."
Many retailing executives and Wall Street analysts said they were puzzled by the slow pace of sales. The nation's overall economic picture bode well for the holiday season, with surveys of consumer sentiment showing a rise in confidence. Unemployment is low, and the economy is growing at a healthy pace.
Some explanations for the disappointing season come from a study of the holiday shopping period completed in the last week by Tactical Retail Solutions Inc., a retail consulting firm. Almost three-quarters of the 400 people surveyed across the country said they would be saving more and spending less this year, in part because they intended to pay off credit card debt.
Isaac Lagnado, a principal at Tactical, estimated that higher interest rates have already taken between $5 billion and $10 billion of discretionary income out of consumers' pockets. Almost two-thirds of those surveyed said higher expenditures for home purchases and mortgage refinancings would curtail their spending. "I think we started to see the effect of higher interest rates just as the holiday season began," Mr. Lagnado said.
And 44 percent said they would spend less because prices were too high.
But Daniel D. Barry, a financial analyst who follows retailers at Merrill Lynch & Company, looked to a time-honored excuse for poor sales. "Weather killed the Christmas season -- that's the only explanation for what's happened," he said, referring to moderate temperatures in many places.
But while such weather may hurt sales of coats and mittens, consumers still had to buy something to put under the tree.
When they did buy, they showed a distinct preference for merchandise for the home, computers, software, consumer electronics and jewelry. For the apparel industry, it was an unhappy year.
"There's still a wide divergence between apparel and hard goods," said Susan Silverstein, a retailing analyst at Gruntal & Company. "Unfortunately, you would expect some significant apparel sales for the holiday season, but it hasn't happened."
Given shoppers' predilection for merchandise like flannel sheets, flashlights with flexible stems and decorating items with holiday themes, it was easy to predict which retailers did well. Pier 1 Imports, a merchandiser of furniture and housewares, said it was expecting double-digit increases in sales at stores that were open a year earlier -- what the industry calls same-store sales, which provides a measure that is not swollen by new stores. Williams-Sonoma Inc., the housewares catalogue and retail company, reported strong sales increases.
The Lowes Companies, a hardware and housewares retailer, said it was expecting its best holiday season ever. "I don't know how it is for others, but we have not had a wait-and-see attitude among our consumers at all," said Cliff Oxford, vice president of corporate relations.
Some analysts said the May Department Stores Company, another retailing powerhouse; Gap Inc. stores; the Limited Inc., and even Toys "R" Us were among the companies hinting that December sales had been disappointing. Spiegel Inc., the large catalogue company that owns Eddie Bauer, and the Brookstone Group had already foreshadowed a bleak fourth quarter.
Even Wal-Mart Stores Inc., which usually sets the pace for the industry, was struggling to meet its projections as slow sales of apparel in its discount stores and at its Sam's Club warehouse stores dragged down stronger sales of hard goods, like electronics, kitchen appliances and compact disks and software.
Mr. Barry of Merrill Lynch said that among the retailers he follows, only Sears, Roebuck & Company, and two chains selling close-out merchandise, Consolidated Stores and MacFrugal's, were exceeding their targets for holiday sales.
Many retailers had counted on Friday and Saturday to provide enough momentum to meet their goals.
"It's always difficult to predict until the last numbers come in," said Gail Dorn, a spokeswoman for the Dayton Hudson Corporation, which operates the Target and Mervyn's chains and several department stores in the Midwest. "We haven't changed our forecast, which was for increases in the low- to mid-single digits."
The calendar's gift of an extra shopping day between Thanksgiving and Christmas, compared with last year, was expected to create a sales windfall of as much as 2 percent. But analysts said the benefits afforded by the calendar would not offset the lackluster pace of sales.
"If you haven't made it over the last two weeks, I wouldn't bank my fourth quarter on these two days, especially with the markdowns they're taking," Ms. Silverstein of Gruntal & Company said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Lerner New York store at 17 West 34th Street in Manhattan reflected the desperation of retailers this holiday season, foreced to cut prices as many shoppers held off buying until just before Christmas. (Dith Pran/The New York Times) (pg. 58)

HEADLINE: U.S. Acts to Halt Bribes for Alien Work Cards

BYLINE: By JOEL BRINKLEY, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 25

 BODY:
Trying to reduce the temptation for employees to take bribes, the Immigration and Naturalization Service has decided to stop issuing work cards in district offices. Instead, starting next spring, the cards will be mailed to immigrants from one or two central offices.
Work cards are given to aliens awaiting hearings on their immigration status. With the cards, they are free to look for work. But the cards are also prized because in some states they can be used to obtain driver's licenses, Social Security cards and other documents that are often used to demonstrate citizenship. As a result, the work cards can be a valuable item on the black market, costing illegal immigrants $500 or more. 
 In one particularly grievous case of corruption, eight employees of the Washington district office of the immigration service were recently convicted of accepting bribes. Altogether, the agency said, they had fraudulently given more than 4,000 people permission to work in this country.
Nor was the corruption in the Washington office an isolated case. Every year dozens of immigration service employees are arrested on charges of corruption or related misconduct. The New York Times reported on the widespread corruption in the agency last September.
The new procedure on issuing work cards, one of several steps the service is taking to deal with its corruption problem, also comes as immigration has become an important political issue.
The recommendation to mail the work cards from central offices came as part of a larger management review this fall of agency procedures. Doris Meissner, the Commissioner of Immigration, has acknowledged that her agency's employees -- some earning little more than the minimum wage -- are more vulnerable to corruption than those in most other agencies of Government because they grant coveted benefits to immigrants willing to pay almost any price to stay in the United States.
This month, the agency is also installing an advanced computer program that will enable internal auditors to track corruption and employee misconduct cases and see whether loopholes in the system made them possible.
"Now, every time we have a case, we have to look through all the other cases" by hand, William G. Rightor, the deputy director of internal audit, said as he demonstrated the new system last week.
The new tracking system is intended to allow auditors to spot common elements in repeated cases and take corrective action, a practice the agency had previously neglected. Had this system been in place before, agency officials said, the immigration service's management might have noticed the work card problem on its own.
Under the new plan for issuing work cards, district offices will approve the applications and then send the paperwork to regional immigration service centers. There, officers will review the papers, produce the work cards and mail them to the immigrants.
"We signed off on this two weeks ago," Chris Sale, the Deputy Commissioner, said last week. "It will be operational in June."

HEADLINE: Nov. 8 Is Long Gone, but the California Election Lingers On, and On and On

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr., Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Dec. 23

 BODY:
Six weeks have passed since Californians trooped to the polls to vote on everybody and everything from governor to a way to slow illegal immigration. Yet Nov. 8 is anything but history.
The election's aftershocks are still being felt. 
 Initially, they brought allegations of extensive balloting fraud, which have since become the subject of a private statewide canvass of voters. More recently, the aftershocks have left the State Assembly in Sacramento leaderless and tied in partisan knots and have set off a flurry of efforts to recall some members.
"It's the election that won't die," said Sherry Bebitch-Jeffe, a political scientist at Claremont College who specializes in California elections. "A lot of voters and politicians still have some anger and frustration left, and it looks like they're intent on venting it."
One politician, Representative Michael Huffington, the Republican from Santa Barbara who put up almost $28 million of his personal fortune in an unequaled political spending effort to unseat Senator Dianne Feinstein, still refuses to concede defeat.
According to the state's official count of ballots, Ms. Feinstein, a Democrat from San Francisco, won with 51 percent of the vote. But Mr. Huffington contends there may have been voting fraud and is awaiting the outcome of a private canvass by supporters. He argues that California does not monitor voter registration carefully enough, especially registration efforts by some of the state's thousands of illegal aliens.
"He's onto something," said Harold W. Ezell, a former Federal immigration official who is directing the canvass.
How many instances of fraud have been uncovered so far in the monthlong investigation?
"We're not quite ready to talk about that yet," Mr. Ezell replied.
Jennifer Grossman, a Huffington aide, added, "Until the figures are released, Mike's not saying another word -- and that includes conceding."
Ms. Grossman denied published reports that Mr. Huffington was so disgruntled with California politics that he planned to sell his home and move back to his native Texas, or perhaps elsewhere.
As for efforts to recall some members of the State Legislature, they are all directly related to the larger fight over control of the Assembly, the lower house.
More than in most other states, California voters have often relied on recall elections to settle political grudges. But the current efforts are the most extensive and serious in years and underscore the tension roiling the leaderless Assembly.
Four members already face recalls, and four others are likely to after getting caught up in the struggle over whether to re-elect Assemblyman Willie Brown of San Francisco to a record-setting eighth term as Speaker of the 80-member body.
The first vote ended in a 40-to-40 tie after Mr. Brown managed to woo over a disenchanted Republican. Now advocates of recall intend to break that tie, and the legislative logjam, by changing the mix of Republicans and Democrats through a set of specially petitioned elections.
Republicans, who hope to elect Assemblyman James L. Brulte of Rancho Cucamonga as Speaker, have begun collecting the petition signatures needed to recall three Democrats, all narrow winners on Nov. 8. They are also canvassing in the districts of three other Democrats to see whether recalls are feasible.
And Republicans are working with a vengeance to recall their apostate member, Assemblyman Paul V. Horcher of West Covina, who not only jumped to Mr. Brown's side but also proclaimed himself to be an independent as he did so.
Democrats, meanwhile, are exploring the feasibility of recalling Assemblyman Steve Kuykendall, a Republican from Rancho Palos Verdes. He narrowly won his seat after accepting a last-minute $125,000 campaign contribution from Philip Morris U.S.A., the tobacco company whose lobbyists are a major legislative force in Sacramento.
Mr. Brulte predicted on Wednesday that he would eventually win the speakership, which after the governorship is the second-most-powerful political post in the nation's most politically powerful state. At the same time, he accused Mr. Brown of a sour-grapes effort to hold on to power despite the Republicans' November surge that erased the numerical edge Democrats had long held in the Assembly.
"Republicans are not about to make Willie Brown the Speaker of the Assembly, not again," Mr. Brulte declared.
Mr. Brown renewed an offer to negotiate a power-sharing arrangement with Mr. Brulte, lest the Legislature, already beleaguered by social and economic troubles, become mired in inaction upon its return to the capital on Jan. 4.
The chairman of the California Democratic Party, Bill Press, was in a less charitable political mood than Mr. Brown. He accused Mr. Brulte of misusing the recall process and engaging in "political blackmail."
"The recall process was clearly intended for malfeasance, criminal activity, failure to perform duties -- not for one vote," Mr. Press said at a news conference on Wednesday, choosing to ignore a possible Democratic effort to recall Assemblyman Kuykendall.
"This use of the recall amounts to blackmail," he added. "We don't tolerate criminal blackmail, and we shouldn't tolerate political blackmail."
In fact, over the years, Californians have used the recall process, or tried to use it, mainly to punish elected officials for ideological malfeasance, not genuine malfeasance.
Most efforts have failed for a lack of petition signatures, the usual requirement being the gathering of enough names to equal at least 10 percent of the turnout in the last election. This has been particularly true of efforts to recall governors, judges and legislators.
In the one recent effort to recall a legislator that actually went to a vote, David A. Roberti, a State Senator who favored stronger gun controls managed to emerge victorious. But he was so battered politically and financially that he was later defeated in a race for another state office.
In other cases where the required number of petition signatures have been obtained and a recall vote has actually been held, a few mayors, city council members and county supervisors have been ousted.
Encouraged by those efforts, some Los Angeles voters are now petitioning to remove the president of the city's school board, Mark Slavkin. He voted for the city's filing a legal challenge to Proposition 187, the hotly disputed ballot initiative that voters approved overwhelmingly on Nov. 8. Among other things, it denies schooling to immigrant children who are in California illegally.

HEADLINE: Abroad at Home;
A Political Mugging

BYLINE: By ANTHONY LEWIS 

 BODY:
Over the last two months there has been a series of savage right-wing attacks on a prospective nominee to be a Federal judge, Peter Edelman. It has the look of an organized campaign, and a curious one: an attempt not to contest the choice in the Senate but to scare President Clinton out of making the nomination.
I have known Mr. Edelman for years. Ordinarily that would keep me from writing on the subject. But the attackers describe a Peter Edelman so unlike the real person that I think comment is appropriate -- with due notice of my connection. 
 The lawyer I know is a man of reason, a consensus-builder widely liked and respected. He worked in the Justice Department and, during Robert Kennedy's years in the Senate, as his legislative assistant. Since 1982 he has been a law professor at Georgetown University. On leave now, he is Counselor to the Secretary of Health and Human Services.
His wife, Marian Wright Edelman, is president of the Children's Defense Fund, on whose board Hillary Rodham Clinton served. The Clintons and Edelmans are friends, a fact that may have something to do with the present hostility.
For some time Mr. Edelman has been on the Justice Department's lists of possible judicial nominees. Last September President Clinton selected him for a vacancy on the United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit. Mr. Edelman was told that the nomination would be formally sent to the Senate some time after the new Congress convened in January.
Then came the attacks, notably in The Washington Times and The Wall Street Journal and in a column by George Will. They painted Mr. Edelman as soft on crime and contemptuous of democracy: notions that I believe a few minutes of conversation with him would lead any reasonable person to dismiss.
The most intense criticism has been of an article Mr. Edelman wrote for the Hastings Law Journal in 1987. It suggests that a basis can be found in the Constitution for a right to a minimum income: enough to stave off desperate poverty.
The article notes that some conservative legal theorists want to go back to the long-abandoned arguments that government economic regulation is unconstitutional. It can be better argued, the article says, that the Constitution assures a measure of economic protection for citizens.
I entirely disagree with that argument. Locating affirmative economic rights in a constitution -- ours or any other country's -- will only weaken negative rights: the protections against official abuse that are found in the Bill of Rights.
But to lay out such an argument as a theory is hardly shocking, or proof of how one would act in the constraining role of a judge. Moreover, Mr. Edelman's Hastings article plainly saw the idea of going to court as a way of dramatizing the issue of poverty in this country. He said the judicial relief he described was "not desirable. The better response to poverty is legislative."
The vicious character of the campaign against Mr. Edelman is made clear by the charge that he is soft on crime. A Wall Street Journal editorial said:
"As director of New York State's Division for Youth in 1978, Mr. Edelman ordered a one-week furlough for a 17-year-old who had knifed a girl during a robbery. While on his furlough, this juvenile Willie Horton was arrested for raping, robbing and trying to electrocute a 63-year-old woman." That charge is completely false. Mr. Edelman had nothing to do with furloughing the 17-year-old. When a judge told him the youth was dangerous, he ordered a bar on any home visits -- but one had already been approved by the director of the facility where he was.
The truth was described in a letter to The Journal by J. Thomas Mullen, president of Catholic Charities in Cleveland, who was Mr. Edelman's deputy in New York. The state's former Governor, Hugh Carey, wrote to say that Mr. Edelman had been an outstanding youth director and had made many wise changes, including "far stricter supervision for violent offenders."
The incoming chairman of the Senate Judiciary Committee, Senator Orrin Hatch, Republican of Utah, would no doubt prefer not to handle controversial nominations. But he is a fair-minded man, and he surely will think it fairer for the committee to examine Mr. Edelman's views than to have him stopped on the way by a political mugging.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: U.S. and Japan to Negotiate On Automotive Trade Today

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS FRANTZ 

 BODY:
American and Japanese negotiators are to meet today in London for hastily arranged discussions on various trade issues between the two countries, including automobiles, auto parts and activity in emerging markets.
Jeffrey E. Garten, Under Secretary of Commerce for International Trade, said yesterday as he was departing for London that the meeting had been requested by Japanese officials. 
 The talks were announced yesterday by senior Japanese trade officials at a news conference in Tokyo. Hideaki Kumano, Japan's vice minister for international trade and industry, said the two sides had "suddenly agreed to have a meeting on various trade issues, including autos and auto parts."
Mr. Kumano said the talks would also cover trade in emerging markets like India, China and Vietnam, and trade with South Africa. Japan will be represented by Sozaburo Okamatsu, vice minister for international trade affairs.
While there has been some recent progress in the talks between the two countries, American negotiators have expressed frustration about the failure by Japan to further open its huge market in automobiles and automotive parts. President Clinton initiated proceedings against Tokyo in the fall that could lead to punitive tariffs on some Japanese auto exports to the United States.
In Tokyo early today, Japanese and American officials said that they did not expect substantive discussion on the issues involved in auto trade itself, only on the ground rules for future talks.
But even setting such ground rules could be difficult. At a meeting with the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, in Indonesia last month, Japan's Minister of International Trade and Industry, Ryutaro Hashimoto, set down three principles under which Japan would resume negotiations. These included that no numerical targets be set and that the discussions be limited to areas within the Japanese Government's scope and not those it deems matters for private industry.
If the United States accepted such principles, it could mean giving up talks aimed at getting Japanese auto dealers to carry more foreign cars or getting Japanese auto manufacturers to buy more foreign parts, two of Washington's key objetives.
Talks between the two countries on finding ways for Americans to sell more cars and parts in Japan have been stalled since September, leading to the President's consideration of sanctions against Japan.
At the meeting in Indonesia in November, held during the Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation forum, American and Japanese officials discussed resuming the talks.
Officials of both countries said they had been trying to arrange a meeting between Mr. Garten and Mr. Okamatsu since last month.
One Japanese official said the two Governments wanted to meet in a place that would be away from most American and Japanese journalists.
The timing of the meeting could also be related to the fact that Tuesday is to be Mr. Okamatsu's last day in his job as the top trade-related official at the ministry.
Both Mr. Okamatsu and Mr. Kumano will retire tomorrow. Mr. Kumano, in particular, is said to be resigning to take responsibility for an internal political matter at his ministry.
Prime Minister Tomiichi Murayama is scheduled to visit Washington in two weeks, and that might also motivate Japanese officials to want to report progress in trade negotiations.
The American trade deficit with Japan rose to a new peak of $6.7 billion in October, according to figures released this month by the Commerce Department. Cars and car parts account for about two-thirds of that, with the annual trade imbalance running at roughly $60 billion.
On Dec. 12, the Clinton Administration said it had finally reached an agreement with Tokyo to assure American companies access to Japan's $4.5 billion-a-year market for flat glass. There, the Japanese market has long been dominated by a cartel of three companies.
Administration officials portrayed the agreement as an important precedent because the Japanese Government agreed to take steps against a keiretsu, the close grouping of companies that hold one another's stock and buy one another's products.
However, American trade negotiators have recently begun to shift their emphasis from Japan to other countries in Asia and Latin America, where the growth in American exports is expected to rise sharply in the next decade.
Along with the increased exports, the political opportunities may be better elsewhere. The chance of a major breakthrough in trade talks with Japan is viewed as slim. And Mr. Clinton's advisers have said that they believe concentrating on other countries could yield an increase in exports and jobs in time for the 1996 Presidential election.

Japan Car Exports Up
 TOKYO, Dec. 26 (Reuters) -- The Japan Automobile Manufacturers Association said today that vehicle exports in November rose 10.7 percent from those of a year earlier, helped by a particularly strong showing in Europe. Earlier this month, the association forecast that Japan's overall vehicle exports would be 4.49 million units in 1994, down 10.6 percent from a year earlier. So far this year, vehicle exports in the 11 months through November have totaled 4,099,574 units, the association said.

HEADLINE: In the Shadow of Stealth Bombers, a Missouri Hamlet Booms

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: KNOB NOSTER, Mo.

 BODY:
Most towns with military installations are bracing for next year's large round of base closings and the economic calamity that they fear will follow. But not this tiny town, home to Whiteman Air Force Base.
Formerly a Minuteman missile base in the middle of west-central Missouri soybean fields, Whiteman is now home to the Air Force's growing fleet of radar-evading B-2 bombers. Four of the black, bat-winged planes, which cost $2.2 billion each, are flying at Whiteman, and 16 more are on the way. 
 Residents here confidently predict that their hamlet will dodge the disaster that hit cities like Charleston, S.C., in the last round of base closings in 1993. The Air Force has poured about $475 million into new roads, housing, maintenance buildings and special hangars for the B-2's. And the incoming Republican-controlled Congress is talking about ignoring a decision by the old Democratic-majority Congress to halt B-2 production at 20 planes, and may buy more bombers.
"This is the base of the future," said Mayor Bob Carr. "I can't conceive of them closing it down."
Indeed, Knob Noster and several neighboring communities are smugly riding a wave of economic gains right to the bank. Home construction in the area is up by 46 percent since 1990. Central Missouri State University in nearby Warrensburg is building a new stadium. Military retirees and urban dwellers fed up with life in Kansas City, 70 miles west of here, are flowing in.
The Whiteman Inn on U.S. 50 built a 36-room wing last summer, mainly to handle the extra construction workers and Air Force Reserve pilots who fly on weekends.
A standard three-bedroom, ranch-style house now costs about $75,000, up from $50,000 three years ago. In the Brookside Place subdivision just outside of town, houses are sprouting in place of cornfields and turkey farms, to the accompaniment of banging hammers and whirring saws.
"They're building new houses left and right; they're going bananas," said Wanda Zinn, an agent with Century 21 Gold Realty.
Knob Noster, population 2,261, derived its unusual name from two round-topped hills that overlook the town. Many people here say they hope the growth does not change the quiet nature of this sturdy Midwestern village.
Besides the Bank of Knob Noster and two restaurants, the town has a grocery store, two hardware stores, a barbershop and several other businesses. The Town Hall, police station, schools and several neatly kept homes complete the cityscape. There is an eight-member police force and a 25-member volunteer fire department.
"Our heaviest industry is a 300-pound Tupperware salesman," said Mayor Carr, who doubles as the town's humorist.
Whiteman pumps a $62.6 million payroll into the community, and the total annual economic impact on Knob Noster and neighboring communities is estimated at $327 million.
"This place would be nothing without the base," said Larry Uptegrove, a real estate agent who has lived in the area for 32 years.
But not everyone is happy with the base. Over pancakes at the Kozy Corner restaurant, Josefine and Richard Schowengerdt complain about the planes' deafening noise.
And the Schowengerdts have a much bigger problem: The Air Force wants to widen the road near the base -- right through their house. They are not alone: about 20 families are facing buyouts or bulldozers.
"They say they're making us fair offers, but if you don't like their offer they'll condemn you," said Mr. Schowengerdt, a 57-year-old retired Army staff sergeant. "People are being ripped out of homes they've lived in all their lives."
The B-2's arrival has also caused a subtle shift in the psyche of this military town. For more than 30 years, scores of citizens lived with the ultimate cold-war symbol -- 150 nuclear-tipped ICBM's -- literally in their backyards. In a doomsday scenario, Knob Noster would have been ground zero in any Soviet attack.
Now, with the end of the cold war, the Air Force is pulling up the missiles one by one like deadly weeds, filling in the silos and offering to sell the fenced one-acre sites back to farmers and ranchers.
Instead of this omnipotently ominous but silent weapon, Knob Noster now has a military neighbor that ties up traffic every time it takes off.
When the first B-2 flew in last December from California, more than 5,000 people jammed the base to see it. Since then, the B-2 has become such a hazard for gawking drivers that the Air Force is putting up signs that warn: "Low-flying aircraft. Please do not pull off the road."
And the swaggering bomber pilots cut a different figure than the missileers, who spent most of their time underground. "Your missile people are typically loners and much more reserved, whereas the pilots are much more outgoing and extroverted," said Gregory Rose, Warrensburg's acting city manager.
So far, Knob Noster seems to be adjusting. Phil Anthony's novelty shop on State Street does a brisk business in B-2 T-shirts, hats, key chains and bolo ties. The Office Cafe's B-2 special -- hamburger, french fries and a red, white and blue sundae for $3.99 -- was a smash. And Knob Noster High School's wrestling team has renamed itself the Stealth Panthers.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Knob Noster, Mo., home to Whiteman Air Force Base, seems to be adjusting to the new Stealth bombers that are replacing the intercontinental ballistic missiles at the base. The town's high school wrestling team has renamed itself the Stealth Panthers, as a poster outside a local shop proclaims. (Todd Buchanan for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Disaster Leaves Imprint on Town

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LANCASTER, Tex., Dec. 22

 BODY:
In a few moments of unforgettable terror, a tornado roared into Lancaster one night last April, killing three people, destroying 280 houses and damaging many more. It literally tore out the heart of the town, transforming the quaint, 19th-century Town Square into mounds of brick and glass and dirt.
Eight months later, Lancaster is finding that recovery is slow. 
 To be sure, people here say, some good has come out of the destruction. Before the tornado hit, this middle-class suburb south of Dallas, home to 22,000 people, tidy ranch houses and picturesque boutiques, was a pleasant enough place to live but not especially close-knit. Now it has been transformed by the common goal of revival.
Rick Eilers, who lost his second-floor photography studio on Town Square and is struggling to reopen, even says that in one sense "the tornado is the best thing that ever happened to Lancaster."
"We have a direction, we have a purpose," Mr. Eilers said. "We're pulling together as a community. It's the town that won't die."
But the challenge is enormous. Today, owners of fewer than one-tenth of the destroyed houses have managed to rebuild; many residents just gave up and moved elsewhere, while others did not have enough insurance to try reconstruction. A few streets are nothing more than a collection of concrete slabs. And on the town square, only a dozen of the roughly 35 stores that were there in April have reopened.
What remained of three magnificent live oaks in the center of the square has been carted away; a fourth has been cut back to a fraction of its former size and grandeur. Other trees have been slashed to stumps little taller than fire hydrants.
Thousands of tornadoes touch down in the United States every spring and summer. Typically, several dozen of them reach populated areas where they can cause major devastation.
On the damage scale, the tornado in Lancaster (residents pronounce it LANK-as-tur) did not necessarily qualify as catastrophic. As awesome as the destruction was in the tornado's path of roughly a half-mile wide and several miles long, broad sections of the city were largely untouched.
And while in the immediate panic of that twister night, one rescue official who had surveyed the damage along Elm Street had screamed at fire officials to expect up to 125 dead, the actual death toll was three. Warning sirens had given Lancaster residents a crucial few moments to head for cover in closets and bathtubs.
But for the people here, as in other communities where national attention, quickly captured, swiftly fades after misfortune, the tornado is a continuing event, an ever-present memory. And for a visitor returning to talk again to some of the people who were left stunned in the twister's aftermath, their comments are a reminder that the true cleanup of a natural disaster can take years.
Perhaps the most potent symbol of the tornado's continued impact on Lancaster is the burned rubble from destroyed houses and businesses piled high at the edge of town, near the airstrip. Eight months after the event, the pile still smolders.
"This is one of the hardest things for people to drive past; they have literally taken our town to the dump," said Jan Pruitt, the executive director of the Lancaster Outreach Center, a social-service agency that coordinates the rebuilding and recruited volunteers to construct several houses.
One resident struggling to come back is Janet (Wink) McCasland, who on April 26, the day after the tornado struck, sat amid the litter on Town Square that had been her trim brick restaurant, the Coach House. "I just have no idea where to go from here," she said at the time. "I don't know whether we can ever restore this place."
The restaurant remains padlocked and the general air of disorder has lessened only by degrees. Ms. McCasland, 48, who had no insurance on the contents, has limped along with a catering business and is still working frantically to reopen, maybe early in the new year, she hopes. But she has virtually no money left after spending roughly $20,000 on repairs and replacement, she said, and to complete the job could require at least $10,000. The experience has taught her a few raw realities.
"You have to have cash to get cash," she said, discussing her efforts to get a bank loan. "That's a fact of life."
Some other buildings on Town Square, which dates to 1854, have been restored, the original orange brick clashing with the newer red, while others remain boarded up or even in jagged disarray.
"When life deals you scraps, make a quilt!" an optimist has spray-painted on a piece of wood board that has replaced a window in one building.
Even those who have managed to rebuild their lives are still coping with bad memories. "Three months ago I started on antidepressants," said Pat Royder, a vivacious woman in a blue sequined pantsuit who reopened her clothing boutique on Town Square this fall with help from a $30,000 Small Business Administration loan. "And I'm telling you, if Pat Royder is on antidepressants, can you imagine how many other people here are on antidepressants?"
Lancaster is definitely on the way to recovery, said Ronnie Lowe, a minister at a Church of Christ church here. But, he added, "People now realize that it is going to take a long, long time to get there completely."
It is one measure of the continuing disarray that the city has not come up with a final figure on the extent of the damage: estimates start at $6 million and rise to four times that. And no one could estimate how much has been spent on rebuilding, other than to say that it is clearly much less than needed.
Still, Lancaster struggles along. On Elm Street, where house after house was destroyed, the droning of bulldozers and the high-pitched warning beep of heavy equipment fill the air.
Elizabeth Staggs spent 44 years in her house on a nearby street until it was destroyed that night as she huddled in a neighbor's home. In its place stands a new two-bedroom bungalow, thanks to the combination of a $12,000 Federal grant, supplies donated by the Outreach Center, and volunteer labor from Mr. Lowe and members of church groups.
"I'm home," said Mrs. Staggs, who is 76. "That's the main thing. I'm on the same lot. Of course the neighborhood looks completely different. But I'm home."
Even those still without homes have erected symbols of their presence. On a lot left vacant after her house was bulldozed away, for instance, Noma Byrum put up a solitary Christmas tree.
The country singers Willie Nelson and Janie Fricke gave benefit concerts last fall that raised nearly $80,000 for materials to build homes for Mrs. Staggs and others. About $175,000 in other donations have come in. The Dallas Parks Foundation donated 1,700 trees to be planted throughout the city -- live oaks, red oaks, cypress. And there is a sense that Lancaster is on the mend.
"There was real despair for awhile," Ms. Pruitt said. "We came off the immediate high of 'Thank God, we're still alive' to the low of, 'We're all alone.' Now we can see it coming back together."
Suzi Weaver, whose family owns several Town Square buildings, some needing extensive repair, added: "I believe in the town. But there are some people who have given up, said it's not worth it, taken the insurance money and gone."
As destructive as the tornado was, there were some important affirmations that people here cling to. For Mrs. Pruitt, one came as the tornado blew out the windows in their house and her children clung to her in terror. The 15-year-old, Chris, thought he was going to die, she said.
"Mom, I just want you to know something," the teen-ager said solemnly as the tornado howled. "I really do appreciate everything you've done for me."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:
Companies
 ITT agreed to sell its commercial finance unit for $4 billion in cash to the American subsidiary of Deutsche Bank. The deal reflects the continuing transformation of ITT, a onetime sprawling conglomerate that has been selling units in an effort to emphasize its growing entertainment and gambling business. [Page D1.] 
 Pepsico sees Chile as a crucial test of the company's ability to expand in South America. While its Pizza Hut and Taco Bell fast-food chains have done well in Chile, Pepsico is attempting a turnaround with its KFC chain. [D8.]
 Fourteen insurance companies have been sued by the Archdiocese of Milwaukee for refusing to make payments to people seeking damages from the Roman Catholic Church because they were victims of sexual misconduct by priests. [A10.]
 Signature Products plans to market two types of bullets that escape a Federal ban on fragmenting rounds because they are made of carbon-based plastics, rather than metal. [A10.]
 
International
 When it devalued the peso, Mexico may have devalued investor confidence even more. The country depends on foreign investment, particularly from the United States, to finance much of its economic activity, and the crisis has led many foreign investors to become suspicious of the Government's economic policies. [D1.]
 Hastily arranged trade talks between the United States and Japan are to start in London today. The talks will deal with opening Japanese markets to American automobiles and auto parts, trade in emerging markets and other trade issues. [D1.]
 OPEC's grasp on world oil prices is slipping as production from countries outside the oil cartel increases. In the North Sea, the North Slope of Alaska and in China, Russia and Vietnam, new exploration and technology and improved corporate efficiency are helping the industry revive non-OPEC production. [D1.]
 A deal with the Ukrainian Government to convert weapons for civilian uses has been bogged down by bureaucracy. The deal by Alliant Techsystems, a large American arms maker, illustrates the difficulties in doing business in the former Soviet Union. [D1.]
 
The Economy
 The Clinton Administration is turning to privatization, proposing to make some agencies Government-chartered corporations. But Government experience in imposing market forms on bureaucratic operations is mixed. For every economic success like Fannie Mae, there is a service like Amtrak that loses money. [A1.]
 The holiday shopping season is not over yet, to which throngs of shoppers across the nation, from inner-city sidewalk marts to hermetic suburban malls, could attest yesterday. [A1.]
 Sales of personal computers surged this holiday season, up 33 to 35 percent from a year ago, a market research company said. Multimedia computers were among the big sales gainers. [D5.]
 The Wall Street area is no longer the home of financial might it once was. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani wants to revive the area and has proposed reducing taxes and changing zoning rules. But the district's ebbing fortunes may reflect deeper problems: poor public transportation to the suburbs and obsolete buildings that discourage renovation for either housing or offices. [B1.]
 
Today's Columns
 Best Buy has grown quickly, but its stock price has slumped since the company said earlier this month that earnings would be below Wall Street expectations. Some analysts suggest that Best Buy could have more disappointing news in its current quarter, largely because the consumer electronics chain has about $100 million more in inventory than expected. Market Place. [D6.]
 A stock exchange is not an easy thing to sell. But the American Stock Exchange, the smallest of the three big national exchanges, is aggressively trying to make its case in a series of bold print advertisements using conceptual art. Advertising. [D8.]

HEADLINE: 3 Empowerment Areas Must Create a Wish List

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Having been selected as one the nation's first Federal Empowerment Zones, with $300 million in public money available for a broad spectrum of new projects and services over the next 10 years, Harlem, Washington Heights and a part of the South Bronx must now agree on a shopping list. And it may not be easy.
"Now comes the really hard part," said Representative Charles B. Rangel, the Democrat from Harlem who was one of the chief Congressional architects of the empowerment zone program. 
 Mr. Rangel told a group of local leaders in Harlem on Wednesday that the challenge now "is getting people in this community to read from the same page."
The empowerment zone designation, announced last Tuesday, means that the area stands to reap a huge windfall in direct government assistance at a time of fiscal cutbacks at every level of government.
While the Federal commitment under the program is $100 million, both New York City and New York State say they will match the Federal funds. The city and state pledges, made more than a year ago by former Mayor David N. Dinkins and Gov. Mario M. Cuomo, were reiterated last week by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and Governor-elect George E. Pataki.
Unlike the procedures in a succession of antipoverty programs that tried to revive the blighted neighborhoods of New York City, decisions on how the money will be spent in the new empowerment zone will be left largely to people in the zone itself, not to agencies in Washington, Albany or lower Manhattan.
Officials in the zone must abide by an outline they submitted in their application for the Federal designation. The outline included adult education; AIDS prevention and treatment; day care; job training; small business loans; cleaning up vacant lots; improvements to La Marqueta, the shopping strip along Park Avenue between 110th and 116th Streets, and jump-starting the long-delayed plan for the Harlem International Trade Center on 125th Street.
But the specifics of which programs will be financed, and with how much government money, seem certain to provoke intense competition.
The Rev. Calvin O. Butts, the pastor of the Abyssinian Baptist Church, said he would lobby aggressively for only one new program envisioned for the empowerment zone: a small-business assistance program called the One Stop Capital Shop, in which government aid would be used to provide low-income loans and counseling for entrepreneurs.
Percy E. Sutton, the Harlem broadcasting executive and former Manhattan Borough President, said his shopping list was longer. Emphasizing the need to attract high-technology business and train high-technology workers, he said the government aid should be used for a computer training center to provide the public schools with better classroom computer equipment and to create a computerized marketing system that would link business people in the zone with customers and suppliers in Africa and Latin America.
Other ideas seem certain to come from the South Bronx, where Fernando Ferrer, the Bronx Borough President, had waged an unsuccessful fight to have the boundaries of the zone changed to include more of his borough. Although only a sliver of the South Bronx is included in the zone, it includes Yankee Stadium, and many officials expect aggressive efforts to steer part of the government aid to improvements in or around the stadium, particularly since the city is concerned that the Yankees may leave the area.
"Yankee Stadium is an economic generator, and that is what the program is designed for," said Donald J. Cogsville, chief executive officer of the Harlem Economic Development Corporation, the state agency in charge of applying for employment zone benefits. Nonetheless, he said, any government assistance would probably be confined to "the blocks and the people surrounding the stadium," instead of the structure itself or the Yankees organization.
Clint Roswell, a spokesman for Mr. Ferrer, said that Mr. Ferrer, while opposing any direct government aid to the stadium itself, wanted to steer some of the empowerment zone funds to the Bronx Terminal Market, a blighted retail area that separates Yankee Stadium from the Harlem River, where ferry service, new stores and parking are planned.
He said Mr. Ferrer also hoped the influx of government money would be used to help revive the Harlem Rail Yards, an abandoned industrial tract in the South Bronx near Port Morris, with new freight train service and a paper mill for waste recycling.
Under the rules of the program, details of how the money will be spent are to be left to the boards of two as-yet uncreated local development corporations -- one for Harlem and Washington Heights, with its members appointed by Mr. Rangel, and the other for the South Bronx, with its members appointed by Mr. Ferrer.
Although the local development corporations will have most authority for distributing government aid, they will report to another yet-to-be-created board. That board will include representatives from Harlem, the South Bronx, the city and the state.
Although administration of the zone is most likely to be complex, Mr. Cogsville said that most of the money would be dedicated to social service programs, not to construction projects.

HEADLINE: OPEC Rivals Curb Cartel's Impact

BYLINE: By ALLEN R. MYERSON, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: DALLAS, Dec. 26

 BODY:
The oil production of non-OPEC nations, declining since 1988, has begun an unexpected revival this year that will continue into the next century, tempering price increases and limiting the world's dependence on the oil cartel.
Already, higher supplies from oilfields beyond OPEC's domain have helped to keep prices low despite booming demand and OPEC production quotas. 
 New technology, improved corporate efficiency and the opening to exploration of vast foreign tracts, energy experts say, promise to increase non-OPEC production 6.5 percent by 2000.
On Alaska's North Slope, new finds and modern techniques for sucking more from existing wells have kept oil flowing at rates nearly two-thirds higher than the state predicted.
In China, Vietnam and Russia, Western exploration and production crews are swarming to areas that had been long closed to foreign investment.
In the North Sea, fields thought to be entering their dotage after 25 years of pumping are producing 20 percent more oil than last year, helped by lower production costs and new discoveries.
For the first time since 1988, output from non-OPEC nations will rise this year, to about 39.1 million barrels, up 600,000 barrels from last year, according to Cambridge Energy Research Associates, a leading consulting firm. The Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries itself has recently confirmed this turnaround and predicted its continuation.
"The non-OPEC production increase," Dr. Subroto, OPEC's former secretary general, said in a recent speech, "changes the rules of the game."
Though more oil comes from outside the cartel, OPEC's coordination, vast reserves and spare capacity, especially in Saudi Arabia, have given its member nations disproportionate influence. By 2007, the cartel expects to be producing a majority of the world's oil. Still, in the late 1980's, nonmembers rebuffed OPEC's suggestions to cooperate on pricing.
There are no guarantees that higher production will keep prices stable. In the United States, new oil tanker insurance standards and reformulated gasoline mandates might roil the energy markets early next year. More Russian or Middle Eastern turmoil, or especially rapid growth in global demand, could still create shortages. In the long run, the largest part of the world's increasing thirst for oil can only be met by OPEC.
But for next year, the International Energy Agency, which represents oil-consuming nations, predicts that more than half the world's increased demand of 1.1 million barrels a day will be met by producers who do not belong to OPEC.
"All this non-OPEC oil is postponing the day when OPEC will be required to fill the gap," said Robert W. Esser, a Cambridge Energy senior consultant.
The latest supply figures and prices contrast remarkably with the outlook a few years ago. The consensus of industry executives and analysts in 1990 was that oil, then $20 a barrel, would go to $30 by 1995. They cited rising worldwide demand and lagging production.
The demand has materialized, especially among developing nations. But so has new capacity. Oil prices, which rose above $36 a 42-gallon barrel in 1981, have recently been less than half that because of increased supplies.
Oil companies have more than accommodated themselves to high production at low prices. "I don't think there's any question that the industry is learning to do things, like deepwater drilling, at $17 a barrel and $2 a cubic foot, that couldn't be done two or three years ago," said H. Laurance Fuller, Amoco's chief executive, referring to today's low oil and natural gas prices. "When life is tough you find ways to do things better."
Although most of the technological advances and foreign ventures are known, their combined impact is only now becoming clear. Even the production declines in the United States are slowing, because of new sources ranging from the Gulf of Mexico to the North Slope of Alaska.
New technology is rendering the rig count, long the most trusted guide to the domestic oil industry's health, less useful. Faster drilling and pinpoint targeting result in more discoveries and production for each drilling rig.
Twelve years ago, drilling 8,000 feet deep took 17 or 18 days. More durable drill bits have greatly reduced time spent pulling long strings of tubing to the surface, so the same depth now takes just five days.
"If we extend this trend,' joked Forrest E. Hoglund, chief executive of Enron Oil and Gas Company, in a recent speech, "by 1997 we shouldn't need a rig."
Others, however, are truly drilling without rigs. On Alaska's North Slope, Atlantic Richfield is using motorized drill bits at the end of flexible tubing to drill sidetracks to existing holes.
Newly developed North Slope fields and new drilling techniques have greatly slowed the decline in Alaska's flow. In 1988 the state predicted that daily production would fall from 2 million barrels then to 1 million by now. Current production: 1.6 million barrels a day.
Fields thought to be played out in other countries, including Venezuela, an OPEC member distant from the jittery Persian Gulf, are coming back to life with horizontal drilling and enhanced recovery techniques.
Methods for mapping underground reservoirs have improved the odds of a strike, promoting new production. What geologists call 3D-seismic technology bounces sound waves off underground formations, then uses computers to create three-dimensional pictures. These techniques help give oil companies the confidence to build billion-dollar platforms in the Gulf of Mexico.
The gulf is the leading site for deep-water production, which has been extended to 2,860 feet from 1,351 feet in the last six years. Oil companies are laying plans to bring the same technology to West Africa and South China. Using what they call directional drilling, they can sink wells miles from a platform, in much deeper water, without the cost of separate platforms.
Oil companies have also cut costs by reducing staff, making new projects more economical. Employment in the domestic oil industry has fallen by more than half in 12 years, from 920,000 to 450,000 today. Amoco's new data processing center in Tulsa, Okla., will do with 1,000 people next year what 1,800 did last year.
Lower costs and newer discoveries have allowed the North Sea to sharply increase its daily output by about 900,000 barrels, to 4.7 million, this year. In 1990, Britain predicted that its share of North Sea production would go from 1.9 million barrels then to 2.1 million now -- far short of the current rate of 2.5 million.
Other countries are giving the oil companies' capital and expertise an increasingly warm welcome. China, Vietnam, Venezuela and the former Soviet republics are only some that in recent years have been opened to foreign oil investment.
The rules governing these investments, often imposed by officials new to capitalist notions of a fair return, can be murky and changeable. Some oil companies are already disappointed by their earnings.
Still, an energy conference in Houston earlier this month, sponsored by the Arthur Andersen accounting and consulting firm, seemed like a global investment bazaar. Chinese and Vietnamese officials vied with OPEC leaders in appealing for oil company executives to help plumb their supplies.
In a speech there, even Dr. Subroto, the former OPEC leader, cited the advances of OPEC rivals while insisting that the West had to invest much more in OPEC nations to satisfy its future oil demand.
In 1990, Dr. Subroto predicted that world demand for OPEC crude oil would reach 29 million barrels a day by next year. But OPEC is now pumping only about 25 million barrels a day and plans to maintain that level in 1995.
Daily non-OPEC production of crude oil and associated liquids fell from a peak of more than 41 million barrels in 1988 to 38.5 million barrels last year, according to Cambridge Energy. Mr. Esser sees the rate returning to about 41 million barrels in 2000, then leveling off. OPEC has recently adopted almost exactly the same outlook.
Others are more optimistic. John H. Lichtblau, chairman of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation, sees output by OPEC rivals continuing to rise through the year 2005 for a total daily gain of about five million barrels from this year.
In the United States, the world's largest oil user and importer, increased technology and efficiency are slowing the declines in output. Since 1985, production has fallen from 10.6 million barrels a day to an estimated 8.3 million this year. Cambridge Energy predicts 7.5 million barrels a day in 2000.
Mr. Esser's challenge in predicting supplies is estimating how much more expenses will fall. "Costs are coming down so much that the minute you put a dot on a graphic," he said, "it could be wrong."
