HEADLINE: Battle Over a Baby's Future Raises Hard Ethical Issues

BYLINE: By GINA KOLATA 

 BODY:
Ryan Nguyen was born on Oct. 27, six weeks before his due date, asphyxiated and with barely a heartbeat. His doctors at Sacred Heart Medical Center in Spokane, Wash., revived him after heroic efforts. But a few weeks later, they decided that aggressive medical care would only prolong his suffering and that he would never survive infancy. They suggested that his life support be withdrawn.
His parents refused, setting off an emotional battle over the baby's medical care. In the two months since his birth, Ryan Nguyen (pronounced win) has become a cause celebre in the Pacific Northwest. 
 On one side are Ryan's parents and their lawyer, who say the doctors at Sacred Heart were ready to allow the baby to die because his care was so expensive. On the other side are Ryan's doctors at Sacred Heart and other medical experts, who say the baby's case is hopeless because of his multiple problems, including kidney failure, bowel obstructions and brain damage.
And finally, there are the doctors at a medical center in Portland, Ore., who, reading about the baby in a newspaper, said that they were willing to treat him and that he was likely to survive. Almost immediately, Ryan was transferred to their care.
The arguments over the baby's future have evoked some of the most difficult issues in medical care: When should treatment be withdrawn? Who decides? Who should speak for a baby? And should there be limits on how much society will pay for a long shot at saving a life?
It is, ethicists and medical specialists say, a case that goes far beyond the circumstances of Ryan Nguyen's life.
Public debate on ending life has focused on the terminally ill and the elderly. This case raises the same questions, but at the start of life.
Dr. Mark Siegler, director of the MacLean Center for Clinical Ethics at the University of Chicago, said Ryan's case "summarizes some of the unsettled and unresolved difficulties in American medicine, and not just in pediatric medicine."
Dr. Siegler continued: "We thought that after 20 years of debate over end-of-life decisions that we had at least agreed on some empirical rules that would apply. But we find that when the rules are challenged, and particularly when the challenger is arguing for a pro-life decision, the guidelines tend to collapse."
He also pointed out that advance directives, living wills and claims that treatment was futile all fell by the wayside if an interested party, particularly a family member, wanted treatment to continue.
Dr. Arthur Caplan, director of the Center for Bioethics at the University of Pennsylvania, asked: "If you can't say 'no' in a situation where there is permanent and apparently irreversible kidney failure, an inability to feed properly and moderate to severe brain damage -- a prognosis that is as grim as possible -- then do we have any credibility talking about rationing anything in our health care system? If you can't say 'no' here, then when would you say 'no'?"
But Dr. Randall D. Jenkins, director of pediatric nephrology at Legacy Emanuel Children's Hospital in Portland, where Ryan is being treated, disagrees. He said that although Ryan had "multiple problems," including brain damage and kidney failure, and that he most likely would need a kidney transplant, he nonetheless had "a significant chance of surviving and having a good quality life."
George Annas, a health law professor at Boston University, said that no matter how dismal a patient's outlook, "if you look hard enough, you can always find someone who will treat."
"There is no such thing as a medical standard that says you shouldn't treat," Professor Annas added, "and that's the problem. Even after we had this two-year debate about our health care system that everyone knows costs too much, we can't stop. Money's no object."
Dr. Carl Bodenstein, a neonatologist at Sacred Heart, said that for a baby as sick as Ryan, medical costs could average $2,000 a day.
Ryan's medical problems are severe, Dr. Bodenstein said. Although his kidneys produced urine, it was almost entirely water; they did not filter toxins from his blood. His intestines were blocked, and he had to be fed intravenously. And CT scans showed signs of brain damage. He also had recurrent seizures that sometimes could be controlled only if he was given three anti-epilepsy drugs, another indication of potentially severe brain damage, Dr. Bodenstein said.
For the first few weeks of Ryan's life, Dr. Bodenstein and his colleagues kept him alive by feeding him intravenously and giving him dialysis to substitute for his failed kidneys. But eventually they decided that his kidney problems made it almost certain that Ryan would die in infancy. To survive, Ryan would need to be maintained on dialysis for about two years and then have a kidney transplant. Experts in pediatric kidney disease who were consulted by Dr. Bodenstein thought it was virtually impossible to pull off that feat.
Dr. Ellis D. Avner, a professor of pediatrics and director of the kidney program at the Children's Hospital and Medical Center in Seattle, was one of those experts. Dr. Avner said his assessment was that "long-term dialysis would not only be inappropriate but would be immoral." He added, "It would prolong pain and agony in a child that has no likelihood of a good outcome."
Dr. Avner explained that in the best of circumstances, when a baby is born full term and has nothing wrong except failed kidneys, long-term dialysis of infants requires enormous effort by doctors, families and nurses, and still frequently fails.
It is, he said, "an often grueling and heroic effort in which we have success maybe 50 percent of the time, if that, in reaching a point where a child is large enough and healthy enough for a renal transplant." Even then, Dr. Avner said, 25 percent of those who receive transplants die.
Dr. Bodenstein and his colleagues suggested to Ryan's parents, Darla and Nghia (pronounced nee), that they stop dialysis and let Ryan die.
Mr. and Mrs. Nguyen found a lawyer, Russell Van Camp, who got a court order to keep the dialysis going while the hospital and Mr. Van Camp's wife, Teresa, a nurse, searched for a medical center that would take Ryan.
"I'm a pro-life attorney and this is pro-life, keeping the baby alive," Mr. Van Camp said in a telephone interview. "He was dying because his kidneys weren't working too good, that's all. A child is a precious gift; give him a chance."
Mr. Van Camp said that an issue in the doctors' decision to allow Ryan to die was that his parents are unemployed and on Medicaid, and that the hospital was unwilling to spend large sums of public money, especially on a baby that "doesn't have blond hair and blue eyes." Mr. Nguyen is a Vietnamese refugee, and Mrs. Nguyen is an American Indian.
Dr. Bodenstein vigorously denies those accusations.
Doctors at other major medical centers also did not want to treat Ryan, saying it would be futile. Dr. Nancy Binder, a neonatologist at Oregon Health Sciences Center, in Portland, who was consulted on the case, said she and her colleagues took it to the hospital ethics committee and determined that aggressive treatment would "involve all sorts of invasive procedures, pain and suffering, and we still couldn't see the baby eventually having a transplant and going home."
All it would mean, Dr. Binder said, was "a longer, more miserable death."
But on Dec. 13, Dr. Jenkins and his colleague at Legacy Emanuel, Dr. Patrick Lewallen, director of newborn intensive care, read about Ryan in The Portland Oregonian. They called the doctors at Sacred Heart and reviewed the case with them. And then, Dr. Lewallen said, "we sat down with our physicians and administrators and basically decided that we would take this patient."
On Dec. 16 doctors at Legacy Emanuel operated on Ryan's blocked intestines, removing several inches of bowel. "It's a moderate amount, but we have seen many babies lose that amount of bowel and survive," Dr. Lewallen said.
He added: "As medical director of a 40-bed nursery, I go in there, and Ryan is not the sickest. There are other cases that are harder. At this point, we don't have ethical issues surrounding his care."
On Sunday, Dr. Lewallen said that Ryan's condition was "guarded" and that the baby was "hanging in there." He also said that Ryan had "lots of problems but medically he's pretty darn stable."
Dr. Lewallen said he hoped that when Ryan was able to eat and get stronger, he would eventually be able to have a kidney transplant, and at that point he should have a 75 percent chance of survival.
Mrs. Nguyen, speaking in a telephone interview, said she, her husband and her 17-month-old son, Austin, plan to move to Vancouver, Wash., to be near Ryan during his expected two-month stay in the Portland hospital and yet not lose their Washington Medicaid payments.
Washington's Medicaid program has agreed to pay for Ryan's care in Oregon. But medical experts said that even Oregon's Medicaid program, which has determined not to pay for some expensive and medically debatable procedures, would also pay for Ryan. The reason is that the state considers each condition separately, and each of Ryan's medical problems by itself would qualify.
Mrs. Nguyen said Ryan was much better than the Sacred Heart doctors had told her. He does not have brain damage, she said, and "his kidneys are getting better." Mrs. Nguyen said her hope was that Ryan would "grow up to be healthy and be with his bigger brother."
Others are less sanguine.
"The real problem here is, what do you do in a life and death situation in which the absolute majority of educated, unbiased medical opinion favors death and in which, to some extent, society doesn't allow for death without going to the absolute nth degree?" Dr. Bodenstein asked. "Yes, technically, a lot of people can do a lot of things for this baby, yet a significant bulk of educated medical opinion feels it is not in this baby's interest to have death prolonged. Where do you go from there?"
And, even more troubling, Dr. Bodenstein said, is the question of why the decisions became so contentious.
"Where did this all go so wrong?" he asked. "Where did rational, civil processes with this family get lost? Where did we lose it with this family? That's the hardest part."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Nghia and Darla Nguyen with their 2-month-old son, Ryan, at a hospital in Portland, Ore., where the baby is being treated for multiple medical problems, including brain damage and severe kidney failure. (Don Ryan for The New York Times) (pg. A12)

HEADLINE: Wall Street, No Longer Financial Epicenter, Struggles to Cling to Cachet

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Perhaps more than any other street in America, Wall Street has come to represent a concept beyond itself. As a synonym for big business, it has lent its name to movies, television shows and a national newspaper.
But these days, the large brokerage houses that once lined Wall Street and its cavernous side streets have spread far and wide in Manhattan, a reflection of how the area south of Chambers Street is no longer the dominant financial services center it once was. With aging buildings that cannot accommodate huge computers, and a declining need for financial companies to be near each other, The Street and its neighborhood are mere reminders of what they once were. 
 To combat that decline, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani this month proposed reducing taxes and changing zoning rules to revive the area. With the inducements, officials hope not only to keep the companies that are already there, but also to attract new housing and a broader diversity of business to Wall Street and the surrounding blocks of lower Manhattan.
Whether these changes will reverse the area's decline is open to debate, as downtown's ebbing fortunes may reflect deeper problems: poor public transportation to the suburbs and obsolete buildings that discourage renovation for either housing or offices.
For 25 years, New York City's investment banks and brokerage houses have been moving away from Wall Street, shifting their computers and clerical staffs to Brooklyn, New Jersey and as far away as Tampa, Fla., while headquarters and trading operations have been widely dispersed in Manhattan, from TriBeCa to the upper reaches of midtown, in Rockefeller Center.
In fact, Wall Street, with only one of the city's 20 largest brokerage houses, is one of several neighborhoods in Manhattan to cling to an undeserved cachet. Madison Avenue, still known as the center of the nation's advertising industry, retains only 2 of the city's 20 largest agencies. And of the city's 35 Broadway theaters, only 7 are on Broadway, though many are on the surrounding streets.
Many of the brokerage houses say they are committed to staying in the city, but feel they do not have to be near the stock exchange.
"We are still a community that wants to be in Manhattan, but the fact is that we don't need to be neatly geographically settled," said Robert Connor, a spokesman for Smith Barney, which was the first major firm to break from the cluster of securities companies that were around Wall Street, if not on it, in 1969. It moved that year to midtown, at the Avenue of the Americas and 54th Street.
Mr. Connor and other Wall Street executives say the need to be close to the city's stock exchanges -- and each other -- has been all but eliminated by computers, fax machines and other innovations.
In a sign that brokerage houses are willing to move to new and untested locations, Smith Barney is moving again, this time to Greenwich Street in TriBeCa, just off Franklin Street. Its move, announced a year ago, followed word in August 1993 from Morgan Stanley & Company that it had bought a headquarters in another unlikely location: at the corner of 47th and Broadway, in the glitter of Times Square.
Morgan Stanley now says it will move into the Times Square building, at 1585 Broadway, over the next six months. The move shows that if there is still a financial epicenter, it is the whole island of Manhattan, not just downtown.
"Two years ago, if I told people a securities firm was moving to Times Square, they would look at me like I was from Mars," said Martin Turchin, vice chairman at Edward S. Gordon, a commercial real estate brokerage firm that deals extensively with Wall Street clients.
Even though the firms deserting downtown are not necessarily leaving the city, the Giuliani administration feels it is necessary to give tax breaks to keep a critical mass of businesses downtown, which remains the third-largest business district in the country, behind midtown Manhattan and downtown Chicago. It also hopes that by attracting housing and a broader diversity of businesses, it would create a more vibrant, 24-hour neighborhood -- something that does not exist today.
To many urban planners and business executives, the dispersal of Wall Street has actually been a vote of confidence in New York City. Although it has become routine for securities companies to threaten to leave the city, thereby extracting lucrative long-term tax breaks from City Hall, no major firm has moved its headquarters or principal trading operations outside Manhattan, although many have moved their computers and data processing out.
To be sure, Wall Street retains the symbol of finance, the New York Stock Exchange (its front entrance is on Broad Street, but there is a side door on Wall Street). And the street remains home to several well-known brokerage houses that do not rank among the city's largest, including Gruntal & Company and D.H. Blair & Company.
Morgan Guaranty Trust, one of the nation's largest banks, built a new headquarters on Wall Street in the late 1980's, and operates a stock and bond trading subsidiary, J.P. Securities, from the same building, at 60 Wall Street. But for most of the blocks south of Chambers Street, including Wall Street, the flight of major brokerage houses and investment banks has left the neighborhood burdened by old office buildings, with nearly a quarter of their space vacant, and with their prospects of luring tenants undermined by small floors, poor ventilation and wiring, and outdated architecture.
"Modern technology has reduced the premium that companies are prepared to pay to be in immediate proximity to the New York Stock Exchange," said Carl Weisbrod, president of the newly formed Alliance for Downtown New York, the nation's largest business improvement district. Its members, all private lower Manhattan property owners, have agreed to contribute $8.6 million a year to upgrade the neighborhood's streets, improve public transportation and attract a greater variety of business and housing.
"We cannot rely on one industry to sustain any part of the city," said Fran Reiter, the Deputy Mayor for Planning and Community Development, who was one of the chief architects of the incentive package.
Mayor Giuliani's plan to attract more diverse business includes proposals for express taxi lanes and new bus routes from midtown Manhattan, and even envisions express train service for suburban commuters, linking the neighborhood to Grand Central Terminal and Atlantic Terminal in Brooklyn.
Ms. Reiter said a top priority remains keeping lower Manhattan's brokerage firms and investment banking companies in the area.
"Those that remain are looking to move out, and finding suitable space in midtown Manhattan is becoming a problem," she said. "Unless we do something to turn lower Manhattan around, they may be lost."
Experts say the decline of Wall Street can be traced to the years following the stock market crash of 1929, particularly 1933, when Congress passed the Glass-Steagall Act, requiring the powerful Wall Street banks created by J. P. Morgan and other financiers to stop trading in stocks and bonds. The separation of banks and brokerage houses remained largely intact until the 1980's, when Morgan Guaranty Trust and a handful of other large banks seized on provisions in Federal law that enabled them to re-enter the brokerage business.
During the 1930's, the old-line Wall Street banks were forced to surrender their brokerage business to a variety of more specialized trading and investment companies, which often took space nearby in lower Manhattan.
For these businesses, the biggest period of growth -- and movement away from the blocks surrounding Wall Street -- came from the mid-1960's to the late 1980's. In those years, a huge expansion of international business, waves of corporate mergers and acquisitions, and soaring increases in stock trading volume combined with new computer technology to prompt a search for new, larger and more modern office buildings.
The main lower Manhattan destinations became the World Trade Center, constructed between 1962 and 1977, where Dean Witter and Salomon Brothers are based, and the World Financial Center, built between 1985 and 1988, where four major firms, including Merrill Lynch, are based.
Seven of the biggest brokerage houses moved to headquarters in midtown Manhattan, where they remain. They include Paine Webber, on the Avenue of the Americas off 49th Street; Bear, Stearns, at Park Avenue and 45th Street, and Lazard Freres, in Rockefeller Center.
Jeffrey Schaefer, chief economist for the Securities Industry Association, a trade group, said the dispersal of the major securities companies was in part an attempt to find more prestigious locations that were easier for their employees and clients to reach from the New York suburbs and the region's airports.
Now, while they still need larger and more modern buildings than can be found on the blocks around Wall Street, he said the priority of many of the securities companies is to find the best deals they can strike on corporate real estate, with few reservations about moving off the beaten path in Manhattan.
"Firms need to consolidate their operations under one roof, but they don't need to be closely linked to other firms," Mr. Schaefer said. "They are driven by economics."

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:
CUOMO STILL AN ENIGMA AFTER 12 YEARS
 When Governor Cuomo steps down after 12 years on Saturday, he will bring to an end one of the lengthiest and most celebrated governorships in recent history. Almost as soon as he was elected, Mr. Cuomo was a national figure. He was twice a shadow candidate for President and was regarded by many as the most inspiring speaker in contemporary politics. Yet despite these achievements, there hovers about Mr. Cuomo the sense, predicted so long ago in his diary, that he has disappointed many. A1.
 
A DAY TO SHOP FOR THE SHEER THRILL OF IT
 As much as yesterday might have been proclaimed a holiday of rest, throngs of happily incorrigible people paid tribute to it as the first shopping day of the rest of their lives. And retailers across the region awaited them with relief, for the early business measurements were that the pre-Christmas buying season this year was weaker than anticipated. A1.
 
NEW YORK CITY
 

GIULIANI DENIES DELAY IN DRUG SWEEP
 Mayor Giuliani denied yesterday that he had postponed a planned police initiative against drug dealers in Queens, disputing a report that he had deemed the program too costly. "No decision has been made," the Mayor said of the program, which would flood sections of Queens with 600 undercover and uniformed narcotics officers beginning around Jan. 15. B3.
 
DECISION TIME IN EMPOWERMENT ZONE
Having been selected as one the nation's first Federal Empowerment Zones, with $300 million in public funds available for a broad spectrum of new projects and services over the next 10 years, Harlem, Washington Heights and a part of the South Bronx must now agree on the specifics of which programs will be financed, and with how much government money. B3.
 
BOY, 12, DIES WHILE PLAYING BASKETBALL
A 12-year-old boy collapsed while playing basketball in a holiday tournament in Brooklyn and died later at a hospital. Efforts to revive the boy, Steven Riley of Flushing, Queens, failed as stunned players and spectators stood by silently, some of them praying. B4.

A CHRISTMAS WITHOUT LIGHTS
 The residents and their holiday guests at more than 125,000 Connecticut households that lost electricity over the weekend as a result of a northeaster celebrated Christmas Eve and Christmas morning the old-fashioned way, with candles and lanterns and resourcefulness. B5.

 The New York State Senate's chief advocate of capital punishment said yesterday that the Legislature was likely to adopt lethal injection in a widely expected move to restore the death penalty next year because it is regarded as more humane than the electric chair and might appease death penalty critics. HEADLINE: Casualty of the Peso: Investor Confidence

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Dec. 26

 BODY:
As the dust settles from Mexico's worst economic breakdown in more than a decade, it is becoming clear that the Government's desperate actions last week in floating the peso may have devalued investor confidence even more than they devalued the currency, making the recovery of trust essential to Mexico's rebuilding.
More than any other emerging nation, Mexico has depended on the confidence of foreign investors to finance much of its economic activity. Foreigners, particularly those from the United States, buy nearly 40 percent of Mexican treasury notes and account for almost 30 percent of the stock market's capitalization. 
 "Mexico is unique in the magnitude of its dependence on short-term foreign capital," said Lacey Gallagher, a Mexico analyst at Standard & Poor's, which on Friday lowered its ratings on the Mexican Government peso-denominated notes and said it would closely watch the country's foreign debt. "There is no other emerging market in which foreign investors hold as much local currency and short-term Government debt as they do in Mexico."
After losing about 30 percent of its value during what is already being called Mexico's "Christmas crisis," the peso appeared to have calmed down somewhat on Friday. However today, the peso fell to a rate of 5.075 to the dollar, compared with 4.7 late Friday. On the Mexican stock exchange, the Bolsa index rose 0.04 percent, to 2,342.79.
Many investors said they were wary of putting too much faith in the Government's overall economic program or the specifics of its hastily arranged recovery plan.
The investors said they wanted to see how officials would pick up the pieces and move forward on the lower-inflation path that Mexico has followed for six years. But the crisis has left all the Government's economic targets and policies suspect.
Analysts say the previously announced projections of 4 percent economic growth and 4 percent inflation are unrealistic, and officials acknowledge that they have no idea what will happen next year.
Government pledges to freeze prices throughout the country, even as airlines and small stores are already raising them, are considered unenforceable. The Government says that wages will not be allowed to rise, but Mexican union leaders insist they will. The Government promises that personal savings, now almost nonexistent, will increase, but with some mortgage rates climbing as high as 50 percent, few analysts think that Mexicans will have much to save.
"The Government is totally missing the point," said Claudio Brocado, director of research at the New York City office of Valores Finamex, a Mexican brokerage firm. "The major wild card in what happens to Mexico next year is investor confidence, but I don't think they understand what they've done to lose that confidence. It's not going to be an easy thing to regain."
The core of the problem is not so much the decision to devalue the peso, but the tactics used.
A number of investors and economists have argued for some time that the peso had to be devalued, and many expect last week's moves to reduce Mexico's damaging trade imbalance substantially. But their confidence was shaken by the way the three-week-old Government of President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon handled the affair. And they worried about Mexico's resolve to stay the course that had lured so many foreigners to invest here.
After 1987, Mexico moved to quickly open its economy, and formerly restricted imports flooded in so fast that the nation built up a staggering imbalance of imports over exports. That trade deficit grew to $23 billion in 1993 from $4 billion in 1987 and was, before the devaluation, expected to total $30 billion next year.
With domestic savings very low, the only way to cover the imbalance was with foreign money, usually raised through the sale of treasury notes and bonds. To entice foreign investors, Mexico took the bold step of guaranteeing that its peso would not be abruptly devalued and backed it up with a rigidly controlled trading range. The Government pledged to buy pesos if the peso slipped outside that range.
The glue holding it all together was the confidence that the Government of President Carlos Salinas de Gortari had worked hard to build. His Finance Minister, Pedro Aspe, was a distinguished aristocrat who proved to be an effective salesman of Mexico's new maturity. Mr. Aspe developed a network of contacts with important New York investment bankers and money-fund managers, who were consulted before any significant shift in economic policy.
The peso's trading band worked well until this year, when investor confidence was sorely tested. Mexico experienced several political shocks, beginning with the uprising in Chiapas state that started on Jan. 1. Then came assassinations of two major political figures, high-visibility kidnappings and Machiavellian political intrigue leading up to the Aug. 21 presidential election.
Over the year, the stock exchange has lost 43 percent of its dollar value, Baring Securities calculated, and even before the "Christmas crisis," the peso had depreciated about 11 percent. Beyond all that, even more fundamental financial changes were taking place.
Starting in February with the first of several interest-rate increases by the Federal Reserve in the United States, investors began to switch money from Mexico and other emerging markets back into the United States. As Mexico's trade deficit ballooned, it became increasingly clear to some analysts -- and acknowledged by a few outspoken members of the Government -- that the deficit could not be financed and the peso's value could not be maintained.
By September, when President-elect Zedillo was assembling his Cabinet and preparing to approve the periodic renewal of a complex pact between Government, business and labor that sets the value of the peso, and has been the centerpiece of the inflation battle, high-ranking economists in the administration were pushing for a controlled devaluation.
An official who was involved said the Government considered temporarily widening the trading band, which would have allowed the peso to settle at a more natural level. After a few weeks or months, they intended to reimpose the previous restrictions.
But President Salinas felt that to have done so would have sent a signal of no confidence in Mr. Aspe, who had consistently denied the need for any devaluation. So the Government-business-labor pact was signed on Sept. 24 with the exchange-rate policy unchanged.
Then, on Dec. 19, came news of a new rebel offensive in Chiapas, which led to a run on the peso and backed the Government into a corner. Even though the reports of violence were exaggerated, the central bank was forced to intervene to protect the peso's value against speculation, lowering its reserves to a dangerous level. When the bank warned that it could no longer defend the peso, the Government was forced to let it float freely against the dollar for the first time since 1987.
In New York, bankers who were used to getting through directly to Mr. Aspe found that the new Finance Minister, Jaime Serra Puche, who had announced the devaluation on television, would not return their calls.
In the last week, Mr. Zedillo's administration appears to have been chastened. Mr. Serra acknowledged that he handled the situation badly and flew to New York on Thursday, for what turned out to be a hostile meeting with investment bankers. Mr. Zedillo said Friday that Mexico was living through a crisis that would test his abilities.
"In the end, you've got to ask whether this is a matter of the credibility of the country or the credibility of the currency," said Eduardo Cepeda, managing director of J. P. Morgan here. "Frankly, I don't think these guys deserve to lose all the credibility they've built up over the years. What happened is the end of parity, not the end of Mexico."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Regaining investor trust is central to Mexico's efforts to rebuild its damaged financial system. Money exchange is illegal any place but in exchange facilities. (Associated Press for The New York Times) (pg. D5)

HEADLINE: OUR TOWNS;
At Year's End, Revisiting Five Towns Left in Limbo

BYLINE: By Evelyn Nieves

 BODY:
AT year's end, time to update some 1994 Our Towns columns:
In March, Our Towns chronicled the story of Mariko Westbrook of Newark, who boarded a bus after a post-Christmas downtown shopping trip, but never made it home. She had suggested to a woman with a little girl seated near her that the child looked uncomfortably bundled. The woman beat Mrs. Westbrook about the face and kicked her off the bus with such force that she landed on her head, lapsed into a coma and died four days later. 
 Almost a year after her death, no arrests have been made. The description of Mrs. Westbrook's killer -- a tall, 200-pound young black woman -- has yielded some tips, all dead ends. Mrs. Westbrook's son, Peter, said he and his sister are in constant contact with Newark detectives. But the lead detective on the case has retired. Calls always bring the same reply: no break yet. "It's like it's a dead issue," Mr. Westbrook said. He plans to meet this week with a citizens' anti-violence group that offered to help with the case.
The Avon, Conn., police department made the news last spring, when its first and only black officer, Alvin Schwapp, sued both the department and the town for racial discrimination. Now, more trouble.
Among the claims in Mr. Schwapp's $15 million lawsuit is that Avon officers were directed to stop black and Hispanic motorists passing through from Hartford on the way to a park and reservoir in Barkhamsted. While the N.A.A.C.P., the State Commission on Human Rights and Opportunities and the F.B.I. started their own investigations, Avon's police chief appointed three veteran sergeants to conduct an internal probe. In September, the sergeants finished a report that concluded there is indeed racial bias in the Avon Police Department.
Officer Schwapp had a basis for his complaints, the report said. The Barkhamsted Express, the practice of stopping black and Hispanic motorists passing through town on Route 44, exists.
The sergeants' findings weren't revealed until last month, when they filed a $1 million lawsuit of their own against the town and the department. Their suit claims the police chief, James A. Martino Jr., refused to accept their findings and threatened to fire them if they did not alter the report to his ends. Town officials say Chief Martino has turned the matter over to the state's attorney for an independent review.
REMEMBER the poor, beleaguered Elmsford Animal Shelter? One of the rare animal shelters that refuses to kill dogs or cats or turn them away, the Elmsford Animal Shelter has crammed up to 450 animals on a third of an acre in Westchester County. As it waited and waited for funds for a bigger, better space, it has faced run-ins with local health officials and complaints from neighbors about noise and odors.
Now the good news: the shelter is moving. Its new digs, a 25,000-square-foot building on almost five acres, will be ready for animals in about two weeks, said Mimi Stone, the director. By March, the shelter, which will comfortably house 1,000 animals, should be fully operational and ready for animal-loving guests.
Sabatini's Restaurant, on a prime corner of Pacific Avenue in Atlantic City, is staying put, for now. That's the 30-year-old family restaurant that the New Jersey Casino Reinvestment and Economic Development Commission is planning to claim under eminent domain so that Trump Plaza can expand. The Sabatinis have been fighting to keep their restaurant for more than a year. Since the fall, they've had to break in a new lawyer. Their first lawyer happened to have worked for Trump, and Trump's lawyers successfully challenged his representation as a conflict of interest.
The family also recently heard that Donald Trump claimed to have made them a buyout offer of $2.5 million. They say they were never approached. They go to court in January, when a judge will rule whether the casino commission is entitled to seize the property. Both sides plan to appeal if the decision doesn't go the way they want.
THE first "Our Towns" of the year visited Bill and Jane Schneider, sole residents of the Sullivan County, N.Y., hamlet of Mongaup. The Schneiders had refused to move when the rest of their neighbors agreed to a buyout from Orange and Rockland Utilities, which operates the Rio Dam hydroelectric plant above their house.
Officials from the utility had told residents of Mongaup that because of a change in Federal regulations and the possibility of a flood, they would do well to leave. The Schneiders stayed when they found out that there was no change in Federal regulations. Besides, there had never been a flood in the 30 years they lived there.
The State Attorney General's office, which has been investigating Orange and Rockland officials, is also looking into the utility's reasons for buying out Mongaup. Only Mrs. Schneider and a son lives there now. In August, during his morning bicycle ride along the winding paths near home, Bill Schneider had a heart attack and died. Mrs. Schneider said he had a lovely funeral. Just about everyone in the town of Lumberland showed up, including all their old neighbors in what was once the hamlet of Mongaup.

HEADLINE: U.S. Official Heads for North Korea to Seek Release of Copter Pilot

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Tuesday, Dec. 27

 BODY:
The United States has sent a senior diplomat to North Korea in hopes of speeding the release of a captured American helicopter pilot, the State Department said Monday in Washington.
The trip, being arranged in response to an request received in Washington early Monday from North Korea, raises the prospects that Chief Warrant Officer Bobby Hall, whose helicopter crossed into North Korean territory on Dec. 17, would soon be freed. 
 But in a seemingly contradictory move, North Korea also issued a blistering attack on the United States about the same time, accusing Mr. Hall of being on an espionage mission and suggesting that North Korea was not yet ready to free the pilot.
"In view of the seriousness of the incident, we cannot but investigate the truth of the incident more deeply" the Government-controlled Korean Central News Agency said in a statement issued Monday, Washington time. It said the North Korean armed forces "will never pardon anyone who intrudes into their land, sea and sky even 0.001 millimeter."
The two events, coming after days in which there was little movement on the issue, left Washington "hopeful" but cautious, according to one United States official. He said that Deputy Assistant Secretary of State Thomas Hubbard was being sent to Pyongyang, the North Korean capital, because the North Korean mission to the United Nations had suggested this would "facilitate the resolution" of the situation.
"The wisest thing to do is very carefully and deliberately pursue the diplomacy that was suggested by North Korea," another senior State Department official said. He said the broadside issued by North Korea "does not change the Hubbard mission."
Mr. Hall, who was based in South Korea, has been held by North Korea since his two-person Army helicopter crossed over the North-South border on Dec. 17 and was either shot down or crashed. His co-pilot, Chief Warrant Officer David Hilemon, was killed in the incident and his body was returned to the United States last week.
United States officials, who had been hoping Mr. Hall would be freed by Christmas, have repeatedly warned that failure to free the pilot would endanger the progress that has been made in improving relations between North Korea and the United States since the two nations signed an agreement in October that aims to curb North Korea's suspected nuclear weapons development program.
Mr. Hubbard left by military plane Monday afternoon in Washington and is expected to arrive in Seoul, South Korea, this evening. He is expected to cross the demilitarized zone into North Korea on Wednesday, the State Department said in a statement.
Mr. Hubbard was chosen because he is an expert on East Asia and is well-known to Pyongyang, one United States official said. Mr. Hubbard was the deputy to Robert L. Gallucci on the team that negotiated the nuclear agreement with North Korea.
On Monday, the United States and North Korea held a high-level military meeting on the border between South and North Korea. The American military based in Seoul said that "no substantive progress" toward freeing Mr. Hall was made in that meeting, but some officials said it was encouraging nonetheless that generals from both sides were engaged in dialogue.
The United States has maintained that the pilots were on a routine training and orientation flight and that they strayed across the border by accident because a navigational error.
In Washington Monday, a State Department spokeswoman said the United States "categorically rejects the unfounded accusation" that the helicopter was on an espionage mission.
"At this point, it is fundamentally important that the D.P.R.K. promptly release Chief Warrant Officer Hall, who has been held for nine days now and who is responsible for nothing more than an inadvertent error," she said, referring to North Korea by the initials of its formal name, the Democratic People's Republic of Korea.
It is unclear what North Korea was trying to accomplish by the seemingly contradictory moves today. One theory that has been advanced recently is that there is a split between the Foreign Ministry, which is more interested in improving relations with the United States, and the military, which takes a harder line. It could also be that North Korea is trying show that it is not giving in to Washington too easily and is defending its sovereignty.

HEADLINE: Music Returns to Haiti With the Spirit of Hope

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Dec. 23

 BODY:
For two months now, ever since President Jean-Bertrand Aristide returned from exile, one particular song has been playing everywhere in Haiti: on the radio, in the dance clubs, on the streets, in the fields. Called "Reconciliation," it has become a symbol of the mood of hopefulness that now prevails here.
But until this week, the Phantoms, the group that wrote and sang this infectious anthem of the new, democratic Haiti, could not themselves be found anywhere in their homeland. Like the president they admire, they had been forced into exile in the United States, and recorded "Reconciliation" there during the summer, hoping that their vision of peace would actually come to pass. 
 To Haitians, therefore, the return of the Phantoms and other performers who had had to leave the country, including Boukman Eksperyans and Manno Charlemagne, is also a sign that the worst is finally over. After three years of a brutal military dictatorship that routinely used censorship and intimidation as weapons to control artistic expression, Haitian music is flowering again.
Songs and videos that were banned because of the coded political messages they contained are once again being broadcast on radio and television. Bands that could not perform in public because of assassination threats against them have resumed playing to enthusiastic audiences. Record companies that had to shut down because of an economic embargo, which meant they could no longer import tape or press compact disks, are back in business.
"The police aren't dragging people out of our shows at gunpoint any more, it's safe for us to rehearse after dark, and I've even gone two or three weeks without scanning the crowd for weapons," said Richard Morse, lead singer of the voodoo rock group Ram, which had several hit songs banned from the airwaves by the military but chose to remain in Haiti. "It's a wonderful feeling, but I almost don't believe it after the trauma we've been through."
The creative resurgence here comes at a time of heightened interest abroad in Haitian music. Several compilations of Haitian music have been released by labels in the United States that specialize in the "world beat" sound, a Ram song, "Ibo Lele," was included on the soundtrack of the movie "Philadelphia," and a Haitian band opened for the Rolling Stones at several stops on their recent "Voodoo Lounge" tour.
But for Haiti's seven million people, the restoration of free expression through music is seen as an essential part of the democracy they hope to build. In a country where three out of four people cannot read, songs are more than a form of entertainment: they are one of the principal means by which ideas and opinions about politics, society and religion are disseminated and debated.
"As a top Haitian band, we're in a position to teach Haitians what democracy is all about, what human rights and education are worth," said King Kino, lead singer of the Phantoms. "The problem in this country is one of information and how to get it to the people, and that is something we have a duty to do."
Many Haitian bands have routinely sought to play that role, and it was that habit that got them into trouble with the military authorities in the first place. For Boukman Eksperyans, things got particularly difficult after they wrote a song called "Dangerous Crossroads," which includes such lyrics as "if you're an assassin, get out of here, if you're a thief, get out of here," to protest the September 1991 overthrow of Father Aristide.
"The military never attacked us directly, but a colonel told me never to go out at night," said Theodore (Lolo) Beaubrun, founder of the group, which is named for the leader of an 18th-century rebellion against French colonialism. "There were shows we did where we felt we were near death, but nobody in Boukman was happy when we left Haiti," he said.
As a Haitian band well known abroad, Boukman Eksperyans was able to leave the country to perform pretty much at will. This year, the band had just finished a European tour when the economic embargo against the military regime was broadened to include travel restrictions on Haitians, and the group found themselves unable to enter the United States, where they were scheduled to play about 20 concerts, or to return to Haiti. On May 21, Michel Olicha Lynch, a percussionist and bass player in the group, died of meningitis in Haiti, in part, Mr. Beaubrun said, because the embargo made it difficult for him to obtain medicine he needed.
"We are not angry at anything that has happened to us, because we are people of the spirit, and we think of all of this as just another experience," Mr. Beaubrun said. "Other people suffered more, and that gives us courage to sing to our people."
Just about the only Haitian musician of note who still has not been heard from is Manno Charlemagne, a singer and composer whose fiery songs of social protest have led him to be compared with the early Bob Dylan. Mr. Charlemagne, who did not respond to requests for an interview, has visited Haiti since the dictatorship fell, but has not performed in public here, limiting himself to occasional shows in Miami, where he has been living in exile.
"Manno didn't like the American invasion," said a friend of the singer, referring to the landing of 20,000 United States troops here three months ago, which paved the way for the restoration of Father Aristide. "He does not want to have to criticize Aristide, so he is not saying anything at all."
The Phantoms, however, are eager to return to the fray and make up for lost time. Interviewed recently in Miami, where the group has a large following among Haitian exiles and Haitian-Americans, King Kino said that just the thought of returning home had inspired a new burst of creativity and enthusiasm among the band members.
"No one should have to be in exile," he said. "It's very frustrating to have a country and know you can't go there. Everyone belongs in their own country, because in a foreign country you feel like an alien from space. Everything is new and strange, and you never really get used to it."
The only positive aspect of exile, the musicians agreed, was the opportunity to expand artistically. Boukman Eksperyans spent the last few months before the fall of the military in neighboring Jamaica, recording a new album that is to be released in the United States early in 1995 and forming friendships with the reggae performers Jimmy Cliff, Ziggy Marley and Third World.
"We met a lot of great people and learned a lot about how to make records while we were there," Mr. Beaubrun said. "Those Jamaican engineers are great. We loved the sound they get for the bass and drums, and that has changed the way our music sounds."
Haitian music has always been open to foreign influences, but new records being released here reflect the increased exposure to the outside world that has taken place over the last three years. Splashes of reggae, rock and rap are now commonplace, merging with the traditional lilting beat of Haitian compas music and the heavier rhythms of voodoo in fascinating and unexpected combinations.
Many of the new records also have a strong political content, expressed through parables and allegories. The military dictatorship may be gone, and it may no longer be quite so dangerous to speak out, but musicians say they want to make sure that their audience does not become complacent.
"People may want to think everything is all right now that Aristide has returned," Mr. Beaubrun said. "But we have to go further than that, and not let the politicians divide the people again. It is really important for us to be here and feel what's going on and talk about what we feel, to awaken the people and change the system."

GRAPHIC: Photo: King Kino, lead singer of the Haitian group the Phantoms, which recorded a song that has become an informal anthem for their country. (Patrick Douge for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Astronomers Debate Conflicting Answers for the Age of the Universe

BYLINE: By JOHN NOBLE WILFORD 

DATELINE: PASADENA, Calif.

 BODY:
THE axis between polar extremes in one of the most critical and fervently contested battles in science today could be said to run along the second-floor corridor of the Carnegie Observatories building here on Santa Barbara Street, one of astronomy's intellectual addresses.
The dispute is over a seemingly simple number, the long-sought value for the Hubble constant, that would establish the expansion rate of the universe and enable cosmologists to calculate the billions of years that have elapsed since the beginning of space and time. Depending on the outcome, important theories may totter and require revision. But when reputable scientists doing excellent research find themselves getting dramatically different but defensible answers, no one can be sure who is right. 
 At one end of the Carnegie corridor is the office of Dr. Wendy L. Freedman, leader of an international team of astronomers that used the Hubble Space Telescope to get the most precise measurement yet of the distance between Earth and a far-off galaxy. From this they derived a high value for the Hubble constant and thus a relatively young age for the universe of between 8 billion and 12 billion years.
When the results were announced in October, Dr. Freedman and her team cautioned that it was premature to believe that the problem of determining the Hubble constant had been solved. "It has not," they asserted, noting that reliable distances to many more galaxies would be needed to confirm the high value. Still, with other scientists using different techniques and arriving at similar results, there is a growing impression that the battle may soon be won by the young-universe forces.
Down the corridor is the foremost defender of a low Hubble constant and so of a much more slowly expanding and older universe of perhaps 15 billion to 20 billion years. He is Dr. Allan R. Sandage, who learned his astronomy under the great Edwin P. Hubble, the orbiting telescope's namesake. Hubble was the astronomer who in 1929 discovered that the universe is expanding uniformly in all directions, thereby kicking off the search for the elusive number for that expansion rate.
Dr. Sandage has spent his career trying to clock the expansion, regularly getting a low number, and is by no means ready to concede defeat. Complaining of a kind of bandwagon effect favoring his opposition, he recently offered other astronomers some wry advice he had read in Dostoyevsky's "The Idiot," which was, he said, "Don't cross the river until you find the bridge."
Dr. Sandage pointed to what he said were subtle biases in the selection of targets for the recent distance measurements that could have tilted the results toward the higher values being reported for the Hubble constant. Early next year, he and Dr. David Branch, an astronomer at the University of Oklahoma, plan to report Hubble telescope observations of two exploding stars, or supernovas, of the type Dr. Sandage has relied on for his measurements giving the low values.
"We've seen nothing in these experiments that change our early results or our position," he said.
The spirited conflict over the age of the universe is only part of the ferment throughout astronomy, the welcomed consequence of major advances in ways of investigating the depths of the universe.
"We are in an extraordinarily exciting period for astronomy," said Dr. Vera C. Rubin, an astronomer at the Carnegie Institution in Washington. "Any confusion we are experiencing is just because so much is being learned so fast."
New instruments in astronomy are usually designed to create new possibilities for discovery, whether because they see farther or exploit different regions of the electromagnetic spectrum. The Hubble telescope, perched above the cloying atmosphere that for centuries has clouded humankind's view of the heavens, has made so many new discoveries in its first year of full operation that it is in danger of creating a revolution.
Since astronauts corrected its flawed optics a year ago, findings by the Hubble telescope in Earth's orbit have charged astronomy's atmosphere with surging excitement. Expectations among astronomers were never higher that they may at last be close to answering some truly cosmic questions about the origin, evolution and fate of the universe. A rush of spectacular pictures has both confirmed and confounded scientific thinking.
The telescope has provided the most definitive evidence yet for the existence at the core of a galaxy of a dense gravitational mass called a black hole. It photographed a dust cloud that could be giving birth to planets. It also took pictures of surprisingly mature galaxies from the time when the universe was only one-tenth its present age and discovered that there are apparently too few dim stars to account for much of the "missing mass" of the universe.
But one of the telescope's primary objectives was to find the value of the Hubble constant, one of the essential numbers required to explain so much about the universe -- its age, expansion rate and destiny, whether it will expand forever, reach some equilibrium or eventually collapse in what is called the Big Crunch. Progress is stalled for the moment, however, because of the conflicting distance measurements being produced by the Hubble telescope and other instruments.
As much as they want the matter resolved, scientists accept the disagreement as another example of science at work and count on a resolution in a few years, if not sooner.
"It's a common occurrence in a forefront area of science, where the questions are tough and the measurements extremely difficult," said Dr. David N. Schramm, an astrophysicist at the University of Chicago. "You have different groups using different methods and they get different answers. You see it all the time, and the public rarely notices. But when it happens to be in cosmology, it makes headlines."
The persistence of the disagreement does worry scientists that somehow they are overlooking some subtle mistakes in their observations or calculations, or else that their understanding of the cosmos is limited in some unrecognized but fundamental respect.
Theorists, in particular, are concerned and busily trying to reshape their ideas to conform to either a young or old universe. It would make their lives easier if Dr. Sandage proved to be correct, for there should then be ample time for cosmic evolution under the simplest Big Bang models. But if Dr. Freedman's group should prevail, the standard theory would have to be modified, and theorists are already pondering such fixes as an idea postulated by Einstein but later discarded, which is that a repulsive force known as the cosmological constant is accelerating cosmic expansion.
Although arguments over high and low Hubble constants and young and old universes have been raging for two decades, they came into sharper focus with the observations by Dr. Freedman's group. The Hubble telescope was able to detect Cepheid stars in the M100 spiral galaxy out in the distant Virgo Cluster. Astronomers have long established a correlation between the pulsations of these stars and their absolute brightness. This makes them excellent "standard candles," objects of known intrinsic brightness so that their distances can be estimated by comparing their observed brightness with the known intrinsic brightness. The more distant a galaxy is from gravitational perturbations of the Milky Way, scientists think, the more reliable it should be as an indicator of the expansion rate.
In the first definitive observations of Cepheids that far away, the Hubble data indicated that M100 galaxy is about 56 million light-years from Earth. By combining this number with the galaxy's recession velocity, a much easier number to obtain, Dr. Freedman and her colleagues inferred a value for the Hubble constant of 80 plus or minus 11 miles per second per megaparsec (a megaparsec is 3.26 million light-years). Depending on the density of matter in the universe, this puts the date of the Big Bang between 8 billion and 12 billion years ago.
Other astronomers praised the complex analysis and could find no obvious errors. They noted that the exact position of the galaxy in the Virgo Cluster was not determined, which could affect the results, but Dr. Freedman's team said it had taken that into account in assigning a wide margin of error to the estimate. But one aspect gave everyone pause. The new findings made it appear that the universe was younger than the oldest stars, which have been estimated at 14 billion to 16 billion years old.
Perhaps these stellar ages are wrong. In recent weeks, scientists have busily checked their figures and found, Dr. Schramm said, the age for the oldest stars of 14 billion, plus or minus 2 billion, is a "pretty robust number." The estimates are based on an understanding of nuclear physics and the rate at which hydrogen converts to helium in stars. Even the 12 billion age, Dr. Schramm cautioned, involved "bending over backwards with assumptions."
By resorting to versions of Einstein's old cosmological constant, theorists were testing ways of explaining how the cosmic expansion might have been slower in an earlier epoch, allowing more time for the evolution of stars and galaxies. The influence of an energy force of unknown nature could have speeded up cosmic expansion by now. This means that the true age of the universe might be greater than indicated by the present expansion rate.
Such theoretical contortions may not be necessary, if Dr. Sandage is right. He has raised at least two procedural objections to the high Hubble Constant values.
The high numbers, he contended, are derived by astronomers who use a higher estimate for the velocity of the Virgo Cluster's core, independent of local motions. Only a small reduction in that estimate, which he said was justifiable, "removes this one source of the difference between our results and others," he said.
Dr. Sandage has also argued that in many recent experiments using Cepheids and other targets, the selection of only the brightest known objects in the class being examined introduced a bias that had a "pernicious effect" on the results.
But one of Dr. Sandage's own experimental assumptions is also being challenged.
In recent years, Dr. Sandage's targets of choice for distance measurements have been type Ia supernovas, exploding stars whose light is assumed to reach the same maximum brightness. Used as a standard candle, these objects have consistently given Dr. Sandage and his collaborators Hubble Constants of about 50.
But Dr. Mark M. Phillips, an astronomer at the Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory in Chile, recently found that not all Type Ia supernovas have the same brightness characteristics. Astronomers complain that Dr. Sandage has yet to correct his data to reflect this particular behavior.
In a study to be published next week in Astrophysical Journal Letters, Dr. Robert P. Kirshner and his colleagues at the Harvard-Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics in Cambridge, Mass., report that they got a Hubble Constant of 55 when they used the same supernova method as Dr. Sandage's. But when they corrected the data for the brightness variation, the constant rose to 67, give or take 7.
Nearly all astronomers agree that no definitive answer has yet been reached.
Dr. Peter L. Galison, a historian of science at Harvard and author of "How Experiments End" (University of Chicago Press, 1987), said such disputes in science generally are resolved sooner or later. New tests or a whole new generation of experiments could prove decisive. Another possible outcome could be that the immediate partisans in the battle may never agree, but the weight of evidence so favors one side that the rest of science eventually accepts it.
In some of the classic conflicts of the past, the historian said, disagreement persisted because scientists failed to recognize statistical errors or background effects distorting the data. "In cases of enormously delicate measurements involving strong theoretical expectations," he said, "often scientists may stop looking for background effects when they get the result they are expecting."
Dr. Michael Pierce, an astronomer at Indiana University in Bloomington, thinks the issue would have been settled by now in favor of the high number if the theoretical implications were not so troubling.
Dr. Schramm of the University of Chicago said a resolution might come in the next 5 to 10 years, "without having to wait for the principals to die." His remark recalled an observation by Max Planck, the originator of quantum theory, who proclaimed that old ideas in science die only with those who hold them.
Dr. Kirshner said, "I don't think it's going to be resolved in a sudden stroke." But he believes agreement could come in two to three years.
Several new tests are being tried, and if their findings begin to converge on a particular number, high or low, then astronomers may be satisfied that they have the answer. If the numbers still diverge widely and persistently, scientists may have to concede that even in this time of breathtaking astronomical discoveries, some things like the age of the universe remain for now beyond human understanding.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Dr. Allan R. Sandage sees a low value for the Hubble Constant and thus an older universe. (Bart Bartholomew for the New York Times); Dr. Wendy L. Freedman, analyzing Hubble Space Telescope data, found a higher value. (Observatories of the Carnegie Institution) (pg. C9)

HEADLINE: Italian Coalition Didn't Fight Corruption

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Your Dec. 22 front-page article on the resignation of Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi of Italy had a misleading subheading: "Fall of a Coalition Formed to Fight Corruption." 
 For whatever reason the coalition was formed, it never fought corruption. It only fought the magistrates who were fighting corruption, the so-called Mani Pulite (clean hands) pool, until it succeeded in forcing the resignation of their leader, Antonio DiPietro, who was investigating corruption in Mr. Berlusconi's own economic empire.
The Government also did very little to deliver on its other electoral promises, which would have been very hard, as they were mostly contradictory promises -- reducing taxes and cutting the deficits, or pie in the sky, increasing employment by a million new jobs.
Instead, the Government spent most of its time advancing its own interests, such as fighting attempts to resolve the conflict of interests arising from Mr. Berlusconi's business empire, which represents a substantial fraction of Italian private wealth. It includes a private television monopoly, which he reinforced through control of public television.
Meanwhile, the Italian economy, which is my main professional concern, has been deteriorating, at least compared with other European countries, as the world financial community lost confidence in the Berlusconi Government.
The clearest evidence is the rise in the premiums investors require to risk investing in Italy, rather than in a safe country like Germany. That premium has increased from 2.5 percent at the end of the previous Ciampi Government to 5 percent on one-year maturities, more for the longer term. The resulting high interest rates are strangling the Italian economy. These are the real reasons for the Berlusconi Government's fall.
 
FRANCO MODIGLIANI
Cambridge, Mass., Dec. 23, 1994
The writer is a professor emeritus, Sloan School of Management, Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Battered Best Buy investors may need to brace for more bad news.

BYLINE: By Andrea Adelson 

 BODY:
THE stock of the Best Buy Company, a fast-growing consumer electronics retailer, has already fallen more than 25 percent this month after the company disclosed earnings and sales would be less than Wall Street expectations. And there's a broad view that more bad news is on the way for the company's investors.
Even at its current $29.875 a share, investors are paying 20 times estimated fiscal 1995 earnings for Best Buy. The price-earnings ratio is 12.9 at Circuit City Stores Inc.; 12.5 at the Tandy Corporation, which owns Radio Shack and Incredible Universe stores, and 11 at the Good Guys Inc. 
 And analysts suggest Best Buy could have more disappointing news in its fourth quarter, which ends Feb. 25, even though holiday shoppers are opening their wallets for consumer electronics. The problem is that Best Buy has about $100 million more in inventory than expected, most of it in televisions, according to reports issued this month by David C. Childe, who follows the stock for Johnson Rice & Company of New Orleans.
The chain expects to work through the excess inventory without markdowns, said Allen U. Lenzmeier, Best Buy's chief financial officer.
The 204-store chain, based in Eden Prairie, Minn., earned $29.5 million on $3.1 billion in sales in the first nine months of this year. Profits rose by 46 percent.
Best Buy also will have $4 million, higher than anticipated, of costs in the fourth quarter as it opens a number of new stores, Mr. Childe predicted. Best Buy has added more than 50 stores since last November.
Higher-than-budgeted opening costs in the third quarter contributed to the collapse in Best Buy's shares on Dec. 1.
Mr. Lenzmeier said the fourth-quarter costs were closer to $3 million.
"Management has remained quite guarded about providing significant additional Q4 guidance," Mr. Childe's report said. Telephone calls to him were not returned.
Edwin L. Underwood, vice president of the brokerage firm Scott & Stringfellow, is not recommending Best Buy. He predicted that rising interest rates would slow consumer spending on high-ticket items, cutting into Best Buy's sales growth: "I think it will be tougher going forward."
Terrence McEvoy, who follows Best Buy for Janney Montgomery Scott, concurred. "This is a risky investment," he said. While the company's overall business has been strong, he said, sales have shifted to lower-profit home-office equipment, which could squeeze margins.
So far, Best Buy's "growth rate has deserved a lofty multiple," he said, adding, "Whether they will generate earnings to justify that, I don't know. Make a bet on Santa Claus."
Ned Sherwood, president of ZS Fund L.P., which owns about 20 percent of Sun Television and Appliances, a 40-store chain based in Columbus, Ohio, does not want to bet. He contends Wall Street analysts focus too narrowly on same-store sales gains in evaluating consumer electronics retailers and should look at return on capital, which is dependent on profit margins.
By his analysis, which uses a return-on-capital ratio based on fiscal 1994 figures, Best Buy is the worst buy of the lot with a return of 4.5 percent. This analysis puts Sun on top, with 19 percent. Circuit City comes in at 11.5 percent and Good Guys at 5.4 percent.
"Sun, Good Guys and other regionals are getting punished because of fears over what the two major chains will do," Mr. Sherwood said. It is presumed that continued expansion by Best Buy and Circuit City would hurt growth elsewhere.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
 Mexico's economic crisis worsened as the peso plunged nearly 10 percent more against the dollar and stocks fell. Despite calls from business leaders and foreign investors, the Mexican Government gave no public indication of its economic strategy. [Page A1.]
A weaker peso means cheaper Mexican vacations for United States tourists. But that could hurt tourism in the Caribbean. [D1.]
Trade talks between the United States and Japan appeared to make some procedural progress toward resolving a deadlock over the sale of American cars and car parts in Japan. [D2.]
 
Companies
 ITT continues to sell off finance operations, announcing one deal and saying it was near another. The sales are aimed at raising cash for the company's expansion into entertainment and gambling. ITT agreed to sell Island Finance to Norwest Bank for $600 million. ITT also said it had received a bid of $1.8 billion from a buyer it did not identify for three financial services units. [D1.]

The Economy
 The holiday shopping season was less robust than what many retailers had anticipated. But the sluggish gains came amid increases in the use of credit cards, suggesting that higher debts may keep shoppers out of stores in the coming months. [D1.]
After the harvest comes the accounting. For many large-scale farmers, this was a good year, with record harvests in corn, soybeans, rice and cotton, as well as a large wheat crop. Now, farmers are negotiating with food processors, seed companies and grain merchants over what they will grow next year. [D1.]
The I.R.S. proposed charging taxpayers $43 for the privilege of paying in installments. Though the agency already has several user fees, the plan was criticized by former I.R.S. officials. [D1.]
Sales of previously owned homes fell in November, hurt by higher mortgage rates, a real estate trade group said. [D6.]
 
Business Technology
 Extracting oil from tar sands is economically feasible, thanks to technology like computerized dump trucks. Oil sands operations in Alberta has surged in the last 10 years, and now account for a quarter of Canada's total crude oil production. [D5.]

 Stocks advanced, though retailing issues sagged. The Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks rose 2.64 points. [D6.]
Treasury prices gained in thin trading. [D15.]
The dollar was little changed against leading currencies. [D16.]
Orange juice prices tumbled on signs of an abundant supply and favorable weather. Energy prices rose in a late rally. [D16.]

HEADLINE: With Each Fall in Peso, Tourism Bargains Rise

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
While the devaluation of the peso has caused an exodus of investment dollars from Mexico, travel agents say American tourists will be going in the opposite direction, heading to Mexico, where the dollar will now buy much more.
According to early estimates, that could make a big loser out of the Caribbean, which has been waging a vigorous advertising and marketing campaign to woo more American tourists. 
 "Mexico and the Caribbean both attract much the same clientele -- a lot of middle-class American tourists, including many repeat travelers and many very young ones," said Richard D'Ambrosio, a spokesman for Thomas Cook Travel/American Express. "They both tend to be price sensitive, so now when the dollar goes a lot further in Mexico, it's likely to siphon off a lot of business from the Caribbean."
Joann Mazzotta, executive vice president of Friendly Holidays in Lake Success, N.Y., concurs. "A lot of people figure, why not go where you get the best value for your money, and right now that's Mexico."
But for Mexicans planning vacations in the United States and elsewhere, the trips have just as suddenly become more pricey.
"We're already feeling the effects," an agent at Thomas Cook Travel in Guadalajara said yesterday. "International airline tickets are in dollars, so a $2,000 ticket that cost 7,000 pesos a few days ago now costs more than 9,000." As a result, the agent said, many clients have canceled reservations.
Price changes for travel from the United States on package tours are not expected immediately. The prices of such tours are generally set months in advance and are paid in dollars.
Before last week's devaluation, travel agencies were offering seven-day packages that ranged from a trip from New York to Cancun for $425, including hotel and round-trip air fare, to an eight-day stay in Acapulco for $479, including air fare. Caribbean prices began at $315 for three days in Jamaica to $959 for seven days in a resort that includes meals and recreation.
However, tour operators expect prices to come down in the spring if inflation in Mexico holds steady.
For the moment, lower prices are available for independent travelers making their own reservations at hotels in Mexico.
"What will be a bargain for all American tourists in Mexico," said Manny Corral, director of sales and marketing for the Ritz Carlton/Cancun, "are purchases of food and beverages, gifts, local travel and sightseeing."
Officials of the Caribbean Tourism Organization are confident they will not be hurt by the devaluation.
"We're well aware of it, but I don't see it as a big factor, at least for this winter," said Michael J. Youngman, director of marketing for the 31-member organization. "In fact, last year's cold winter got us a lot of advance bookings, and so did the Old Farmer's Almanac prediction of another cold winter."
Caribbean tourism this year is expected to rise 5 to 6 percent above last year's 13.3 million visitors, Mr. Youngman said. Fifty-two percent of the visitors are from the United States.
Three years ago, Mexican tourism officials set a goal of 10 million visitors by 1994, up from 6.4 million in 1990. But the total in 1993 rose to only 6.6 million people, who brought in more than $4 billion, according to the Ministry of Tourism. In the first six months of this year, 3.4 million visitors -- about 70 percent of them from the United States -- spent more than $2.15 billion in Mexico.
Nevertheless, even a tourism industry that hasn't lived up to expectations is important to Mexico: A recent report by Solomon Brothers noted that Mexican tourism revenues have more than doubled since 1986. It also said that tourism is the second-largest industry, after oil.
As part of its goal to increase tourism from the United States, Mexico made it easier for American tour buses to operate in Mexico.
In meetings with tour bus officials, Mexican authorities had pledged to improve the country's highways and cut its extensive red tape, including eliminating a requirement that foreigners switch to Mexican buses and use a Mexican tour operator after crossing the border.
"They've done everything they promised they would," said Jean Anderson of Anderson Travel Tours of Houston, which takes about 100 bus groups to Mexico a year. As a result, American companies bringing group tours to Mexico have increased to an estimated 150 in 1994 from 14 in 1991, according to the Mexican Tourism Office.
The issue of how much American cities will suffer from the peso's devaluation is still not certain, "The biggest tourism from Mexico is day visitors from Tijuana, most of whom come over to shop," said Dr. Charles E. Nathanson, executive director of San Diego Dialogue, a research organization of the University of California at San Diego. "But they're not coming just for price, they also shop for quality and the availability of products."
Furthermore, 40,000 Mexicans cross the border each day to work legally in Southern California, earning up to $700 million a year, about one-tenth the gross regional product of Tijuana. And more than 200,000 visits a month to San Diego from the Tijuana area are for entertainment and dinner.
"So adding up the effects of a strengthened dollar and all the purchases of Americans who shop in Tijuana," Dr. Nathanson said, "the devaluation is probably pretty good news for Tijuana."

GRAPHIC: Photo: A tourist boom is expected from the Mexican peso's steep drop in value against the dollar. Yesterday, several American tourists checked out the hats at Centro Artesanal Buenavista, the big craft market in Mexico City. (Associated Press for The New York Times)
 
Graph: "Falling Further" shows Mexican pesos to the dollar from 12/5/94 to 12/26/94 (Source: Bloomberg Financial Markets)
