HEADLINE: BUSINESS TECHNOLOGY;
Unlocking Oil in Canada's Tar Sands

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: FORT McMURRAY, Alberta

 BODY:
For millions of years, crude oil has been locked within the tar sands that abound along the Athabasca River, 300 miles north of Edmonton, but until recently it looked doubtful that the estimated 300 billion barrels could be recovered economically.
Now a couple of companies representing a broad array of Canadian, American and Japanese interests have shown that such unconventional fuels can not only make money but also augment Canada's output of domestic conventional crude, which has declined over the last 20 years by 500,000 barrels a day. 
 From nothing a little longer than two decades ago, oil sands output has jumped to 390,000 barrels a day, doubling over the last 10 years alone. It now represents one-quarter of Canada's total crude oil production.
The two oil extraction operations here at Fort McMurray are run by Syncrude Canada Ltd., a consortium led by Imperial Oil Ltd. and 69 percent owned by Exxon, and Suncor Inc., which is 55 percent owned by the Sun Company of Philadelphia.
Once the operations were considered white elephants. But the costs of recovering the oil have fallen below $12 a barrel, thanks to such modern technology as computerized 240-ton dump trucks riding on rubber tires twice the height of their drivers. And both companies see their costs dropping below $10 per barrel within three to four years.
The oil, from which most of the polluting sulfur is removed, contains bitumen. This tarry substance is treated to produce a "sweet" crude that is particularly attractive for refineries that have to meet increasingly strict environmental standards. It now sells for at least $6 a barrel more than it costs to produce. The benchmark West Texas intermediate crude has been pushing up from about $18 a barrel.
"So you can see we've developed quite a margin there," says Eric P. Newell, the former Imperial refinery manager who has run the Syncrude operation since 1989.
"We have a very high volume, very long life and very low technical risk," added Mr. Newell, a 50-year-old chemical engineer. "In a low-price environment, we still make money. It's a steady, lower rate of return, like an annuity. But if the price goes up," he noted, cracking a smile, "we just print money."
Two other tar sands operations in Alberta, one run by Shell at Peace River to the west of here, and the other managed by Imperial at Cold Lake, which is southeast of here, have reported similar success.
All this oil once lay below the bed of an ancient sea. As waters dispersed, the oil rose into the sandy areas of the former sea floor, lighter hydrocarbons evaporated, leaving the heavier hydrocarbons like bitumen.
Over millions of years, nature covered the bitumen-impregnated sands with boulders, clay, gravel, and muskeg.
In the summer, the tar bubbles up. Indians used the bitumen to caulk canoes, patch teepees, and burn smudge fires to repel black flies and mosquitoes.
"During my childhood," said Katharine Govier, an Edmonton-born novelist, "there were periodic flutters of excitement in the public consciousness about tapping these sands, but no one knew exactly what that entailed, and anyway something always happened to prevent it."
Nothing prevents it now. The so-called overburden of rock and gravel is peeled off year-round in huge open-pit mining operations. The sands, frozen steel-hard at this season but gooey in summer, are dumped onto conveyer belts leading to plants where the bitumen is separated and refined. The specialists here say the extraction involves no new high-tech breakthrough that might be transferable to other geographical conditions.
"The challenge here is removing a lot of dirt," said Edythe A. Parkinson, the 46-year-old executive vice president of Suncor's Oil Sands Group. "But miners know how to do that. All you have to do is become really good at mining."
The mining operations continue 24 hours a day through summers that are short, hot and buggy and winters when temperatures can plunge to 40 below. The mining and refining operations, begun here in the late 1960's, have transformed Fort McMurray from a sleepy river town into one of the most populous communities in northern Canada.
The 40,000 inhabitants, 8,000 of whom work directly in the oil extraction operations, live in a modern community with shopping centers and a golf course. The paved road from Edmonton becomes a dirt track just beyond the Syncrude plant 25 miles north of town.
Fort McMurray benefited from the sharp rise in oil prices in the 1970's. After oil prices fell sharply in 1985, the town faced grave questions about its commercial viability.
But Suncor and Syncrude cut costs and raised productivity with some of the most heavily computerized manufacturing operations in North America.
"We've come of age," said Ms. Parkinson, who has headed Suncor's operation at Fort McMurray for three years. "We've proven to people that we can sustain and maintain a competitive source of crude oil. This is an operation that makes both sense and money,"
Suncor and Syncrude, which share some information and work together on research projects, are gearing up for expansions that will increase their production by about 20 percent over the next three to four years.
Their combined output hit a billion barrels in 1992, making the oil sands Canada's second-largest cumulative oil producer, outranked only by Alberta's Pembina field with more than 1.2 billion barrels. By 1998 the combined production at Fort McMurray is expected to exceed Pembina's.
After a large loss in 1992, Suncor, which has conventional oil and gas assets elsewhere, reported earnings for the first nine months of 1994 that jumped 70 percent over those of the corresponding period in 1993. Its shares, which are traded on the Toronto and American Stock Exchanges, have nearly doubled since they were first listed two years ago.
Syncrude, which has made money for the last 16 years, expects another good year in 1994 with earnings and cash flow within range of last year's $73 million and $255 million, respectively.
Despite Syncrude's profitability, some of its partners have been cutting back for their own reasons. Petro-Canada reduced its 17 percent holding to 12 percent in 1991 to free up cash for other parts of its business, including the $7 billion Hibernia oil rig off Newfoundland. Mocal Energy Ltd., a subsidiary of the Mitsubishi Oil Company of Japan, bought the 5 percent from Petro-Canada for $132 million.
The province of Alberta, which owned a 16.74 percent stake, cut back last year by selling 5 percent to the Murphy Oil Corporation of Arkansas for $150 million. Alberta's cash-short provincial government wants to sell more and has been talking to the Chinese Petroleum Corporation of Taiwan, among other enterprises.
The 300 billion barrels of potentially recoverable oil that geologists believe is hidden in the Alberta tar sands exceed the 265 billion barrels estimated in Saudi Arabia. But extracting the oil in Saudi Arabia is easier and costs far less -- well below $1 a barrel.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Oil squeezed from the sands of northern Alberta now accounts for one-quarter of Canada's crude oil production. Conveyors move bitumen to Suncor's extraction plant amid temperatures of 30 degrees below. (Elisavietta Richie for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Vancouver Journal;
Planet Earth's Preacher, With Canada His Pulpit

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: VANCOUVER, British Columbia

 BODY:
He recycles like mad to cut his family's garbage down to one bag a month, uses refillable fountain pens, cloth handkerchiefs and reusable chopsticks, and gets around on buses, bikes and skates.
David Suzuki is the first to concede the triviality of such individual pursuits to conserve the planet's resources. But Mr. Suzuki, Canada's best-known advocate of environmental causes, says it is important to start somewhere to conserve a world threatened by unbridled economic growth -- and to live by one's beliefs. 
 "We just can't go on this way," he said in an interview in a tiny office cubicle lined with books near the University of British Columbia, where he is a professor of zoology. "We live in a finite world. Nothing can continue to increase forever. We have to pull back and let nature recover to the best of its ability."
He is not very optimistic about persuading other people to pull back to save the planet. He points to the virtual disappearance of environmental issues from the global screen after the 1992 United Nations "Earth Summit" in Rio de Janeiro. His daughter, Severn, who was then 12, made a moving plea there for conservation for a new generation.
Mr. Suzuki says that an estimate by a friend, the Harvard biologist and entomologist Edward O. Wilson, that more money is spent in New York City bars in two weeks than on studying biodiversity for a year makes him even more dispirited.
"That shows you what our economic priorities are," said the 58-year-old Mr. Suzuki. A quarter of a century ago, he turned away from a promising career in genetics to focus on the environment. He has evolved into an icon, combining environmental crusading of an almost religious fervor with a highly successful effort to popularize science.
Such CBC television shows as "Suzuki on Science" and "The Nature of Things" (which has been seen on public television in the United States, as has his 1993 series, "The Secret of Life"), his more than 20 books and weekly columns in at least 30 newspapers have given his forest of graying tresses, laser-like gaze and goatee instant recognition in Canada.
He is not without critics. Two prominent newspapers have dropped his column. The Globe and Mail in Toronto complained about his "narrow focus." The Vancouver Sun said he had become "repetitious and boring." Even his usually admiring brother-in-law, Pieter Cullis, a biochemistry professor at the University of British Columbia, calls his environmentalism "religious zealotry."
Mr. Suzuki does not take exception to that characterization. "Yes, the issues ultimately are spiritual," he said. "They have to do with what we are, what is our place, how we were meant to live and the future of our children."
Back in 1969 his work at the University of British Columbia exploring mutations in fruit flies earned him an award as Canada's outstanding scientist under age 40. But the study of genetics itself and its link to his own bitter childhood memories drove him out of that field.
In 1942, in the national panic during World War II, his Japanese-Canadian family was stripped of its assets, split up and sent to internment camps.
After he grew up, he said, "I began to read up on the social implications of genetics, and my God! I discovered the Nazi Holocaust was fueled by geneticists, and that it was geneticists who laid the groundwork for people believing it doesn't matter whether you're born in Canada for three generations -- once a Jap, always a Jap."
The internment "seared my soul," he said.
While he was a student in the United States -- he earned his doctorate at the University of Chicago in 1961 -- he joined the American civil rights and peace movements.
"That's when I began to develop such a rage that my first wife said to me, 'You're becoming a racist -- like you hate people because they're white,' " he said. "The rage was directed against the discrimination against blacks, and as a victim myself I identified totally with them."
Mr. Suzuki said it was a "natural step" from there into environmental advocacy.
A love of nature had been stirred by his father, Kaoru Carr Suzuki, who took him camping and fishing, teaching him how to bait a fishhook with morning grasshoppers and to stand perfectly still when a bear crossed the path.
"It became clear by the late 1960's that the major issue confronting us was planetary ecological change," he said. "The human numbers were increasing at such a rate, the consumptive demand of our species was so great that the earth was already having trouble responding."
His hope is to build environmental consciousness at the community level. He started a foundation to promote such concepts as the "ecocity," helping urban dwellers to shrink their ecological footprint, for example, by reducing the amount of garbage they create.
One Vancouver-based organization sends out women who hold the equivalent of environmental Tupperware parties to promote recycling and changes in buying habits.
Mr. Suzuki contends that women are the most committed environmentalists. "If you look at people who start environmental organizations, who become often the leaders, but also the workhorses, invariably they are women," he said.
Why? he was asked. "Women think about children," he replied. "And they think about the future."

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Prices Rise For Bonds in Thin Trading

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Treasury securities rose yesterday, and the yield on the 30-year bond fell to a three-month low in thin holiday trading.
The price of the 30-year bond rose 25/32 , to 971/32 , for a yield of 7.76 percent, down from 7.83 percent on Friday. The rates on the shorter-maturity bills were steady, and at the Treasury's weekly auction of three- and six-month bills, the discount rate declined for the third consecutive week. 
 For the second consecutive trading day, the yield on the five-year note, at 7.78 percent, was higher than the yield on the longer-term 30-year bond. Because the yields of longer-term securities are usually higher, with a greater risk of loss, that "inversion" is getting some notice.
Yet such an occurrence is not unusual when the Federal Reserve is pushing short-term interest rates higher to slow economic growth and curb inflation. When the Fed began to move short-term rates higher, as it did last February, long-term rates rose.
But as the Fed moved short-term rates even higher, investors began to become more confident that the economy would slow and inflation would subside. And because the fear of inflation drives long-term yields higher, they can fall as confidence in the Fed grows.
Traders said it was too soon to tell whether the slight inversion would last. It could be a product of light holiday trading, when movements in yields tend to be exaggerated.
Bond prices seem to get a push from the view that the economy may be slowing, though the economic data released yesterday left the outlook unclear.
Kevin W. SigRist, a corporate economist for the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis, said yesterday's Johnson Redbook sales report for the third week of December showed an increase of 3.3 percent from the previous week. Sales of clothing were soft, as expected. But sales of electronics were stronger, as expected, and more than made up for the weakness in clothing, he said.
Market participants said there were reports that holiday sales in many cases had failed to live up to the high expectations of many retailers, forcing them to mark down prices during the final days of Christmas. That hurt profitability but apparently helped to move merchandise.
Some economists said the move would put the estimates of the gains in year-over-year sales at 5 to 6 percent.
While hardly the blockbuster Christmas that some had looked for against the backdrop of healthy economic growth, the holiday sales should still be adequate enough to maintain a modest increase in the report on December retail sales. That in turn will translate into growth in real consumption approaching a robust annual rate of 5 percent in the fourth quarter of this year, said Greg Jones, an economist for MMS International, a market analysis and research firm.
The Conference Board reported that consumer confidence in December rose to 102.2, with the November number revised slightly lower, to 100.4, from 101.3. The level for December was the highest in four and a half years. Some economist said that those looking for signs of a faltering economy would not find any in thatnumber.
Mr. SigRist said the recent reading on consumer confidence indicated that consumer spending was still robust. "I think going into next year, consumer consumption still looks pretty healthy," he said, "although we're nearing a peak in terms of growth."
In the medium term, he said, "bonds in particular will be vulnerable to signs of stronger-than-expected economic growth."
"When everybody gets back to their desks after the holiday we may see bonds suffer a little," he said.
The decline in existing-home sales reported yesterday may have been greater than many had expected, and that buoyed early trading and encouraged some traders to cover short positions. Short-selling occurs when some market participants sell borrowed securities, hoping to buy them back later at lower prices and keeping the difference as profit. But if prices rise, those participants must cover their short positions by buying back the securities in order to limit their losses.
Some analysts say the decline in home sales may be a sign that rising interest rates are beginning to slow the economic momentum, but continued growth in employment and income should prevent significant erosion.
Here are the results of yesterday's auction of three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Merchants Fear Fewer Sales Ahead

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

 BODY:
Even as retailers take final markdowns as they prepare to bring in new merchandise, they worry that 1995 is shaping up as a difficult year.
The holiday selling season, which ended with a whimper on Saturday, was softer than many anticipated. And many merchants expect shoppers to suffer holiday hangovers nonetheless, when they begin receiving credit card bills next month. 
 Visa and Mastercard reported strong increases in purchases made with their credit cards. Mastercard International said it authorized 35.2 percent more transactions using American Express, Discover, Visa and Mastercard from the day after Thanksgiving through Christmas Eve, compared with the period last year. Visa's Holiday Spendtrak showed a 25.6 percent rise in purchases with Visa cards in the period compared with a year earlier.
While that might suggest some sort of retail sales strength, analysts noted that the number of stores accepting the credit cards has risen and that the transactions also reflect an increase in the use of credit cards to pay for daily expenses like groceries.
And regardless of why credit card use increased, the rise in consumer debt suggests lower spending in the months ahead.
As a result, "those number mean bigger debts and bigger payments in the upcoming months," said Michael C. Bozic, chief executive of the Hills Stores Company. "I think, unfortunately, they may be a harbinger of even slower growth in the coming year for retailers."
Mr. Bozic said sales at Hills, a chain of discount stores scattered from New England to the Mid-Atlantic states, would probably eke out a 5 percent gain for December, "but it was no picnic getting there." During the last three Decembers, Hills has averaged sales gains of 10 percent.
Retail industry analysts spent yesterday lowering estimates of fourth-quarter earnings because they expect profit margins to suffer from the panicky price-slashing that many companies adopted. Many retailers made their sales projections only after sacrificing profits to drastic markdowns.
Kimberly K. Walin, a retailing analyst at Lehman Brothers, reduced her estimates for the fourth-quarter earnings of nine retail companies, including such powerhouses as J. C. Penney, May Department Stores, Dayton Hudson and Toys "R" Us.
"While sales in the Christmas week were solid, they were not sufficiently strong for a blockbuster Christmas over all," she said.
Several retailers entered the last week of the holiday season with much slower sales than they had anticipated, but last-minute shoppers helped push up sales.
Telecheck Services Inc., a check authorization service, said the dollar amount of purchases made using checks rose 5.1 percent on a comparable-store basis between Thanksgiving and Christmas, compared with the year-earlier period.
Last week, Telecheck said that the dollar amount of purchases made using checks was up only 2.2 percent on a comparable basis for the period between the day after Thanksgiving and last Wednesday, compared with a year earlier.
"The unusually heavy surge of late spending was driven by an extra shopping day this season and the fact that Christmas Eve fell on a Saturday," said William Ford, Telecheck's economic adviser. "Those two factors helped boost a lagging shopping season up to the lower end of our retail check sales prediction."
The sluggish holiday sales confounded most retailers. Yesterday, the Conference Board, a business research group, reported that its survey of consumer confidence rose to its highest level in four years, and other indicators show that the economy is percolating along.
But while unemployment is low, income is not growing. "Despite signs that the economy is forging ahead, people aren't feeling it in their pockets," Alan M. Rifkin, a retailing analyst at Salomon Brothers, said.
Still, some retailers did well, notably those selling jewelry, consumer electronics, home decorating items, appliances and other hard goods.
"We'll be up percentage-wise in the single digits by the end of the month, and we're very pleased with that, since the beginning of the month was pretty slow," said Alan M. Zimmer, president and chief executive of Reeds Jewelers, a jewelry chain in the Southeast.
Unlike retailers that loaded up with merchandise in the expectation of robust sales, Reeds kept its inventories tidy. "We stayed pretty lean and used overnight shipments when we needed to," Mr. Zimmer said.

HEADLINE: A Rare Mix of Tenants;
Working Residents Create a New Model for Welfare Hotels

BYLINE: By LISA W. FODERARO 

 BODY:
In the ornate lobby of the former Times Square Hotel, a remarkable cross-section of people come and go.
There's the model who used to appear in J. Crew catalogues but now suffers from AIDS-related Kaposi's sarcoma. There's the actress who scrimps by as an extra on television shows like "Law and Order" and "One Life to Live," waiting to make it big. And there's the Vietnam veteran who has coped with depression and post-traumatic stress syndrome since the war. 
 At the Times Square, once one of New York City's most notorious welfare hotels, a social experiment of sorts is under way. The formerly homeless and the mentally ill are living together with almost an equal number of working people, neighbors in a newly restored building that used to be a neighborhood blight.
For the two nonprofit groups that provide a range of social services for the residents, the experiment at 255 West 43d Street was intended to demonstrate that single-room-occupancy hotels can be salvaged, that they can become more than just dumping grounds for people with problems. The groups saw clear, pragmatic reasons for offering many low-rent apartments to working people, as it would make it easier not only to manage the property, but also to earn the community's acceptance.
But their primary motivation was therapeutic. They knew that the mix of tenants would be healthy for their clients.
"Most of our special-needs tenants have never lived in a place where they had examples of people who got up in the morning and went to work, people whose lives functioned," said Rosanne Haggerty, executive director of Common Ground Community, a nonprofit housing and economic development organization that owns and manages the building. "To observe that, instead of having it described to you, is a really powerful thing."
Common Ground wants to repeat the model in the Prince George, a former welfare hotel that stands empty at 14 East 28th Street, near Madison Avenue. The group has preliminary financing commitments from the city and state, but must sell the idea to a community that still recalls the horrors of that once-elegant hotel's most recent incarnation.
At the Times Square -- "Hotel" was dropped from the name to reflect its new use -- some residents are obviously ill. But because the working residents and special-needs tenants are spread out on all the floors, it is hard to tell who has AIDS or a mental illness or a history of homelessness, and who does not. "We lose track," said Ms. Haggerty, referring to the 73 staff members.
Rob Skinner, a 56-year-old Vietnam veteran with chronic depression, has found residing with regular working people and going about his business without the label of mental illness liberating. "You haven't been stereotyped, so it brings out the best in you," he said, "and you strive to fit right in."
The residents who are there because they meet the income requirements, making $13,000 to $25,000 a year, witness occasionally zany, sometimes tragic behavior. But some say they get a charge out of the diversity of the tenants and could never afford such a nice studio apartment in a nonsubsidized midtown Manhattan building.
"There's just a great energy here," said Peter Zazzali, a 28-year-old actor who pays $474 a month for his studio. "There are people stricken with god-awful diseases and older, destitute folks whose issues are being dealt with. Then there are a lot of artists. It's not to say there aren't discouraging moments at times, but in general there's a positive quality to everything going on here."
Sometimes, Jennifer Ann Galvez, a 25-year-old actress, has to put up with dates whose teasing about her choice of residence verges toward ridicule. And she once rode an elevator with a woman screaming that Mussolini had been her husband.
But then, Ms. Galvez thinks of her actor friends who are doubled up in one-bedroom apartments in genteel buildings with doormen and swimming pools.
"They're temping in the daytime and waiting tables at night, and they don't have time to act," Ms. Galvez said. "With the money I'm not spending on rent, I can see Broadway shows and travel."
It was a housing concept that almost did not happen.
The Times Square Hotel, a rundown single-room-occupancy hotel and a huge source of affordable housing with 735 units, was used by New York City in the late 1980's to house hundreds of homeless families. And it was managed by Tran Dinh Truong, a notorious landlord whose Kenmore Hotel on East 23d Street was seized by Federal authorities last summer because of poor conditions there.
With stabbings, fires, drug dealing and rats all rampant in the Times Square Hotel, according to court papers, the city government took over management from Mr. Truong in 1990 and sought proposals for an economy-class tourist hotel. Its plan called for the 205 original single-room-occupancy tenants, many of them elderly and mentally ill, to live in a part of the building that was sealed off and had a separate entrance.
But Common Ground and the Center for Urban Community Services starting talking up their own plan -- a plan that did not write off the original tenants but included them -- with local officials and community leaders in the Times Square area.
Some nearby businesses initially recoiled at the prospect of adding a permanent group of mentally disabled and formerly homeless individuals, as well as people with AIDS, to a neighborhood already burdened by teen-age runaways, drug addicts and criminals. Government officials doubted that such an experiment could work on the scale of such a large building.
But, responding to the enthusiastic support of Community Board 5, Mayor David N. Dinkins approved the plan. The Department of Housing Preservation and Development contributed $28.8 million through a loan program for the purchase and renovation of the building. And Federal historic rehabilitation tax credits paid for the restoration of the lobby's elaborate gilt and cream plaster ceiling, terrazzo floors, marble stairs and original chandeliers.
Money for social services has come from a range of government agencies: the city's Division of AIDS Services, Department of Homeless Services and Department of Mental Health; the state's Department of Social Services and Office of Mental Health, and the Federal Department of Housing and Urban Development.
Now, after a two-and-a-half-year gut renovation, the 15-story building's facade and interiors practically gleam. The number of units was reduced from 735 to 652 so that all the residents would have private bathrooms and 80 percent would have their own kitchens. Each floor also has a special activities room, from an art studio and a computer lab to a music rehearsal space and a weight room.
Rodney W. McCray, a former model with AIDS, was living in a boarding room without a bathroom before moving to the Times Square. "I took two friends who have really nice apartments on a tour here," he said. "They were impressed. And they're snobs."
Many early skeptics now praise the project. "It's a small miracle," said Rebecca Robertson, president of the 42d Street Development Project, a division of the state's Urban Development Corporation. "From an economic development point of view, it has cleaned up what was a serious eyesore."
Residents who are mentally ill or who were once homeless have many opportunities to work in one of the 100 jobs in the building, whether stocking the soda vending machine two hours a week or working full time as security guards.
What has perhaps attracted the most notice from the neighborhood is the addition of a Ben & Jerry's ice cream shop on the building's ground floor, at the corner of Eighth Avenue and West 43d Street -- a yuppie beacon on a stretch of blocks dominated by sex shops and scruffy souvenir outlets.
One of the assistant managers and all of the ice cream scoopers live in the building. Because Ben & Jerry's donated the $25,000 franchise to Common Ground, all profits go back into the jobs program, as well as economic development plans for the building's remaining commercial space.
The breakdown of tenants is based on a rather complex formula. There are 165 original single-room-occupancy tenants remaining. As they move out or die over the years, they will be replaced on a 50-50 basis: half of the new tenants will have low incomes, while the other half will qualify for the social services. Common Ground plans to limit the number of residents with AIDS to 50 and the number of people with mental illness to 200.
While some other projects in the city have integrated the same kinds of populations, the income ceilings are generally lower, so the working people tend to be poorer. In addition, they might make up only a fifth of the residents, Ms. Haggerty said. And the Times Square actually recruited some of the actors who work in the surrounding theater district, adding an arty sophistication to the building's makeup.
Whether Ms. Haggerty and her colleagues can pull it off again at the 13-story Prince George remains to be seen. Their first move was to change the name of the building to the Madison Square to dissociate the project from the 1980's welfare hotel.
Real estate interests and neighbors have already expressed concern. But Community Board 5 appears to have faith in Common Ground, with its housing and human services committee recently voting unanimously in favor of the plan. Anticipating community opposition, Nicholas Fish, the community board's chairman, cautioned his neighbors to have an open mind.
"We cannot allow the bad facilities to crowd out the good proposals," he said, "because we don't get many good proposals."

HEADLINE: Home Resales Declined 2.6% Last Month

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Sales of previously owned homes fell in November to the lowest level in 17 months, as rising mortgage rates pushed buyers out of the market, a real estate trade group said today. The Midwest posted the only advance.
The group, the National Association of Realtors, said sales of existing single-family homes dropped 2.6 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 3.81 million, down from 3.91 million in October, when sales rose five-tenths of 1 percent. 
 The November rate was the lowest since June 1993, when it was 3.7 million annually.
Home sales, while down this year, defied expectations by remaining relatively strong in the face of rising interest rates. But, with the most recent increases in interest rates, they seem to be heading down.
Surveys by the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation show that 30-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 9.18 percent in November, up from 8.93 percent in October and 7.15 percent last February.
A jump from 7 percent to 9 percent adds $209 to the monthly payment on a $150,000 mortgage.
"We expected sales to slow down, given the direction rates have taken," said Edmund G. Woods Jr., president of the realtors' group.
Last December, the sales rate reached a new high of 4.35 million. That was two months before the Federal Reserve began raising interest rates to slow the economy and cool inflationary pressures.
Looking ahead, John A. Tuccillo, chief economist at the realtors' group, predicted further declines, "but nothing catastrophic."
Despite the drop in November, Mr. Woods said the pace of sales remained strong, adding that "we expect it to stay that way."
The trade group estimated that sales for all of 1994 would reach 3.97 million units, well above the 3.8 million homes sold in 1993.
The median price of an existing home was $108,100 in November, up from $107,100 in the corresponding month a year ago. The median price is the midpoint, meaning half the homes cost more and half cost less.
Sales rose 1.9 percent in November in the Midwest, to an annual rate of 1.09 million. The median price was $86,600, up from $85,500 in November 1993.
Sales were unchanged at a rate of 560,000 in the Northeast, where the median price was $134,900, down from $140,700 in November 1993.
But sales fell elsewhere, including the South, where they plunged 7.7 percent, to an annual rate of 1.32 million. Sales fell 1.2 percent in the West, to a rate of 830,000.
The median price of an existing home in the South was $94,600, down from $95,700 in the corresponding month a year earlier, and in the West it was $145,000, up from $140,400.

HEADLINE: Woman in the News;
A Disciplinarian to Shepherd Pataki's Budget: Patricia Ann Woodworth

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS 

 BODY:
When Patricia A. Woodworth was recruited as Michigan's budget director in 1991, she inherited a deficit equal to nearly 25 percent of the state budget as well as a history of Federal mandates and state court decisions that left little leeway to reduce spending. She immediately took aim at Michigan's welfare program and aggressively lobbied the Legislature to scrap relief for employable single adults -- a goal that skeptics figured she could not accomplish.
"We just eliminated it," Ms. Woodworth recalled yesterday. "Not in the first year, but we got it done in the second. At that point, one house of the Legislature was Democratic, but the Democrats went along. Public sympathy was for cutting taxes and reducing government spending and they read the writing on the wall." 
 As the new budget director for Governor-elect George E. Pataki, Ms. Woodworth will need a generous dose of public sympathy as she tackles a projected $5 billion budget deficit come January. She acknowledges that the budget gap is daunting, though proportionately smaller than the one she wrestled with in Michigan, but the challenge she will face in New York seems familiar.
"It's changing the core philosophy of government, looking at different ways of doing things, working better and smarter and with less," she said.
"I try and put myself in the position of the average taxpayer earning in the high $20,000's or low $30,000's," said Ms. Woodworth, who made $87,300 as Michigan's budget director, "and wonder if this is a dollar we should take out of their hands and put into government's." She will earn about $105,800 a year in the new post.
Ms. Woodworth, a 39-year-old classical pianist and accomplished oboe player, may be known best to her neighbors in Battle Creek and East Lansing, Mich., for her green thumb. Which also may account for her fiscally conservative budget ideology: that selective pruning helps a plant stay healthy and grow.
As budget director in Michigan and, before that, in Florida, she earned a reputation as an aggressive disciplinarian over spending.
The legacy of which Ms. Woodworth is the most proud is a computerized bookkeeping system that just began operation in Lansing. But the most notable fiscal reform with which she is associated is Michigan's decision earlier this year to raise the state sales tax to 6 percent, from 4 percent, and to triple cigarette taxes in order to virtually end the traditional dependence of local schools on property taxes.
Helped by an influx of Federal aid, she creatively contained costs and mined new revenue sources to generate cuts in taxes on property, business and income in Michigan and to propel its Republican Governor, John Engler, to prominence in the reinventing-government movement.
"This only happened because we had a Governor who was willing to set the budget direction, and a budget director who was tough enough to enforce the discipline needed," said State Representative Dan Gilmer, a Republican who heads the Appropriations Committee. "I would never want to get into a bluff poker game with Patti Woodworth."
State Senator John F. Kelly of Detroit, a Democrat who battled with Ms. Woodworth over her cuts in social services, said: "When Patti gets the directive from the Governor, she follows it to the maximum. She takes no prisoners. She's very good with numbers, but she's even better with reductions. She did some very creative work in keeping programs that should be kept and in making transfers that were difficult for opponents to block."
"I was in the trenches trying to figure out ways to humiliate them about their lack of compassion," Mr. Kelly added. "But I must admit, in retrospect three years later, the number of complaints other than by the providers was small."
Another adversary, David C. Hollister, who was chairman of the welfare budget committee in the House before being elected the Mayor of Lansing last year, said of Ms. Woodworth: "She's smart, tough -- compassionate doesn't come to mind. She's kind of a bulldog for the Governor. She's got a singular sense of purpose, which is to serve the Governor, and the rest of it, be damned."
In Michigan, Ms. Woodworth is credited with bringing representatives of the Governor and Legislature together to agree on joint economic forecasts -- a device that had been used effectively in Florida where she was Gov. Bob Martinez's chief adviser on budget, tax policy, education, environment, criminal justice and social services.
"She didn't do it by herself, but they came up with a lot of creative ways to balance the budget without raising taxes," said Robert J. Kleine, senior economist with Public Sector Consultants, a public policy group in Michigan.
Patricia Ann Woodworth was born in Cheverly, Md., Jan. 10, 1955. The daughter of an Agriculture Department lawyer and a homemaker, she was raised in the Maryland suburbs and graduated magna cum laude from the University of Maryland in 1977.
After serving as a management intern with the Social Security Administration in Washington, she held budget jobs with the Senate Finance Committee, the Department of Health and Human Services and the Office of Management and Budget, where she was a special assistant to the director, David Stockman, during the Reagan Administration.
Her husband, Dr. John Schwarz, is a Republican State Senator from Battle Creek and a practicing surgeon. They plan to commute between New York and Michigan on weekends. As to the state of the couple's own budget, Ms. Woodworth said, "suffice it to say, it's balanced."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Patricia Woodworth, Michigan's budget director, was named to the same post for New York State. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Man Indicted In Bombing On Subway

BYLINE: By JOHN KIFNER 

 BODY:
Edward J. Leary was indicted yesterday on charges of carrying the firebomb that injured more than 40 people on a crowded subway on Dec. 21 as part of an extortion plot to hold the city's subways ransom.
The terse, two-page grand jury indictment handed up at the close of court business last night listed one count each of attempted murder in the second degree, first-degree assault, attempted grand larceny and possession of a weapon against Mr. Leary, a 49-year-old unemployed computer expert. 
 Yesterday's charges were, in effect, an interim indictment required by a state penal code rule that grand jury charges must be brought soon after arrest -- in this case, within 144 hours, or exactly six days. Officials in the Manhattan District Attorney's office said they planned to bring a more detailed superseding indictment, which would probably carry more counts against the defendant.
"The investigation is continuing," said Barbara Thompson, spokeswoman for the District Attorney's office. "At this point, some of the victims can't even give testimony because they are still hospitalized."
Stephen J. Murphy, the lawyer for Mr. Leary, has proclaimed his client's innocence and yesterday held a news conference in a restaurant in the Sheepshead Bay section of Brooklyn with Mr. Leary's wife, Marge Schaller.
Wearing a maroon knitted cap pulled low over her head, dark sunglasses and a scarf, Ms. Schaller, a nurse, said she knew nothing of a bomb plot and believed her husband was innocent.
"No, I don't think he did it," she said, breaking into sobs at several points. "I think he's a victim. He's not the sort of person who would do something like that. He loved New York."
Mr. Leary was arrested in his hospital bed at the New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center burn unit around 2 A.M. on Thursday morning after police officers searching his 10-room house in suburban Scotch Plains, N.J., said they found a half-dozen pages of angry handwritten notes referring to what they said was an extortion plan.
Prosecutors said the search also turned up bomb-making instructions as well as glass jars, batteries and kitchen timers identical to those that the police say were used to build the firebomb. The Manhattan District Attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau, originally said Mr. Leary would be charged with 45 counts of first-degree assault, one for each of the injured passengers.
The police say they believe the firebomb that exploded on the southbound No. 4 train on Wednesday was actually Mr. Leary's second bomb, coming less than a week after a similar device caused a fire on a subway train passing through Harlem, badly burning two teen-agers. The police at first suspected that one of the teen-agers was carrying the firebomb in her backpack.
Mr. Leary, who has third-degree burns over 40 percent of his body and is heavily sedated with morphine, is listed in critical but stable condition, awaiting two operations on his badly burned legs. Two other passengers are also listed in critical condition with extensive burns.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Marge Schaller, the wife of subway bombing suspect Edward J. Leary, walked yesterday on a Brooklyn street with a bodyguard. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Surges Against Peso In a Day of Light Trading

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
The dollar surged further against the peso yesterday, while ending mixed against other currencies in very light trading.
Besides the slide of the peso, there was no significant movement elsewhere in foreign-exchange trading. Many currency dealers have already closed their books for the year, and financial markets in London, the most active center of foreign-exchange trading, were closed because of a holiday. 
 Attention focused on the peso crisis, which erupted last week when the Mexican Government abruptly scrapped a policy of defending the currency's value and allowed it trade freely against the dollar. Since then, heavy speculative selling of pesos has depressed the currency by more than a third.
The United States Under Secretary of the Treasury for International Affairs, Lawrence H. Summers, expressed concern about the tumbling peso yesterday, saying in a statement that "recent movements in the value of the Mexican peso have gone far beyond what can be justified by Mexican economic fundamentals." Mr. Summers also said that excessive depreciation of the currency "is in no one's interest."
The statement in itself was considered unusual and seen as an attempt to calm nervous investors, who have been witnessing the value of their peso-denominated assets wither by the day. But currency dealers said the Treasury statement had no significant effect on slowing the peso's descent.
In late New York trading, the peso fell to 5.700 pesos to the dollar from 4.700 pesos in New York on Friday. On Monday, while financial markets in New York were closed, markets in Mexico City were open, and the peso was quoted at a rate of 5.075 to the dollar.
The severity of the drop has drastically reduced the value of Mexican assets held by United States investors, and that has prompted more selling and left many currency professionals wondering when the peso will stabilize.
"That's the zillion-dollar question," said David Solin, a partner at Foreign Exchange Analytics, a New York currency investment firm. "People had a rude awakening in Mexico. They learned there's risk involved. I think, quite frankly, the peso drop reflects all these people running for the doors."
In other trading in New York, the dollar slipped to 1.5757 German marks from 1.5790 marks on Friday. It rose to 100.32 Japanese yen from 100.12 yen. "It was extremely quiet," said Hubert Pedroli, manager of foreign-exchange trading at the New York office of Credit Suisse. "There's no fundamentals, no volume."
The last week of the year ordinarily is one of the slowest times for foreign-exchange dealings.

HEADLINE: MEXICAN CURRENCY RESUMES ITS FALL, OFFICIALS SILENT

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Mexico's economic crisis took a new turn for the worse today as investors dumped more of their holdings in pesos, driving the value of the Mexican currency down by nearly 10 percent more against the dollar.
Despite a brief rally late in the trading day, the peso closed at 5.70 to the dollar, or about 40 percent less than it was worth when the crisis broke out a week ago. 
 Mexican stock prices also fell, bringing demands from both Mexican executives and foreign investors that the Government explain how it would try to stabilize the economy.
Instead, for the fourth straight day, Government officials gave virtually no public indication of their plans. Spokesmen for President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon denied reports that he would speak to the nation tonight, appparently contributing to the confusion and concern.
"People this morning were hoping to see some demonstration of leadership from the Zedillo administration or from Zedillo himself, but it didn't come," said Marla Marron, a Latin American equities analyst at Salomon Brothers in New York.
The peso's continuing fall added to the huge losses -- on paper and in some cases, real -- of Americans who invested tens of billions of dollars in Mexican stocks and bonds.
It also heightened concern that at a time of considerable political tension in Mexico, the Government will have to cut spending deeply, hold down wages and raise interest rates sharply to avoid a return of runaway inflation.
The cheaper peso did make Mexico more attractive as a destination for foreign tourists [Page D1.].
Rumors persisted about what the Government might do. With the peso already well below the levels where many economists expected it to settle after the Government floated the currency last Thursday, speculators played on fears that Mexico might not be able to cover some of its short-term debts or might impose exchange-rate controls.
Despite a commitment from the United States last week to provide $6 billion in emergency credits to help Mexico cope with the depletion of its hard-currency reserves, pressure appeared to be increasing on the Clinton Administration to come to the rescue with more substantial aid.
Such a move could put the Government in the uncomfortable position of bailing out United States investors who had made considerable profits on stocks they knew to be risky. Yet a further deterioration of the Mexican economy could increase the flows to the United States of cheap Mexican exports and of undocumented migrants seeking jobs.
In Washington today, Under Secretary of the Treasury Lawrence H. Summers, said the peso had depreciated far more than was justified by the Mexican economy's condition.
"Excessive depreciation is in no one's interest," said Mr. Summers, who is the senior official involved in talks with Mexican officials.
Still, there was little sign this afternoon that his remarks had buoyed investors' confidence. Some analysts of Mexican securities predicted that even an economic-stabilization program that included deep cuts in public spending would only be persuasive if it were endorsed by the International Monetary Fund or some other pillar of international finance.
Mexican officials who spoke on condition of anonymity said the day's turmoil reinforced their conviction that they should not present a stabilization plan until they were certain they had a plan that would work. Two senior officials said this would not be before next Monday, although another said it might be finished Thursday or Friday.
"If we come out with any document that is not 100 percent sure and 100 percent credible, it is going to be counterproductive," a senior economic official here said. "We cannot take the risk of putting out something that is not perfect."
Such caution may reflect one of the more painful lessons learned by the economic team that took office with Mr. Zedillo at the start of the month. The Government found itself low on the dollar reserves used to support the peso when its value fell to the bottom of a range within which it was allowed to trade, so last Tuesday the Zedillo administration widened the so-called trading band, effectively letting the currency be devalued about 15 percent.
But many economic analysts said Mr. Zedillo erred in not devaluing the peso further and absorb all the pressure for an adjustment. When the currency's value quickly fell to the bottom of the new trading band, the Government was forced on Wednesday night to announce that it would eliminate the band altogether and let the peso float freely.
Part of the reason that both the peso and the Mexican stock exchange were whipsawed today, analysts said, was that the New York markets had been closed Monday. Thus, there were relatively few buyers even for what might seem like bargains next week. American mutual funds that held sizable positions in Mexican securities were also said to have begun selling shares.
"Even in this crisis, Mexicans are on vacation," said David L. Roberts, a vice president at Duff & Phelps Credit Ratings, which continued this week to rate some Mexican debt issues as investment grade. "It is the people in California and New York who are working this week who had to sell positions. All the decisions are being made by sellers."
Today, stocks fell 65.89 points on the Mexican stock exchange, to close at 2,276.90, a drop of 2.81 percent. In a sign that foreigners were instrumental in driving down the market, one of the stocks most widely held in the United States, Telefonos de Mexico, plummmeted more than 6 percent.
The analysts said investors also worried that the Government might have trouble paying off more than $5 billion worth of short-term debt, which falls due over the next six weeks and is pegged to the prevailing exchange rate. Yet because the debt is due in pesos, it is doubtful that the Government would be caught short even if the bonds were not rolled over.
The challenge that Mr. Zedillo and his advisers now face is how severe an adjustment to make. If it is too sharp, it could strangle the economy, said Nora Lustig, an economist at the Brookings Institution in Washington. But if inflation is allowed to rise quickly, it will frighten away investors, bring demands for higher wages and erode the effect of a cheaper peso in making exports more competitive.
"Until we see what they are going to do in the short run, what they are going to do in the middle run, we are in a no man's land," she said. "The irrationality in the markets is going to continue."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Players and Owners File Complaints With Labor Board

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Unable to settle their duel over baseball's economic system at the bargaining table, striking players and owners filed dual unfair-labor-practice charges with the National Labor Relations Board yesterday.
The exchange of charges is the first volley in a new phase of the four-and-a-half-month-old strike that will now continue more in legal briefs and evidentiary documents than at the bargaining table. Congress, which has indicated that it will look at baseball's antitrust exemption, and the courts are expected to join the N.L.R.B. as new playing fields before the dispute is resolved. 
 Daniel Silverman, the labor board's New York regional director, said he didn't expect the cross-charges to delay his staff's investigation of the dispute, which centers on the owners' implementation of a salary cap. Silverman said that cases generally take four to six weeks to investigate, which means the two sides should know the outcome of this initial phase in the first half of February.
"I think it won't affect adversely the timeliness of our investigation," Silverman said of the dual, or dueling, charges. "We fully anticipate that we'll be able to begin the investigation immediately and make a recommendation to the general counsel as soon as possible."
The board's general counsel, Fred Feinstein, on Silverman's recommendation, could decide to issue complaints against both sides, against only one or against neither. A hearing before an administrative law judge would follow the issuance of a complaint.
If they issue a complaint, Silverman and Feinstein also will decide if seeking an injunction in United States District Court would be appropriate. For the players, an injunction would prevent the clubs from operating under the salary cap until it was determined whether the owners' declaration of a bargaining impasse and implementation of new work rules were proper. For the owners, an injunction would require the players to return to the bargaining table and negotiate in good faith.
It's not very likely that much bargaining, if any, will go on while this scenario is being played out.
Gene Orza, the union's associate general counsel, called the owners' charge a delaying tactic, but Chuck O'Connor, management's chief lawyer, said: "We haven't been the ones talking about litigation. We've been trying to negotiate a new agreement. I still believe this will be resolved in a room that contains a bargaining table and not in a courtroom or the halls of Congress. Unfortunately, the union is more disposed to and more comfortable in the environment of litigation and legislation, not negotiation."
The union charged that the clubs have "interfered with, restrained and coerced" players in violation of the National Labor Relations Act. Specifically, the union said that the clubs have refused to bargain in good faith and should not be permitted to make changes unilaterally in the working conditions.
In a letter to Silverman, Michael Weiner, counsel to the union, asked that the board, if it decides to issue a complaint against the clubs, also seek a restraining order that would prevent them from using the salary cap as major league baseball's new economic system.
The clubs charged that the union has violated the labor act by, among other things, refusing to negotiate wages on a collective basis and instead insisting on "the individual negotiation of wages and salary arbitration." In short, the players haven't bargained in good faith, the owners said.
"Why the association, as opposed to the clubs, would have any interest in not reaching an agreement is beyond reason," Donald Fehr, the union head, said in a statement. "This move by the clubs, just like their last charge, which was dismissed by the board, is a transparent attempt to divert attention from their own misconduct."
The board last week dismissed the clubs' charge that the union violated the labor act through allegedly threatening comments made by Bobby Bonilla and John Franco aimed at striking players who might report to training camps next spring.
The board, on the other hand, issued a complaint against the clubs for their failure to make a $7.8 million payment to the players' pension and benefit plan Aug. 1. The board accused the clubs of not bargaining in good faith and scheduled a hearing before an administrative law judge March 14.
In commenting on the union's latest bad-faith bargaining charge, Fehr said, "From the beginning, the clubs have had one and only one thought in mind: forcing a salary cap upon the players."
"As the investigation proceeds," his statement continued, "we are confident the board will come to see that the clubs entered into negotiations with no intention of reaching an agreement other than upon the clubs' preconceived terms."
The clubs, at the same time, said in a news release that from the time they made their first salary-cap proposal June 14 until talks broke off last Thursday, "the union refused to negotiate over any aspect of a system that would set the wages of its members on a collective basis."
Management's goal in negotiations was to induce the players to accept 50 percent of the industry revenue, thereby establishing the clubs' labor costs before any individual contract negotiations were conducted. The clubs implemented the salary cap last Friday in a set of new work rules that also eliminated salary arbitration.
O'Connor said that baseball's "biggest cost item" is player compensation, adding, "To refuse to negotiate that, we feel, was irresponsible and we don't feel it's permissible under the labor act."
O'Connor said he hoped the two sides would return to the bargaining in the near future.
"The union has placed so much emphasis on litigation and legislation, it had to get it out of its system," he said. "I would hope we would not let the month of January go by without trying to negotiate this."

HEADLINE: Advanced Placement Exams Suffering an Erosion in Value

BYLINE: By WILLIAM CELIS 3d 

 BODY:
For more than 40 years, Advanced Placement tests have been a standard fixture in high school for America's ablest students. While they attended high school, the students earned college credit by taking demanding classes from art and Latin to calculus and physics.
But as the number of Advanced Placement courses -- and students taking them -- has proliferated, more and more colleges and universities are tightening the number of credits they will award to students. Some institutions have increased the minimum test score they will accept. Others are refusing to accept the credits as substitutes for college work, saying that as they have redesigned curriculums, the tests have become outdated. 
 All of this puts students like Adriana Izquierda, a 19-year-old freshman at Johns Hopkins University in Baltimore, in an unexpected spot. During her senior year in Buffalo, Ms. Izquierda thought she had taken enough Advanced Placement courses to skip her freshman year of college and save the money. The tests cost about $72 each, or about $22 a credit, far cheaper than the actual cost of a typical college-level course.
Harvard University, which accepted her, agreed to give Ms. Izquierda credit for all her advanced placement classes, offering her 52 undergraduate credits in American history, French, German, English composition, economics, calculus and biology, and placing her in the sophomore class.
Instead, Ms. Izquierda decided to go to Hopkins, because as a pre-med student she believed it had a stronger science curriculum and more research opportunities. But Hopkins, like a small but growing number of private, competitive colleges, was unwilling to give her credit for all the courses.
After she enrolled, she learned that the university would give her only 22 credits, leaving her 2 short of the minimum 24 credits that Hopkins requires of sophomore students. That meant she started as a second-semester freshman.
"I was surprised and startled that they didn't accept more," said Ms. Izquierda, a double major in biology and history.
She plans to appeal to the economics department to accept two additional courses for 6 college credits before trying to get other departments to accept her other advanced placement work.
The changing attitudes of some institutions toward the advanced placement tests come as more students are taking the tests -- a record of nearly 400,000 in 1994. Colleges encourage this by taking the tests into consideration for admission and for merit-based scholarships. And students are increasingly encouraged by state education departments that will, in the case of South Carolina, even pay for the courses and exams because of the critical thinking skills built into the instruction of advanced placement courses, not the rote learning that still often occurs in more traditional high school courses.
But, said Kathy Christie, an analyst with the Education Commission of the States, an education research and policy organization in Denver, "if it looks like the advanced placement tests are not matching the curriculum that universities are offering or if it looks like too many students are learning by rote, then these are two good reasons for demanding higher scores or not taking the scores at all."
Not a factor, Ms. Christie said, is the financial windfall that institutions receive by having students repeat courses they took in high school. Students like Ms. Izquierda are rare, and the total population of students with advanced placement credit represents a fraction of many institutions' enrollment.
Some institutions, like Amherst College, have never accepted the credits, and this fall a faculty committee at the private, four-year liberal arts college in Amherst, Mass., voted yet again to make freshmen take the college's full course load.
"I have raised the question of why this college has this policy," said Jane Reynolds, who has been Amherst's dean of admission for six years. "I said: 'Look, we are using it as an evaluation tool. Why aren't we giving credit for these courses like our competitors?' From a consumer standpoint, I wanted to know why."
The faculty committee's response: It feels comfortable giving credit for only the work the college administers.
"I have been around the argument enough that I agree with them," said Ms. Reynolds, who added that students and families generally accept the college's position when it is explained.
Few college administrators and professors question the rigor of the advanced placement tests, which were written for the College Board by the Educational Testing Service, the Princeton, N.J., testing and education research company that also administers the Scholastic Assessment Test, the Graduate Record Examination and other tests used for college admissions.
Advanced placement classes and the subsequent exams, first introduced in 1953, now cover 16 subjects and 29 tests under a broad program that continues to grow. In the 1996-97 academic year, statistics and environmental science will be added to the courses and test offerings, which attracted a record 400,000 high school students this year, some taking several tests. Students passed a record 361,125 of the nearly 600,000 tests administered in 1994.
The test includes multiple-choice questions, writing essays and reading long passages that encourage the use of critical thinking skills. The tests -- which are graded on a scale of 1 to 5, with 5 being the highest score and 3 passing -- have been popular methods for years to reduce tuition costs for students and parents. At minimum, a student could save a few hundred dollars to several thousand in his first year of college, depending on the number of tests he passed and his institution's tuition.
Although most institutions still accept advanced placement credits as suitable substitutes for college work, the explosion of students taking the tests has triggered fundamental changes in how institutions use the tests. Some colleges and universities have questioned their wisdom in giving credit to students who score a 3 because it is considered a sort of middle ground in which professors say student ability varies greatly.
"The 5's are spectacular," said Judith de Luce, a professor in the classics department at Miami University in Ohio, and who is the chief grader for the College Board on its advanced placement Latin test. "The 4's are solid. The concern lies at the 3 score.
"The top 3's are really very good, but compared with the 4's and the 5's, some of the 3's at the low end are not nearly as good."
College and university officials said they had found that students who score a 3 have much more trouble with advanced courses. Not surprisingly, the student who scores a 3 is more likely to withdraw from the course or hold others back.
This scenario pushed Miami's English and physics departments two years ago to increase minimum test scores to 4 from 3.
"We began to worry that we were not doing these students any favors," said T. William Houk, chairman of the physics department who has helped write some of the advanced placement physics tests. Struggling in the first year of college, Dr. Houk added, "doesn't help their maturity and doesn't help their confidence."
The department's curriculum also was revamped in recent years, said Dr. Houk, and the advanced placement physics courses and tests no longer covered a lot of the material discussed in the department's curriculum.
Decisions on whether to increase the minimum test score or whether to accept advanced placement credits at all are still largely left to individual academic departments at a university, and only a small number of institutions -- like Clemson University in South Carolina -- have institution-wide policies.
Although Clemson generally accepts 3's on the range of advanced placement tests, entering freshmen at the public, four-year college of 16,500 students receive advanced placement credit for only the second half of Western civilization, a course that runs two semesters.
Jerome V. Reel, senior vice provost and dean of undergraduate studies at Clemson, said the advanced placement course in Western civilization began at A.D. 1500 whereas Clemson's course started before Christ, with some of the earliest settlements in Africa.
The changing climate has not been lost on students. Amy Pate, who graduated last May from a Nashville high school, would not attend an institution that did not accept her advanced placement credits.
"I knew I wanted to double major, so one of my requirements was that they were going to have to accept advanced placement," said Ms. Pate, who took advanced placement courses and passed tests in European history, American history, chemistry, biology, the first part of calculus, both Latin courses, comparative government and politics, United States government and politics, art history and English.
In all, she amassed 51 hours of college credit -- enough to make her a second-semester sophomore in only her first year of college. Ms. Pate settled on Miami University in Oxford, Ohio, which accepted all of her courses except comparative government and politics, for which the university had no equivalent course. She enrolled this fall as a second-semester sophomore and in January starts her junior year.
Because of her academic ability, Ms. Pate also received scholarships, which further reduced the Pates' financial obligation. Of Miami's annual $14,000 out-of-state tuition, room and board, Ms. Pate's mother, Ann Pate, said she and her husband, Thurman, would pay only about $2,000 this year.
Mrs. Pate said, "Advanced placement not only helped financially but also really prepared her for the rigors of a college curriculum."

HEADLINE: Asian Workers Lift Themselves by Bootstraps

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Bob Herbert's use of a tragic fire in a Thailand toy factory to attack Asian "sweatshops" (column, Dec. 21) shows an almost willful ignorance of global economics. 
 The wages in these countries may be "grotesquely low" in the opinion of Mr. Herbert, but they are probably appealing to the workers who receive them. For many of these workers, this may be their first step up into a cash economy from a largely subsistence rural existence. And as they spend their meager wages, they create a demand for food and products that lifts the living standards of their entire country. Only 30 to 40 years ago, Japan, Hong Kong, Taiwan and Korea were the targets of similar attacks by Mr. Herbert's ideological predecessors -- and look where those countries are today.
As the wages and skills of these Asian successes have advanced, the 80-cents-an-hour jobs have been transferred to other, less-developed countries like China. This starts a new cycle of economic growth. By no stretch of the imagination is China dragging down the living standards and working conditions of its neighbors. Rather, it allows them to leverage themselves up.
By United States standards, working conditions in developing countries are horrific. So are living conditions. The only way to improve conditions is to develop the economies.
Where people like Mr. Herbert see misery, the workers themselves see opportunities: to buy their first sewing machine or bicycle, or sneakers, or a cow for the family plot.
Sad as it may be to liberals, there is no reason greedy multinational corporations and their sordid subcontractors should pay above the prevailing wage in these countries, particularly since the labor force is largely unskilled. But as more companies enter the market, local wages tend to rise; and as workers become more skilled, they can command not only higher wages but also better working conditions. That's why the process is called a market economy.
When given the choice of buying toys or sneakers or sugar produced by underpaid foreigners -- and the whole purpose of tariffs and sanctions is to prevent the exercise of that choice -- the average consumer will seize the opportunity. And he or she should. The actions of those "greedy" corporations benefit both their "exploited" third-world workers and their "insensitive" first-world customers -- because if the profits aren't there, those jobs won't be either.
 
KEITH McDERMOTT
Greenwich, Conn., Dec. 21, 1994

HEADLINE: Asian Workers Lift Themselves by Bootstraps;
Meanwhile at Mattel

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Let's see if I understand the economic situation at Mattel Inc. that led to its announcement to terminate 1,000 employees (Business Day, Dec. 20). The toy company made record profits this year, acquired major competitors, saw its stock price climb 19 percent, and announced a stock split and dividend increase to benefit shareholders. Revenues and profits have increase steadily for six years. Yet Mattel decides to dismiss 4.5 percent of its work force to pursue "efficiencies" and is lauded for this by corporate analysts. 
 To hear the so-called experts: record sales, record profits, fewer competitors and a booming stock price all represent a need to lay off people.
Does anyone still wonder why so many Americans are skeptical of those who tout an economic rebound and a brighter future for the average worker?
 
DAVID J. SMOLLAR

HEADLINE: It's Official: Texans Outnumber New Yorkers

BYLINE: By KAREN DE WITT, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 27

 BODY:
Population estimates for 1994 issued by the Census Bureau today showed that Texas has surpassed New York as the second-most-populous state as a result of fewer people moving to the Empire State from other parts of the country.
"New York is a study in contrasts," said Edwin Byerly, a Census Bureau statistician and demographer who worked on the annual report that constitutes the official estimates of the nation's population changes. "On the one hand, New York has the second-highest rate of international migration, but it's last in terms of the rate of domestic migration, people moving there from other states." 
 The report today showed California was the most populous state with 31.4 million people, Texas second with 18.4 million and New York third with 18.2 million.
Earlier this year, a Texas demographer had predicted that Texas would pass New York in the national population rankings, and Census Bureau statisticians agreed in the spring that their own projections showed such a change had probably occurred in January.
Today, with the bureau's official confirmation of the shift, Gov. Mario M. Cuomo of New York had no comment, a spokesman for his office said. But when told of the projection in the spring, Mr. Cuomo attributed Texas's advance to "a whole lot of people pouring over the border," and said it was not "a sign of economic growth or of added confidence."
But today's report suggested that migration from outside the country was stronger in New York than in Texas. The report dealt only with states and included no figure for metropolitan areas.
Indeed, Texas's growth in the period covered by the report, July 1993 to July 1994, was due more to its having the second-highest excess of births over deaths (California was first) and the second-highest net migration with other states (Florida was first). It was also third highest in net international migration from July 1993 to July 1994 (behind California and New York).
The last time Texas changed population rank was in 1973, when it passed Pennsylvania to become the third-most-populous state.
Nationally, immigration continued to play a significant role in raising population, the Census Bureau said. About 30 percent, or 762,000, of the national population growth was attributed to immigration.
California ranked first in net international migration with 272,000 immigrants, and New York was second with 101,000. Texas had 75,000.
Mr. Byerly said the population data on California continued to "show how bad things have gotten" with the state's economy.
In the 1980's, California was the fifth-fastest-growing state. But in last year's reporting period, it was 35th in its rate of growth. Mr. Byerly said that in contrast to the boom days of the mid-1980's, people who would have moved to California had shifted their destinations to the Rocky Mountain states.
Today's report said that the only places showing population declines were Connecticut and Rhode Island, each of which lost about 3,000 people, and the District of Columbia, which lost 9,000 people.
The report also showed continuing growth in the South and West. Eight of the 10 fastest-growing states were in the Rocky Mountain region. The South had the largest population increase of any region, at 1.3 million people.
Over all, the nation expanded its population to 260.3 million in 1994 from 257.8 million in 1993, a growth of 2.6 million, or 1 percent.

CORRECTION-DATE: December 29, 1994, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
A chart yesterday showing Census Bureau estimates of state population gains and losses over the last decade misstated the 1994 population of Texas. It was 18,378,000, not 19,378,000.
