HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Padres and Astros Make a 12-Player Swap

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS 

 BODY:
Operating on the economic theory that a club has to spend money to make money, the new owner of the San Diego Padres reversed the free fall yesterday and restocked his team with some talent. In the biggest baseball deal in numbers in 37 years, the Padres made a 12-player trade with the Houston Astros, acquiring third baseman Ken Caminiti and center fielder Steve Finley in their group of six players.
"We want a competitive, winning team, an exciting team," said Larry Lucchino, the Padres' new chief executive under the new owner, John Moores. "To improve this team, on the field and in stability and prosperity, expenses and revenues have to go up." 
 In adding $8.6 million in salary for Caminiti and Finley for next season, the Padres insured that their expenses will rise. Their payroll would have been $13.2 million for a full 1994 season under an ownership that had stripped the team of expenses and talent.
"With an improved team," Lucchino said by telephone from his law office in Washington, "improvement will come on the revenue side."
Besides Caminiti and Finley, the Padres received Andujar Cedeno, the Astros' starting shortstop; Roberto Petagine, a minor league first baseman, and Brian Williams, a sometime starting pitcher, plus a player to be named or $50,000 in cash.
The Astros, who suddenly more closely resemble the previous Padres than the present Padres do, succeeded in their quest to shed salary and become a younger, cheaper team. They received two starting outfielders, Phil Plantier and Derek Bell; two pitchers yet to establish themselves, Pedro Martinez and Doug Brocail, and two infielders, Ricky Gutierrez and Craig Shipley.
If the trade remains a 12-player transaction, it will be the largest in numbers since the Detroit Tigers and the Kansas City Athletics exchanged 13 players in 1957, including Billy Martin, Gus Zernial and Duke Maas.
The general managers, Bob Watson of Houston and Randy Smith of San Diego, began discussing this trade at the general managers meetings in Phoenix in mid-November.
"As we got closer to new ownership, Larry Lucchino and I talked," Smith said from San Diego. "They were prepared to add some salary if I felt it could improve the club. We kept adding to it and put it to bed yesterday."
Although Smith and Watson worked out the details, Lucchino said he and Tal Smith, the Astros' new president and Randy's father, agreed to it on Tuesday. Watson chose to view the deal from the baseball viewpoint rather than the economic one, although it has been widely known that the Astros were out to make serious inroads into their $30.5 million payroll. They already had traded Pete Harnisch, a $3 million pitcher.
"It doesn't leave us short," Watson said from Houston, discussing the loss of three front-line players. "There are some very positive things."
The Astros, he explained, got a right fielder, Bell, whom they have liked for a long time; a left-handed power hitter, Plantier, to help protect the right-handed Jeff Bagwell in the lineup, and a left-handed pitcher, Martinez, whom they needed. Furthermore, Watson said, the trade opens spots for two rookies, shortstop Orlando Miller and third baseman Phil Nevin.
"We're positive that this was the right baseball move," Watson said. "To be real honest, Caminiti and Finley were going to be free agents next year. Under the new system, we have control of these players for at least three years and some five years."
Caminiti batted .283, hit 18 home runs and drove in 75 runs last season. Finley batted .276. Cedeno committed 23 errors, most in the major leagues.
Plantier, struck by injuries and slumps, fell from 34 home runs and 100 runs batted in to 18 and 41 in the strike-shortened season. Bell batted .311 and had 24 stolen bases.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
AT&T Seeks to Broaden Rate Increases

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 28

 BODY:
The AT&T Corporation, having received Federal approval to increase monthly long-distance rates, is now seeking to raise other rates, including those for calling cards and operator-handled calls.
The company filed the proposed increases today with the Federal Communications Commission and said the new rates would generate $259 million in additional revenue. 
 The plan would affect far fewer callers than the rate increase for monthly long-distance service that went into effect on Dec. 16, said Joseph Nacchio, president of AT&T's Consumer Communications Services unit. Exact figures were not available.
An AT&T spokesman, Jim McGann, said the proposed higher rates were a result of inflation and increased costs for providing the services.
If approved, the new rates would take effect on Jan. 10.
Under AT&T's proposed pricing, domestic calling card and operator-handled calls would go up by 3.8 percent. Direct-dial calls from the United States to some countries would increase by 1.3 percent and monthly subscription fees for some domestic savings plans would increase by 2.4 percent.
The most popular savings plans, True USA and True World, will not be affected because they do not charge subscription fees, Mr. McGann said.
Among the affected plans are Any Hour Saver, in which the $10 monthly subscription would rise to $10.75, and Reach Out 24 Hour, in which the fee would rise to $9.50 from $8.70, he said.
On Dec. 16 AT&T increased monthly long-distance rates by about 35 cents a customer, generating about $274 million in additional revenue annually.
The new pricing request also seeks to increase rates by an average of 5 percent for USA Direct Service calls. That service allows a person in another country to place a call to the United States using an American operator. The proposed increase would apply to 27 countries.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
 The United States is preparing a huge-scale rescue of the peso. The Clinton Administration has begun discussions on providing billions of dollars in assistance to help Mexico support its currency and make payments on its short-term debt. [Page A1.]
The peso and Mexican stocks began to steady after further sharp losses on Tuesday. The markets' improvement gave the Government breathing space as it continued pulling together a recovery plan. But many Mexicans are worried about inflation. [D1.]
American bankers cautiously welcomed efforts to provide a rescue package for Mexico. Bankers and analysts estimate the package will be worth $15 billion to $20 billion. Still, confidence in Mexico's economy is likely to remain fragile, they said. [D6.]
 
Companies
 The casino industry is trying to attract younger gamblers as part of an effort to keep its growth from slowing. Gambling companies are also drawing interest from corporate suitors. [D1.]
Apple Computer picked its first Macintosh clone maker: a new company led by the designer of the Leading Edge Model D. [D1.]
A New York City life insurance agent was indicted on Federal charges that he defrauded five insurance companies through his collection of nearly $1 million in commissions. [D1.]
The F.D.A. approved two drugs marketed by Wellcome P.L.C. The British drug giant is seeking two more approvals this week. [D4.]
Upjohn suspended trials on humans of the drug Freedox after unexplained deaths. The drug, for the treatment of head injuries, is considered important to Upjohn. The stock fell sharply. [D4.]
The Merry-Go-Round chain plans to close another 200 stores atop the 200 it has closed since it filed for bankruptcy in January. [D4.]
The Justice Department is investigating possible anticompetitive practices in tissue pricing by paper companies. [D4.]
Signature Products said it would delay the manufacture of new armor-piercing rounds after criticism from police groups. [A16.]
 
The Economy
 Orange County, Calif., is asking top brokerage firms to devise ways for it to sell bonds to help pay its debts. [D2.]
The nation's oil supply has apparently been unaffected by tough new antipollution rules on oil tankers. [D4.]
 
Markets
 Stocks retreated, with the Standard & Poor's 500 index falling 1.61 points. Retailing issues continued to be weak. [D8.]
Treasury prices declined in thin trading. [D17.]
The dollar tumbled against the mark and other currencies on concerns over Mexico's financial crisis. [D18.]
Silver and gold prices rallied on the decline in the value of the dollar. Crude oil prices advanced. [D18.]
 
Today's Columns
 One image for two brands is the goal of "co-branding," a hot trend in marketing. In one such joint venture, Wavy Lay's potato chips carry the trademark of the Hidden Valley Ranch brand of salad dressing. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D1.]
Eastman Kodak's trade case against Fuji Photo Film of Japan helps to illustrate contradictions between antitrust and international trade laws, contradictions that Corporate America is discovering can be exploited at the expense of foreign competitors and American consumers. Peter Passell: Economic Scene. [D2.]
A patent issue has created uncertainty for some drug stocks. Glaxo says it will benefit from a patent provision under the new world trade pact, but analysts are not so sure. Market Place. [D8.]

HEADLINE: Bailout Plan For Mexico Wins Praise

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

 BODY:
Bankers and financial analysts gave a cautious welcome yesterday to reports that the United States was preparing a multibillion-dollar international aid package for Mexico. But they cautioned that any revival of confidence in Mexico's battered currency and economy would remain fragile for a long time to come.
"The aid should give the Mexicans time to show the new measures they are taking to support their devaluation are going to work," said James Colmenares, vice president for portfolio management at Morgan Stanley Asset Management. 
 The Clinton Administration is leading an effort to put together a rescue package for Mexico, according to people with knowledge of the plan. They would not give details about the size or the way it would work. But the Treasury has discussed some possibilities with investment bankers and others to test the sentiment in financial markets.
Mr. Colmenares and other bankers and Mexican analysts said they believed that the United States, with possible help from Canada, was preparing a $15 billion to $20 billion aid package to help Mexico repay billions of dollars of Mexican treasury bills held by foreigners. Those bills, known as tesobonos, fall due in the coming months and must be redeemed if investors decide to cash them in.
One approach could be a line of credit upon which Mexico could draw, effectively borrowing the money from countries participating in it, the bankers said.
A second, more complex, approach involves turning billions of the tesobonos into three-year notes, which would give the Mexican Government a longer time to pay them off. Tesobonos have maturities of three months to one year.
To make such three-year notes attractive to investors, they would offer an interest rate of about 12 percent to 15 percent, or about twice what is now paid on a three-year United States Treasury note. Yesterday, those notes were trading at a yield of 7.76 percent.
In addition, the notes, which would be paid off by the Mexicans, would also carry some kind of United States guarantee that would assure that the dollars would be available to pay both the interest and principal during the next three years.
President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon plans to outline his strategy for restoring international confidence in Mexico's economy in an address to the nation scheduled for Monday. Mr. Colmenares said he expected the Mexican leader to make "a very concrete statement" on the Government's plans for further sales of state-run companies, including the electric utility industry, to the public.
But he warned that there could be new problems when the 60-day wage freeze, to which unions agreed last week, expired. "Will the Government be able to hold the line on wages then?" he asked.
Mexico needs a new injection of emergency financial aid because billions of dollars of tesobonos will come due this year, many of them owned by foreigners, especially Americans.
With confidence in the peso so low, foreign holders of these bonds, whose interest and principal is calculated at the current value of the dollar, are expected to cash them in as they mature.
But the dollar reserves of the Mexican central bank, which stood at about $29 billion early in the year, are currently thought to be near $7 billion and far too low to meet the expected outflow.
Bankers do not agree precisely on the number of tesobonos that will fall due in the near future or on the number that are owned by foreigners. But Morgan Stanley Capital Management estimates that $29 billion will mature in the year ahead and believes that one-third of those, valued at $10.6 billion, are owned by foreigners.
Alfredo Viegas, Latin American strategist at Salomon Brothers, said an aid package in the $10 billion to $15 billion range "is the minimum that would be credible." He said if that could be done, he would expect the peso, which closed yesterday at 4.975 pesos to the dollar, to rally to "the low fours to the dollar." That would mean a peso would be valued at about 24 cents, compared with yesterday's close of 20 cents.
But Mr. Viegas was cautious about Mexico's chances of attracting a new inflow of foreign investment funds to help cover its large foreign trade deficit. "Mutual fund redemptions and inflows tend to lag bad news," he said, adding that investors might pull out of Mexican funds or at least stop putting new money into them early next year as a result of the losses this year. "Where is the new money coming from?" he asked.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Silver Prices Rise Sharply Amid Currency Gyrations

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Silver prices leapt yesterday as a sharp drop in the dollar set off buying of precious metals.
Analysts also attributed silver's gains to civil and economic problems in Mexico, the largest producer of silver, and to a seasonal tendency toward higher commodity prices. The Commodity Research Bureau index rose 0.65 point, it's sixth consecutive daily gain, to 236.84. 
 "I think what we're seeing here is a year-end rally in commodities in general and the metals are benefiting from it," said Peter Cardillo, research director for Westfalia Investments Inc. in New York.
Silver for January delivery rose 10.2 cents on the Commodity Exchange in New York, to $4.884 an ounce, the highest daily settlement for near-term deliveries since Dec. 1. The March contract rose 10.3 cents, to $4.93 an ounce.
February gold rose $2, to $385.30 an ounce, on the Comex. January platinum rose $3.10, to $417.80 an ounce, on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
The surge was silver's strongest one-day gain since Sept. 20.
The metals rose as the dollar tumbled against other world currencies on worries that Mexico's financial crisis would adversely affect the dollar and Treasury securities. There were reports that Mexico would try to increase a $6 billion credit line with the United States and use the borrowed money to buy pesos.
"Yesterday and the last couple of days, the markets have told us the U.S. dollar and the U.S. stock market were havens of safety," said Leonard Kaplan, vice president and chief trader of precious metals at the Imperial Bank in Los Angeles. "Today, the markets said, 'No, we didn't mean the dollar, we meant the German deutsche mark,' which is farther away from Mexico."
Silver futures also were pushed up by a "knee-jerk response" to civil tensions in Mexico, said George Milling-Stanley, an analyst at Lehman Brothers Inc.
Elsewhere, crude oil futures rose on the New York Mercantile Exchange, lifted by strong gains in heating oil on forecasts for colder weather this weekend in the Midwest and Northeast. February crude rose 15 cents, to $17.79 a barrel, and January home heating oil surged 0.86 cent, to 50.11 cents a gallon.

HEADLINE: Orange County Seeks Advice on Bond Sales

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Orange County, Calif., is asking some of the nation's top brokerage firms to devise ways for it to sell bonds to help pay its debts in the wake of its bankruptcy filing.
Salomon Brothers, the county's financial adviser, has sent a request for proposals to 17 municipal bond firms, asking them for plans to underwrite securities sales "in connection with a restructuring of the county's financial liabilities." 
 Orange County and an investment fund it managed for about 180 municipalities declared bankruptcy Dec. 6, after the fund lost more than $2 billion on risky investments that fell when interest rates rose. The fund's assets total about $5.4 billion.
Separately yesterday, Orange County bailed out two financially troubled Southern California cities that placed money in its investment fund. Santa Ana and Montebello each received about $14 million to cover emergency payments on their debts and prevent further defaults.
 Montebello, located in Los Angeles County, owes noteholders $25 million due on Dec. 30. Santa Ana, the Orange County seat, defaulted on Dec. 15 on a $5.24 million payment. Its bondholders will be paid on Jan. 3, an official said.
The Orange County board of supervisors is considering various plans to raise money to pay the county's debts, including the sale of the county's John Wayne Airport and other properties, budget cuts and securities sales.
A bond sale is not necessarily going to occur, Orange County officials said. The request for proposals "does not constitute a commitment on the part of the county to effect an offering," the three-page document said.
The plan is being drawn up "in the event the county wants to go to the bond market," said Sandra Sternberg, an Orange County spokeswoman. "There is no offering on the table. There is no date or time" even tentatively set for a sale.
There are precedents for bond-financed bailouts of municipalities.
When New York City teetered near insolvency in the mid-1970s, New York State legislators created the state Municipal Assistance Corporation, a bond agency that financed the city through its fiscal crisis.
Philadelphia also weathered a cash-flow crisis in the early 1990's with the help of a specially created state bond agency, the Pennsylvania Intergovernmental Cooperation Authority.
"Anything's possible," said David Herships, municipal bond analyst at Kemper Securities Inc. in Chicago. "As part of an overall plan it's feasible" if the county can identify sources of revenue that would back the bonds.
Orange County is probably just trying to determine whether bond firms are interested in taking on what is likely to be a daunting task, some municipal market participants said.
For the time being, the county's financial advisers are concentrating on liquidating the pool's investment holdings to protect the fund against further losses and to raise cash to pay lenders and investors. In the last two weeks, Orange County's investment pool raised $3.34 billion through the sale of securities, raising $1.81 billion for the pool and $1.53 billion to pay back lenders.
Orange County solicited a bond plan from some of the top securities firms. The 17 companies asked for proposals included Goldman, Sachs; Lehman Brothers; Smith Barney; CS First Boston; PaineWebber; Bear, Stearns; J. P. Morgan Securities; Morgan Stanley; Prudential Securities; BA Securities; A. G. Edwards & Sons; Dillon, Read; Stone & Youngberg; Merrill Lynch; Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette; Artemis Capital Group, and Pryor, McClendon, Counts.
The proposal asked underwriters to acknowledge that they would be paid fees only at completion of an underwriting. It also asked each firm to describe the services it would provide and to provide its qualifications, a list of personnel assigned to the underwriting and experience in such types of underwritings.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Declining Dollar Pushes Treasury Prices Lower

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities fell yesterday in thin trading, hurt by a sharp decline in the value of the dollar.
Prices of the longer maturities declined as some investors sold bonds and turned to the short end of the market to wait out current jitters. The investors were concerned that a falling dollar would discourage foreign investors from buying United States debt securities. The dollar was affected by worries about the possible impact of the financial crisis in Mexico. 
 The bond market views a weaker dollar as inflationary, said Jim Madelmayer, a vice president at S. G. Warburg & Company, because it means it will cost Americans more to import goods.
But traders noted that thinly traded markets almost always exaggerate price movements. Thin trading in the currency markets also exaggerated the dollar's decline.
The price of the 30-year bond fell 26/32 , to 967/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, rose to 7.83 percent, from 7.76 percent on Tuesday.
Short-maturity issues, particulary the three- and six-month bills, were the only maturities benefiting from year-end demand and continued flows of investment out of Mexico.
 Before yesterday's decline, Treasury securities were steady in light trading as the yield gap between a 2-year note and a 30-year bond narrowed to as little as 7 basis points -- the smallest since May 3, 1990. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
By late in the day, the gap had widened to 14 basis points. A narrower spread suggests rising investor expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise short-term interest rates enough to control inflation.
The market turns its attention today to the release of the Government's leading economic indicators for November and a bill auction.
The influence of the index of leading indicators, however, has diminished, James Madelmayer, a vice president at S. G. Warburg & Company said, because of the lag in reporting. "It used to be important; now, it's probably not even in the top 10 movers of importance to the marketplace," he said.
In other economic data that could move the market, new-home sales for November are scheduled to be released tomorrow.
The Treasury said yesterday that it would auction $14 billion of 16-day cash management bills today. The bills will be issued on Jan. 3, 1995, and mature on Jan. 19, 1995. Minimum bids will be in amounts of $1 million.
Trading in municipal bonds saw little change yesterday, as investors with only two sessions left before the year's end, focused on swapping bonds to record capital losses for tax purposes, traders said.
The average yield to maturity in the Bond Buyer's index of 40 actively traded municipal issues was unchanged yesterday, at 6.90 percent. Helping to buoy municipal prices were the actions of some securities firms that are buying bonds and building inventories, anticipating higher prices in January because issuance will not be as high over the next 30 days, The Bond Buyer reported yesterday.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Posts Broad Retreat In Side Effect of Peso's Crisis

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar tumbled in thin trading against the German mark and other leading currencies yesterday as Mexico's financial crisis roiled financial markets in the United States.
Steady for most of the day, the dollar plunged more than 3 pfennigs in early afternoon trading in New York. 
 "This has everything to do with the peso," said Laurence Hayward, a trader at Nationsbank of Texas in Houston.
The dollar fell as low as 1.5410 marks, its lowest level since Nov. 15, before recovering to 1.5450 marks in late New York trading yesterday, down from 1.5757 marks. It was the steepest one-day fall against the mark since July 11.
The dollar also slipped as low as 98.75 yen, its lowest level since Nov. 29, before recovering to 99.30 yen, from 100.32 yen on Tuesday. The dollar also fell to 1.3040 Swiss francs from 1.3295 francs on Tuesday. The British pound rose to $1.5700 from $1.5495.
Traders said the dollar fell quickly yesterday because trading in the final week of the year is typically much less active than usual. With so many people out of the market, there were fewer buyers for the dollars available, dealers and analysts said.
Meanwhile, the dollar fell 12.7 percent, to 4.9750 pesos from 5.700 pesos on Tuesday. That lessened the dollar's gain against the peso to 44 percent since the Mexican Government decided last week first to devalue its currency and then to let it trade freely against the dollar.
Speculation that the United States may do more to help support the peso helped bolster the Mexican currency, traders said.
The Canadian dollar also tumbled yesterday, falling as low as 71.14 United States cents, its lowest level since March 1986. In late New York trading, the Canadian currency settled at 71.16 United States cents, down from 71.41 United States cents on Tuesday.
The Canadian dollar continues to suffer from concern about Canada's large debt burden, the movement for secession in Quebec and the Bank of Canada's failure to match the magnitude of the interest rate increases made by the United States Federal Reserve.
"The whole region of North America has turned into a peso bloc," said Richard Koss, currency sales manager at Canadian Imperial Bank of Commerce in New York.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
From Kodak, two images of its competitive position.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
AS far as the Eastman Kodak Company is concerned:
(a) It's a big, bad world out there, one in which the giant American film company is engaged in a grueling duel with Fuji Photo Film for domination of the global market. 
 (b) The past era of monopoly is long gone, now that half a dozen other multinational corporations have made huge investments in the color film business.
"They can't have it both ways," says Lawrence White, an economist at the Stern School of Business at New York University.
Actually, maybe they can. In the last two years, notes Greg Rushford, publisher of a Washington-based newsletter called The Rushford Report, Kodak's lawyers have positioned themselves on both sides of this argument. What's more, they have won both times around.
To some, this only demonstrates that consistency is the hobgoblin of small minds. But, more to the point, it also reflects the contradictions between America's antitrust and international trade laws -- contradictions that Corporate America is discovering can be exploited at the expense of both foreign competitors and American consumers.
Start with the trade case. Kodak filed dumping charges against Fuji in 1993, contending that Fuji, the world's second-largest producer, was competing unfairly in the American market for photographic chemicals and paper. Kodak's experts, Jerry Hausman of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology and Paul Krugman of Stanford, hypothesized that Fuji was selling billions of dollars' worth of products below cost in the American market to discourage the no-longer-invincible Kodak from investing in the next generation of photo technology. Once Kodak slipped behind, they reasoned, Fuji would be able to reap the rewards of its commanding position by raising prices.
If such arguments sound familiar, go to the head of the class. Kodak repackaged the old case against so-called predatory pricing in the new language of strategic trade theory. And it worked: the International Trade Commission, a quasi-judicial Federal agency, ruled in October 1993 that Fuji was selling photo paper at less than fair value. Before the Commerce Department had a chance to calculate the penalty, Fuji agreed to raise prices.
What was good for Kodak was also good for American consumers -- or so Kodak suggested. According to the Kodak version, Fuji would have been willing to absorb huge losses in the short run to drive Kodak from segments of the film business and then reap monopoly profits.
But even as Kodak was warning that it was in danger of losing this battle of titans, it was explaining separately to Federal District Court Judge Michael Telesca in Rochester that Kodak should be released from two antitrust consent decrees because the world market for film was too competitive to be dominated by one company.
Kodak was hobbled by the courts earlier in the century in response to what its critics thought were attempts to monopolize the market for amateur film. In 1921, Kodak was barred from selling what are now called private-label brands -- the house brands sold in great volume by discount chains like Wal-Mart. And in 1954, it agreed to stop tying sales of unexposed film to film processing, thereby handicapping its capacity to dominate the processing business.
But Kodak argued -- and the judge agreed -- that the consent decrees were obsolete. Thanks to computer-based technology, color film can now be processed by thousands of independent shops. And while film in the bright yellow box may still command a premium price, public acceptance of high quality film from Fuji, Konica, Agfa and 3M virtually guarantees that the market will remain competitive, however aggressively any company tries to dominate it. In lifting the consent decrees, Judge Telesca cited a 1986 Federal court ruling that "predatory pricing schemes are rarely tried, and even more rarely successful."
Judge Telesca may well be right. The globalization of markets, the fickle nature of consumer preferences, the unpredictable path of technological change and the relative ease of raising capital all make predatory pricing a highly problematic strategy. But by the same token, they make Kodak's theory that Fuji was selling cheap today in order to sell dear tomorrow look rather dicey. What, after all, could Fuji hope to gain by losing scads of money if the prospects for successful predation later on were so remote?
The anti-dumping laws, says F. M. Scherer, an economist at the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard, are grounded on ideas that economists no longer take very seriously. But what in other circumstances might merely be cause for chuckles about the tangled webs that lawyers weave, Mr. Scherer views as deeply ironic. "Competition from Fuji," he explained, "is largely responsible for the rising quality and falling price of color photography."
Perhaps the best news for consumers, then, is that it took so long before Kodak discovered that investments in lawyers pay as well as investments in film technology.

HEADLINE: Mexico: Don't Panic Over the Peso

 BODY:
Mexico's peso crisis has obviously shaken Mexican officials and foreign investors alike and could damage Mexico's short-term economic prospects. But it does not reflect fundamental flaws in Mexico's newly liberalized economy. Moreover, President Ernesto Zedillo's economic ministers have responded to the challenge competently, avoiding premature efforts to peg a new exchange rate. The markets will eventually stabilize. Yesterday the peso rebounded some 15 percent.
Devaluations inspire temporary crises of confidence, and this one is no exception. It is likely to lead to slower growth, faster inflation and fewer imports from the U.S. A larger gap between U.S. and Mexican wages could tempt more Mexicans to cross the border seeking work. 
 Opponents of the North American Free Trade Agreement will be tempted to say they told you so. But without Nafta, these negative consequences might have been even more pronounced. Nafta also helped bring emergency financial support from the U.S., in the form of $6 billion in credits aimed at shoring up investor confidence. A far larger package is now reportedly being prepared.
It is up to Mr. Zedillo, however, to reassure poor Mexicans that after years of waiting for the rewards of economic liberalization to trickle down, they will not be put off again with promises. His task will be all the harder because restoring confidence abroad will require a degree of budgetary austerity at home that could force Mr. Zedillo to defer some of his planned new social spending for awhile.
But he need not retreat from his promises to open up Mexico's political system by disentangling the ruling party from the government and assuring more honest state and local elections. There are risks in liberalizing a semi-authoritarian political system at a time of disappointment. The risks of not opening up would be far graver.
Traditionally, outgoing Mexican administrations take responsibility for any devaluations during a transition period. But Mr. Zedillo's predecessor, Carlos Salinas, gambled that foreign investment would soon recover from the slowdown it experienced amid last year's political troubles and that no devaluation would be necessary. It was reasonable to bet that Mexico needed to woo investors with a period of exchange-rate stability and could afford the cost of growing trade deficits.
But those investors held back, and Mr. Zedillo had little choice but to let the peso fall. His Finance Minister has been criticized for denying the possibility of devaluation until the last moment, but that is standard practice everywhere. Now there are demands that the Government intervene to defend a new exchange rate, but that would be premature before the market begins to settle down on its own.
Mr. Zedillo, who won the ruling party's presidential nomination only after the original nominee was assassinated, and won the presidency after the fairest vote count in modern Mexican history, is being faulted in some quarters for not being a take-charge leader like his predecessor. Yet he could turn out to be the right man for these times.
He understands the economic imperatives of the moment and is right to resist calls to defend an indefensible fixed peso rate. He also seems to understand the political imperatives just as clearly and has committed his administration to long-overdue political reforms. He should remain confident, disregard panicky advice and maintain his sensible course.

HEADLINE: Time to Look at Baseball's Exemption

 BODY:
The owners of baseball's 28 major-league teams have declared an impasse in wage negotiations and imposed what their players dreaded most -- a cap on team payrolls. The players vow to continue their strike and seek legal remedies. The result is that baseball's wealthy owners and players have left the game in even sorrier shape than last year, when their failure to reach a new labor agreement destroyed the end of the regular season, the playoffs and the World Series.
This year there could be no games at all -- or at best games played by impostors. The two sides have a responsibility to resume talks. The owners may not want to, but two influential U.S. Senators -- Daniel Patrick Moynihan, Democrat of New York, and Orrin Hatch, Republican of Utah -- think they have hit upon the right stimulus. They will offer legislation ending the owners' cherished, 72-year-old exemption from antitrust laws. That exemption gives the owners uniquely broad powers over wages and work rules that, the Senators believe, dampen their incentive to compromise. 
 The idea deserves a hearing. Absent a rapid negotiated settlement, baseball's immediate future is dismal. The players have lodged an unfair-labor-practice charge with the National Labor Relations Board that, they hope, will eventually lead to a court injunction against the salary-cap plan. But such an injunction could trigger a lockout by the owners, who say they cannot afford to continue under the old economic rules.
With spring training less than two months away, the owners are already scrambling for replacement players ("Can you hit the curve ball? Yes? You're hired.") But will the replacements be major-league strikebreakers? Minor leaguers hungry for a shot at the big time? Retreads? In any case, they will not be the polished professionals whom people pay increasingly good money to see.
Peter Angelos of the Baltimore Orioles, a maverick owner who opposed the cap, predicts artistic disaster followed by financial disaster. "I looked in the phone book for replacement players and I couldn't find any," he said, suggesting that baseball's greatest need may soon be replacement fans.
It is this relentless destruction of the game that has now focused the attention of Mr. Moynihan and Mr. Hatch on the antitrust exemption. In labor-management struggles, the exemption has the effect of allowing the owners to act as a cartel, imposing industrywide salary limits while enjoying substantial immunity from any lawsuits the players might file. To Donald Fehr, the players' negotiator, that means the owners have little incentive to bargain seriously because, at the end of the day, they can do pretty much what they want.
Some critics want to revoke the exemption entirely, reasoning that no other professional sport is given such license. Others say the survival of the minor leagues depends, in part, on the power to control payrolls and transfer players permitted by the exemption; they would narrow the exemption but not eliminate it.
Either way, changing the exemption will not solve baseball's troubles. It would limit the owners' ability to impose a cap unilaterally. But it will not solve the problem that has bedeviled baseball ever since the players negotiated the right to move more freely from team to team. That problem is to find ways to alleviate the effects of a bidding system that has inflated payroll costs and put a few franchises in peril -- without at the same time destroying the players' right to sell their services on a relatively free market. That question can only be answered by the people whose egos and livelihoods are bound up in the game -- the players and owners.

HEADLINE: Proponent of Work for Welfare Is Among 9 Pataki Appointments

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER 

 BODY:
Governor-elect George E. Pataki yesterday named Westchester's Commissioner for Social Services to do for the state what she has done at home -- dramatically reduce the cost of welfare. But unable to persuade Commissioner Mary E. Glass to sign on for the long term, he agreed to limit her appointment to 90 days.
Aides to Mr. Pataki, who named eight other top members of his administration yesterday, said that even if Ms. Glass were unable to complete the task of revamping the Department of Social Services, he would have sent a strong signal that he intended to make good on a campaign pledge to change welfare. 
 In the last five years, Ms. Glass, 53, has overseen an initiative in which 60 percent of those on home relief, the welfare program for single adults, were dropped from the Westchester rolls because of a requirement that able-bodied people work and other efforts to cut down on costs. Mr. Pataki's spokeswoman said he wants Ms. Glass to duplicate the program throughout the state. Some counties already have similar programs in place.
"It's important to put forth that we are committed to workfare, not welfare," said Zenia Mucha, a spokeswoman for Mr. Pataki.
While some critics question the initiative's compassion, as well as its long-range ability to wean people from welfare, Mr. Pataki's aides estimated that it could save more than $450 million, or 1.3 percent of the state's general-fund budget of $34 billion. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has announced his intention to pursue a similar program.
"I think the American people and the people of New York are a generous people, if people are willing to help themselves," said Ms. Glass, who said she wants the state job only for three months because of greater loyalties to her county position. "If they are not, that's what makes the taxpayers resistant to programs."
Along with Ms. Glass, the appointments yesterday, while only a portion of the top jobs in the bureaucracy, began to reveal the contours of Mr. Pataki's government. The nine names included old friends, campaign compatriots like Bernadette Castro collecting chits from Mr. Pataki, and seasoned professionals in business or government.
And while some are steeped in the substance of their agencies, others, like Ms. Castro, who will become Commissioner of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation, seem to be novices in the area of government their agencies will oversee. Ms. Castro, 50, the furniture heir who ran unsuccessfully this year against Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, conceded that she had not seen many of the state's parks and, while she wanted to protect them and encourage greater use, she was not sure exactly what her mandate would be.
"You know, I have to visit them first, I really do," said Ms. Castro, who apparently survived her public complaints that Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, Mr. Pataki's political patron, had undercut her campaign. "There are a lot of treasures that New York has that I think have been kept a secret."
Mr. Pataki, who did not appear in public today, released a statement saying the appointments reflected his commitment to a new direction for government. "These appointments represent New York's best and brightest," he said. "Each is committed to sweeping, fundamental change and in bringing hope and opportunity to New York State."
The Governor-elect has now announced his choices for 14 of the state's top jobs, perhaps a quarter of the major appointments he must make. The salaries of those hired today -- all of whom except the Secretary of State require confirmation by the state Senate -- range from $90,832 to $95,635.
Of those appointed so far, 10 are men, 4 are women; 12 are white and 2 are black; 12 are Republicans, 1 is a Democrat and 1 is not registered in any party.
In addition to Ms. Castro, Mr. Pataki appointed two other people with close ties to the campaign. Joseph E. Holland, a Harlem lawyer and entrepreneur, was named Commissioner of Housing and Urban Renewal. Mr. Holland, 38, who is black, had been a Republican candidate for Attorney General but stepped aside at the party convention this year and was made Mr. Pataki's co-campaign chairman.
Mr. Pataki also named Alexander F. Treadwell, 48, a vice chairman of the New York State Republican Committee, as Secretary of State, a job that Gov. Mario M. Cuomo once held.
Mr. Pataki continued to tap old friends for jobs. He appointed Richard E. Jackson, a lifelong friend with whom he ran track and played basketball in Peekskill, Commissioner of the Department of Motor Vehicles. Mr. Jackson, 49, the Governor-elect's second black appointee, was elected Mayor of Peekskill after Mr. Pataki held the same position, and he has been a mathematics teacher since 1968. He does not have a background in transportation.
"His administrative background and experience qualify him to serve as Commissioner of the Department of Motor Vehicles," Mr. Pataki said.
Mr. Pataki retained one official appointed by Mr. Cuomo: James W. McMahon, 51, a career state trooper who is not registered in a party, will remain as Superintendent of the State Police, a job he was appointed to last April.
For three other appointments, Mr. Pataki chose experts from the private sector. He named Neil D. Levin, 40, a vice president and investment banker at Goldman, Sachs and Company, to be Superintendent of Banking. Mr. Levin, who has been with Goldman, Sachs since 1985, is a former aide to Mr. D'Amato, and the two have remained friends.
George C. Sinnott, a private arbitrator and member of the State Employment Relations Board, was named Commissioner of the State Department of Civil Service. And Michael H. Urbach, 51, an accountant in Albany, was named Commissioner of Taxation and Finance.
The Westchester welfare program, under Ms. Glass, had two main components. First was an intensive screening to insure that people on home relief were actually eligible for it, and that people who were sick or addicted to drugs or alcohol were shifted to a Federal benefits program. The second element was a requirement that able-bodied recipients work 20 hours a week -- usually performing menial chores like scrubbing graffiti or removing debris for local governments.
The result was that in Westchester, where there are roughly 6,000 people now on home relief, the program grew less than 1 percent over five years, saving an estimated $40 million, Ms. Glass said. In comparison, the growth in New York City over the same period was 49 percent.
But some skeptics say the work requirement has hampered people's ability to search for permanent jobs. It is also unclear how easily the program could be transferred immediately to New York State or New York City, because of the much greater numbers and difficulty in duplicating the computer-tracking system of recipients that Westchester has in place.
Ms. Glass said she believes such a rapid expansion could be accomplished during her short tenure, and that software developed in Westchester could be upgraded readily to handle the entire state.
"This is not hard," said Ms. Glass, who is unmarried and lives in Lake Peekskill. "I'm going to jump-start it because I have real-life experience with programs over a number of years."
Michael J. Dowling, who resigned yesterday as Mr. Cuomo's social services commissioner, called the Westchester program effective but said it was just one of many similar efforts the state has been trying to encourage among counties. While praising the savings, he said the program represents only a limited approach to welfare change. It does not, for example, affect the largest welfare program, Aid to Families with Dependent Children, which serves single people with children.

CORRECTION-DATE: December 30, 1994, Friday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about Governor-elect George E. Pataki's choice for Commissioner of Social Services referred incorrectly in some copies to his administration's plan for reductions in the home-relief welfare program. The changes would not require legislative approval. In some editions, the article also misstated the breakdown by sex of Mr. Pataki's 14 top appointments so far. The group includes 10 men and 4 women, not 11 and 3.

HEADLINE: Programs Seek to Stop Trouble Before It Starts

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
The subject for the day in Natalie Nash's sixth-grade class was "dissing" and the lesson was how children could keep their self-respect when challenged in real life.
Two boys in baggy sweat shirts were assigned to act out roles from a textbook on what happens when one child insults another. "Man, you're so dumb you don't know nothing," said Hector, assuming a practiced swagger. Faster than a magician, the second student, Angel, pretended to punch Hector in the face. 
 The class in the James G. Blaine Elementary School here, a hulking old edifice in a poor neighborhood, laughed knowingly. "That's right," Ms. Nash said. "That's what happens when you don't think about the consequences."
The class is part of a pioneering program with 5,000 students in a dozen Chicago-area schools that tries to prevent juvenile violence by teaching children -- and in some cases, their parents -- amicable ways to resolve disputes.
Financed by the National Institute of Mental Health, the Chicago project is only one of thousands of anti-violence programs that have sprung up in schools, clinics, churches and neighborhoods around the country in the past few years in response to the epidemic of violence among young people.
A growing number of experts agree that the approach favored by the public and politicians -- threatening criminals with longer and longer sentences -- has little impact on adolescents, especially those from poor, already stressed families. Instead, they say, the best hope lies with early intervention, working with children as young as 6 and their parents to head off antisocial behavior.
"Early aggressive behavior is the single best predictor of later violence and delinquency," said Patrick G. Tolan, an associate professor of psychiatry and psychology at the University of Illinois in Chicago, who is co-director of the school project here.
Dr. Tolan and others said that the most worrisome signs of trouble in young children were irritability and impulsiveness and a propensity to fight, lie, cheat, steal or be cruel to animals.
With such children, the earlier an older person intervenes, the better, said Gerald Patterson, a psychologist and co-founder of the Oregon Social Learning Center in Eugene, an agency widely respected for its work with antisocial youth. Treatment is most effective on children from the ages of 4 to 9, Dr. Patterson said, while those older than 11 are much more difficult to reach.
"After 14, it is extremely hard to make any changes," he said.
The most popular violence-prevention programs have been "conflict resolution" curriculums introduced in schools in the past few years, not only in Chicago, but also in hundreds of places like New York, Boston, the Los Angeles area, Tucson, Ariz., and Washington.
Whether these programs, some of them sold commercially in the form of textbooks and videos, have any real benefit remains a matter of debate, since few have been scientifically evaluated.
"I think what they can change is fighting in the cafeteria line," said Terrie Moffitt, a professor of psychology at the University of Wisconsin. "But that is very different from changing violence on the street corner."
But Dr. Deborah Prothrow-Stith, a professor at the Harvard School of Public Health and a developer of one of the most widely used curriculums, said it was wrong to dismiss the school programs.
"I think violence is a learned behavior and getting along is a skill, just like basketball," Dr. Prothrow-Stith said. "You need to practice."
The problem confronting any simple prevention strategy, like the school curriculums, said John Cuie, a professor of psychology at Duke University, is that violence has complex roots.
It can arise out of living in a poor neighborhood, where violent crime, unemployment and drugs are rampant. It can spring from growing up in a family where the parents are abusive or neglectful. Or it can grow out of the difficulties an impulsive child has on entering first grade, and facing the necessity of sitting still and learning to read.
"A successful intervention needs to attack all these factors at once," Professor Cuie said, and continue over many years.
He has developed one of the most highly regarded prevention programs, Families and Schools Together, or FAST Track, which is being used in 50 schools in Durham, N.C., Nashville, Seattle and rural western Pennsylvania. Professor Cuie's program combines training for parents, including visits to the homes by trained staff members, and special enrichment classes for selected students, the 10 percent seen as most troubled by their teachers and parents.
The very thing that puts these children at risk -- that they become angry easily, that they do not use language as effectively as other children -- makes them harder to help.
There are as yet no hard data on the results of the Duke and Chicago projects, both of which started in 1991, but those involved in the projects say they believe they are slowly making progress.
And experts take encouragement from recent findings by the Perry Preschool program run by the High/Scope Educational Research Foundation in Ypsilanti, Mich. The study was conducted in the 1960's among poor black children, combining parent training and academic enrichment. Now, rechecking the participants at age 27, a study has found that graduates of the program are five times less likely to have serious arrest records than those who were in a control group.
In the search for effective policies, experts are intrigued by an often-overlooked truth: the vast majority of children who grow up in the poorest, most blighted neighborhoods do not turn to violence.
Researchers say they are paying new attention to the importance of the parenting skills of those who rear young children -- skills that Mr. Patterson said were profoundly lacking among many of the 15- and 16-year-olds trying to rear children in difficult circumstances.
"They yell and scream or hit the child, even in our presence," Mr. Patterson said. "I'm afraid parenting is something that is being lost in America. Parenting is not some natural instinct. You really have to learn it at the foot of a master, your own parents, if you are lucky enough."
Mr. Patterson tries to teach more consistent parenting, helping mothers and fathers learn to monitor their children's activities and discipline them without corporal punishment.
"The magnitude of change in the child is correlated with the magnitude of improvement in parenting skills," Dr. Patterson said.
Other experts suggest that all teen-agers be taught parenting skills.
Joan McCord, a professor of criminal justice at Temple University in Philadelphia, suggests that teen-age girls learn mothering skills, by working with real babies, before they become pregnant.
Dr. Dorothy Otnow-Lewis, a professor of psychiatry at New York University School of Medicine, suggested that, to identify families that need help, children entering school should be routinely screened for evidence of abuse and neglect, just as they now are given physical exams.
One reason there is so little progress in curbing violence, despite all the public concern, is that little money is spent on research, said Alfred Blumstein, a criminologist at Carnegie Mellon University who calculates that the Federal budget for crime research is under $50 million a year, compared with $310 million for research into Alzheimer's disease, $1.3 billion for AIDS research and $2.1 billion for cancer.
Recent research has yielded some practical strategies. Studies by Lawrence Sherman, the chief criminologist for the Indianapolis Police Department, has found that 3 percent of the addresses in some cities account for more than half of the crimes. Based on these findings about what he calls "hot spots," police in some cities, including St. Petersburg, Fla., have freed teams of officers from answering routine 911 calls to allow them to focus on certain geographic areas.
Selected officers in St. Petersburg have also been assigned to work as mentors with adolescents living in "high risk" neighborhoods, spending time with them and their families.
Research by Mr. Sherman and others has led police departments in several cities, including New York, to begin pursuing illegal guns the way they long have drugs, using arrests as a starting point to build cases, looking for dealers and making deals for information. Although it is illegal in virtually every state for juveniles to possess handguns, the police have until recently done little to crack down on illegal guns.
Based on suggestions from criminologists, the Justice Department is preparing to finance the development of a gun detector that could identify hidden weapons at a distance, enabling the police to confiscate more firearms.
Politically, the most popular response to youth violence is the wave of tough new laws mandating longer sentences for youthful offenders and requiring such offenders to be tried in adult court. But few criminologists or law-enforcement authorities believe that the new laws will have much deterrence.
"The evidence is overwhelmingly clear that incarceration does not work as a deterrent, especially for kids, for whom life is an adventure," said Professor McCord. The United States has doubled its prison population over the last two decades, at the same time the rate of homicides among young people 15 to 19 years old jumped about 150 percent.
What might help, Professor McCord said, would be to change the policy in family courts in which judges often throw out a juvenile's first and second offenses.
"There ought to be penalties for first offenses, and serious consideration to restitution, like service kinds of work, so we don't send the wrong message," he said.
The most that can be said for the effect of the new laws is that they may keep dangerous children off the street longer, cutting down the number of crimes they commit, said Howard N. Snyder of the National Center for Juvenile Justice in Pittsburgh.

President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon of Mexico moved to take control of that nation's economic crisis by outlining an emergency plan that includes an international aid package totaling $10 billion to $15 billion. He also announced the resignation of the Finance Minister. D1
 
FRANCE FACES UP TO ALGERIA
For France, the successful storming of an airliner hijacked by Algerian terrorists has brought only temporary solace. France knows the war will go on, and, whether it likes it or not, it knows it is a party to the growing conflict between Islamic militants and Algeria's army-backed Government. A2
 
RUSSIANS SLOW DOWN IN CHECHNYA

SECTION: Section A; Page 24; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 3199 words

HEADLINE: What Can Be Done About the Scourge of Violence Among Juveniles? The Experts on Different Fronts of the Battlefield Discuss Strategies

 BODY:

Reuben Greenberg
Chief of police
Charleston, S.C.
During the daytime, according to state law, children from ages 6 to 17 have got to go to school. The truancy law gives us the right to stop them whether or not they're engaged in criminal activity. We pack them up and deliver them to school. We've delivered 3,200 kids back to school in the three years we've been doing this, some from as far as 50 miles away. 
 It is not an education program on our part; it's a crime-prevention program. We took six and a half hours that they had the opportunity to engage in criminal activity in, and took it away from them. If they are suspended or expelled, we can't take them to school, so we take them home.
After school, we don't have any leverage till midnight. Then we have what we call Operation Midnight. If we see children walking around the streets after midnight, we pick them up and return them to their parents. We don't take them to jail; we don't arrest them.
We call the parents and say, 'Do you know your son is out here?' If they say, 'Yes,' then it's O.K. Otherwise, we bring them home. And if they think a 12-year-old can be out at 4 in the morning, we call a social worker.
The overall daytime crime rate has dropped 27 percent. Not only have children stopped committing crimes during the day, they have also not been the victims of crime. Not a single kid in this town has been shot or shot at during school hours. No kids have been shot from midnight to 6 in the morning either, and only one kid was stabbed. We did this with no arrests, no going to court, not putting anyone in jail and no overtime.
We have also invented our own kind of gun-buyback program. We'll pay $100 to anyone who calls the police with detailed information about people who illegally carry guns in public places. Nobody needs a gun in school for protection.
Kids are the greatest snitches in the world to begin with, and when you give them $100 they become super snitches.
 
Elvin L. Martinez
Florida State Representative who chairs the Legislature's Criminal Justice Committee, and is a major sponsor of the state's tough juvenile justice law, passed in April
We changed public policy from decades where the 'best interests of the child' were the first thing considered, to a policy where the first consideration is public safety.
So we decided that now, any juvenile who commits three violent felonies can be waived into adult court at any age and must be waived at 14. It's three strikes and you're an adult.
We made punishments more severe. We tripled the amount of spaces we have for commitment programs. We created boot camps. We created a new level of offender -- maximum risk -- that can be kept until age 21 or for 36 months.
We authorized judges to take juveniles into custody for contempt of court. If a judge tells a truant to go back to school, and the truant does not go back, now he is disobeying the judge's order.
The law also does away with the confidentiality of records for violent crimes committed by juveniles. And we attack the gang problem with RICO. If a gang initiation involves stealing a car, then all the members of the gang can be charged with car-theft.
We made it more difficult for a judge to impose juvenile sanctions in an adult court. He must enter a written order and find certain things, like why he thinks the child exhibits hope. Otherwise he must treat the child as an adult who falls under the sentence guidelines and goes to jail.
We allow the court to have jurisdiction over the parent, or the caretaker. The court can find them in contempt and make them pay for their children's actions. It will call attention to the fact that they have responsibilities for their children. We will provide parenting classes if they need them.
The most dramatic thing is that children no longer will be able to believe that the system is a joke.
 
James Q. Wilson
Professor of management and public policy
University of California at Los Angeles
There are one million people in prison. We are not going to change them. We have boys on the streets; we can't change them. So we have to push the intervention back to where we know it is possible to make a difference: in childhood.
We certainly know the importance of disorganized families, of abusive parents, of parental criminality, of impulsive temperaments, of verbal deficits that lead to difficulty in school, of early drug use.
Not everybody who has these things is going to become criminal, but they are the best general predictors.
The crucial turning point is the third grade. It is not an iron law. There are a few late bloomers who turn to crime in their teens. But if kids are going to do badly in school, third grade is when it starts.
What I would do is consolidate fiscally all the current welfare programs -- A.F.D.C., Medicare, Medicaid, food stamps -- and aim them better at children to improve the life course of those children.
I would tell all teen-age mothers with children living in poor neighborhoods, white or black, that if they want to get Government subsidies they have to do one of several things. They have to move to a group shelter supervised by adults, where mothers are trained to be mothers. Drugs and alcohol won't be allowed. Or an alternative is to make available publicly funded boarding schools, with vouchers, where mothers can send their kids, away from the mean streets.
Suppose all the benefits that go to a 15-year-old with a child were taken out and endorsed to a group shelter. It might be possible that by pooling resources over 30 to 40 mothers per place you might be able to run a reasonably good shelter. The main goal in welfare reform ought to be saving the children, not necessarily making mothers work.
 
Vince Lane
President
Chicago Housing Authority. Grew up in a cold-water flat on the city's South Side.
You've got to get at the root causes -- people living in poverty, families losing their values, kids not being able to get or hold jobs, the loss of religious beliefs --so that now young people will do anything at any time regardless of the consequences.
What I am trying to do is undo one fundamental flaw, the way we deliver public housing for poor people, so that neighborhoods can function properly. Well-intentioned people set up a standard for poor people, giving them welfare and public housing and then pushing them all off to one side.
But you can't maintain a viable population where most of the people living in a place are out of work and dependent on welfare. We now have third and fourth generations in public housing waiting for welfare checks. I met a little girl who said she couldn't wait to get pregnant so she can get welfare and her own apartment, because she doesn't like living with her grandmother.
I would like to demolish 12,000 to 15,000 of our 40,000 public housing units in Chicago. I would transfer the $3 billion of assets of our public housing authority, to get the real cash flow from them, and build or acquire single family houses or condos. If people need money down, I will provide 25 percent down.
To get them there, I would enter into a pact with the Federal and local governments that created the problem of overconcentrated public housing and agree that we would never have two poor people living side by side. That would be low enough that home owners wouldn't have to worry about their values or schools, and it wouldn't create gangs.
There are now 150,000 people in Chicago in public housing. If you spread them over the nine million people in the city's metropolitan area, it would be no more than 1.5 percent per block. That would take us back to where we used to be traditionally, where we always had poor people, but they weren't so concentrated, and churches and community organizations could help them.
 
Mary Taylor Previte
Administrator
Camden County Youth Center, a pretrial juvenile detention center in Gloucester Township, N.J.
In 20 years in this business, I've discovered that the kids that are turned around in almost every case are attached or glued to one decent human being. It can be a family member, or a person in the neighborhood.
One of the causes for kids falling apart is that these children have no one paying attention to them. As a result, they have no memories, no rituals and no traditions. I asked one kid, 'Give me a memory, something your family does every year,' and she couldn't think of one. She was 16.
That may not sound like a big deal, but memories of a daddy building a snow fort become a road map for the future, when they have a family. In here, there is a lot of father hunger. I see it in how the kids try to model themselves after our officers, asking to look at pictures of their families. The kids say, on the street where I live, there ain't one house has a father. Grown-ups have abandoned kids.
And the level of anarchy is getting worse. I am now getting the children of the kids I had years ago. Grandmothers at 28.
So any answer has got to start with families, with people spending time with their children. When I was a little girl, I was locked up in a Japanese concentration camp and separated from my parents for three years. The reason I didn't crumble was that we had teachers who gave us a safe feeling.
You also have to give children comforting predictable rituals, so that they know that every day they will get up at a certain time and go to school at a fixed time. The problem now is kids are in chaos. They don't know what is going to happen. Violence is the response to not knowing what is going to happen next. They become self-protective. Emotionally their fists are up at every moment, ready to punch out someone. Only now it is guns. They are so severely damaged, they expect to die.
 
Judge Judith Sheindlin
Supervising judge
Manhattan Family Court
 We are spending substantial sums of money for the care of juveniles in placement facilities -- $70,000 to $80,000 a year apiece in New York State. But there is a recidivism rate of 75 to 86 percent, depending on whose statistics you believe. If you were in business and getting three-quarters of your product back, you'd soon be out of business. Taxpayers are not getting their money's worth.
I think by age 15 there is a small number of kids, who for whatever deprivations they have suffered, are cooked, as my grandmother would have said. They are gone. Whatever you do is not going to make a difference. They are functionally illiterate. They have a hopelessness they can't get over. So they have no empathy, no social morality. It is very hard in 10 months to inculcate those values and then send them back to the same dysfunctional society and say, 'Do your best.' You haven't given them anything.
I think the solution is to pare down the money we spend on those kids who are placed, which means you have spartan institutions. The residents in them get three meals a day, with set chores and schools, but that's it. You want exercise, go out in the yard and run. You have fewer staff. And you have made a statement to these bad kids, jail is not going to be a pleasant place.
With the money you save, you invest in the vast majority of kids who come from the same environments and are right on the cusp. Give them better schools, and after-school programs to help them get jobs. You need to say to those kids, we know you are not getting an adequate break, that you come from a dysfunctional family with parents on drugs, and we appreciate that you haven't yet crossed the line to criminal offending.
People will say, You are stigmatizing the bad kids. Well, if you have 36 kids in a classroom and 4 are throwing chairs at the teacher, you have to excise them. Otherwise all will be lost. This is a war.
 
Dr. Carl Bell
Director
Community Mental Health Council on Chicago's South Side. A former gang member who became a psychiatrist.
The mistake people make is that they do this broad-brush sweeping thing with poor people. They damn them. They talk about lost generations of black children. Give me a break. There are so many efforts to save kids it is not funny. I constantly have uncles, sisters, cousins bringing kids in, saying they want to save this child. Their mother is on coke. There is a safety net in the black community. That's why 95 percent of the children who grow up in poverty don't become bad.
We need to be clear that most kids who carry guns do it to relieve a traumatic sense of helplessness. They turn to guns as a solution to being victims of violence, the Batman syndrome. Unfortunately, we've got many studies about the impact of violence on television, but very few about the impact of real-life violence.
The issue is, how do you take kids who have been victimized by violence and turn them into good guys?
Because studies show that children who are not wanted by their parents catch hell, I would prevent women from having unwanted children. Offer them birth control and education about how children turn out. An unwanted child is unrocked, unfed, unchanged, untaught, and as a result grows up deprived and out of control. They are sad, and as they get older, this gets covered over with anger. Then you have a kid with an attitude.
I would also do parent training. You need on-the-job training, staying with them. We use grandmothers. One proposal is, take every woman on welfare, select the ones that are good parents, and give them a job to help take care of a teen-age mother.
That's how Alcoholics Anonymous works. To do something about violence we are going to have to take an A.A. approach. Nice people are not going to to go into these places. Take a welfare mother and give her a role in life.
 
Clementine Barfield
Founder
Save Our Sons and Daughters, a national support group based in Detroit for parents of children killed by violence
There is a lot more that we need to be doing to stop gun violence. We know guns are made to kill, but when they kill children, people seem to be surprised.
We have finally made the legislators pay attention to us, but now the legislators have introduced all kinds of stupid laws that won't change anything. We have not halted the manufacture and importation of guns.
Right now, there is still a free market for guns coming into the community, and the only people being hurt are the people in the community.
We can't expect children to be what we are not, to be peaceful if we are not. If we are consistently putting guns at their disposal, children will get killed.
Young people today have been exposed to so much. A lot of times we have to replace the ugly images they have, of friends being killed, of abuse, of seeing neighborhoods run down.
We have to replace these images with the image that I can get better, that yes, there is a future for me, that my neighborhood can be better, my school can be better.
In Detroit, there is a group of men, Brothers on Patrol, who go into a housing project each morning to make sure the children have a safe corridor to go to school. Every morning and every afternoon, before and after school, they set up a safe corridor for kids to get to school and home. Some of them are fathers, some of them are just young men in the community.
We started our organization when we found that black people don't have support groups. We have always had the church and the family, but we haven't had organizations outside them to help us grieve.
 
James Garbarino
Director
Family Life Development Center at Cornell University
 There are three things I would do.
At the earliest age, we need the home health visitor. You are trying to minimize the earliest damage and neglect, which includes everything from poor prenatal maternal nutrition to preventing neglect by high risk parents. If these very young kids are hurt, you are building up the pool of high risk kids.
The key policy thing is the home health visitor ought to be thought of as part of the infrastructure of a community like potable water and sewerage. You can imagine that if we didn't have those things in place, we'd be faced with cholera victims support groups.
With elementary school children, I would try to identify and work with the traumatized kids, who have what amounts to post-traumatic stress disorder. This is important because repeated findings in research show that aggressive behavior at age 8 is predictive into adulthood unless you intervene. The same program that is 75 percent effective with elementary school age children is only 25 percent effective with adolescents.
Make sure when children are in school to get a health history. Some of these children have been hit in the head. They may need medical help. The chances are, they will also need programs that teach them discipline and how to calm themselves.
At the adolescent level, if I could do one single thing it would be to ensure that teen-agers are not in a high school bigger than 400 to 500 students. The research shows that small high schools create a situation that elicits more participation and have lower drop out rates. These effects are most pronounced for the most economically marginalized children. These marginal kids desperately need forces to draw them in to the school.
 
John J. DiIulio Jr.
Professor of politics and public affairs
Princeton University
 If you have enough decent family members, friends and go to church enough, you will probably be all right. This is what we call socialization.
On the other side, the other thing that keeps people from committing a crime is coercion. But there is no amount of prison or jail or probation or treating kids as adults that will do as much as proper socialization.
The situation we face now is new: we don't know how to rebuild families. It is one thing to talk about replacing infrastructure, roads and bridges, or even political institutions, but what can't be rebuilt from the ground up are these incredibly intricate family networks.
I think the only practical answer is pretty simple. You have about one-half of 1 percent of children at any given time who are severely abused and neglected, kids sitting unchanged in dirty diapers, who are being beaten, who witness or participate in drug deals. By the time they are 5 or older they will have multiple contacts with government agencies -- social service agencies, the police, the courts.
In all this fog of debate about welfare and orphanages, you have to remove these kids, under existing law, say after the second time they are seen by an agency.
The best way I know is to think about creating a black urban kibbutz. They should be black-run and black-staffed, with people from the neighborhood who have led good, stable lives. These are not orphanages and not boarding schools. The children will know who their parents are.
Ask yourself the following question: Suddenly one of your loved ones is transported to the inner city. What are you going to call for? More cops? More prisons? You might. But I know what you would call for in a second. You would want to get the child out of there and to a place where there are decent loving adults around.

HEADLINE: Japanese Less Confident of Economic Future, Poll Finds

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

 BODY:
The Japanese have become less confident that their country will dominate the world economy in the next century, and Americans have grown more certain that they can meet Japanese competition, a new poll shows.
In the survey, conducted early this month in both countries by The New York Times, CBS News and the Tokyo Broadcasting System, only 25 percent of Japanese respondents said they believed their country would be the world's premier economic power over the long term. Three years ago, 53 percent of the Japanese expected Japan to emerge as the most important economic superpower. 
 Now, the survey shows, there is far more awareness in Japan about the economic rise of China -- traditionally Japan's biggest competitor for influence in Asia -- than there is among Americans. A quarter of all Japanese identified China as the coming dominant economy in the world; in the United States, only 4 percent cited China.
The survey of 1,337 Japanese and 1,147 Americans showed a marked improvement in the sour view each country took of the other only 18 months ago, with more Japanese and Americans describing relations between their two countries as "friendly." But the poll also indicated a growing suspicion on the part of the Japanese that the United States is no longer a reliable military ally.
For the first time since 1990, when the question was first asked, less than half the Japanese -- 47 percent -- expressed confidence that Washington was an ally they could depend upon. And for the first time a majority of Japanese -- 53 percent -- said they thought Japan should consider amending the section of their American-written Constitution that has prevented the country from creating a military force able to project power beyond its own shores. In 1993, only 42 percent in Japan said such a change should be considered.
Each poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 3 percentage points.
"This is a dramatic change from the past," said Richard Samuels, a political scientist at the Massachusetts Institution of Technology and the author of a recent study of Japan's postwar military policy. "Fifteen years ago, you could not talk about a change in the Constitution."
The future of the alliance is certain to be a major theme in a meeting in Washington on Jan. 11 between President Clinton and Japan's first Socialist Prime Minister, Tomiichi Murayama. State Department officials have already made clear that they hope to steer the discussions away from the trade disputes that usually dominate such sessions and to focus on other aspects that, in the words of one senior State Department official, "have clearly been neglected in recent years."
A changing agenda may fit the national mood. For the first time since the question was first asked in 1985, a majority of Americans -- 51 percent -- judged the overall outcome of trade with Japan to be good for the United States. In 1985, at Detroit's low-point in manufacturing quality, only half of all Americans felt that way. But among Japanese, 82 percent said that Japan's cars are a better value.
Japanese also believe overwhelmingly -- 83 percent -- that their blue-collar workers still work harder than Americans, a figure that has barely budged since the question was first asked in 1985. A majority of Japanese and a plurality of Americans also said they still believe Japan makes consumer products that are technologically more advanced than American-made goods.
