HEADLINE: Mexican President Outlines Rescue Plan

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Dec. 29

 BODY:
President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon moved to take control of Mexico's economic crisis today by outlining an emergency plan to stabilize the economy and announcing the resignation of the Finance Minister who oversaw the devaluation of the peso that led to the collapse of the Government's economic strategy.
Breaking a public silence that had lasted for five days and confounded investors, Mr. Zedillo told national radio and television audiences of a plan "in which everyone will have to do their part and no one will be exempt from sacrifice." 
 His Finance Minister, Jaime Serra Puche, had offered to resign several times in the face of criticism from both Mexicans and foreign investors who had lost billions of dollars in the peso's fall. Today, the resignation was accepted.
Mr. Zedillo, who was inaugurated as President four weeks ago, announced that Mr. Serra would be replaced by Guillermo Ortiz Martinez, 46, a Government economist favored by New York investors. As under secretary of finance in the previous administration, Mr. Ortiz tried last fall to persuade President Carlos Salinas de Gortari to devalue the peso in an orderly fashion. His proposal was rejected.
Details of Mr. Zedillo's plan will not be released until a few hours before he gives another speech to the nation Monday night. But several key advisers made clear in interviews today that the new strategy would require severe concessions from labor, deep cuts in public spending and a temporary slowdown of economic growth -- all in an attempt to cut by more than half the $30 billion trade imbalance that lies at the core of Mexico's crisis.
The ambitious plan to rebuild investor confidence, includes an international aid package totaling $10 billion to $15 billion in loans from the United States, Canada, the European central banks and several private American banks, including Citibank.
The money, which includes a credit line of $6 billion already authorized by the United States, will be used to go into the currency markets and buy pesos to support the currency's value and to help Mexico meet obligations on $5 billion in short-term bonds that fall due next month.
In Washington, Congressional experts who also insisted on anonymity said that rather than ask a Republican Congress for money to lend to Mexico, the Treasury Department was likely to use its Exchange Stabilization Fund. The little-known fund, intended for use at the Treasury's discretion in exchange crises, held $20.4 billion as of Sept. 30, a recent Federal Reserve publication said.
During an interview today with news-agency reporters, President Clinton implied that the United States would help Mexico. When asked about the Mexican currency crisis, he replied: "It is something that I take seriously, and we are looking at it very seriously. We're talking to the Mexican Government about what we can do."
The President continued, "I would have to say that if you look where Mexico is today, compared to where they were several years ago, they have made a serious commitment to economic reform, to social reform, to cultural reform and to political reform." Mr. Clinton then added, "I would like to see that commitment rewarded, obviously, in so far as it can be in having those folks build a stable and growing economy."
Most details of the international economic package, parts of which became known Wednesday, have been settled with the help of Treasury officials, Mexican officials said. But the domestic section dealing with wages and inflation controls will not be settled until Monday, when business and labor leaders are expected to reach a politically viable consensus with the Government on its plan to stabilize the economy.
Since the peso was devalued and a day later allowed to float freely against the dollar, it has lost about 30 percent of its value. Today, the peso closed at 4.875 to the dollar, up modestly from 4.975 on Wednesday.
The Mexican stock market also rose sharply today, closing at 2,411, an increase of 3.14 percent over Wednesday.
Mexican officials said they expected the currency to settle somewhere between 4 and 4.5 pesos to the dollar. That rate would then become the basis for a new exchange policy. Canada has a similar plan that pegs the value of the Canadian dollar to what is called a monetary conditions index. Under such a plan, the value of the peso would be linked to short-term interest rates and other important indicators, so that as rates rise, the peso weakens and vice versa.
Lawrence B. Goodman, vice president for emerging-markets research at Salomon Brothers, said that such an exchange policy was considered experimental and had never been used in a nation as big as Mexico -- with a population estimated above 92 million. He said it would be more realistic to see this combined with a more traditional approach that used an index tied to the currency of a nation's primary trading partners, such as was done in Chile and Israel.
Either way, the Mexican Government is likely to settle on a policy that gives it far more flexibility. Before the decision on Dec. 21 to let the peso float, Mexico had relied on a trading band that very slowly dropped the band's lower limit. By maintaining the band, experts say, Mexico overpriced its exports and invited a flood of imported products.
A Mexican finance official involved in the new stabilization plan said the old exchange system had to be abandoned because "it was the variable in the Government's economic strategy that blew up."
Economists say the challenge for Mexico now is to maintain the price advantages that come with 30 percent-plus devaluation, without setting off inflation. The Government hopes that the higher import prices and lower real wages caused by the devaluation will suppress demands for imports, and thereby drop the country's trade deficit from $30 billion to about $14 billion or $15 billion.
Officials said the Government would also reduce public spending by 2 or 3 percent of the gross domestic product. It will restrict economic-development loans and public financing that had been used to stimulate the economy and will accelerate the privatization of Government-owned ports, petrochemcial plants and power-generating stations.
The elements of the international rescue plan include a swap line of about $6 billion from the Bank for International Settlements, which is owned by the central banks of Europe, and a smaller amount from American commercial banks.
Contrary to rumors, a Mexican official said, the International Monetary Fund had not been asked to provide any big loans. Rather, he said, Mexico had sought the fund's approval of the stabilization plan to bolster investors' confidence.
The success of the plan, the official said, rested on the Government's ability to control inflation. Economists in the United States have estimated that inflation in Mexico will rise to 20 percent next year, from about 9 percent this year. But Mexican finance officials today projected the rate closer to 15 percent, largely as a result of the recent crisis.
It is not clear at the moment what role Mexican banks will play in extricating the country from the crisis or whether the Government will encourage other foreign banks, including American, to become more active. The Government in October authorized 18 foreign banks to operate in Mexico.
Throughout the day, business, labor and Government leaders met privately to negotiate final details of the stabilization plan. In the last seven years, a pact among those forces has been the primary tool for controlling inflation, even though it often resulted in suppressing the real wages of workers, who have less buying power now than in 1980.
The Government needs the leaders of the unions and of the peasants' movement to accept pay increases substantially below the inflation rate, further reducing their purchasing power.
Mr. Zedillo, in his address today, conceded that his administration had "underestimated the problem" of the peso's fall, but now wanted to convert "a critical situation into an opportunity for growth."
He said the emergency measures would last only until the economy was back on track. An official estimated that could take as long as eight months.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
 Mexico's President, breaking silence, outlined a plan to stabilize the economy. The plan, which depends on a political consensus of labor, peasants, business and the Government, requires deep cuts in spending and international aid of as much as $15 billion. Some details of the plan were disclosed as President Zedillo announced the resignation of his Finance Minister. [Page D1.]
Once the best and brightest of Mexico's technocrats, Mr. Serra became an early casualty of the devaluation fiasco. [D2.]
 
Companies
 Viacom is close to a sale of its cable systems to a group that includes Tele-Communications for more than $2 billion, people close to the negotiations said. [D1.]
The Pentagon endorsed the merger of Martin Marietta and Lockheed. The $10 billion deal still awaits F.T.C. clearance. [D4.]
Unisys plans to eliminate 4,000 jobs, or 8.5 percent of its worldwide work force, in a large cost-cutting effort. [D4.]
M-G-M movie theaters in Europe were put up for sale by Credit Lyonnais, which is trying to sell $3.5 billion of assets. [D4.]
International Lease Finance placed firm orders for 30 new passenger jets with Airbus Industrie. [D4.]
Spectrum Information Technologies ended a lengthy search for a new chief executive, naming a consultant to the post. [D5.]
 
The Economy
 Orange County, Calif., plans to dip into a special reserve fund to repay some bonds to conserve its limited cash. [D2.]
The Commerce Department's index of leading indicators rose three-tenths of 1 percent in November. [D16.]
 
Markets
 Stocks ended mixed. The Dow Jones industrial average fell 6.06 points, while the Nasdaq composite index advanced 7.07 points, lifted by gains in technology issues. [D6.]
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed. [D16.]
The dollar recovered slightly against most leading currencies in light trading. The Canadian dollar continued to weaken. [D15.]
Copper prices surged on signs of increased demand in Asia. [D15.]

 Investors are sticking with stock mutual funds, despite a growing nervousness about the market. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]

HEADLINE: Haiti Bids au Revoir to Francophilia, Says Yo to G.I. Joe

BYLINE: by LARRY ROHTER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Dec. 23

 BODY:
American soldiers are still patrolling the streets, but now they are being joined by American advisers to Government ministries, American aid workers and American business executives. Teen-age Haitians from wealthy families, eager to appear trendy, now drive around with their car radios tuned to the American armed forces station that broadcasts here.
For a nation whose language is French-based and which has traditionally identified itself with France and French culture, three months of living under an American military presence has brought about a basic shift in attitude. 
 Though the country's Francophile elite are resisting, Haiti is rapidly slipping out of the French orbit and looking more than ever to the United States as its main foreign model and patron, many Haitians and foreign diplomats say.
"One of the results of the Haitian crisis of the last three years is that France and French culture are going to take a back seat, and their role is going to be diminished here," said Lionel Delatour, a Haitian business consultant and former diplomat. "I think the momentum is unstoppable, and I think it's a healthy development for this country."
An American official, speaking on condition he not be identified, was even more categorical. "Sept. 19 was a death knell for French influence in this country," he asserted, referring to the date 20,000 American troops began landing here to restore Haiti's exiled President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide. "They basically ceded the territory to us, and now they are steamed about it."
As a result of the intervention, the United States has thousands of soldiers here, has pledged more than $200 million in foreign aid, and maintains an embassy with more than 75 diplomats. France is represented by 100 gendarmes and an aid package of about $60 million, and the French Embassy has a small staff headed by a charge d'affaires who says "the vision of Haiti as a battlefield" between the United States and France is "pure rubbish."
"We are not here in order to have a competition with the Americans or the Canadians," the French official, Philippe Selz, said in an interview. "We are here to help the Haitians get out of the mess they have been in for so many years."
But many Haitians argue otherwise. The French "these days are working double time to reconquer their lost terrain, first by multiplying their missions to Haiti and then by taking the initiative to push for the replacement of American forces by those of the United Nations," a front-page editorial in the daily Le Nouvelliste said recently.
Diplomats, too, say they have detected signs of a French effort, which they regard as futile, to regain the dominance they once enjoyed. "There is room for only one great power here, and everyone except the French recognizes who that is," said a representative of one Western Hemisphere nation.
Haiti is the poorest nation in the Americas, and the international effort to rebuild the country is expected to bring a foreign aid flow of well over a billion dollars, with the nations most friendly to Haiti expected to win the bulk of the lucrative contracts. In addition, the Haitian middle and upper classes are avid travelers and consumers, and spend large sums abroad in the countries they visit.
Since President Aristide returned to power in mid-October, France has sent its Minister of Cooperation, a parliamentary delegation and cultural groups on official visits. They all stressed the historical, linguistic and cultural ties between France and Haiti. French companies have also been active, hoping to snare Government contracts and other business opportunities that are part of the reconstruction of the shattered economy.
But that effort has been overshadowed by the American presence in the streets of the capital and in the provinces. "You can send ministers here every week," said Mr. Delatour, who studied in the United States. "But what the average Haitian sees is a man in green uniform, and he is not French."
Even before the troops arrived, American culture had been gradually displacing many French traditions and attitudes here over the last 15 years. As many as a million Haitians live in the United States, compared with 40,000 in France, and more Haitians study at American universities or visit Miami and New York on business and shopping trips than go to France.
As a result, a growing number of Haitians, the young in particular, have acquired a taste for things American. Denzel Washington and Eddie Murphy have replaced Alain Delon and Jean-Paul Belmondo as movie idols, the Chicago Bulls appear to have a larger following than the Marseilles Football Club, and rap has largely pushed aside French popular music.
The deployment of American troops has also speeded the process, especially among the vast majority of ordinary Haitians too poor to travel abroad. Having so many American soldiers, who bring American habits and entertainments with them, so close at hand offers those Haitians "a new insight into our culture that they have never witnessed before," one United States official explained.
The presence of many black American soldiers, including several hundred who are of Haitian descent and speak Creole, seems to have particularly impressed the average Haitian. Adults and children alike flock around them with questions about American life and culture, and some Haitians say they have been inspired by the sight of black officers commanding white, Hispanic and Asian-American soldiers.
"It's a good thing, a revolution for our country, because now we see that Haitians can be the same as Americans," Francois Eddy, a 24-year-old student, said as he observed an American military encampment near the main airport. "I suppose that could also be possible with the French, but the Americans seem to have more warmth and the French to be more hard-headed."
Among Haitian intellectuals there is also a sense of gratitude at the role that black American political leaders like Randall Robinson, the Rev. Jesse Jackson and members of the Black Congressional Caucus played in liberating Haiti from military rule. That has fostered a greater sense of identification with the United States and its values, they say.
But some students of Haitian culture and history argue that it would be premature to write off the French, who were Haiti's colonial masters until the country achieved independence in 1804. The United States, they point out, occupied Haiti from 1915 to 1934 and left little influence behind, while France's legacy still permeates daily life.
"The French have one advantage over you Americans, which is that they have programmed the Haitian people," Georges Michel, a historian who has written extensively on the American and French roles here, said. "They can enter the password at any time and go into our memory -- the language and the mentality. You can never do that."
But this time the influx of Americans occurs as the dominance of French culture and language, and of the predominantly light-skinned elite who are its most ardent advocates, is already being challenged by Creole, the language of the majority of Haitians. In that decades-old cultural struggle, partisans of Creole tended to regard the American language and culture as tools that can be used to weaken the French influence they consider baleful.
The desire to embrace and emphasize Haiti's Creole roots and language has been particularly strong among members of the Lavalas political movement, which swept President Aristide into office in elections held four years ago this month. Many of the Francophiles, on the other hand, supported the military coup that overthrew him in September 1991, and have seen their prestige evaporate with his return.
Last month, for example, Haiti's Minister of Culture, Jean-Claude Bajeux, said he intended to push for the "Haitianization" of the country's culture, which implies less emphasis on French elements. And unlike previous presidents, President Aristide prefers to make his official declarations in Creole, not French, and has even injected English into some recent speeches.
"In the past, Aristide appeared not to be in favor of French," Mr. Selz, the French charge d'affaires, acknowledged. "He had roots among the people and noticed who their oppressors were: those who spoke French and despised Creole. But I think that tendency was stronger in the past than now."
Haitians need to be encouraged to see French as "an asset, not a liability," Mr. Selz said. "We don't want Haiti to go back to colonial times. But this rivalry approach is not a good one. It's wrong for Haiti, and not good for either you Americans or for us. Haiti can only lose time and energy if it goes in that direction."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The presence of United States soldiers has paved the way for a cultural revolution in Haiti in which French influence has given way to American. (Cindy Karp for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Computer Use Indictment Is Thrown Out

BYLINE: By PETER H. LEWIS 

 BODY:
Declining to prosecute a computer-age crime with telegraph-era law, a Federal judge in Boston yesterday dismissed an indictment against a student at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who had been accused of operating a computer system used for distributing pirated software.
The student, David LaMacchia, a 21-year-old electrical engineering and computer science major from Rockville, Md., was indicted in April for using two of the university's computers as secret electronic bulletin boards, where computer users linked to the Internet could trade illegally copied software. 
 Prosecutors said that while Mr. LaMacchia apparently did not profit from the trading, his operation was one of the largest hubs in a worldwide electronic black market that trades billions of dollars worth of illegally copied software each year.
Judge Richard Stearns of Federal District Court ruled that while Mr. LaMacchia's conduct might have been "heedlessly irresponsible" and perhaps in violation of civil copyright laws, he could not be prosecuted under Federal wire fraud felony statutes without making criminal "the conduct of not only persons like LaMacchia, but also the myriad of home computer users who succumb to the temptation to copy even a single software program for private use."
"It is not clear that making criminals of a large number of consumers of computer software is a result that even the software industry would consider desirable," the judge wrote.
Tens of millions of personal computer users routinely make copies of software programs, often for legitimate reasons like emergency backups or transferring programs to new machines. Software industry associations contend, however, that illegal copying of software deprives software companies of as much as $13.8 billion in potential revenue each year.
Mr. LaMacchia was accused of operating the software repositories, known on the Internet as Cynosure, from November 1993 until the university shut them down in January 1994. Donald Stern, the United States Attorney in Boston, said more than $1 million worth of illegally copied software, including popular games and business programs, had passed through the system during the few weeks it was in operation.
Mr. LaMacchia, who the authorities said used the pseudonyms Grimjack and John Gaunt, was not accused of using the software himself or of profiting from the trading. Rather, Grimjack orchestrated the flow of software in and out of the systems and admonished its many anonymous users to keep the site's location on the Internet a secret.
Judge Stearns, saying "this case presents the issue of whether new wine can be poured into old bottles," suggested that it was up to Congress to draft new laws to deal with copyright issues in the computer software era. "What the Government is seeking to do is to punish conduct that reasonable people might agree deserves the sanctions of the criminal law." But, he concluded, the Government had erred in trying to use the criminal penalties of the wire fraud statutes against what was, at most, a civil violation of copyright laws.
Judge Stearns used unusually strong language to express his disapproval of Mr. LaMacchia's actions, writing, "If the indictment is to be believed, one might at best describe his actions as heedlessly irresponsible, and at worst as nihilistic, self-indulgent, and lacking in any fundamental sense of values."
Mr. Stern, the United States Attorney, said prosecutors had not yet decided whether to appeal the ruling.
"I intend to raise with the Department of Justice whether it should file legislation explicitly dealing with willful, multiple infringements of copyrighted software to remove any uncertainties," Mr. Stern said.
Harvey A. Silverglate, Mr. LaMacchia's lawyer, noted that his client was not accused of trying to profit from the trading in illegal software, and said the charges did not constitute a criminal violation of existing copyright law.
"So the Government has taken this old Federal wire fraud law, which predates computer software, and has claimed they can recast this copyright case as a wire fraud case and prosecute it as a five-year prison felony," Mr. Silverglate said. "If the court allows prosecutions of noncriminal activity under these circumstances, it sends a chill down the spine of anyone operating a computerized bulletin board system."

HEADLINE: Cuomo Names 3 Supporters to State Board

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ALBANY, Dec. 29

 BODY:
In one of his final acts as Governor, Mario M. Cuomo today appointed three longtime officials in his administration to jobs on the state's Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, giving them job security for between two and four years and salaries of more than $68,000.
The appointments do not require confirmation by the State Senate and cannot be rescinded by Governor-elect George E. Pataki, who takes office on Sunday. 
 One of the appointees is Robert B. Adams, Mr. Cuomo's Commissioner of the Office of General Services. Another is Allen Cappelli, who took a leave this year from his job as a top official in the state Insurance Department to run get-out-the-vote operations for Mr. Cuomo's unsuccessful re-election campaign. The third appointee is Kenneth M. Mackintosh, an administrator at the state's School of Public Health in Albany and the former deputy of Mr. Cuomo's appointments office, the clearinghouse for gubernatorial patronage.
Mr. Adams, whose term will expire in 1996, and Mr. Mackintosh, whose term will expire in 1997, will receive annual salaries of $68,330 for the full-time positions. Mr. Cappelli, whose term will expire in 1998, will receive $76,421 because Mr. Cuomo named him chairman of the board. The five-member board hears appeals of unemployment insurance claims that have been denied by the state Labor Department.
The vacancies on the board have existed for between two and four years, with the positions filled by their previous occupants in a holdover capacity. Unlike with many gubernatorial appointments to boards and commissions, Mr. Cuomo did not announce today's actions in a news release. His decision to make the appointments in the waning days of his administration, and to attempt to do so quietly, provoked outrage from Pataki transition officials.
"This is politics as usual," said Mr. Pataki's spokeswoman, Zenia Mucha, appropriating the Cuomo campaign's favorite charge against Mr. Pataki. "It's an outrage and it should be stopped. It's particularly ironic that Mario Cuomo would choose the unemployment appeals board to reward political operatives, given New York's current economic condition."
After the election, Mr. Pataki asked Mr. Cuomo to freeze hiring and promotions during the remainder of his tenure to help the incoming administration close a large budget gap. Mr. Cuomo declined to impose a freeze, but said he would limit hiring as much as possible. He also said, after meeting with Mr. Pataki on Dec. 1, that Mr. Pataki "is the Governor right now as far as I'm concerned."
Despite that statement, the Cuomo administration has hired nearly 1,000 employees during the last six weeks, some of them seemingly far from essential to the health and safety of the state's clients and consumers. And Mr. Cuomo's spokesman, David Egner, explained Mr. Cuomo's three appointments today by saying that "the Governor was elected to serve his full four-year term and he would really be abdicating his responsibility if he were to cease functioning as Governor before his term has expired."
Mr. Egner added, "He felt that these were very talented individuals who were valued public servants and that the state would benefit by their services."

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

THE FIGHTING MAYOR OF NEW YORK CITY
One year after he was narrowly elected as the first Republican mayor of New York in more than two decades, Mayor Giuliani's combativeness and the zeal he retains from his days as a prosecutor have brought him a series of victories and, many leaders say, have touched a chord in a city longing for leadership. A1.
Mayor Giuliani unilaterally imposed nearly $800 million in spending cuts. A1.
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
GRADUATION RATE FALLS IN NEW YORK
The New York City schools in 1994 produced one of the lowest graduation rates in eight years, according to a report issued yesterday by Chancellor Ramon C. Cortines. But he said the system's dropout rate has been improving partly because more students who failed to graduate in four years are remaining in school. B3.
TIMES SQUARE NEWS ZIPPER IS SAVED
A British media conglomerate has stepped in to preserve the zipper, the moving illuminated bulletin board on the triangular building at 42d Street and Broadway. B3.
 
REGION
 
PATAKI NAMES HEALTH OFFICIAL
Dr. Barbara Ann DeBuono, 39, the health director for Rhode Island who began a program there to enroll poor people in managed-care programs, has been nominated by Governor-elect Pataki to be his health commissioner. B4.
PATAKI PREDICTS $100 MILLION DEFICIT
After meeting with his new budget director and with Cuomo administration budget officials, Governor-elect Pataki said he expects to have to close a deficit of at least $100 million before the state fiscal year ends on March 31. B4.
CUOMO APPOINTS SUPPORTERS TO BOARD
In one of his final acts as Governor, Mario M. Cuomo appointed three longtime officials in his administration to jobs on the state's Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board, giving them job security for between two and four years and salaries of more $68,000. A Pataki spokeswoman called the appointments "politics as usual." B4.
WHISTLEBLOWER RECEIVES $4 MILLION
George Denoncourt, a whistleblower who quit a $470-a-week job as a New York State social services trainee four years ago and accused his department of misusing Federal funds, has won more than $4 million as part of a settlement in which the state has agreed to pay the Federal Government $27 million. B5.
VIOLATIONS SUSPECTED IN DEADLY FIRE
Officials investigating a fire in Orange, N.J., that killed nine members of a Haitian immigrant family said there appeared to have been zoning and safety violations in the house. B5.
A NEW MISSION ON THE MEAN STREETS
Two years ago, the Rev. Jim Watson was executive director of a mission in Fairmont, W. Va., that ran a soup kitchen, a 100-bed shelter and a 111-acre children's camp. But now Mr. Watson, 41, a Baptist minister, is working on the streets of Bridgeport, running a shelter and feeding the homeless in the most dangerous sections of Connecticut's largest city. B5.

HEADLINE: Orange County to Draw On Reserve for Payment

BYLINE: By LESLIE WAYNE, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Dec. 29

 BODY:
In a sign of further belt-tightening, officials of Orange County, Calif., said today that the bankrupt county would dip into a special reserve fund to paying interest to bondholders, rather than cash on hand.
The move, intended to conserve the county's limited cash, is also aimed at assuring Wall Street that the county will maintain a tight grip on its financial resources and continue to meet its debt payments. 
 The action is intended to save the county from spending $53.4 million to repay short-term tax and revenue anticipation notes due this month and next. The money to pay bondholders will now come from reserves that were established when the bonds were first issued to serve as an emergency fund for just such circumstances.
Gaddi H. Vasquez, the new chairman of the Orange County Board of Supervisors, said the move would "allow us to keep open our lines to the financial markets, while enabling the county to conserve cash for the immediate operating needs." The option the county is trying to avoid is an outright default on the bonds, an action that many fear could cut off its access to credit markets.
The move should prove comforting to bondholders because it clearly signals that Orange County does not want to cut off payments to them. Still, it also raises concerns because the county is acknowledging that it does not have enough money in its current account to meet its near-term interest payment obligations.
Today's action came as Orange County tries to devise ways to close a budget shortfall that is now estimated at as much as $120 million. The shortfall was caused by the $2 billion loss in a county investment pool that resulted from an aggressive bet on falling interest rates by Robert L. Citron, the county's former treasurer. Mr. Citron resigned earlier this month, only a day before Orange County filed for bankruptcy on Dec. 6.
A week ago, the county said it would have to make cuts in its budget of nearly $40 million, including layoffs of some workers.
"This is a technical default," said Joe Mysack, editor of Grant's Municipal Bond Observer in New York, who still termed the action "prudent."
Mr. Mysack added: "This is an unusual situation. The county needs to have money on hand to meet pressing needs. It can replenish its bond reserve fund further down the line."
When bonds are sold, reserve funds are usually set aside to cover the possibility that the bond issuer might not be able to meets interest obligations. But those funds are only rarely drawn upon. Typically, a bond issuer will set aside money from its incoming receipts a few months before an interest payment is due, using that cash to pay its bondholders.
In this case, the county supervisors decided to suspend payments into a set-aside account of $34.7 million in December and $18.7 million in January. The interest payments are due in June and July.
"This is part of the county's cash management plan," said Jane Eddy, an analyst with Standard and Poor's Rating Group. "It is not a signal that the county intends to default on these securities. We understand why they are doing this."
The announcement came on a day that the supervisors took several actions. The board said the county would, subject to the approval of a bankruptcy judge, make $3.9 million in interest payments on six bond issues. It also plans to dip into reserve funds to make immediate interest payments of around $1 million on notes issued in 1985 and 1986.
"This is a very intelligent move by the board of supervisors to keep access to the capital markets," said Gedale Horowitz, a senior managing director at Salomon Brothers, the county's financial adviser. "It says to the market that the county cares about the investor. But it doesn't know where this will all come out yet."

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Sale to Fannie Mae
 SANTA ANA, Calif., Dec. 29 (AP) -- Orange County arranged the first big sale of risky securities from its investment pool today, opening the final phase of liquidating the fund. The deal was with the Federal National Mortgage Association, or Fannie Mae, which agreed to buy back $630 million in face value of a species of derivatives known as structured notes, for $577 million in cash. The county said it would use $92.2 million of the $577 million to reduce the fund's heavy borrowings. The rest will help improve the liquidity of the fund. The county's financial adviser, Salomon Brothers, earlier had sold about $3.3 billion in less risky securities from the fund.

HEADLINE: Nicholas G. Pappas, New York Official And Writer, 66

BYLINE: By WOLFGANG SAXON 

 BODY:
Nicholas G. Pappas, a writer and former economic development official for New York City, died on Wednesday at New York University Medical Center. He was 66 and lived in Brooklyn Heights.
The cause was complications after heart surgery, said Bill Neugebauer, a family friend. 
 Mr. Pappas was born in Brooklyn and grew up in the shadow of Ebbets Field. At his death, he was working on a book of reminiscences and reflections of prominent people recalling the Brooklyn Dodgers and their move to Los Angeles. Mr. Neugebauer said that Mr. Pappas had interviewed nearly 400 people for the book but had been able to finish only two chapters.
Mr. Pappas attended Columbia College for two years before he joined the Army in 1951. Assigned to the Signal Corps, he became a sergeant-instructor in telegraphy.
His 20 years of city service stemmed from his work for Robert F. Wagner's first mayoral campaign in 1953. Fluent in Greek, Mr. Pappas got out the Greek-American vote, and Mr. Wagner made him a deputy commissioner in the Department of Economic Development.
In that post, he worked as a liaison between minorities and city agencies dealing with their business and economic concerns. He was also a speechwriter for Mayor Wagner and his successor, Abraham D. Beame.
Mr. Pappas became one of the city's more visible middle-level managers. He directed safe-street programs and projects that improved street lighting, turned stretches of the city's thoroughfares into pedestrian malls at dusk and dotted neighborhoods with vest-pocket parks.
He resigned in 1974 to write full time. He maintained close ties with the city's Greek-American community and frequently contributed to Greek-American newspapers and magazines.
Mr. Pappas is survived by a brother, James G., of Dix Hills, L.I.

HEADLINE: Unlikely Team Prepares to Battle for Rowland's Budget

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: HARTFORD

 BODY:
When Lorraine M. Aronson was asked to become deputy budget director in Governor-elect John G. Rowland's administration, her reaction was instantaneous.
"Get out of the car," she yelled at Reginald L. Jones, the future budget chief, whom she had picked up for what was to be a friendly lunch. 
 Ms. Aronson, a longtime state administrator, said she knew that the job of shrinking state government would be a brutal 20-hour-a-day ordeal. But though she is a Democrat, she ultimately accepted the post in the Republican administration, because, she said, it offered her a chance to work with Mr. Jones on their mutual policy passion: reinventing state government.
To meet the goals set by Mr. Rowland, the budget team will have to cut about $800 million from next year's budget, roughly 8 percent of what is needed to maintain current services. This would be the first time the state lowered its real spending since 1960.
Such a change is needed for Mr. Rowland to keep his campaign promise of reducing the income tax, the state's largest source of revenue, by 20 percent in his first year in office.
Mr. Jones and Ms. Aronson say the task cannot be accomplished by tinkering with programs or even indiscriminate across-the-board cuts, but requires a large-scale rethinking of state government.
"The Rowland vision says, in effect, don't just scissor the margins to get a few cuts," Mr. Jones said. "I want to look at the substance and essence of everything we do to reorganize it to get the best service at the lowest cost. It's part of this public wave for change, the need to address people's discouragement that no matter who they vote for, the bureaucracy keeps spending the same way."
But in a radio interview on Thursday, he warned that it would be difficult to meet Mr. Rowland's tax-cut goal in the first year of his administration.
"We're going to have to take another look at out how fast tax rates can be brought down," Mr. Jones said on WPOP-AM in Hartford. "My immediate goal is to establish a budget next year at a spending level no higher than what we're spending now. If I can accomplish that, I think there are tax options available."
But by the second year, he said, the administration should be back on schedule and able to cut the income tax by one-third.
On paper, Mr. Jones, 68, and Ms. Aronson, 43, are an unlikely team.
Mr. Jones, a Republican, spent his career in business. After retiring as a managing partner at Arthur Andersen, the accounting firm, he served for six years in the State House of Representatives. Ms. Aronson has devoted her professional life to government, serving most recently as Deputy Commissioner of Education after a three-year stint as Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr.'s deputy budget director, the job she is about to hold again.
While Mr. Jones led the fight against the state income tax imposed in 1991, Ms. Aronson was part of the team that persuaded the Legislature to enact it.
But the two agree that state government has become cumbersome, and both are known for their skill at cutting spending and revamping programs. Over the years, they have developed a warm friendship.
When Mr. Rowland asked Mr. Jones to be his budget director, Mr. Jones knew immediately that he wanted Ms. Aronson as his deputy.
"I didn't know that there would be much chance of her taking this job," he said in an interview at the Office of Policy and Management, "but she apparently likes it over here."
"No, I like you," Ms. Aronson remarked. "I know better than to like the job, having done it."
Both acknowledged that in preparing Mr. Rowland's budget, they face a daunting challenge.
Mr. Rowland's critics say that Governor Weicker has taken major steps to control state spending over the last four years, leaving little room for deeper cuts. They note that the number of full-time state employees has fallen by 164, from 52,580 in June 1989 to 52,416 in June 1994, even while the state was following court orders to expand its prison system and hire hundreds of social workers for the Department of Children and Family Services.
Mr. Weicker has frozen welfare benefits for families since 1991, has reduced Medicaid reimbursement rates for nursing homes, has decreased spending on state retirement benefits and has cut several hundred million dollars from towns and cities' requests for school aid.
Jerome P. Brown, president of the New England Healthcare Employees Union, District 1199, said that poor and working people had suffered enough cuts and that Mr. Rowland's push for a drop in real spending was uncalled for, because revenue has exceeded expenditures in the last few budgets.
"I don't know whether you need to spend more or just spend the same amount more intelligently, but I'm sure you don't need to cut it," Mr. Brown said.
But advocates for further cuts said that Mr. Weicker failed to stop the growth in the budget and that at the present pace, spending will soon surpass revenues.
The Connecticut Policy and Economic Council, a nonprofit budget research group, said state spending grew twice as fast as the economy in the last fiscal year, and spending now equals 9.2 percent of the state's aggregate personal income, up from 7 percent in 1985 and 8.5 percent in 1990. Despite a constitutional amendment imposing a cap on expenditures, state spending in the last fiscal year increased more than it had in four years.
The growth has been particularly notable in these three areas:
*Medicaid, which now takes about one of every five state dollars, increased 70 percent over the last four years.
*Debt service, which comprises about one-tenth of expenditures, has grown by about 67 percent since 1990.
*The Corrections Department, which has grown by 85 percent, now takes about 4 percent of total state spending.
"The accelerating rate of state budget increases indicates that Connecticut's spending giant remains untamed," said John Carson, president of the Connecticut Policy and Economic Council.
Mr. Jones and Ms. Aronson see the solution as a fundamental restructuring of state government. While Mr. Jones and Ms. Aronson say it is too early to provide a detailed plan for achieving the savings Mr. Rowland has requested, Mr. Jones offered a seven-point outline of his approach to the budget: restructuring agencies; changes in "system engineering," like purchasing and inventories; reconsidering entitlements; renegotiating employee fringe benefits; examining financing alternatives for the state debt; reviewing state assets that could be sold, and looking at other expenses.
Mr. Jones mentioned privatizing state services as a way to attain some savings, citing the Department of Motor Vehicles and state prisons as possible candidates.
Restructuring and privatizing alone, however, are unlikely to provide enough money for Mr. Rowland's income tax cut. Both Ms. Aronson and Mr. Jones granted that some big immediate savings would come from rethinking the amount spent on entitlements and other programs, like Medicaid reimbursement to nursing homes and education aid to towns and cities.
Mr. Jones also mentioned issuing new bonds to cover as much as $359 million in state debt, which he said would be palatable if accompanied by substantial cuts in the issuance of bonds in future years.
When the two come up with a budget, they then will have to overcome opposition in the legislature, where proposed cuts in programs and benefits have often been overturned.
But Mr. Jones said the public mood has changed, making it easier to get such a package approved.
"Whether you're going to eat your broccoli depends on how hungry you have become," he said. "People are now ready for some of these ideas."

HEADLINE: Old Building, New Finances;
Lean Times Don't Stop Renovation at City Hall

BYLINE: By ANDREA KANNAPELL 

 BODY:
Some 180 years ago, New York's new City Hall, with its gracious French Renaissance facade and elegant Georgian interior, was considered one of the most handsome buildings in the country.
Today, after 56 mayoral administrations, several technological revolutions and multiple fiscal crises, City Hall is a mixture of the grand, the pedestrian and the crumbling. Gawky metal coat racks line graceful corridors. A grandfather clock insists it is 2:35, 24 hours a day. The rotunda's white marble is chipped and discolored. 
 The Giuliani administration would like to see City Hall returned to splendor, and then kept that way with a self-perpetuating maintenance budget. In fact, it has set about devising, for the first time, a long-term renovation and maintenance plan for the building.
But in these days of belt-tightening and private-sector financing, city officials have found themselves looking at some admittedly novel ways to foot the bill.
John S. Dyson, the Deputy Mayor for finance and economic development, has already altered one longstanding city policy to raise money, allowing the building to be used for filming in exchange for a fee. Castle Rock Entertainment paid what Mr. Dyson called a "small honorarium" -- reportedly $50,000 -- to get its cameras inside recently to shoot a new movie, "City Hall," starring Al Pacino.
Officials are also considering renting out, for receptions, two stately City Hall chambers -- the Committee of the Whole and the Governor's Room. "I could imagine somebody might pay $5,000 to welcome somebody," Mr. Dyson said.
Other ideas include installing vending machines in the lobby and putting carts out front to sell food and drink (but definitely not a cafe, Mr. Dyson stressed). Or paid tours of City Hall on weekends. "We could show the Mayor's office," Mr. Dyson said. "He's usually out at a parade or something at those times."
The administration is also exploring the idea of opening a gift shop inside City Hall offering "I Love New York" mugs, Department of Sanitation neckties and other souvenirs. Citybooks, the marketing arm of the Department of General Services, currently sells souvenirs through a catalogue and from a small storefront on Chambers Street just north of City Hall.
"These are all ways to just get enough money, maybe $20,000 a year, to have the building maintain itself," Mr. Dyson said.
Private donations, the result of one-time fund-raising efforts, have often supplemented city money in financing the occasional renovations of City Hall over the years. Mr. Dyson credits Richard H. Jenrette, chairman of the Equitable Companies and a longtime restoration proponent, with underwriting much of the current restoration of the Committee of the Whole room, to be unveiled in early January.
 "We want to make one example," said Mr. Dyson, who is hoping that making a showpiece of the Committee of the Whole room will attract other corporate donations for the next phase: restoring the Governor's Room, followed by the Public Hearing Room and the City Council Chambers.
Ways of maintaining public buildings vary. A bond appropriation is financing up to $10 million in maintenance work at Chicago's turn-of-the-century City Hall.
In Washington, souvenir sales are the bread and butter of maintaining another important historic building, the White House. The nonprofit White House Historical Association has provided $11 million for the White House building, grounds and furnishings since the association was established by Jacqueline Kennedy in 1961. The group's revenue comes largely from the sale of books, ornaments, note cards, post cards and the like sold from a catalogue and shop.
In New York, the Gracie Mansion Conservancy, a private nonprofit group, finances renovations of the Mayor's residence as well as maintaining the house. A 1991 city report showed that the conservancy spent $143,500 on Gracie Mansion.
A similar sponsorship for City Hall was approved by the Dinkins administration but was never established. It remains an option under consideration by the Giuliani administration.
Whatever financing is developed, City Hall needs more than mere decoration. Peg Breen, president of the New York Landmarks Conservancy, estimated that structural work could cost as much as $10 million. Only the exterior, rotunda and City Hall Park are landmarked, but a "historic structures" report, at a cost of $100,000, is needed to assess the building's problems. Ms. Breen said work was likely to include repairs of the leaky roof, electrical rewiring and installation of central air conditioning (as much for the preservation of the collection of early American paintings in the Governor's Room as for human comfort).
While Mr. Dyson declines to be specific about the cost of the restoration of the Committee of the Whole room, an official involved in the restoration said it was around $50,000. Draperies dyed light green to match the new silk carpet cost $14,000 by themselves; shining up and rewiring the brass chandelier was $5,000.
Equitable's decorator, William Thompson of Berrytown Associates in Berrytown, N.Y., referred to a turn-of-the-century engraving of the room for ideas, seeking a historical flavor if not period-perfect accuracy. The room, whose most prominent feature is its domed ceiling, has been painted ivory. The new carpet matches the one in a portrait of James Monroe that now hangs over the refurbished fireplace. A grand circular mahogany table was donated by Equitable.
The Committee of the Whole room, originally the Common Council Chamber, was later used by the Board of Estimate for committee meetings. (In a committee of the whole meeting, all members get together to discuss issues of common concern.)
Completed in 1814, City Hall cost New York nearly $500,000 to build, almost half the city's tax revenue during the 10 years it was under construction. Designed to accommodate all branches of the municipal government, the three-story building now houses mayoral and City Council operations and has five public chambers and less than two dozen offices.
The Governor's Room, which the Art Commission last renovated in 1983, served as an office for New York governors through Alfred E. Smith's term in the 1920's. It now displays early American portraits, including the work of John Trumbull. In the room's entryway, the bodies of Abraham Lincoln and Ulysses S. Grant lay in state. (As the story goes, the rotunda's curved, cantilevered staircases were so newfangled that for years visitors refused to climb them -- until thousands decided that viewing Lincoln made the risk worth taking.)
Mr. Dyson himself donated $800 for paint for the Committee of the Whole room; a history buff who has made the restoration his pet project, he winces at the cavalier way City Hall has sometimes been treated.
"There's a lot of Rube Goldberg stuff in this building," he said. "I'm not sure if Rube Goldberg was in fact a New Yorker, but he would have been proud of some of the things we found here." He pointed to a contraption by a window in the Committee of the Whole room that holds, suspended, an electric cord that leads to floodlights by the parking lot. "Why they just didn't put it under the ground I can't quite figure out."
He agrees that the project is daunting. The battered Blue Room in the west wing, where the Mayor's news conferences are held, has frayed carpeting and folding metal furniture. George Washington's desk, on the second floor, is bathed in a stream of damaging sunlight.
"If we have a wonderful building like this that we allow to deteriorate, it says something about us, about our spirit as New Yorkers and where we're going, what kind of city we are, what kind of a people we are," Mr. Dyson said. "A tiny bit of attention can make a big difference."

GRAPHIC: Photos: A turn-of-the-century engraving guided restoration of the Committee of the Whole room (above). (Suzanne DeChillo/The New York Times) (pg. B1); John Dyson, New York City's Deputy Mayor for finance, has made it his mission to restore City Hall to its original grandeur. Mr. Dyson standing recently on the building's marble staircase. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times) (pg. B3)

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
International Lease Is Buying Airbus Jets

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: PARIS, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Airbus Industrie, Europe's large aircraft maker, said today that the International Lease Finance Corporation of the United States had placed firm orders for 30 new passenger jets with options for 10 more.
The order, estimated by industry sources to be worth at least $1.5 billion, calls for deliveries to start in February 1996 and continue to 2000. 
 This brings Airbus' total orders this year to 110, putting it almost neck and neck with its rival the Boeing Company, the Seattle-based plane maker that won 111 orders in 1994.
Airbus is a consortium comprising France's Aerospatiale , British Aerospace P.L.C., Construcciones Aeronauticas S.A. of Spain and the Deutsche Airbus subsidiary of Germany's Daimler-Benz A.G.
International Lease, a subsidiary of U.S. financial giant American International Group Inc., is the world's second-largest leasing firm providing airlines with aircraft. GPA is the largest.
International Lease had been expected to clinch the order since it signed a $1.5 billion syndicated loan with commercial banks in December.
Jean Pierson, Airbus managing director, aims to wrest market leadership from Boeing, but the chairman of the United States company, Frank Shrontz, is determined to defend Boeing's share, which he estimates at 60 percent.
The plane makers have always disputed market shares, but Boeing, founded in 1903, has held an undisputed lead since it brought out its B707 in 1955. Airbus was set up in 1972 and delivered its first planes in the mid-1970's.
In the middle of 1994, Airbus boasted that it had taken more orders than Boeing for the first time. Airbus's Mr. Pierson later said he was aiming for more than 50 percent of the world market, a statement that irked Boeing.
Airbus, Boeing and the McDonnell Douglas Corporation, based in St. Louis, have been hit hard by a worldwide recession that began with the 1990 Iraqi invasion of Kuwait and that is only now showing signs of bottoming out.
Thursday's transaction includes firm orders for 8 A319s, 13 A320s and 9 A321s. ILFC also took options on three A319s, three A321s and four A320s.
The A319, which seats about 124 passengers, is a shortened version of the A320 and the final assembly of the first plane of this type is expected in March next year.
The A320, in service since 1988, is a short-to-medium-haul twin-engine single-aisle plane that can seat 150.
The A321, which went into service this year, is a stretched version of the A320, seating 186.
"There has been growing airline lesse demand for A319, A320 and A321 aircraft from a large number of I.L.F.C. customers worldwide," the company chairman, Leslie Gonda, said in a statement.
International Lease is the single largest customer of Airbus, with 155 aircraft already under order. Of those, 40 have been delivered.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The International Lease Finance Corporation has ordered 30 new passenger jets, including 13 A-320's, from Airbus Industrie, the large European consortium. The A320, in service since 1988, can seat 150 passengers. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Whistleblower, Out of Job, Wins $4 Million Settlement

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. McFADDEN 

 BODY:
A whistleblower who has been unemployed since he quit a $470-a-week job as a New York State social services trainee four years ago and accused his department of misusing Federal funds has won more than $4 million as part of a settlement in which the state has agreed to pay the Federal Government $27 million.
The whistleblower, George Denoncourt, 48, filed the unusual lawsuit -- and won 15 percent of the settlement -- under the Federal False Claims Act, a Civil War statute originally intended to combat war profiteering, but amended in 1986 to encourage whistleblowers to expose modern skulduggery against government. 
 "He feels good about it," Stuart Pierson, a Washington lawyer representing Mr. Denoncourt, said yesterday as state officials acknowledged that they had settled the case, albeit without admitting any wrongdoing. "He and his family suffered a lot of financial privation to pursue this case."
Mr. Pierson said his client, a former resident of Troy, N.Y., who lives with his wife and four children in New Hampshire, received $4,050,000 as his share of the settlement, and $225,000 from the state for "being forced to resign" from a job that, he charged, was part of a state fraud against the Government.
Mr. Denoncourt, while a trainee with the Social Service Department's Office of Human Resources Development from January 1989 to October 1990, complained that Federal funds intended for training employees like him were being spent improperly on equipment and unauthorized workers, Mr. Pierson said.
"He felt his office was engaged in criminal activities having to do with Federal funds, and they would not give him a transfer to an honest job, so he had no recourse but to resign," Mr. Pierson said. His client then cooperated with a state investigation that ended in a critical report but no charges.
Continuing to pursue the case, Mr. Denoncourt in 1992 filed suit in Federal Court in Washington, charging that $14 million in Federal funds over 12 years had been spent improperly by state agencies on computers, fax machines and other equipment and on hiring janitors and other unauthorized workers.
The Justice Department did not join the suit, though it was a party to the $27 million settlement -- half of it for repayment, half as a civil penalty. Separately, Mr. Pierson said, the Justice Department has filed its own claims for $26 million in social-service training funds that it says were improperly used by the state. Negotiations over those claims are continuing, he said.
Terrance McGrath, a spokesman for the Social Services Department, said the state had settled only to save heavy litigation costs and treble damages if the suit was ultimately lost. He said the case involved only a small fraction of the $15 billion in Federal funds in his department's $30 billion budget.
Insisting that state employees had merely followed practices that dated to the 1970's and had done nothing improper, Mr. McGrath called the case a dispute over the interpretation of complex Federal rules governing the social-service training funds. Albany and Washington are always in disagreement over such funds -- with an average of $1.5 billion in dispute all the time, he said.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Are Mixed In Pre-Holiday Trading

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Prices of Treasury securities were mixed yesterday with some of the longer maturities down slightly in thin pre-holiday trading.
The market was weighed down by a report showing a larger-than-expected gain in the Government's chief forecasting gauge and by only a modest recovery in the dollar after a sharp decline on Wednesday. 
 The price of the 30-year bond fell 3/32 of a percentage point, to 96 4/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, rose to 7.84 percent from 7.83 percent on Wednesday. Rates on short-term bills were down slightly. The three-month bill rate was unchanged at 5.45 percent, while a six-month bill declined 6 basis points, to 6.17 percent. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
Tony Crescenzi, director of fixed-income trading at Miller, Tabak, Hirsch & Company, a New York brokerage firm, said trading yesterday "did have some volatility, and that might be because it is in the midst of some consolidation, following its recent countertrend rally in the long end."
"This might be just the consolidation that the market needs in order to make further gains," he said, adding, "The failure of the dollar to barely recoup its losses left people in the bond market unsure on where the dollar would head next."
The Commerce Department reported yesterday that its index of leading indicators rose three-tenths of 1 percent in November, after a decline of one-tenth of 1 percent in October.
Thomas F. Carpenter, chief economist and managing director for ASB Capital Management, noted that the leading indicators had been stagnant lately, suggesting that by the end of the second quarter of 1995 the United States economy may slow in response to interest rate increases.
The market turns its attention today to a report on new-home sales for November. The data are often volatile and may not get a lot of attention from the market, Mr. Crescenzi said. The Chicago Purchasing Managers report will also be issued today.
The Public Securities Association, a trade group of securities firms, recommended that the Treasury market close at 2 P.M. today.
In the municipal market, prices fell as investors continued to sell bonds to record tax losses. Prices fell as much as 3/8 of a point, with few bonds trading. Investors got few clues about the direction of prices with little trading and only $263 million in new issues sold.
The average yield to maturity in the Bond Buyer's index of 40 actively municipal issues rose to 6.91 percent, from 6.90 percent.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Added Demand From Asia Sends Copper to 6-Year High

BYLINE: By Reuters 

 BODY:
Copper prices rose to their highest levels in six years yesterday as economic growth in the United States, Japan and Germany continued to fuel a rally.
Demand from Asia has also been increased by the prolonged closing of the Philippines' only smelter, which was shut before the Christmas holiday because of typhoon damage and was not expected to reopen until next week. 
 "The market continues to attract Asian demand," an analyst at Paine Webber, Bernard Savaiko, said.
Copper futures on the Commodity Exchange in New York set contract highs for most months, with the contract for March delivery closing up 0.15 cent at 138.05 cents a pound, just below the day's peak of 138.40 cents.
On the Metal Exchange in London, copper for delivery in three months climbed above $3,000 a metric ton for the first time in six years.
Since the beginning of the year, copper prices have soared about 70 percent.
Demand for cooper is expected to grow more than 3 percent next year. Since no new mine or refinery output will be added until the latter half of 1995, analysts expect copper reserves to fall steadily.
Cotton prices also rose yesterday, approaching their highest levels since May 1991. Cotton for March delivery rose 0.46 cent, to 89.86 cents a pound, on the New York Cotton Exchange.
Sharon Johnson, cotton analyst with Frank Scheider & Company, said the United States remained the only place in the world with cotton available to sell.
Despite the gains in copper and cotton, the Commodity Research Bureau's index slipped 0.49 point, to 236.35.
Among the commodities losing ground was crude oil, despite a surge in the January gasoline futures contract, which is due to expire today.
January unleaded gasoline jumped 0.93 cent, to 54.99 cents a gallon, on the New York Mercantile Exchange. But February crude oil fell 7 cents, to $17.72 a barrel.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Stages a Rebound From Mexico-Related Loss

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
The dollar rose against leading currencies in light trading yesterday, recovering from a sharp drop on Wednesday.
The dollar suffered its biggest one-day loss against the German mark in five months on Wednesday as Mexico's financial crisis roiled markets in the United States. 
 "The dollar is recovering today because it was dramatically overdone yesterday," Hunt Taylor said late in the trading day yesterday. He is managing director of the Reynwood Trading Corporation, a currency fund manager in Short Hills, N.J., with $100 million of assets.
Elizabeth Bussanich, a currency adviser at the Bank of Montreal in New York, said, "A lot of investors feel the dollar is a good bargain at these levels." She noted that the dollar was also supported by expectations that the Federal Reserve would again raise short-term interest rates at the end of January.
Higher rates typically bolster a currency by attracting investors to the rising yields of fixed-income securities denominated in the currency. The likelihood of higher rates means the dollar could reach 1.60 marks next month, she said.
In New York trading late yesterday, the dollar rose to 1.5527 marks from 1.5450 marks on Wednesday, and to 1.3125 Swiss francs from 1.3040 francs. The dollar edged up to 99.70 Japanese yen from 99.30 yen. The British pound fell to $1.5610 from $1.5700.
The dollar closed at 4.8750 pesos, down 2 percent from Wednesday's close of 4.9750 pesos. The dollar has risen 40.7 percent against the peso since the Mexican Government first devalued its currency last week.
But the peso did not have much effect on the dollar's value against other currencies yesterday, traders said. "I think the worst is over," said Earl Johnson, currency adviser at Harris Trust and Savings Bank in Chicago.
In other currency trading, the Canadian dollar fell to a new eight-year low of 70.98 United States cents, before recovering to 71.15 cents in late trading, down slightly from 71.16 cents on Wednesday.
The Mexican crisis compounded the weakness of the Canadian dollar, which has been weighed down by the country's budget deficits and questions over Quebec.
Fund managers with holdings throughout North America sold Canadian assets in recent days to compensate for their losses on Mexican stocks and bonds, traders said. After selling Canadian stocks and bonds, the fund managers converted the Canadian dollar proceeds to United States dollars, traders said.

HEADLINE: U.S., Edging Higher, Ranks as World's 7th-Richest Nation

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
The United States has advanced one notch to become the world's seventh-richest nation, but American shoppers get more for their money than residents in every country but Luxembourg, the World Bank said today.
The multilateral lending agency said the United States' economic output per person rose to $24,750 in 1993 from $23,830 in 1992. 
 In seventh place, the United States is behind Sweden and ahead of Iceland -- or about where it has been most years since at least 1980. Germany was seventh in 1992.
The data, included in the 27th edition of the World Bank Atlas, showed that Switzerland remained the world's wealthiest nation, with economic output per person of $36,410, down $320 from 1992.
Others in the top 10 were: Luxembourg, $35,850; Japan, $31,450; Denmark, $26,510; Norway, $26,340; Sweden, $24,830; Iceland, $23,620; Germany, $23,560, and Kuwait, $23,350.
By contrast, Mozambique remained the poorest country in the world, with output of just $80 a person.
While the United States ranked at the lower end of the top 10 list, Americans can take consolation from one fact: they have more purchasing power because prices are cheaper in the United States than overseas.
For the first time, the atlas shows the buying power within each country, after adjusting for differing price levels among nations.
Costlier goods and services reduce consumers' buying power, thus lowering the "real" per capita income in wealthier nations. Conversely, the lower the prices, the further the money will go, thus raising the effective consumer income.
Luxembourg had the highest real output per person of $29,510, followed by the United States at $24,750. The United States consistently has ranked among the top two on this basis. "Everybody loves coming to the United States because prices are cheaper here," Michael Ward, a World Bank economist, said.
Others rounding out the "real" top 10 were: Switzerland, $23,620; the United Arab Emirates, $23,390; Qatar, $22,910; Hong Kong, $21,670; Japan, $21,090; Germany, $20,980; Singapore, $20,470, and Canada, $20,410.
Such rankings have long been disputed, and the World Bank has long shied away from them, because they make poor countries look a little less poor and possibly less deserving of foreign aid.

HEADLINE: U.S. Index Signals Slower Growth in '95

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
The Government's chief forecasting gauge signals slower economic growth next year, the Commerce Department reported today.
The department's index of leading indicators posted a gain of three-tenths of 1 percent in November, after a decline of one-tenth of 1 percent in October, the first setback in 15 months. 
 While the November gain was better than expected, the performance of the index has clearly been signaling for the last six months a slowdown in growth but no outright downturn, analysts said.
"The leading indicators don't suggest a recession but there are clear indications that the economy will slip into a pattern of low growth next year," said Allen Sinai, chief economist at Lehman Brothers in New York.
Many analysts are forecasting that growth next year, as measured by the gross domestic product, will be below 3 percent. That would be significantly lower than the 4 percent growth rate expected for 1994.
In a second report today, the Labor Department said that the number of Americans filing initial claims for state unemployment benefits dropped by 3,000 last week, to 322,000, reflecting continued strength in labor markets.
That report was in line with other indicators that 1994 was ending at a robust pace. That strength has confirmed analysts' expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise short-term interest rates for the seventh time since February when its policy-making committee meets in late January.
The Fed has been raising rates to slow growth and keep inflation from accelerating. Analysts said there were hints in the November leading- index report that this effort was starting to bear fruit with interest-sensitive sectors like building permits showing weakness.
The leading indicators report showed an even split with five business barometers showing strength and five others flashing signs of weakness.
The biggest contributor to the gain was a sharp rise in orders for new plant and equipment. Other components showing strength were gains in factory orders for consumer goods, a rise in unfilled orders for durable goods, a decline for the month in average weekly unemployment claims and a rise in prices of sensitive materials.
The biggest negative factor was a slowdown in growth of the money supply followed by a fall in stock prices, a drop in building permits, changes in business deliveries and a drop in consumer expectations.

HEADLINE: Ringing In The New With Music

BYLINE: By JON PARELES 

 BODY:
ON New Year's Eve in New York City, it's a seller's market for revelry. The streets are swarmed and the restaurants packed with prospective merrymakers, all seeking the right place and atmosphere to share the ballyhooed moment when the calendar changes. Live music, with its promise of instant community and Dionysian prospects, is a potent lure; from the jazz cafes to Madison Square Garden, music packs 'em in, as people choose to spend the evening with others who share their taste or dance to the same beat.
For many people, putting on a party hat and slipping a CD into the home stereo -- or watching the Guy Lombardo Orchestra on PBS -- just doesn't provide the same excitement. Even at places that don't usually present music, New Year's Eve is the time to book a band. 
 For regular club- and concertgoers, New Year's Eve is a hint of what it must be like to live in Argentina; the overnight inflation rate is astonishing, more than enough to cover typical extras like free noisemakers and Champagne at midnight. It's also a night when things run even later than scheduled, since no one wants to leave the party early. But it's a special occasion, sometimes enough to make performers put out extra soul or spunk.
Planning ahead for New Year's Eve is a good idea, especially in New York City. Concerts sell out, clubs fill up and even at places that aren't filled, admission prices are significantly higher for those who decide at the last minute. On page C30 is a listing of pop critics' choices for clubs and concerts where tickets were still available at press time. In New York City, there's something for every taste, from standards to punk-rock, big bands to funk.

HEADLINE: Don't Abolish Meals Program for Elderly

 BODY:
To the Editor:
For 30 years the Older Americans Act has provided vital services to people 60 years old and older, particularly those with the greatest economic and social needs. One of the most important services has been the nutrition program through which well-balanced meals are provided at senior citizen centers or are delivered to the homebound. 
It has proved to be a shining example of a low-cost, locally managed, volunteer-assisted, family supportive government program.
Early next month Congress will be presented with a legislative package, part of the Republican Contract With America, that would place the nutrition program into a block grant with food stamps, school lunches and other such programs. This will be accomplished through the personal responsibility-welfare reform bill.
The Older Americans Act is not an entitlement and has never been considered "welfare." Its loss would be a tragedy.
 
BOB WILLISON
Exec. Director, Southwestern
Pennsylvania Area Agency on Aging
Monessen, Pa., Dec. 23, 1994

HEADLINE: Mexican Trade Won't Gain From Devaluation

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Apropos "Mexico's Devaluation: Not So Scary" (editorial, Dec. 24): Some discussions in support of the Mexican peso's devaluation, though not this particular discussion, argue that currency values can be manipulated to manage trade. This was the essence of mercantilist trade theory, which died in the 19th century with the global influence of the British gold standard. 
 The idea crept back to produce the Depression's competitive currency devaluations. It surfaced again in persuading President Nixon to break the dollar's link to gold in 1971 in order to devalue the dollar against the Japanese yen and European currencies for trade advantage. One should be wary of embracing it.
Classical economic theory holds that it is not possible to change the terms of trade between two people or two countries by changing the value of the unit of account.
If three loaves of bread will trade for three bottles of wine when the dollar is valued at one unit, the same terms will hold if the government devalues the dollar to half a unit. The mercantilist model holds human behavior constant in promising that the producers of bread and wine will trade two loaves for four bottles.
The only confusion occurs in the short term, when the currency change overrides trade contracts that had already been signed. In that case, the vintner is legally forced to supply four bottles in exchange for the baker's two loaves.
When new contracts are made, the terms re-establish themselves. Currency changes usually occur when one trading partner can exert political influence to force the change, for short-term mercantilist advantage.
The point is made clearer by studying currency changes in countries that have contracts of short maturities. In Mexico last week, the Government devalued the peso almost 40 percent to gain advantages for its export industries. Before the week was out, Mexican labor leaders demanded 40 percent wage increases to offset the loss of purchasing power, insisting that business meanwhile freeze domestic prices.
All the new Mexican Government accomplished was to undermine confidence in its management of the peso as a commercial unit of account, which will make it more difficult for capital to flow to Mexico or for capital to form inside Mexico.
Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman would say that the Fed cannot control trade flows by altering the dollar's value. He might even note that since 1971, when we tried to lower our trade deficit with Japan, the dollar lost 75 percent of its value relative to the yen, yet our trade deficit with Japan increased severalfold.
 
JUDE WANNISKI
Morristown, N.J., Dec. 27, 1994
 
The writer is a political and economic consultant.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Letter

COUNTRY: MEXICO (96%); JAPAN (93%); UNITED STATES (79%); EUROPE (79%);

HEADLINE: Mexican Trade Won't Gain From Devaluation;
Oversold Agreements

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Mexico's Devaluation: Not So Scary" (editorial, Dec. 24), asserting all is well with the North American Free Trade Agreement, states that "the purpose of trade is not to raise employment -- which the Federal Reserve now controls," but "to steer workers into high productivity jobs." 
 This is a reasonable and defensible position. Unfortunately, it is at odds with the positions put forward by the Clinton Administration and others who sold the North American trade agreement on promises of hundreds of thousands of new jobs.
It is also at odds with arguments in support of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade in recent editorials, in which you maintained the agreement would produce economic gains of $100 billion (Oct. 5) or $200 billion (Sept. 29) a year.
These estimates of gains from GATT came from what are known as "computable general equilibrium models." In these models, trade affects employment directly (that is, the Federal Reserve does not control employment).
But these huge estimated gains are almost entirely attributable to the demand stimulus that is associated with a tax cut (tariff reduction), without any offsetting cut in spending. The sort of gains from the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade that would be associated with your new position are less than one-hundredth this size.
The GATT debate, like the North American Free Trade Agreement debate before it, would have been far more productive had exaggerated forecasts of economic gains not been given such extensive publicity.
 
DEAN BAKER
Washington, Dec. 24, 1994
 
The writer is an economist at the Economic Policy Institute.

TYPE: Letter
