HEADLINE: Two Confident Fighters Ready to Take On a New Year;
Clinton's Course: Islands of Constants in Ocean of Political Change

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 29

 BODY:
As Bill Clinton has struggled to right his Presidency in the grim weeks since Election Day, his advisers say, he has consulted friends old and new, far and wide, and heard them out at all hours of day and night. But he appears to have listened mostly to the man who has been his chief political strategist for 20 years: Bill Clinton.
If there was any doubt about the President's resolve to keep his job, he removed it today, taking the opportunity of a year-end interview with news-service reporters to declare unequivocally that he would run for re-election and to allow gamely that he was "not worried" about the possibility of a Democratic primary challenge. 
 Whether in talking politics, drafting two recent major speeches, or settling the scope of his proposed tax breaks for the middle-class, Mr. Clinton's hand has been heaviest, and sometimes all but alone. The President's aides suggest he has passed from shock to fury to the show of upbeat acceptance he displayed today.
"The battle over this all is going to be inside the President's own head," one Democratic Congressman said of the White House's effort to adapt to the new Republican ascendancy and the chorus of Democratic doubters. "He thinks he can make the best decisions, and so he basically says, shovel in the facts and opinions and I'll decide."
That has long been Mr. Clinton's approach -- for good, and often for ill. In the glum lull of his 1992 campaign, after the agonies of the New Hampshire primary and before the uplift of the Democratic Convention, he essentially functioned as scheduler, consultant, fund-raiser and factotum -- with exhausting and uneven effects. The solution then was to put James Carville, the crafty Cajun campaign consultant, in firm control.
The task of governing now is much more complex, Mr. Carville's stock is lower and interviews with White House aides and Democrats in Washington produced this rough consensus on the shifting status of those who have Mr. Clinton's ear, and whose advice leavens his actions:
*Hillary Rodham Clinton, whose public appearances since Nov. 8 have mostly been limited to ceremonial events, remains de facto chief domestic policy adviser. She is present at meetings on topics from health to welfare, and together with George Stephanopoulos, the senior presidential adviser, was a staunch advocate of the proposed tax breaks. In the news-service interview, Mr. Clinton said, "I hope she will continue to be an activist."
*The White House chief of staff, Leon E. Panetta, brought in last summer to help control the President's time, has succeeded well enough that he can now be a bottleneck, in such demand among Cabinet secretaries and old Capitol Hill colleagues that he is harder to reach than Mr. Clinton himself. Meetings are shorter, attended by fewer aides, with fewer tales told out of school. But Mr. Panetta, an old deficit hawk, could not deter Mr. Clinton from proposing tax breaks that would make it just that much harder to keep the deficit in check.
*The luster of Mr. Carville and his partner, Paul Begala, the pollster, Stanley Greenberg, and the media consultant, Mandy Grunwald -- the quartet that led Mr. Clinton's campaign -- is much dimmed. They are blamed in varying degrees for bad and costly advice and commercials for the Democrats this year, for apparent indiscretions in a spate of mid-term chronicles of the Clinton presidency, and their access to presidential deliberations has been diminished.
*Vice President Al Gore, always a central adviser, has assumed even greater importance and a renewed bond with the President, especially on questions of reshaping the bureaucracy and cutting the budget.
*Harold M. Ickes, the labor lawyer who ran Mr. Clinton's New York campaign in 1992 and is now deputy White House chief of staff, remains in the thick of the action, perhaps the individual trusted most by Mr. and Mrs. Clinton. He is perpetually mentioned as a likely candidate for a senior job in the re-election effort next year.
Much else, by the senior staff's own acknowledgment, is in flux, as Mr. Clinton prepares to name a new press secretary, new political aides, a new Democratic Party chairman and, by spring, at least a skeletal campaign staff for 1996. Not even the oldest hands in a White House that remains overwhelmingly young have any experience in dealing with a Republican Congressional majority.
"A lot of this is still seeing through a glass darkly," one senior aide said last week.
In the interview today, Mr. Clinton said he expected both cooperation and "hard fights" with the Republicans but predicted "an exciting and productive time for America if we really try to help the country instead of to help ourselves" in the next election. "I intend to seek re-election but that is not uppermost in my mind," he said, adding: "I get up every day fighting for the American people."
But the President is just as certainly fighting for his political life, and that has led him in recent weeks to lash out at advisers, formal and informal, whose advice he took in the fall campaign.
Several aides said that former Representative Tony Coelho, who was among those urging the Democrats to campaign against the Republican "Contract With America" -- a strategy that Mr. Clinton embraced, but that backfired badly -- had personally rubbed the President the wrong way with a manner franker than Mr. Clinton was accustomed to.
In meetings in the Oval Office, in telephone conversations, at holiday parties, the President has buttonholed old friends like Gov. Ned McWherter of Tennessee; Dick Morris, the Arkansas political consultant who advised his early gubernatorial campaigns; defeated Congressional Democrats like Dan Rostenkowski and Thomas S. Foley, and disgruntled moderate Democrats like Representative Dave McCurdy of Oklahoma and Senator John B. Breaux of Louisiana, who advised Mr. Clinton to shake up a White House staff they thought remained too liberal.
Precisely what comes out of such sessions is much harder to summarize, and always has been.
"Oh, I couldn't tell you how he makes up his mind," James Blair, the lawyer for Tyson Foods in Arkansas and one of Mr. Clinton's longtime advisers, recalled earlier this year of the the discussions that led up to the 1992 campaign. "He loves to take advice. He loves to talk. He frequently gives people signals that they misinterpret because he will take the opposite side of whatever your viewpoint is, and then you think that's what he believes. He just uses that as a device to milk your mind."
The President is tapping some minds less these days. One senior aide said that Mr. Begala, the consultant, has been in bad odor, especially with Mrs. Clinton, since the publication last summer of "The Agenda," Bob Woodward's book on the formulation of Mr. Clinton's economic plan last year. In the book, unattributed anecdotes that appear to come from Mr. Begala have the effect -- perhaps unintentional -- of belittling the President.
Several Democrats said one remaining question is whether Mr. Panetta will consolidate his authority even more. If Mr. Ickes should leave to work on the campaign, and if the other deputy chief of staff, Erskine B. Bowles, should become the coordinator of economic policy, filling the slot vacated by Treasury Secretary-designate Robert E. Rubin, Mr. Panetta might get to bring in his own team.
"Leon's been the best thing that's happened there," one friend of Mr. Panetta said. "But it was never going to be that the President would say, 'Here, Leon, run it.' "

CORRECTION-DATE: December 31, 1994, Saturday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about President Clinton's advisers misidentified the home state of Dick Morris, a political consultant and pollster who worked on Mr. Clinton's campaigns for governor. He is based in Connecticut, not Arkansas.

HEADLINE: With Hope and Caution, U.N. Is Trying to Extend the Cease-Fire in Bosnia

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: ZAGREB, Croatia, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Warring factions in the conflict that has convulsed Bosnia and Herzegovina for 33 months continued today to inch toward a four-month cessation of fighting, which United Nations mediators hope will be a prelude to a political settlement of the war.
Seeking to conclude the accord by this weekend, the senior United Nations commander, Lieut. Gen. Sir Michael Rose, met in Sarajevo with the Vice President of Bosnia, Ejup Ganic, then traveled to nearby Pale for talks with Bosnian Serb leaders. 
 Sporadic combat continued around the northwestern town of Bihac, but the rest of the country was calm as both Government and Bosnian Serb forces continued to observe the cease-fire negotiated last week by former President Jimmy Carter.
"I don't think there's been a cease-fire in my time here that's held as well as this one," said General Rose, who has commanded United Nations troops here for 11 months. "I think that's an indication of good intentions and of goodwill toward the peace process."
Leaders of both sides echoed the General's optimism.
"I hope that if we have a cease-fire in Bihac and a withdrawal of Croatian Serb troops from the area, we can proceed further and come to a cessation of hostilities," Vice President Ganic said.
The Bosnian Serb leader, Dr. Radovan Karadzic, reported that negotiations were "going very well."
"We expect the U.N. to discuss it with the Muslims and then we can go, let's say, to Geneva or a ship in the Adriatic Sea and sign it solemnly," Dr. Karadzic said. "I hope this will be done before Jan. 1."
United Nations officials in Zagreb said they were expecting the chief United Nations envoy to the former Yugoslavia, Yasushi Akashi, to attend any signing ceremony. The officials said they expected any signing to take place in Sarajevo, but Dr. Karadzic seems to prefer another venue.
A Bosnian Government official said in Sarajevo that Mr. Akashi would travel to Sarajevo on Saturday morning in an effort to clinch an accord on a cessation of fighting. The official, who asked not to be identified, told Reuters that Mr. Akashi would seek to bridge "serious differences" between the two sides.
"He has some work to do if there is going to be a deal by Jan. 1," the official said.
If the accord is signed, mediators will then face the more daunting challenge of persuading the Bosnian Serbs to accept the comprehensive peace plan proposed earlier this year by the five-nation "contact group," which consists of the United States, Britain, France, Germany and Russia.
The Muslim-dominated Government has already accepted the plan, which would give it and its Croat allies control over 51 percent of the country's territory and Bosnian Serbs the remaining 49 percent. Bosnian Serb leaders have complained that the land apportioned to them under the plan is militarily indefensible and economically unproductive.
The self-appointed Bosnian Serb parliament, meeting in Pale on Thursday, approved a statement saying its side was ready to enter negotiations "based on the existing plan." That language fell short of what negotiators want. They have presented the contact group plan as a finished document not subject to change, and insist that the Bosnian Serbs accept it as it stands.
In the Serb-held region of Croatia today, Serb soldiers fired machine-gun bursts at two United Nations military observers and then forced them to lie on the ground while they were interrogated at gunpoint, the United Nations spokesman, Thant Myint-U, reported.
Mr. Thant Myint-U said the observers, a Ghanian and a Jordanian, were following a Serbian convoy "at a discreet distance" when they were fired upon. The observers turned back but were chased by Serbs, pulled from their vehicle, searched and interrogated before being released.
"We consider this completely unacceptable behavior," Mr. Thant Myint-U said.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
 
The Internet has made hoaxes and frauds more insidious because of the reach and immediacy offered by the network. The growing use of unsigned messages over computer networks has spawned a debate about how to prevent abuses without stifling the right to speak freely -- even anonymously. "Anonymity is power," says a specialist in intellectual-property law, "and I think it will be abused on the Net." [Page 1.] One cyberspace prank that was widely circulated was a faked Associated Press report asserting that Microsoft was acquiring the Roman Catholic Church. [53.]
 
Sales of new homes fell 2.5 percent in November. The housing market is being weakened by higher mortgage rates. [42.]
 
 
Markets
 
Wall Street rang out the year humbler, and perhaps wiser. A market year that began with traders and analysts confidently predicting that the Dow Jones industrial average would rise above 4,000 ended with the Dow up 1.01 point, at 3,834.44. The Standard & Poor's 500 index fell 1.89 point, down 1.3 percent for the year. [41.]
 
Treasury prices declined in a light trading session. Prices were little changed in the municipal bond market. [51.]
 
Pork prices rose sharply after a report indicated that hog farmers were planning to reduce the size of their herds. [52.]
 
The dollar slipped against the German mark and the Japanese yen in thin, preholiday trading. [52.]
 
 
International
 
The United States is threatening China with trade sanctions in an effort to force Beijing to halt abuses of copyright and patent protections. The proposed sanctions will be disclosed today. [41.]
 
Mexico's new Finance Minister lacks the political ambitions of his predecessor, which may prove to his advantage as he seeks to regain the confidence of investors. The new minister, Guillermo Ortiz Martinez, is known as a bulldog administrator, and he takes the helm of an economy battered by the plunge in the peso. [41.]
 
 
Companies
 
Health Systems International is a billion-dollar takeover target. FHP International has offered $1.69 billion in stock, and Foundation Health has offered $1.55 billion in stock. Other bids are expected for Health Systems, which has one of the biggest publicly owned health maintenance organizations in the West. [41.]
 
Advanced Micro Devices won a victory in its long-running legal dispute with Intel over a 1982 licensing deal. [41.]
 
Merry-Go-Round Enterprises outlined a reorganization plan that would get it out of bankruptcy by the middle of next year. The plan, to which creditors have agreed in principle, has apparently resolved a dispute over the troubled chain's management. [43.]
 
Gaylord Entertainment is selling its cable-television systems for $370 million to focus on its cable country-music networks. [43.]
 
Standa of Italy plans to sell its seven Euromercato hypermarkets, which have been valued at close to $600 million. [43.]
 
MCI Communications filed a complaint against Pacific Bell ahead of the opening of California's local toll-call market. [43.]
 
 
Your Money
 
The rich are different in at least one respect: they can invest with the money management services of Bessemer Trust, whose minimum portfolio is $5 million. For those who won't be seeking personal service at Bessemer's oak-paneled offices, the firm has outlined its investment suggestions for the coming year. [39.]
 
Real estate investment trusts -- REIT's -- have been on an upturn after a roller-coaster year. Real estate mutual funds, which buy REIT's, were the best-performing fund sector in December. [39.]
 
Some brokerage firms are lowering fees on wrap accounts, but investors may need to ask for the lower prices to get them. [39.]

HEADLINE: Dogged Doctor for Mexico's Morass

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, Dec. 30

 BODY:
After being passed over for Finance Minister and being forced to watch the man who got the post send the Mexican peso into a tailspin, Guillermo Ortiz Martinez finally has the job he wanted, and all the worries that go with it.
 With the resignation on Thursday of Jaime Serra Puche, Mr. Ortiz, a Stanford-trained economist who was Mexico's under secretary of finance for six years, takes control of an economy battered and thrown off course by the 30 percent devaluation of the peso. 
 Mr. Ortiz, 46, is known as a bulldog administrator -- short on style but tough enough to take on anyone who crosses him. He is already respected by Mexican and foreign business executives for his grasp of practical economics, which he proved in his work in privatizing Mexican banks. Now he must regain for Mexico the confidence of investors who pulled billions of dollars out of the market and threaten to remove more unless they see a credible rescue plan.
 Because he cannot be blamed for the recent clumsy devaluations, Mr. Ortiz is in a better position than his predecessor to devise and carry out a stabilization plan.
 The plan's details are not expected to be made known until Monday, but officials in interviews have sketched an austere program to further cool Mexico's sluggish economy, reduce already uneven consumer purchasing power and take advantage of higher import prices and lower prices on exports caused by the devaluation.
 By sharply reducing imports and cutting public spending, Mexico hopes to halve the $30 billion trade imbalance, which the Government now recognizes as the country's fundamental financial problem.
 Mr. Ortiz, within hours of taking over, was on the phone to New York, talking up investors and soliciting help from American commercial banks like Chase Manhattan.
 Such an open style is worlds apart from the way Mr. Serra worked during his 29 days in office. Mr. Serra was used to giving answers, not asking questions. His relationship with investment bankers was strained almost to breaking during a disastrous meeting at the New York Federal Reserve offices last week.
 The biggest difference between the new and old ministers, mutual acquaintances say, is that Mr. Serra had political ambitions and considered himself a potential presidential candidate, while Mr. Ortiz does not think of himself as anything other than an economist.
 "One of the advantages he has over Serra is that he does not try to give political answers; he's direct and to the point," said an American analyst who has worked extensively with Mr. Ortiz. "He's a hands-on person who knows the international community very well."
 Like Mr. Serra, Mr. Ortiz is a close friend of President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon. All three come from a generation of Mexican economists who earned graduate degrees at universities in the United States and are now running Mexico. When Mr. Ortiz earned his Ph.D. from Stanford, the title of his dissertation was "The Accumulation of Capital and Economic Growth, a Financial Perspective of Mexico."
 He has taught at Stanford and at Mexican universities, and from 1984 to 1988 worked with the International Monetary Fund in Washington, in time as an executive director representing Mexico and seven other Spanish-speaking nations. He was particularly active in currency issues.
 As undersecretary in the Finance ministry, Mr. Ortiz was responsible for the lucrative sale of Mexico's Government-owned banks, which took in about $12.4 billion.
 The privatizations hit some rough spots. In one instance, the same investors backed two separate bids to buy Banco Mexicano Somex. After their higher bid for $845 million was accepted, the investors pulled out, saying the price was too high. Some of them joined the runners-up, who paid $242 billion less for the bank. Despite criticism of the sale, Mr. Ortiz said the bidders did nothing illegal.
 In September, Government regulators filed charges against Carlos Cabal Peniche, a flamboyant businessman who had bought two of the privatized banks with the blessing of authorities, including Mr. Ortiz. He is now a fugitive, accused of illegally lending himself $700 million from the banks he had purchased.
 Mr. Ortiz handled the potentially embarrassing situation with candor. He called in reporters, briefed them on the details and conceded that supervision would have to be toughened. But he also said the actions taken against Mr. Cabal showed that Mexico was willing to go after law breakers.
 Mr. Ortiz's banking experience should prove useful as Mexico tries to undo the damage from the peso's slide. Some banks are expected to find themselves short of capital as a result. And American bankers here say that the Government is considering a change in the law that would let foreigners purchase Mexican banks.
 A slightly built man with curly hair, Mr. Ortiz as under secretary was overshadowed by the dignified presence of his former boss, Pedro Aspe, with whom he did not always see eye to eye.
 This past fall, Mr. Ortiz argued that the peso had to be devalued because Mexico could no longer sustain its trade imbalance. He suggested that the devaluation be carried out in an orderly way by widening the trading band that governed the peso's value, and increasing the daily depreciation allowed within the band by about one-third. But Mr. Aspe, and President Salinas, refused to take action.
 A pressing issue now facing Mr. Ortiz is what to do with the peso. Government officials working on the stabilization plan say there is a reluctance to return to the previous sort of semi-fixed exchange rate. The Mexican central bank is studying the monetary index used in Canada as a possible model.
 Under such a plan, the Government would abandon its policy of maintaining the exchange value of the peso in a tight trading band. Instead, it would set a broader target range for the peso, but would not be committed to keeping the currency's value in that range. This means that the Government would be more able to adjust more easily to changes in the flow of international investment.
 But at the same time, it might be more difficult to sufficiently control the domestic economy so that the Government could set attainable goals for inflation and limits for wage and price increases.
 The target range under the new plan is expected to be between 4 and 4.5 pesos to the dollar, officials said.
 In the future, if foreign investors find the local currency too attractive, running up its value and making exports more costly, short-term interest rates could be lowered to cool things off. Conversely, if the local currency is weak, the Government could raise interest rates to make the peso, and peso-denominated investments, more attractive.
 Mexican business and labor leaders are meeting with officials to agree on wage and price controls that will help Mexico through this difficult period. Workers are bound to suffer because wage increases next year are expected to lag behind the inflation rate. Government officials are estimating an inflation rate of 15 percent for 1995. But some outside economists believe that inflation of 20 percent is more realistic.
 A Mexican official working on the stabilization plan since the crisis last week said Mr. Ortiz had been able to step right into Mr. Serra's job "because he knows all the details of Mexico's financial working."
 President Zedillo is expected to outline the plan when he addresses the nation on Monday night. Mr. Ortiz should not have trouble completing the plan in time, the official said.
 "He doesn't like to do things in convoluted ways," the official added. "He goes right to the point."

HEADLINE: New Year's Eve: Sizzle and Fizzle;
Partying at Home If Partying at All: Who Needs Crowds?

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON 

 BODY:
Just last year, Jed Feuer had fistfuls of invitations for New Year's Eve parties where the guests stayed until well into the new year, huffing and puffing on noisemakers, hurling confetti at each other and guzzling glass after glass of champagne.
 This year, he has received not a one. And tonight, he will welcome 1995 at home, alone. 
 "I'm disappointed there's no fun party," said Mr. Feuer, a composer whose credits include the score for the play "Eating Raoul." "But I would rather be by myself than go to a party that's not fun."
 Maybe it's post-Christmas fatigue among party givers. Or that partygoers are worried about drunken drivers on the way home. Some have had their fill of potentially rowdy celebrations, like the one in Times Square, which largely attracts tourists, teen-agers and onetime-only New Yorkers. Others fume at restaurants and nightclubs that charge double or triple their usual prices for New Year's Eve.
 Judging from conversations with dozens of New Yorkers this week, there will be fewer lampshade-wearing blowouts tonight and more people singing "Auld Lang Syne" at home, if they bother to sing at all.
 Just because New Yorkers are not going far does not mean that they have decided it's time for austerity. Sherry-Lehmann Inc., the liquor store on Madison Avenue, has sold 5 to 10 percent more champagne than last year. "But I haven't heard about as many things this year," said David Tindall, the store's manager. "Nothing huge and excessive."
 The word from Petrossian, a boutique and restaurant that sold $55,000 worth of caviar on New Year's Eve a year ago, is much the same. "People aren't having big bashes," said Kate Merlino, a spokeswoman for Petrossian. "They're taking what they buy home or going to a friend's apartment and having a quiet celebration."
 But some celebrations will not be totally silent. Alexandra Hughes invited 100 friends for a New Year's Eve party last year. The wine and the cheese, the turkey and the ham cost more than $1,000. Not this year.
 "I'm just telling a few friends, go to the bar across the street," said Ms. Hughes, a mezzo-soprano who lives on the Upper East Side of Manhattan. "It's been a shaky year workwise, and I have an audition on the second of January. I thought, am I going to be able to recuperate and get it together and go and sing well 24 hours later?" So Ms. Hughes is venturing across the street.
 Paulette Hemmings, a nurse, is not venturing out at all. She said she would stay at home and watch television, counting down the minutes to midnight and watching the ball descend in Times Square. In past years, she has gone there to see the same thing, live and in person. Not tonight. "You get squashed," she said, "and I don't feel safe there."
 Other non-table-hoppers say it's the people at the next table they want to avoid. "It's amateur night out there," said G. David Newman, a financial consultant from Morris Plains, N.J. "It's a night when people who don't go out the rest of the year go out. We go out enough the rest of the year that we don't need to go out New Year's Eve. It's just not worth bothering. And I dislike forced frivolity."
 This is not to say that there won't be crowds out and about, some bigger than last year. Advance ticket sales for the alcohol-free First Night celebration in midtown Manhattan are up from last year and registration for the New York Road Runners' Club midnight run in Central Park is about the same as in 1993. They are signs, the race's organizers say, that the lure of old-fashioned parties is dimming.
 No wonder professional party planners say this is shaping up as a slow night. Margie Beck of Bravo Entertainment in Manhattan, which arranges for everything from magicians to stripograms, has only three events on her schedule.
 "It used to be a very busy night," she said.
 But party-hopping is the last thing Samuel Sanders wants. A couple of years ago, he bounced from party to party on New Year's Eve. He made it to four in all before the night was out. Not this year.
 "I seldom really get a chance to just stay at home and do nothing," said Mr. Sanders, a pianist who is scheduled to travel to six cities in Florida in six days next month. "You're so programmed to always be busy, do things, go out. This is a time when you just want to make believe you're away even when you're home. It's like taking a vacation right in your own home."
 Gerald Celente, the author of "Trend Tracking" (Warner Books), agrees that Americans will be partying less tonight. Stricter drunken-driving laws, he said, have made some would-be partygoers party closer to home -- within walking distance if not within their own homes. Also, he said, there is no longer a national focus on a particular New Year's Eve event. In other words, Guy Lombardo. "Tuning into Dick Clark just isn't a kick," he said.
 But Mr. Celente maintains that the biggest party pooper is a phenomenon he calls "futurephobia."
 "Whether it's Generation X or the baby boomers or the generation ahead of them," he said, "they're fearing the future. They fear it in terms of jobs, in terms of disease, in terms of the economy, in terms of world situations. And so they don't see anything to celebrate about a holiday that looks to the future."
 Deborah Faria of Manhattan plans to pay some bills. That might sound as pleasant as, say, a pre-midnight root canal. But Ms. Faria, a marketing consultant at the New York Convention and Visitors Bureau, has her reasons.
 "I don't want to start the year with a hangover," Ms. Faria said, "and I'm tired from the holidays. I want to feel like I'm a little bit organized, since the entire rest of the year I'll just be falling behind."
 So she will bake a batch of chocolate-chip cookies and, at midnight, treat her cat to a steak.

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

CUOMO LIFTS RULE ON HOUSING HOMELESS
In a parting favor to Mayor Giuliani, Governor Cuomo relieved New York City of an 11-year-old obligation to immediately shelter homeless families. If the new state rule withstands court challenges, it will mean the city can legally leave families to sleep for two nights or more on the tables, chairs and floors of city offices while they wait for shelter. In the last four years, the city has routinely failed to place thousands of families as quickly as the state required, and as a result, state courts have fined it more than $5 million. Page 1.
 
 
NEW YORK CITY
 
BROOKLYN WORRIES ABOUT DEVELOPMENT
 
The Giuliani administration's plan to revitalize the real estate fortunes of Wall Street has provoked concern among political leaders and developers in Brooklyn that an enhanced market in lower Manhattan could imperil Brooklyn's downtown development. Despite successful development in recent years, nearly half a dozen major development sites in the area remain vacant. Page 29.
 
STATE CRITICIZES HOSPITAL IN RAPE CASE
The New York State Health Department has criticized Woodhull Medical Center in Brooklyn for failing to provide "considerate and respectful care" to a rape victim who was left unattended for two hours in October, wearing only a hospital gown, in an emergency room where handcuffed male prisoners were also awaiting treatment. HEADLINE: U.S. Preparing Trade Sanctions Against China

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Unable to resolve a dispute with China over American accusations of piracy of computer software, movies and tapes, the United States is preparing a list of punitive tariffs that the Chinese warn could bring on a trade war.
 Officials close to the negotiations said today that the threatened trade sanctions would involve more than $1 billion in Chinese products. The goal is to force the Chinese to halt what United States officials say are abuses that cost American companies $800 million annually in patent and copyright piracy. 
 A formal announcement of the proposed sanctions will be made Saturday by the United States trade representative, Mickey Kantor, said the officials, who spoke on condition that their names not be used.
 The sanctions will not go into effect until after a 30-day comment period. American importers will be given the chance to argue against sanctions in particular areas that might cause extensive harm to American businesses and consumers.
 In Beijing, Chinese officials were taking a tough line as well, saying they had increased efforts to halt abuses of copyright and patent protections. They warned that if the United States carried through with its threat, the Chinese Government would retaliate against American products.
 "To threaten trade retaliation will not solve the problem and will do no good," said Shen Guofand, a spokesman for the Chinese Foreign Ministry.
 One United States official said the Chinese products that would face sanctions would include all of the most popular Chinese imports to the United States: textiles, clothing, toys and electronics.
America's trade deficit with China is certain to set another record this year, running at an annual rate of $29.5 billion through October. That puts the China deficit second only to that with Japan, which was running at an annual rate of $64.7 billion through the first 10 months of the year.
 Those figures have made Japan and China the chief targets of United States efforts to knock down foreign barriers in a bid to narrow the overall trade gap.
 As for Japan, United States officials reported they were close to reaching agreement on lowering barriers that have prevented American banks and brokerage companies from expanding their operations in Japan.
 Timothy Geithner, a Treasury Department official who heads the American negotiating team, said today that many contentious issues in financial services had been resolved during talks with the Japanese this week in Seattle.
 "We made a lot of progress across the board and we are close," he told reporters. A Treasury source said that both sides needed more time to draft final language but that an agreement could come soon.
 The prospects for the Chinese trade negotiations, however, appeared less promising. But if the dispute follows the pattern of trade fights with China over the last several years, a deal could still be reached before the sanctions take effect.
 United States officials planned to release a preliminary list of products that could be subject to punitive tariffs of up to 100 percent.
 Last January, Washington withdrew a threat of sharp reductions in China's textile quotas after reaching a last-minute agreement with Beijing. Earlier, during the Bush Administration, $3.9 billion of Chinese goods were put on a list for sanctions, but the sanctions never took effect because the Chinese agreed to a series of market-opening moves.
 American business groups have urged the White House to stand firm in the latest conflict. The Recording Industry Association said today that there were 26 Chinese plants capable of producing 70 million compact disks in violation of copyrights and that such practices had cost American music producers $345 million in 1993.
 American computer software makers and film producers have made similar assertions that their products are being copied illegally in China.

HEADLINE: Bond Prices Off on Mixed Economic News

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices fell in a shortened session yesterday, bringing down the curtain on one of the market's worst years in decades.
A report indicating strong economic growth, coupled with a weak dollar, prompted investors to sell yesterday. The price of the 30-year bond fell 12/32 , to 9524/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from its price, rose to 7.87 percent, from 7.84 percent Thursday. 
 To show how much Treasury securities have fallen this year, Hotchkis & Wiley, a Los Angeles money manager, said that as of Thursday a 6.25 percent 30-year bond had fallen in price by 17 percent from the beginning of the year. If an investor had sold that issue at the peak in yields on Nov. 7, the price would have declined more than 20 percent.
The Commerce Department reported yesterday that sales of new single-family homes fell 2.5 percent, the first drop in five months. While that was in line with analysts' expectations, a separate report yesterday from the Chicago Purchasing Management Association showed that business activity remained at a high level in November in that region. Traders said this was enough to send bonds lower in light trading.
Looking at 1994, Elliott Platt, director of economic research at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, said many market participants came into the year under the impression that economic growth was still weak. Growth accelerated and the Federal Reserve repeatedly raised short-term interest rates.
Investors responded by selling bonds. Mr. Platt noted how severe the selloff had been, with interest rates up as much as three percentage points in some maturities.
According to the Lehman Government/Corporate Index, which some consider a good measure of bond market performance, 1994 will be the first time since the index's creation in 1973 that total return will be negative.
In the municipal bond market yesterday, prices were little changed as very few of the money managers who did work felt compelled to take on new holdings before year's end. There was some last-minute selling for tax purposes, with more than $40 million in municipal paper offered on the secondary market. The Bond Buyer index showed the average yield to maturity up very slightly, at 6.92 percent.

HEADLINE: Poor Schools Avert Seizure By New Jersey

BYLINE: By KIMBERLY J. McLARIN, Special to The New York Times 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
In November, Atlantic City became the newest member of a seemingly ever-expanding club: New Jersey school districts at risk of being seized by the state.
 Every district on the list is urban, as are the two school systems already under state control -- Jersey City and Paterson. And with Newark on the verge of being taken over, New Jersey might seem to be moving steadily toward the day when Trenton will pilot every urban system in the state. 
 But not every urban district that fails to meet state standards ends up under state control. For example, Hoboken won back its certification in 1992 after years of intensive state monitoring. Camden, which ran into trouble in 1986, hopes to be certified in June. Trenton and Asbury Park, while still struggling, have also made significant progress, state officials said.
 What is the difference between a district that makes progress and a district that fails, between a school system that can avert state control and a district that cannot -- between, in essence, Camden and Jersey City?
 "We were wondering that ourselves," said Leo F. Klagholz, the state Education Commissioner. "The politics seem different. In Camden, they appear able to draft a legitimate set of education goals and pursue them and be fairly immune to political considerations."
 Other state education officials say the most important difference is an open and responsive leadership.
 "They're not always on the defensive and they are right up front about the criticism brought by an agency such as ours," said Dr. Louis Acocella, the Hudson County Superintendent, who oversees monitoring in the county. "In Hoboken, they were very open."
 Dr. Edwin Duroy, the Hoboken Superintendent, said too many struggling districts bog down on fixing the problems and do not move to more advanced goals.
 "We knew that was not enough," he said. "We needed to challenge our students because we knew they could do better."
 In their battle against takeover, Newark officials have often grumbled that the state does not understand the city's particular burdens.
 But Camden is New Jersey's poorest city; whatever Newark's problems, Camden has more of the same. Students in Camden, the state's fourth-largest school district, are mostly poor and increasingly diverse: mostly black and Hispanic but with a growing population of Cambodians, Haitians and Vietnamese. Their parents often do not have jobs (the city has a 22 percent unemployment rate) and their streets are unsafe (there are 34 violent crimes per 1,000 people, one of the highest rates in the state).
 The district's mobility rates are high -- 35 percent as a district, higher in some individual schools. That means that more than a third of Camden's students switch schools or districts from one year to the next.
 "They move and come and go and that creates nightmares for us," said Roy Dawson, Camden's Superintendent, especially when it comes to state-required testing. "It's not unheard of for us to have a substantial number of students in April that are youngsters we never really had the opportunity to teach, and they are now going to come and be part of our tests."
 Yet state education officials who can barely conceal their scorn for Newark are full of praise when they speak of Camden. One reason is that the system is relatively free of the mismanagement, financial irregularities and political interference that strangled Jersey City and also afflict Newark.
 "Quite simply, Camden's administration isn't under the control of any covert political machine," Mr. Klagholz said.
 Newark officials vowed to resist state control early in the monitoring process. In 1993, for example, members of a state investigative team were arrested when they tried to inspect an elementary school. Two months later the two sides held a 12-hour standoff over district records.
 But Camden officials accepted the state's criticism, said Dr. John Sherry, the state superintendent for the southern region and the former Camden County Superintendent.
 He said the system had taken care of most of the 130-odd directives listed by the state in its most critical report on the system. Lighting levels were brought up to par, leaking roofs were fixed, substandard classrooms were enlarged or shut down. Curriculum changes were accompanied by staff retraining; for example, because it was important for math instructors to teach more than simple computation, they took classes to reinforce their skills in complex reasoning and problem solving.
 "Each and every time those issues have been brought to their attention, they have been proactive in rectifying those situations," Dr. Sherry said. "It's clear to me that we have a district that is trying."
 Camden has five Comer Project schools, developed by James Comer of Yale University and based on the belief that parents should play a vital role in education. Comer schools have mental health teams that focus on emotional or family problems; they hold retreats for parents and teachers; they stress local school management and local control.
 At one Comer school, the Rafael Molina School in North Camden, an ominous-looking chain-link fence surrounded the asphalt parking lot, separating the school from the withering row homes across the street. But inside, the school was warm and brightly lit. In the computer room, second-grade students stared intently at their screens. Two teachers' aides made decorations in the parents' center, where parents can gather at any time for coffee or to plan events for the school.
 Dr. Sherry said the first time he visited a Camden school, "I thought I had entered the oasis in the middle of the desert."
 "I have spent time visiting in other urban centers," Dr. Sherry said. "I came back saying to myself, 'Thank God I'm in Camden.' "
 None of this is to say that Camden is problem-free. Its biggest problems have been low attendance and abysmal test scores, especially in math. Improvements in those areas have come slowly.
 In 1990-91, 9 of Camden's 34 schools had less than 85 percent daily attendance. Last year, every school but one met that standard.
 The district's overall test scores have risen steadily since 1991. But only 47 percent of eighth graders met or exceeded state standards in math on the 1994 Early Warning test. And only 33 percent passed all three parts of the test.
 Dr. Sherry said he was particularly concerned about the district's sixth-grade math scores. Scores for that test ranged from 50.6 in one school to 97.4 in another. The state standard was 86.6.
 Dr. Dawson said the district had tried to turn the attendance tide by hounding parents, juvenile court judges and social service agency workers to get students back into school whenever possible. It took hundreds of students from one school that had served primarily special-education children and put them into regular education classes.
 The district also created or expanded summer, weekend and after-school tutoring programs, and spent more time preparing students for the various standardized tests.
 Linnea Weiland, director of urban education for the Education Department, said Camden benefited greatly from money from the Quality Education Act, a $1.1 billion public school financing program passed by a Democratic Legislature in 1990. With the money, the district created all-day kindergartens in most elementary schools and opened a medical arts high school at a hospital.
 While it is still too early to say whether Camden will be certified this year, state officials are optimistic. So far, 17 schools have been examined, and all have passed, said Winifred L. Tillery, the Camden County Superintendent and a former assistant state commissioner.
 The district is eager for certification.
 "It takes away the stigma that those educators, those children in urban areas are not capable of doing whatever it is that is expected," Dr. Dawson said. "Everyone in every institution wants to be able to say, 'We're certified and we're doing the right thing.' "
 But even if Camden is certified this year, it will be only part way home. It will have regained its certification under the standards in effect at the time the system ran into trouble. But those guidelines have since been replaced by tougher standards, which the district will have five to seven years to meet.
 The new system uses more rigorous tests, focuses more on individual schools rather than districts, and tries to judge schools more on how its students fare than on how it gets the job done, Ms. Weiland said. The new system also focuses on students in the 4th, 8th and 11th grades rather than those in the 3d, 6th and 9th grades because nationally educators are coming to believe those grades are more crucial, she said.
 And even the tough new standards are considered the least a district should achieve. No good district will be satisfied because it has met state standards, said the Hoboken Superintendent, Dr. Duroy.
 "We accepted the criticisms, we accepted the challenge the state proposed and we met it," Dr. Duroy said. "But that was the beginning for us. We appreciate the state, and we want to keep it at a distance."

HEADLINE: Giuliani's Wall Street Plan Worries Brooklyn Officials

BYLINE: By JOE SEXTON 

 BODY:
The Giuliani administration's new plan to revitalize the real estate fortunes of Wall Street has provoked concern among political leaders and developers in Brooklyn that an enhanced market in lower Manhattan could imperil Brooklyn's downtown development.
 Despite the successful development that has taken place in downtown Brooklyn in recent years -- the $1 billion commercial and academic complex known as Metrotech Center is largely completed and what is done is fully occupied -- nearly half a dozen major development sites in the area remain vacant. In addition, the project to construct a hotel and office tower remains delayed, and the hope of developing more than 1.2 million square feet of office space at the proposed Atlantic Center has been placed on hold. 
 "We recognize the importance of economic development in the city, but the dynamic we don't want to see happen is to have Wall Street given a greater advantage, an advantage to the detriment of Brooklyn," said Robert B. Catell, president of Brooklyn Union Gas, the utility, which relocated its headquarters to the Metrotech complex. "Too much has been done here, too much has been invested, too much remains to be done."
 Administration officials, however, said such alarm was not merited. The Wall Street plan, announced by Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani this month, calls for sharp reductions in property and commercial rent taxes for companies that renew leases or move businesses to the Wall Street area, although they must be in buildings constructed before 1975. It would also ease restrictions on the height and bulk of new buildings.
 But Giuliani aides say that the tax benefits being considered for lower Manhattan were similar to those that have been extended to Brooklyn for years, and that the plan for Wall Street would bar the relocation of businesses from the other boroughs. Further, they contended that the long-term economic health of downtown Brooklyn -- just a subway stop away -- would hinge on a reinvigorated Wall Street real estate market.
 "The potential impact on downtown Brooklyn has been looked at, and the plan for lower Manhattan was crafted to protect Brooklyn's interests," said Deputy Mayor Fran Reiter. "In fact, a healthy downtown Manhattan was what made the success of downtown Brooklyn a possibility in the first place."
 Clay B. Lifflander, president of the New York City Economic Development Corporation, was even more blunt about Brooklyn's stake in the Mayor's proposal for Wall Street.
 "If downtown Manhattan dies, there will be no further expansion whatsoever in Brooklyn," said Mr. Lifflander, who helped draft the Mayor's proposal. "New York has to stop fighting with itself, stop cannibalizing itself. We have to have multiple options for keeping and attracting business. The borough distinction is interesting. It is not paramount."
 Downtown Brooklyn has benefited from more than $130 million in city, state and Federal funds for capital projects over the last decade, and profited from hundreds of millions of dollars in tax abatements. Those inducements -- including a 13-year exemption from real estate taxes for developers -- helped bring about the construction of the Metrotech complex, the building of the 19-story Pierrepont Plaza, and the relocation of financial industry tenants like Morgan Stanley & Company.
 Borough officials say the development in Brooklyn has added 15,000 jobs in the last five years, and they hope as many more new jobs will be created by the end of the decade.
 "I'm not against the plan for Manhattan, but there remain great pockets of need in downtown Brooklyn," said Howard Golden, the Brooklyn Borough President. "The city has never properly or adequately promoted downtown Brooklyn as an option for preserving or attracting businesses. It's almost like we are at adversarial positions. And we don't want a gun at our backs."
 The unease concerning the Mayor's plan felt by elected officials and developers in downtown Brooklyn is deepened by the Mayor's interest in constructing express commuter trains to downtown Manhattan from the Long Island Rail Road terminus at Atlantic Avenue.
 Mr. Golden termed the plan "ludicrous," complained that the Metropolitan Transportation Authority had recently removed the rehabilitation of the Atlantic Avenue terminus from its list of capital projects, and said that keeping the transportation hub for 140,000 daily commuters at Atlantic Avenue was crucial to the future development of Atlantic Center -- the proposed $530 million office, retail and housing complex in the surrounding area.
 Administration officials, though, said they were puzzled by the concern about connecting the Atlantic Avenue terminus with Wall Street, saying again that the effort to salvage lower Manhattan's prospects could only serve Brooklyn's long-range interests.
 "I suppose it is the nature of the political beast to have apprehension," said Deputy Mayor Reiter. "But we do not see this all as a threat."
 The principal players in Brooklyn, however, feel they might be at a critical stage in the development of downtown. Ground has not been broken at sites at 330 Jay Street and within the Metrotech complex, and the hotel project, Renaissance Plaza, has labored for years to attract major tenants. Many developers and local officials consider the hotel to be perhaps the most vital ingredient in downtown's economic expansion. It would be the first hotel built in Brooklyn in decades.
 Earlier this month, the Giuliani administration threatened to end the involvement of the hotel's developer, the Muss Development Corporation, unless overdue payments to the city were made and a financial plan for going forward with the 10-year-old project was presented. Josh Muss, president of the company, said this week that such a financing package was "going to happen very quickly."

HEADLINE: Judge Rejects Computer-Crime Indictment

BYLINE: By PETER H. LEWIS 

 BODY:
Declining to prosecute a computer-age crime with telegraph-era law, a Federal judge in Boston on Thursday dismissed an indictment against a student at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who had been accused of operating a computer system used for distributing pirated software.
The student, David LaMacchia, 21, an electrical engineering and computer science major from Rockville, Md., was indicted in April. He was accused of using two of the university's computers as electronic bulletin boards where computer users connected to the Internet could trade illegally copied software. 
 Prosecutors said that while Mr. LaMacchia apparently did not profit from the trading, his operation was one of the largest hubs in a worldwide electronic black market that traded billions of dollars worth of illegally copied software each year.
Judge Richard Stearns of Federal District Court ruled that while Mr. LaMacchia's conduct might have been "heedlessly irresponsible" and had perhaps violated civil copyright laws, the young man could not be prosecuted under Federal wire-fraud felony laws without making criminal the conduct of "the myriad of home computer users who succumb to the temptation to copy even a single software program for private use."
"It is not clear that making criminals of a large number of consumers of computer software is a result that even the software industry would consider desirable," the judge wrote.
Tens of millions of personal computer users routinely copy software, often for legitimate reasons like emergency backups or transferring programs to new machines. But software industry associations say illegal copying deprives companies of as much as $13.8 billion in revenue each year.
Mr. LaMacchia was accused of operating the software repositories, known on the Internet as Cynosure, from November 1993 until M.I.T. shut down the repositories in January 1994. Donald Stern, the United States Attorney in Boston, said more than $1 million worth of illegally copied software, including popular games and business programs, had passed through the system during the weeks it was in operation.
Mr. LaMacchia, who the authorities said used the pseudonyms Grimjack and John Gaunt, was not accused of using the software himself or of profiting from the trading. Rather, Grimjack orchestrated the flow of software in and out of the systems and admonished its many anonymous users to keep the site's location on the Internet a secret.
Judge Stearns, saying "this case presents the issue of whether new wine can be poured into old bottles," suggested that it was up to Congress to draft new laws to deal with copyright issues in the computer software era.
"What the Government is seeking to do is to punish conduct that reasonable people might agree deserves the sanctions of the criminal law," he said.
But, he concluded, the Government erred in trying to use the criminal penalties of the wire-fraud statutes against what was, at most, a civil violation of copyright laws.
Mr. Stern, the United States Attorney, said prosecutors had not yet decided whether to appeal the ruling.
"I intend to raise with the Department of Justice whether it should file legislation explicitly dealing with willful, multiple infringements of copyrighted software to remove any uncertainties," Mr. Stern said.
Harvey A. Silverglate, Mr. LaMacchia's lawyer, said the charges did not constitute a criminal violation of existing copyright law.
"So the Government has taken this old Federal wire-fraud law, which predates computer software, and has claimed they can recast this copyright case as a wire-fraud case and prosecute it as a five-year prison felony," he said. "If the court allows prosecutions of noncriminal activity under these circumstances, it sends a chill down the spine of anyone operating a computerized bulletin board system."

HEADLINE: Pataki to Freeze Nonessential Hiring

BYLINE: By IAN FISHER 

 BODY:
On the eve of the inaugural festivities in Albany, Governor-elect George E. Pataki yesterday made another round of appointments to the top level of state government and announced that, as one of his first acts as Governor, he would freeze "nonessential" hiring for the rest of the state work force.
 Mr. Pataki said the state's fiscal condition -- made worse by what he said is a $100 million hole in this year's budget -- prompted the need for the freeze. He said he planned to issue an executive order covering the 230,000-person work force in the first few days of his administration and could not predict when the state would begin hiring again. 
 "I would expect that given the magnitude of the fiscal problems we face, it could be for some time," Mr. Pataki said in a telephone interview, when asked about the possibility of a freeze. "I certainly would rather freeze hiring at the beginning and reduce the risk of layoffs than allow the state to continue to hire people and have to go through massive layoffs."
 An exception, he said, would be those workers necessary for the "health and safety" of citizens, but even those might have to be approved by budget officials, he said. The possibility of a freeze was first reported yesterday by The New York Post.
 The other exceptions are top officials to state agencies, authorities and commissions, and yesterday Mr. Pataki made another 10 major appointments and named four other people to key staff jobs.
 James F. Gill, 63, a Democrat and labor lawyer who headed the so-called Gill Commission on school corruption in New York City five years ago, agreed to serve for three months as director of the Office of Employee Relations. He said his job will be to negotiate this spring with the half-dozen state unions whose contracts are to expire. The job now pays $101,505 a year.
 Mr. Hill is the second official Mr. Pataki has appointed for only 90 days; the first was Mary E. Glass, named earlier this week as Commissioner for Social Services. Transition officials said that the two 90-day appointments do not reflect a difficulty in filling top jobs, although they concede that some prospects want more money than the state pays and that, given 20 years of Democratic rule, there is no great pool of Republicans with state-wide governmental experience.
 "We have coaxed them into the administration with specific goals and purposes in mind," Mr. Pataki said about the short terms of Mr. Gill and Ms. Glass.
 Among the other appointments, Charles A. Gargano, 59, the finance chairman for Mr. Pataki's campaign who helped raise $14 million, was named to be director of economic development as well as chairman of the state Urban Development Corporation. The job now pays $90,832.
 A long-time aide to Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, Mr. Gargano was from 1955 to 1984 a vice president and part owner of the J.D. Posillico Company, a construction company based in Long Island.
 During his tenure there, the Suffolk County District Attorney brought a civil racketeering suit against the company and two other construction firms. Mr. Gargano was not a defendant in the suit, which charged that the three companies were involved in fraud and payoffs in connection with the awarding of $1 billion in Suffolk County sewer contracts. The Posillico Company settled the suit for $315,000 without admitting any wrongdoing.
 A routine investigation of Mr. Gargano by the Federal Bureau of Investigation in 1988, part of his appointment by President Reagan as Ambassador to Trinidad and Tobago, resulted in no reports of wrongdoing at his Senate confirmation hearing.
 In total, Mr. Pataki has named 25 people to top jobs in the week before he is to be sworn in. In addition to Mr. Gill and Mr. Gargano, four other appointments require confirmation of the state Senate:
 *John L. Behan, a Republican Assemblyman from Montauk who lost both legs above the knees in Vietnam, was named director of the Division of Veteran's affairs. The job now pays $76,421.
 *Edward J. Muhl, who was the Insurance Commissioner in Maryland from 1982 to 1988, was named state Insurance Superintendent. The job now pays $90,832.
 *Edward Mercado, the former director of the Office for Civil Rights in the Federal Department of Health and Human Services in Washington, was named Commissioner of the state Division of Human Rights. The salary is now $82,614.
 *Peter W. Delaney, the administrator of the Westchester County Department of General Services, was named Commissioner of the State Office of General Services, a job that oversees state purchasing and properties. He is a friend of Mr. Pataki, and also lives in Peekskill. The job now pays $95,635.
 Four other jobs do not require confirmation:
 *Robert J. Bellafiore, a former Associated Press reporter and director of communications for the Business Council of New York State, was named press secretary. His salary has not been set.
 *Donna Arduin, a deputy in Michigan to Patricia A. Woodworth, the newly appointed budget director, was named deputy to the budget director. Her salary has not been set.
 *Donald G. Dunn, the former first deputy under the Republican Comptroller, Edward V. Regan, was named first deputy secretary to the Governor. This is a new position.
 *Louis R. Tomson, a lawyer and former general counsel to the New York State Dormitory Authority, was named deputy secretary to the Governor for Authorities. This is also a new position.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
A Forecast of Fewer Sows Sends Hog Prices Soaring

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
Pork futures prices surged yesterday after an Agriculture Department report indicated that hog raisers were planning to reduce the size of their herds.
On other commodities markets, cotton and sugar prices also rose. The Commodity Research Bureau's index of 21 commodities rose 0.29 point, to 236.64. 
 The Agriculture Department said Thursday that farmers planned to keep 3 percent fewer sows for breeding. That would mean fewer hogs for sale next summer, when demand is strong.
A large number of hogs coming to market this fall sent pork prices to 20-year lows, well under the break-even point for hog farmers, analysts said. "The U.S.D.A. surveyed farmers when hog prices made their lows and hog farmers had to be pretty discouraged at that time," said Dan Vaught of A. G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis.
On the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, hogs for delivery next August surged 1.1 cents yesterday, to 44.70 cents a pound, while hogs for delivery in February dropped 0.125 cent, to 39.35 cents. February pork bellies gained 1.075 cents to 41.375 cents a pound.
Consumer prices for pork products will hold steady in 1995 as the packers pay more for hogs, predicted Robert Manly, president of the Smithfield Packing Company. "Supermarket prices never really went down as we approached the 20-year low," he said.
Cotton prices rose to a three-and-a-half-year high yesterday after the Agriculture Department said cotton sales for export soared last week. It said 510,000 bales of cotton were registered for export in the week ending Dec. 22, bringing the total since August to 2.38 million bales, a 68 percent increase from the similar period last year.
"We have had tremendous demand from overseas, particularly from countries that usually can depend on their own cotton supply like China and India," said Raphael Speck, senior vice president with Volkart International in Phoenix. "The whole world is coming out of a recession simultaneously, and people are starved for textiles."
On the New York Cotton Exchange, cotton for March delivery rose 0.49 cent to 90.35 cents a pound, the highest since May 23, 1991.
Sugar prices rose to a four-and-a-half-year high amid speculation about growing demand from China, traders said. The March sugar contract gained 0.28 cent, to 15.17 cents a pound, on the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange in New York, its highest since May 3, 1990.

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales Fell In November

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Dec. 30

 BODY:
Sales of new homes fell in November for the first time in five months, the Commerce Department reported today, and analysts predicted further declines in 1995 as the effect of higher interest rates reduced demand.
 "It's clear this is the beginning," said David Lereah, an economist with the Mortgage Bankers of America. "The housing sector is slowing." 
 Joseph Blalock, an economist with the Savings and Community Bankers of America, agreed, saying that in addition to higher rates, slower growth in 1995 would also contribute to the sale of fewer homes.
 Housing is one of the most interest-sensitive areas of the economy. Many other areas have yet to feel the effect of six interest rate increases by the Federal Reserve this year.
 The United States Chamber of Commerce, for instance, said that its business confidence index shot up to 65.2 in December, the highest since its inception in May 1990.
"Businesses responded favorably to the robust but noninflationary growth in the economy," a chamber economist, Martin Regalia, said.
 The chamber said its bimonthly survey found substantial improvement in economic outlook, sales and employment expectations.
 But the Commerce Department said new-home sales fell 2.5 percent in November to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 693,000, down from a revised rate of 711,000 for October. It was the first decline since sales plunged 9.2 percent in June.
 The report also showed that the housing market was much weaker than previously thought in August, September and October. The October rate, for instance, had originally been estimated at 726,000.
 The weakening housing market was evident, too, in sales of previously owned homes. The National Association of Realtors said earlier this week that November sales dropped to the lowest level in 17 months.
 Many analysts have predicted a housing slowdown as mortgage rates rose. Fixed-rate 30-year mortgages averaged 9.18 percent in November, up from 8.93 percent in October and 7.15 percent in February, when the Fed began raising rates, the Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation said.
 Still, job and income growth offset the higher cost for many home buyers, and others switched from fixed-rate mortgages to adjustable-rate loans with lower initial costs.
 Despite the November sales decline, analysts do not foresee a collapse in the housing market.
 Michael Carliner, an economist with the National Association of Home Builders, forecast that new-home sales would total 645,000 in 1995. Although that would be down from an estimated 675,000 this year, it would be well above the lows of 534,000 in 1990 and 509,000 in 1991, the deep points of the recent recession.
 The median sale price last month was $128,700, compared with $130,000 in October.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Posts Slight Losses In Slow Trading Session

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
The dollar was steady against the German mark and the Japanese yen in light preholiday trading yesterday.
Trading was thin, with most participants having already closed their books for the year. In New York trading the dollar slipped to 1.5495 marks from 1.5527 marks on Thursday. It also edged down to 99.58 yen from 99.70 yen. 
 The British pound rose to $1.5647 from $1.5610, and the Canadian dollar recovered to 71.33 United States cents from 71.15 cents. The dollar fell to 1.3085 Swiss francs from 1.3125 Swiss francs on Thursday.
The Mexican peso has faded in importance for the currency market since the peso began to stabilize on Wednesday and President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon outlined a plan to end the country's financial crisis, dealers said. The dollar was quoted at 5.075 pesos yesterday, up from 4.875 pesos on Thursday.
Some dealers said yesterday that they expected the dollar to move higher next week in anticipation of a strong employment report.
"We're still creating jobs, which will help make the Fed raise interest rates," said Hillel Waxman, manager of currency trading at Bank Leumi Trust Company in New York.
Many dealers expect the Federal Reserve will raise short-term interest rates for the seventh time since February when its policy-making committee begins meeting on Jan. 31. Higher interest rates often help a currency by attracting international investors to the increasing yields of bonds and bank deposits denominated in the currency.
The broad retreat in the value of the dollar on Wednesday "affords people better levels" at which to buy the dollar next week, said Jim Powers, senior currency dealer at Royal Bank of Canada in New York. The dollar suffered its biggest one-day loss against the mark in five months Wednesday, falling to 1.5410 marks in intraday trading as Mexico's financial crisis roiled United States markets.
Many traders had gone into 1994 expecting the dollar to rise on strong economic growth in the United States. But the dollar defied expectations, touching a record low of 96.12 yen on Nov. 2. The dollar also slipped to a two-year low of 1.4860 marks in intraday trading on Oct. 25.
For all of 1994, the dollar declined 10.9 percent against the mark and 11 percent against the yen.

HEADLINE: INVESTING;
Rebound Bolsters Investors In REIT's

BYLINE: By Nick Ravo 

 BODY:
INVESTORS in real estate stocks have developed a case of whiplash this year.
 On top of three years of double-digit gains -- fueled largely by record low interest rates -- the stocks cruised upward in the first half of 1994 even when rates began to rise. Their strength belied the conventional wisdom that the category was sensitive to interest rates. 
 But the stocks soon slid into reverse. As a pattern of rising rates became apparent and new offerings of real estate investment trusts glutted the market, the total return on REIT's, which are pools of real estate or mortgages that trade as stocks, plunged 10.4 percent from June through November. That pushed the group to a 5 percent loss for the year, the National Association of Real Estate Investment Trusts reported.
 In December, REIT's staged a strong rally. Now money managers and REIT financial analysts are pointing to the rebound as proof that the overall decline this year is nothing more than a long-overdue correction in a continuing bull market.
 At the least, the recent surge has eased some of the pain for investors. Real estate mutual funds, which buy REIT's, were the best-performing fund sector for December, rising 6.89 percent on average through Thursday, according to Lipper Analytical Services, a mutual fund research firm in Summit, N.J. And one young fund, American Capital Real Estate of Houston, gained 9.3 percent. Over all, real estate funds were down 3.28 percent for the year through Dec. 30.
 Many financial analysts expect the rebound to continue for weeks as bottom-feeders troll for cheap shares. And for the longer term, they suggest the worst is over for many REIT's. That makes the sector attractive for investors seeking high income, diversification and maybe even a hedge against inflation.
 "We have seen the bottom of the REIT market, and it has been firming in the past few weeks," said James W. Williams, a senior real estate analyst and vice president at Prudential Securities in New York.
As of Nov. 30, the average yield on REIT's was 8.1 percent, compared with less than 3 percent for the basket of stocks in the S.& P. 500. The spread between those yields recently hit six percentage points, something that occurred only twice before, in the early 1970's and in 1990, Mr. Williams said.
 Furthermore, the commercial real estate market appears to be on the rebound after bottoming out in 1993, said Lee Schalop, a real estate analyst with J. P. Morgan Securities.
 But skeptics can be found. Frederick S. Carr, president of the Penobscot Group, a REIT research group in Boston, warned that the stocks could plunge to the depressed levels of November if rates rise again. He predicts that when REIT's do recover, a new generation of companies will lead the way.
 As promising stocks, he cited Developers Diversified of Moreland Hills, Ohio, which owns shopping centers mainly in the East and Midwest, and Post Properties of Atlanta, which develops and operates apartment complexes in the Southeast. Both companies have experienced management, proven assets, low debt and adequate capital.
 This year gave rise to 40 initial public offerings of REIT's, bringing the number publicly traded to 227, up from 66 in 1982. But the industry owns less than 2 percent of the nation's commercial real estate. "The market is still young," Mr. Williams said, "and we are early in the cycle of securitizing real estate."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
An Attempt To Foul Out Underclass

BYLINE: By WILLIAM C. RHODEN 

 BODY:
DURING the University of Arkansas's drive to the national championship last spring, Coach Nolan Richardson used the winners' podium as a pulpit. He extolled the virtues of opportunity, and condemned college presidents bent on passing regulations he felt would have a disastrous impact on some women, many minority group athletes and low-income whites.
Eight months later, as college football enters its final weekend and college basketball shifts into high gear, the fierce tug-of-war over access to higher education by athletes has intensified. On Jan. 9, at the National Collegiate Athletic Association convention in San Diego, the membership will vote to adopt, rescind or modify new academic standards for athletes. 
 The new rule, Proposition 16, is scheduled to take effect Aug 1. The rule strengthens Proposition 48, which governs initial eligibility.
Under Proposition 48, incoming freshmen must achieve a minimum combined score of 700 on the Scholastic Aptitude Test and earn a 2.0 average in a core curriculum of 11 courses.
The new rule increases the core curriculum to 13 courses, and establishes a sliding scale for grade-point averages and S.A.T. scores that requires higher test scores for students with lower grades. An athlete with a 2.5 average could have a combined 700 score; an athlete with a 2.0 would need a combined score of 900.
The controversy surrounding both regulations is the use of a cutoff on standardized test scores to determine whether freshmen can compete and, more important, receive an athletic scholarship.
On Thursday, a report issued by the McIntosh Commission on Fair Play predicted that higher standards would lock out a high percentage of otherwise qualified members of minority groups. The report criticized generations of N.C.A.A.-sponsored propositions (48, 42 and 16) for their "unethical reliance upon arbitrary test scores cutoffs instead of genuine measures of capacity to do college work."
According to the report, "Higher test scores are not the same as higher academic standards."
The McIntosh Commission is a Florida-based foundation that focuses on civil rights and environmental issues. The founder, Michael McIntosh, has a special interest in providing access to higher education for low-income student-athletes.
What makes standardized tests controversial and confusing is that they do not simply reflect what students learn in school, but also what students bring with them from home. All homes are not equal.
Critics complain that requiring a student-athlete to achieve a minimum S.A.T. score of 700 is not much to ask. That may be true, but only in a relative sense. It's like Michael Jordan saying, "It's just a dunk."
Richardson and the Black Coaches Association think that the new measures are aimed at black athletes. Actually, the rule may be a bit more specific in its reach. Proposition 16 is not "aimed" at the black athlete, but will affect a particular kind of black athlete: the playground talent, the inner-city player who attends a subpar, underfunded public school whose environment overwhelms the educational process. Proposition 16 is part of the momentum that will remove the so-called "ghetto element" from intercollegiate athletics.
It has become too messy. Resources in inner-city schools are being cut, the economic gap between haves and have-nots is widening and presidents have a business to run. Francis Canavan, an N.C.A.A. spokesman, said the association is attempting to balance two goals: "One of access and the other to avoid exploitation."
I suspect that if we follow the truth wherever it leads, we'll find that college presidents -- who bask in the glory of athletic success and fire losers -- want to put a fresh veneer on their sports programs:
Increase graduation rates by eliminating high-risk students from the mix. Bolster public image by selectively admitting a more attractive student-athlete. Doesn't have to be "white" necessarily; just from palatable middle-class and upper-middle class homes, private city schools or lush suburban public schools. Wonderful board scores. Passable jump shots.
Momentum for tougher rules, stricter guidelines, narrower passages to opportunity is mounting. Nolan Richardson will preach the gospel of opportunity this winter, but he may find that the congregation isn't listening.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: HOCKEY;
Small Groups From Each Side To Resume Negotiations Today

BYLINE: By JOE LAPOINTE 

 BODY:
Talks to end the hockey lockout are expected to resume today, with small subcommittees from both sides to meet at an undisclosed site. It will be the first negotiation in the dispute since Dec. 17, when the two subcommittees met for the fourth time of that week.
Neither side would officially announce its bargaining lineup yesterday, the 91st day of the shutdown, which was ordered by Gary Bettman, the commissioner of the National Hockey League, in an effort to win concessions from the union in order to hold down salary inflation. 
 Each team has lost 24 games from its 84-game schedule and another 8 have been trimmed, unofficially. Earlier this week, Bettman told the union that the season must begin by Jan. 16 if a 50-game schedule and a full schedule of playoffs are to be completed by July 1. Owners have given Bettman the power to cancel the season if that many games can't be played by that date.
Since 10 days to 2 weeks of training camp will probably be necessary before beginning the season, it is reasonable to assume that the two sides have about a week to make a deal.
The new talks, at least at first, aren't expected to include Bettman or his counterpart, Bob Goodenow, the executive director of the N.H.L. Players Association. The larger bargaining committees, including those two and numbering about a dozen per side in some meetings, last negotiated on Dec. 6 in Chicago, where talks broke down after Bettman reintroduced the concept of a payroll tax.
The players have charged that the tax acts as a salary cap to discourage teams from paying high wages. They say they have already made enough concessions in other areas, including a rookie salary cap, arbitration and free agency to give the owners the "drag" they seek on pay.
During the last negotiations, the N.H.L.P.A. was represented by two of its lawyers, John McCambridge and Bob Riley. It is possible that they will again bargain on behalf of the union. Management was last represented by Jeff Pash, the league counsel, and Cliff Fletcher, the president and general manager of the Toronto Maple Leafs. Fletcher is expected to return from Hawaii this weekend, but Brian Burke, a senior vice president and director of hockey operations, is expected to replace him on the management side.

HEADLINE: Volunteers Have Cleaned Vacant Lots for Years

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re your Dec. 23 news article on Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's plan to allow corporations to advertise in vacant lots owned by New York City if they pay to clean the lots and turn them into parks and playgrounds: 
 Thousands of New Yorkers have been voluntarily cleaning city-owned vacant lots for years, at no cost to taxpayers. The costs have been entirely borne by New York-based corporations and a few foundations.
Among those who volunteer to wade through the garbage and rubble-strewn vacant lots are members of block associations, schoolchildren, church groups, Army Reserve units and participants in summer youth employment programs.
Special vacant lot cleaners include people who have been convicted of nonviolent crimes and are sentenced to hours of community service, prisoners of state correctional facilities, patients in drug rehabilitation programs, and police officers and members of police precinct community councils.
The city's new plan ignores the history and initiatives of my organization, its volunteers and its benefactors -- the corporate community -- while encouraging companies to lease vacant lots, develop them and install their name on the fence.
The city receives $292 million from the Federal Community Development Block Grant program. Yet it allocates only $10.4 million to the Department of Sanitation for cleaning vacant lots.
The city should allocate a larger percentage of its block grant to cleaning and fencing vacant lots and encourage corporations to direct charitable and advertising dollars to groups that use neighborhood volunteers.
 
EMORY N. JACKSON
President, We Care About New York
New York, Dec. 27, 1994

HEADLINE: Empowerment Zone Will Spur South Bronx

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The designation of part of the South Bronx as one of three Federal Empowerment Zones in New York City (news article, Dec. 27) is more than just a chance for improvements at Yankee Stadium or an attempt to keep the Yankees in New York. The South Bronx empowerment zone will provide employment opportunities for a population desperate for jobs. 
 The Harlem River Transportation and Distribution Center and the Hunts Point Produce Cooperative Market are two local businesses whose expansion and growth will provide at least 2,000 new jobs.
The empowerment zone gives the city a chance to recapture part of the market that has gravitated to New Jersey in search of more competitive services. The Harlem River Transportation and Distribution Center is an enormous economic engine for the Bronx. Its inclusion in the empowerment zone is the jump start needed for the city to recognize that the South Bronx is growing to meet the challenges of the 21st century.
 
ANTHONY RICCIO
Senior Vice President
Harlem River Yard Ventures
New York, Dec. 27, 1994
